THE BIRTH OF KANSAS 


## Page 11

FOREWORD 
This study of the birth of Kansas deals chiefly with the political 
problems relating to the establishment of the State government, and 
omits the Territorial struggle which preceded the years 1859-1860. 
The literature of the period is extremely controversial. An attempt 
is made, however, to present as nearly as possible an unbiased his- 
tory, without destroying the atmosphere of the period. To this end 
it is necessary to pay special attention to the policies and methods 
of the leading actors in the drama. 
Economic questions were closely bound up with the political in- 
terests of the politicians and affected their policies and methods. Of 
the leading characters of the period, some were quite willing to 
sacrifice the welfare of the young State for the sake of personal or 
party aggrandizement; others were willing to thwart the formation 
of the State government if thereby they could retain their power; 
still others forsook their faction for personal gain. The very exist- 
ence of the young State depended upon the contest of the politicians. 
Consequently, a close study of the period does not present an al- 
together flattering picture of some of the “fathers” of the State. 
Their activities, so far as they concerned personal gain, were usually 
kept as private as possible, and for that reason it is a difficult and 
often an impossible task to follow them. In defense of the leaders 
and their irregularities it should be said that these irregularities were 
not peculiar to Kansas, but were rather common during the period. 
If they were more frequent in Kansas it was probably also true that 
conditions were more trying there than in many other places. 
This study owes its existence largely to Dr. James C. Malin, Pro- 
fessor of History at the University of Kansas, under whose auspices 
it was undertaken and who has carefully supervised it. His kind 
suggestions have prevented the commission of many errors in judg- 
ment and fact. It comprises the major portion of the eleven chap- 
ters presented as a doctor’s dissertation at the University of Kansas 
in June, 1937, under the title A History of the Establishment of the 
Kansas State Government, except that it has been revised in the 
light of newly available source material. The author also wishes to 
acknowledge particular indebtedness to Dr. W. E. Sandelius and 
Dr. Frank E. Melvin for their encouragement, and to Professor W. 
(3) 


## Page 12

4 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Rolland Maddox for his suggestions on recent trends in State gov- 
ernment. Much credit is due to Dr. Charles B. Realey and Dr. Seba 
Eldridge, who edited it, and to Dr. Domenico Gagliardo and Dr. 
Carroll D. Clark, who by their kind suggestions contributed much 
to its organization. The author, however, assumes full responsibility 
for all errors in fact and judgment that have been incorporated. 
The author also wishes to thank the staff members of the Kansas 
State Historical Society at Topeka, of the Watson Library of the 
University of Kansas, of the State Library at Topeka, and of the 
Public Library of Kansas City, Kan., for their many courtesies. 
For encouragement, for help in securing the original data, and for 
assistance in many other ways, the writer is especially indebted to 
his wife, Sara Lohrentz Gaeddert. To her this work is gratefully 
and affectionately dedicated.

## Page 15

Chapter I 
The Political Setting in 1859-1860 
Bt THE end of the eighteen-fifties it was generally conceded that 
Kansas would become a free State. With this realization and 
‘with the approach of the presidential election of 1860, the focus of 
the national slavery controversy shifted away from Kansas. Once 
the Free-State men found themselves securely in control in the Terri- 
tory, they became involved among themselves in bitter factional 
wars. No longer was it a question of whether the Northern or 
Southern influences would dominate, but whether one or the other of 
the Free-State factions or combinations of factions would dominate. 
This change in the composition of the parties, however, did not 
soften the character of Kansas political controversies. 
Factions were less pronounced in the Pro-slavery Party because 
it was fighting to regain control. Moreover, the Pro-slavery Party 
had lost popular support to the Free-State Party during the struggles 
of 1856-1857, and had to tread lightly owing to the extreme attitude 
of the “Border Ruffian” wing. In spite of this, however, the national 
factions within the Democratic Party existed also in the Territory. 
There were Southern and Northern Democrats, Douglas and Ad- 
ministration Democrats, and later Douglas, Breckinridge and Bell 
supporters.? 
In the Free-State Party the situation was different. The two 
prominent political leaders of the Territory were both members of 
this party, although their’temperaments were incompatible. Gov- 
ernor Charles Robinson was calm and deliberate. He preferred the 
legal method to the use of force in settling the Territorial questions. 
In business he was shrewd and ambitious. General James H. Lane 
was temperamental, active, and very ambitious. He was impatient, 
radical, and preferred armed action to diplomacy. In politics he 
was an opportunist. He had left Indiana a Democrat and entered 
the Kansas Territory with the statement on his lips that he would 
as “leave " y a Negro as a mule,” and if the agricultural conditions 
of ti ‘lc.sitory favored the growing of hemp he was willing to 
1. Herald of Freedom, March 19, 1859; Atchison Union, March 10, 31, 1860. 
(7) 


## Page 16

8 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
make of Kansas a slave State.2. When he discovered that these views 
would jeopardize his political ambitions he underwent a change of 
heart. The Big Springs convention of September, 1855, already 
found him a leader representing the other extreme, willing to resist 
the “bogus laws” “to a bloody issue,” should peaceable remedies 
fail. (The term “bogus laws” was applied by the Free-State men 
to the Kansas Territorial laws of 1855, passed by a Pro-slavery 
legislature. The Free-State party repudiated these laws because 
they maintained that the Pro-slavery men had won control of the 
legislature by fraud.) As chairman of the Committee on Platform, 
Lane had reported the resolution, unanimously adopted by the con- 
vention, to forget their party differences and remain intact as a 
Free-State Party until they had achieved political freedom, vindi- 
cated their rights to self-government, and become an independent 
State in the Union.2 It was easier for him, however, to make a 
promise than to abide by it, for his next politieal move was to or- 
ganize the Republican Party in Kansas. 
The faction within the Free-State Party which later became the 
Republican Party, cropped out occasionally during the territorial 
period and grew stronger with the passing of time. A few examples 
will suffice to illustrate the nature of this rivalry. In December, 
1855, in a meeting following the negotiations of the treaty of 
Lawrence, it became known that Governor Robinson of the Com- 
mittee of Safety had invited Sheriff Jones, the “notorious border 
ruffian,” to the “Grand Peace Party” to be held at Lawrence. When 
the discussion provoked by this invitation had subsided, General 
Lane arose, “his eyes shone, his hair fairly stood on end, and his 
long arms began to girate. He vociferously recounted the devilish 
actions of the border-ruffian Jones. He pointed with long, bony 
fingers to the room where lay the ‘murdered Barber,’ whose pale face 
was yet wet with the tears of his mourning wife. He declared that 
the men of the Lawrence companies which he commanded would 
not be present if Jones was permitted to become a guest, and he 
ended his fiery speech with the threat that if Jones did come he 
would have to be carried out feet first!” Feelings long suppressed 
found expression that evening.* Another incident grew out of the 
question whether or not to participate in the election of State officers 
2. W. H. Stephenson, ‘“The Political Career of General James H. Lane,” Publications of 
the Kansas Historical Society, III (1930), 42, 43. Hereafter referred to as Stephenson, ‘ ‘Lane.’ 
3. Thomas H. Webb, Scrap Books, V, 188, 184; Herald of Freedom, August 7, 1858, 
quotes the Minneola Statesman on the resolution. 
4. Joel Moody, “Marais des Cygnes Massacre’; Collections, K. S. H. S., XIV, 218. 


## Page 17

The Birth of Kansas 9 
under the Lecompton constitution. The party leaders again divided. 
A Territorial convention was called to meet at the West Congre- 
gational Church in Lawrence. It was December 24, 1857. Governor 
Robinson and Thomas Ewing, Jr., of Leavenworth, were holding 
forth for the second day, persuading Territorial delegates to defeat 
the purpose of the pro-slavery constitution by electing a Free-State 
ticket. General Lane was absent, pretending to be sick. At the 
opportune time he sent his political lieutenant, E. B. Whitman, to 
the church, mounted on a foaming steed. Ta his dramatic way 
Whitman reported the bewildering message that a desperate battle 
was impending at Sugar Mound, eighty miles distant, between the 
Free-State men commanded by General Lane and the Federal 
troops. The convention dissolved immediately and Lane had carried 
his point.® 
Another example of a different character which illustrates the 
growing rift within the Free-State Party grew out of one of the 
Kansas anomalies known as Jayhawking. The term “Jayhawkers”’ 
was applied to a group of men who took the law into their own 
hands. The leaders were active abolitionists who called themselves 
Vigilantes, but their enemies called them Jayhawkers. The most 
noted among them were Captains James Montgomery and John 
Brown, Sr. They operated in southern Kansas as early as 1857, 
especially in Linn and Bourbon Counties, and occasionally reached 
into Missouri. The nature of the movement varied with the time 
and place. In Bourbon County it was partly directed against a 
group of pro-slavery settlers who occupied claims abandoned by the 
Free-State men during the Kansas Civil War days of 1856-1857. 
They were asked to leave their homes with notice not to return on 
pain of death. If they failed to heed the warning, armed conflicts 
would follow. In Linn County the claims troubles were mixed with 
the slavery question, resulting in flagrant violations of the Fugitive 
Slave Law. The excuse that the Jayhawkers gave for their action 
was that the Territorial courts were controlled by the pro-slavery 
officers who decided all cases in favor of the pro-slavery men, on the 
ground that a six-months abandonment of the claims had worked 
a forfeiture of legal title under the preémption law.’ Thus many 
of the Free-State men who had been forced off their claims during 
the Civil War would lose legal title. To counteract this the Free- 
5. Thomas Ewing, Jr., ‘““The Struggle for Freedom in Kansas,” Cosmopolitan Magazine, 
May, 1894. 
6. Kansas Territorial Clippings, 1857-’60, II, 352, Montgomery’s letter marked “D. R.” 
Topeka Tribune, Dec. 8, 1860; Hxecutive Minutes, K. T., 1858-1860, p. 40. 
7. A. T. Andreas, History of the State of Kansas, pp. 1066-67. 


## Page 18

10 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
State men set up their own court in Anderson County and threat- 
ened the officers of the Territorial court. At one time General Lane, 
P. B. Plumb, Major J. B. Abbott, and Colonel William A. Phillips 
were prominent members of this court. Threats were followed by 
depredations, murder, the sacking of towns, and border raids, en- 
gaged in by both factions. They kept the border counties of Kansas 
and Missouri in constant turmoil and distress, climaxing in the raid 
on Harper’s Ferry by John Brown, Sr., in 1859, on the one side, and 
in the sacking of Lawrence by Quantrill in 1863, on the other. The 
radical element of the Free-State Party, led by General Lane and 
T. D. Thacher, editor of the Lawrence Republican, defended the 
activities of the Jayhawkers. On the other hand, the conservative 
element in the Free-State Party, led by Governor Robinson and 
George W. Brown, editor of the Lawrence Herald of Freedom, 
frowned on these movements as revolutionary in character, and dur- 
ing the election of State officers in 1859, and especially during the 
Senatorial campaign of 1860 and 1861, they attributed the activities 
of the Jayhawkers to the leadership of General Lane and also con- 
nected him with the Harper’s Ferry insurrection. These brawls 
occurred again and again, in political campaigns, over the railroad 
questions, in connection with policies of State, and in personal 
matters. They ultimately divided Kansas into Robinson and Lane 
factions. Indeed, the early history of Kansas is not understood un- 
less interpreted in the light of these factions. 
In spite of some victories in the ’fifties, the Free-State Party 
appeared not to hold a promising future for General Lane. Too 
often he could win only about a third of the party vote to support 
his views. Moreover, on the 3d of June, 1858, occurred the murder 
of Gaius Jenkins, which eventually gave Lane title to a disputed 
land claim. The dispute between Lane and Jenkins involved the 
title to a quarter section of land adjoining Lawrence, on which 
both men lived. The immediate cause of the difficulty was the use 
of a well upon the claim, dug by Jenkins, but situated near Lane’s 
cabin and enclosed within his fence. Jenkins and Governor Robin- 
son were personal friends and had been prison mates in 1856. His 
murder of Jenkins weakened Lane’s political possibilities within the 
ranks of the Free-State Party and left him without a political issue. 
To organize a new party might open new possibilities. The Law- 
rence men also divided over the railroad question. Lane and his 
8. Herald of Freedom, Jan. 15, April 9, Oct. 29, Nov. 5, 1859; Lawrence Republi March 1 and 22, 1860; White Cloud Chief, Feb. 3, 1859, March 8, 1860. eure. © 9. Ewing, op. cit. 


## Page 19

The Birth of Kansas 11 
supporters wanted to have the railroad enter Lawrence south of 
the river whereas Governor Robinson and his followers preferred the 
north side.° The radical element felt that the Free-State Party 
had accomplished its main objective and that it would gain by 
affiliating with the National Republican Party. It would give the 
organizers added prestige, provide a temporary issue, and put them 
in line for the more lucrative Federal positions when Kansas became 
a State. Just what motives prompted General Lane and his sup- 
porters to action remains obscure. The Herald of Freedom accused 
them of trying to precipitate a civil war in order to plunder the 
people and dissolve the union.!! 
The first attempt to organize the Republican Party in Kansas 
was unsuccessful. A convention was held at Lawrence on the 10th 
of November, 1858. All it accomplished was to provide for another 
convention to decide on “such changes in the organization of the 
party as may be called for by the exigencies of the times.” The 
majority report blamed the failure of the convention on the weather 
and road conditions. The minority report attributed it to the “doubt 
surrounding its legality.” 12 The convention was summoned by the 
Leavenworth State Constitutional Committee, itself a product of 
the radical wing of the Free-State Party. The apportionment was 
based on the Leavenworth Constitution. Many people had regarded 
the procedure as illegal and the result was that only fifteen delegates 
attended. The central committee of the Free-State Party met in 
September to consider the question of reorganization, but gave up 
the task as too momentous for a committee to decide. 
General Lane now took charge of the reorganization movement. 
On the 27th of January, 1859, he went to Leavenworth and met with 
Champion Vaughan, editor of the Leavenworth Times, to plan the 
procedure. On the 17th of February appeared the notice of the 
call for a convention. The convention would be held at Osawatomie 
on the 18th of May, 1859, with seventy-five delegates. O. C. Brown, 
of Osawatomie, son of John Brown, Sr., was influential in determin- 
ing the place, and the apportionment was arranged, it was reported, 
to favor the radical wing.’ General Lane used good political 
strategy in publishing the call, which bore the signatures of fifteen

1 and 19, 1858; W. Hutchinson, Papers, C. Robinson to W. Hutchinson, Dee. 81, 1858.

and July 3, 1858. 
12. Herald of Freedom, Nov. 18, 20, 1858.

## Page 20

12 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
prominent men, headed by those of Governor Robinson and him- 
self.14 Governor Robinson later explained that he had been deluded, 
saying: “I put my name to the modified call of that convention, on 
my return from the East, because I was told that it was nearly the 
unanimous opinion of the old Free State Party that the time had 
come to change the name, and because I did not want to appear to 
oppose the known wishes of my old Free-State friends.” He now 
doubted the correctness of those representations.° Delegates were 
to be elected by county conventions. The Leavenworth Times, the 
Lawrence Republican, and other papers were to give it publicity. 
To lend inspiration to the movement General Lane and T. D. 
Thacher would supply the oratory. By the 5th of March some 
fourteen counties united in recommending that the convention be 
held.1® In due time T. D. Thacher, of the Lawrence Republican, 
reported that Horace Greeley would attend the convention in person 
“to see some old friends” whom he could find-at Osawatomie more 
easily than by traveling over the broad Kansas prairies.17 Abraham 
Lincoln was also invited but found it impossible to attend. Lincoln 
warned Mark W. Delahay of Leavenworth, a relative, not to lower 
the Republican standard in order to gather-recruits. Such a step 
“would open a gap through which more pass out than pass in; and 
this would be the same, whether the letting down should be in 
deference to Douglasism or to the Southern opposition element. 
Hither would surrender the object of the Republican organization— 
the preventing the spread and nationalization of slavery. This ob- 
ject surrendered, the organization would go to pieces.” 18 Thus an 
attempt was made to lend prestige to the movement by connecting 
it with national leaders. 
The conservative wing of the Free-State Party actively opposed 
the movement. They focused their attention on General Lane, at- 
tacking his character and motives. George W. Brown saw in the 
movement an attempt to place Lane and Champion Vaughan in the 
United States Senate and M. F. Conway in the House of Represent- 
atives, and to leave the State offices to the conservatives. He 
reminded his readers that the papers that were sponsoring the or- 
ganization of the Republican Party had also sustained and defended 
the Brown and Montgomery raids in southern Kansas and Missouri. 
14. Lawrence Republican, Feb. 17, 1859. 
15. Herald of Freedom, May 7, 1859. 
16. Herald of Freedom, Jan. 29, Feb. 5, and March 5, 1859. © 
17. Lawrence Republican, May 5, 1859.

## Page 21

The Birth of Kansas 13 
He criticised the apportionment and could excuse it only on the 
ground that it had all been concocted “in Jim Lane’s office.”!® The 
most ungenerous attack came from the Weekly Western Argus of 
Wyandotte. It told its readers that as soon as the “Legislature had 
passed an act opening the jails of the Territory, excusing the 
murderers and robbers of southern Kansas,” a call was issued for 
a Republican caucus in Lawrence.?° Sol Miller, editor of the White 
Cloud Chief, feared that the movement would split the party, en- 
gender still more hatred, and return the government into the hands 
of the Democratic party.?1. To defeat the movement George W. 
Brown proposed a bolters’ convention to be held at Big Springs on 
the fourth anniversary of the founding of the Free-State Party. The 
call was signed by fifty-six members from Big Springs and fifty-four 
from Franklin County. The Topeka Tribune referred to it as the 
“bantling” of the Herald of Freedom. The convention met and 
organized but failed in its purpose. Thomas Ewing, Jr., of Leaven- 
worth, elected Chief Justice of Kansas in 1859, opposed Brown’s 
bolters’ convention. He suggested instead that the party should 
make an honest effort to unite the factious delegates that would 
gather at Osawatomie, “by endeavoring to secure an organization 
of the Republican or opposition party . . . on a just and ra- 
tional platform, and led by honest and conservative men.” He 
wrote George W. Brown that the Free-State organization was re- 
garded by “a majority of the people as worn out. With the prac- 
tical settlement of the question of freedom in Kansas, ‘its occupa- 
tion’s gone’—I grant it has strength in the affections of many 
earnest men who fought under its banner from the Big Springs Con- 
vention till now. But it has strength only with them, and they are 
but a small minority of the people of Kansas. Everything which 
that party name implies . . . is granted now on all hands.””8 
The time seemed ripe, therefore, for the organization of the Repub- 
lican Party. 
On the 18th of May, the convention delegates assembled at Osa- 
watomie to perfect this organization. The day before had been

to the prairies’ for the Leavenworth Constitution); Pat Delvin (the murderer and abuser of

14 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
significant for caucuses.24 Nearly a thousand people gathered to 
honor the occasion. The convention lasted two days. The forenoon 
of the first day was given to the guest speaker, Horace Greeley, who 
was presented by O. C. Brown, of Osawatomie. True to form 
Mr. Greeley defined the great national issues at stake, reviewed the 
accomplishments of his party, extolled its principles, and denounced 
the opposition. The effect on the assembled multitude was reported 
as “most happy.” The convention delegates reassembled in the 
evening, effected a permanent organization, and chose a committee 
on platform. It appears that both wings of the old party were well 
represented, at least they were both represented on the more im- 
portant committees. S. C. Pomeroy and Thomas Ewing, Jr., were 
among the eight vice-presidents elected, W. Y. Roberts and Ewing 
were members of the platform committee, John A. Martin was one of 
four secretaries elected, and Oscar E. Learnard, of Coffey County, 
was elected president of the convention. Learnard wrote that the 
office was a reward for having rallied the conservative element to 
the convention.2> Among the leading members of the radical wing, 
D. W. Wilder and T. D. Thacher were elected secretaries, and T. D. 
Thacher and -W. A. Phillips members of the platform committee.” 
The names of General Lane and Governor Robinson did not appear. 
Thursday morning the platform committee reported its principles. 
A spirited discussion followed which lasted all day. At the opening 
of the convention the opposing factions were contentious. This had 
been anticipated and was one reason why Horace Greeley was not 
permitted to participate in the convention but had to address the 
people in the open air. Before the day wore away, however, the con- 
vention by unanimous consent agreed upon the following principles: 
“The true basis of government is found in the Declaration of In- 
dependence. We hold that the Constitution does not carry slavery 
into the Territories, but [that slavery] is a creature of special en- 
actment, and has existence only where supported by it [the consti- 
tution].”” The people in an organized Territory should be permitted 
to enact their own laws, free from congressional and executive con- 
trol. The platform denounced the Supreme Court decision in the 
Dred Scott case, opposed the extension of slavery to soil now free, 
criticised the administration in power for failing to enforce the law 
prohibiting the importation of slaves, and asked Congress to pass 
24. O. B. Learnard, “Organization of Republican Party”, Transactions, K. S. H. S., VI, 
314. 
25. Ibid. 
26. Herald of Freedom, May 28, 1859; Lawrence Republican, May 26, 1859; D. W. 
Wilder, Annals of Kansas, new ed., p. 255.

remedial legislation. It asked the Wyandotte Constitutional Con- 
vention to incorporate an article in the constitution prohibiting 
slavery, and joined the Democrats in asking Congress for a liberal 
homestead act giving one hundred and sixty acres of land to every 
citizen who would settle and improve it. It opposed the sale of 
Territorial lands during a season of universal depression. It en- 
couraged internal improvement of rivers and harbors and asked 
Congress to aid in the construction of a Pacific railroad “by the most 
central and practical route.”?" W. A. Phillips, W. Y. Roberts, and 
J. F. Cummings opposed the resolution on the nature of the govern- 
ment and the one asking the Wyandotte Convention to prohibit 
slavery. Horace Greeley denied the charge made by George W. 
Brown that he had dictated the platform. John A. Martin came 
to Greeley’s defense and stated that the platform was the work of 
the members of the committee, that Mr. Greeley had never seen it 
until he received a printed copy in Lawrence and that he had “ 
more to do with getting it up than the editor of the Herald of Free- 
dom.” He explained the origin of the platform as follows: 
The three preambles, the first, eighth, and ninth resolutions and the latter 
portion of the eleventh, were written by us, for the Republican County Con- 
vention in this county; adopted by that convention on the 7th of May, and 
published just as they are in the Territorial Platform in our paper of the 14th 
of May, four days before the Osawatomie Convention met. The second and 
fifth resolutions were written by T. D. Thacher, Esq., of the Lawrence Re- 
publican. The Third, Fourth, Sixth, and Tenth Resolutions, and the first part 
of the Eleventh, were adopted by the County Convention of the Republican 
Party of Leavenworth county, and were published in the papers of that city 
long before the Osawatomie Convention met. They were offered in commit- 
tee by Thomas Ewing, Jr., and we believe were originally drafted by him. The 
Seventh Resolution was written by Mr. Cummings, of the Topeka Tribune.?8 
The provisions of the Osawatomie platform bear a closer resem- 
blance to the National Republican platform of Chicago adopted a 
year later than to the one adopted at Philadelphia in 1856.?9 
With the establishment of the Republican Party in Kansas the 
Free-State Party ceased to be important. George W. Brown soon 
sold his newspaper plant. Governor Robinson saw in the party a 
political future, and one by one the malcontents were silenced, ab- 
sorbed either by the Republican or by the Democratic Party.®® 
The Lane and Robinson factions remained, however, and the friction

between them was destined to grow with the passing of the Terri- 
torial days. 
On the 11th of May, the Kansas Democrats held a convention 
at Tecumseh and adopted a liberal platform on the slavery questicn. 
It probably was used to appeal to the malcontents of the Republican 
Party. The preamble proclaimed: 
Wueregas, The slavery question is practically settled in favor of a Free State, 
beyond the possibility of further controversy; and 
Wuernas, We recognize no difference between Pro-Slavery and Free-State 
men as such: therefore, be it 
Resolved, That we affirm our abiding faith and confidence in the principles 
of the Democratic party as enunciated by Jefferson, Madison, Jackson, and the 
founders of our Government and reénunciated in the Cincinnati platform. 
In the platform it was asserted that free and slave States could 
exist harmoniously together in the Union. It approved of popular 
sovereignty as expressed in the Kansas-Nebraska Act, but also as- 
serted that non-intervention by Congress in domestic institutions of 
the State and Territory was a vital and distinct principle of the 
Democratic Party. It regarded the Negro as inferior and called 
upon the Wyandotte Convention to deny him the right of suffrage 
and to prohibit the free Negro from entering the State. The Demo- 
cratic Party condemned the registry and the unequal and oppressive 
tax laws passed by the last legislature. They preceded the Republi- 
can Party in favoring a liberal homestead act to replace the pre- 
emption law and urged the President to postpone the public sale 
of government lands for a period of twelve months.*! 
The strength of the Kansas political parties was tested for the 
first time since the Republican organization in the election of dele- 
gates to the Wyandotte Constitutional Convention. This will be 
treated elsewhere. Suffice it to say here that the Republicans won 
the election by only 1,219 votes, but elected thirty-five out of a 
possible fifty-two delegates. The Territorial delegate to Congress 
and the members of the legislature were elected on the 8th of Novem- 
ber, 1859. The Democratic Party convened at Topeka the 24th 
of August and nominated Judge Saunders W. Johnson over J. A. 
Halderman, both of Leavenworth, as their delegate to Congress. It 
was a close race. The Republicans met at Lawrence on the 3d of 
August and renominated Marcus J. Parrott.3* In the election Par- 
rott won over Johnson by 2,475 votes. In the legislature four Demo- 
81. Wilder, Annals of Kansas, pp. 254-255. 
82. Topeka Tribune, August 25, 1859. 
33. White Cloud Chief, August 11, 1859. 


## Page 25

The Birth of Kansas uy 
crats and nine Republicans were elected to the Council and sixteen 
Democrats and twenty-three Republicans to the House. The Atchi- 
son Union gave the Democrats five in the Council, but other authori- 
ties listed only four. This placed the Republicans in control. 
“Marion, the Democratic Councilman from Atchison County, was 
ousted from his seat,” wrote the Topeka Tribune, “merely because he 
would not vote for the adjournment [of the legislature] to Law- 
rence.’’34 
The fourth Territorial legislature, which met at Lecompton on 
the 3d of January, 1859, was elected before the Republican Party 
organized. The majority being Free-State men, they removed the 
legislature to Lawrence and remained there until they adjourned 
on the 11th of February. Important business was transacted by 
them. They passed the act which provided for the Wyandotte Con- 
stitutional Convention, established the codifying commission, re- 
pealed the “bogus laws” and replaced them with laws borrowed 
from Ohio and other States. In several instances they created what 
was needed. The codifying commission, composed of William Mc- 
Kay, E. 8. Lowman, and James McCahan, retained the Code of 
Civil Law Procedure substantially as it had been enacted.*® That 
the ardor of the Free-State men had not yet subsided is witnessed 
by the fact that they took the “bogus laws” and burned them in 
the streets of Lawrence “amid great rejoicing.”°® A number of the 
statutes introduced by this legislature still occupy an important 
place in the State laws of today, especially the Code of Civil Law 
Procedure. 
The fifth Territorial legislature was elected after the organi- 
zation of the Republican Party. It assembled at Lecompton on the 
2d of January, 1860, but after organization it also adjourned to re- 
sume business at Lawrence. Governor Medary had tolerated the re- 
moval in 1859, but vetoed it in 1860, on the ground that the com- 
pletion of the new hotel at Lecompton had removed the cause. The 
legislature promptly passed the resolution over the Governor’s veto 
and the Republican members again packed up and moved to Law- 
rence.*7 They remained in session only until January 18, because 
Hugh S. Walsh, acting Governor of the Territory, refused to pay 
their expenses. When they adjourned Governor Medary immedi-

ately reconvened them at Lecompton. The Republicans again over- 
ruled the Governor’s veto. This time they remained in session at 
Lawrence to the end.*® 
The dispute over the place of meeting of the legislature very 
likely was a precursor to the dispute over the location of the 
capital. The Topeka Tribune accused the citizens of Lawrence of 
having offered “corner lots” to members of the legislature to carry 
the vote for removal. On the other hand, the Lawrence Republican 
accused Hugh S. Walsh of having financial interests in the new 
hotel and the legislative halls at Lecompton and charged that this 
had motivated his actions.® 
The legislature passed several laws of more than ordinary im- 
portance. Among them was the act abolishing slavery. The Gover- 
nor vetoed it on the ground that the legislature had misconstrued 
the “true spirit of the organic act.” The legislature promptly over- 
ruled the veto.*® Another act pertained to the Territorial census. 
On the 11th of February, 1859, the Territorial legislature had ar- 
ranged for a Territorial census. In 1860 the Governor reported the 
population of the Kansas Territory as 71,770, based on this census. 
The number was less than that required for a representative in 
Congress. The legislature feared that this report would endanger 
the Territory’s chances for admission and declared the census report 
incomplete. It appointed a committee to make investigations. The 
committee reported that the census had been incomplete and esti- 
mated the population at 100,000.41 The Federal Census Committee 
came to the rescue of the Territory and in due time reported the 
population adequate for admission. 
On the 7th of January, 1861, the last Territorial legislature con- 
vened at Lecompton. Like its predecessors it organized and then 
adjourned to reconvene at Lawrence. Here it remained in session 
until the 2d of February, when the Territorial legislature adjourned 
sine die. Before it adjourned, however, it turned over to the State 
government the Territorial debts and all claims of the Kansas citi- 
zens incurred during the Territorial days.42 Thus ended the history 
of the Territorial legislature in Kansas.

As the time for the National Presidential Conventions approached 
the question arose whether the parties in Kansas should send dele- 
gates to their respective conventions. The Democratic Party di- 
vided on the question. The central committee refused to call a 
Territorial convention, maintaining that the party was adverse to 
the idea so long as Kansas remained a Territory.*2 Nevertheless, 
a convention was held at Atchison on the 27th of March, 1860. It 
had been called by G. W. Purkins and S. A. Stinson, of Leavenworth. 
Only eight counties were represented, but the delegates organized, 
passed resolutions supporting Douglas and elected twelve delegates 
for the National Convention.*4 The Weekly Western Argus re- 
pudiated the action as irregular and without binding force.** 
The Republican Party held its convention at Lawrence, April 11, 
1860. It passed resolutions reaffirming the platform adopted at 
Osawatomie. Since it expected the next session of Congress to 
admit Kansas the convention elected six delegates to represent the 
Territory at the Chicago convention, and three presidential electors 
to represent the State, should it be admitted in the electoral col- 
lege.*6 The delegates were instructed to declare for William H. 
Seward as the first choice. In case the convention decided against 
Seward they were to support the party nominee. Thomas Ewing, 
Jr., wrote Abraham Lincoln that in the nominating convention no 
interest was taken in any subject except that of selecting delegates. 
Although three of the delegates elected were first for Seward from 
choice, all regarded Lincoln “a highly acceptable and available 
candidate.” Delegates to the convention were not selected because 
of personal preferences for any one Presidential candidate, but be- 
cause they had done good service for the party. The great majority 
of the party took no interest in the movement because it was doubt- 
ful whether the Kansas delegates would be accorded seats in the 
National Convention.47 Whatever motive may have prompted 
Ewing’s letter, the fact remains that the preceding winter when 
Lincoln addressed the people in Atchison, December 2, 1859, the 
Freedom’s Champion, a Republican paper, never even mentioned

20 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Lincoln’s presence or referred to him in any way.*® The Republican 
people of Kansas were for William H. Seward, who was fighting 
their battles in the United States Senate. 
Sol Miller, an unofficial observer at the Chicago convention, re- 
ported that the Kansas delegates had been disappointed when 
Seward’s nomination failed to carry.4® When Congress failed to 
admit Kansas in its session of 1860, the Republican Party experi- 
enced another disappointment. It blamed the Democrats for the 
failure of Congress to admit Kansas, and expressed its feelings in 
no uncertain language. The Republicans sought consolation in the 
thought that by “heaping wrongs upon Kansas” the Democratic 
Party was digging its own grave and would be “decently buried by 
the people” in the election.5° Lincoln’s election, therefore, relieved 
their feelings. The Commercial Gazette expressed the sentiment of 
many Republicans in its remark that “the people of the United States 
[at last had] declared themselves independent . . . of a miser- 
able oligarchy of slaveholders and traitors.” 5! It is not difficult to 
understand, therefore, why the State of Kansas so enthusiastically 
supported the War between the States. 
Thus, in 1860, the political situation in Kansas was unsettled. Al- 
though the Republican Party had been organized and was destined 
to control Kansas politics for several decades, it was divided in two 
hostile factions whose conflict would be intensified by every politi- 
cal breeze that might sweep the Kansas prairies. 
The economic problems of the Territory were equally unsettled 
and were closely associated with the political issues. This was es- 
pecially true of the problem of transportation. Great interest was 
shown in the establishment of railroads, for it was common knowl- 
edge that the town and community connected with the railroad would 
have numerous advantages. The result was that about forty-five 
railroad companies were incorporated during the Territorial period, 
a great number of which never built a mile of road.°? The period of 
intense rivalry which followed eliminated-a great number of them. 
A number of factors drew the railroad question into politics. In 
the first place, it was generally conceded that to build railroads the 
companies would have to be subsidized by Federal land grants. The 
precedent had been set in the Illinois Central Railroad Act of 1850. 
48. Freedom’s Champion, December, 1859. 
49. White Cloud Chief, June 14, 1860. 
50. Commercial Gazette, Nov. 3, 1860. 
51. Ibid., Nov. 10, 1860. 
52. Index to Laws of Kansas, 1855-1877, pp. 233-235. 


## Page 29

The Birth of Kansas 21 
So long as Kansas remained a Territory the railroads fought over 
the election of Territorial delegates. After the Wyandotte Constitu- 
tional Convention the companies fought over the election of United 
States Senators, and to a lesser extent over the Representative. If 
a company could elect a United States Senator or Representative 
who would favor its interests, its chances for success would be 
greatly advanced. In the second place, many of the railroad com- 
panies planned their roads over land belonging to the Indians. This 
would necessitate a treaty with the Indians, which would have to 
be ratified by the United States Senate. Again it would be a great 
help if the United States Senators from Kansas were obligated to 
the company seeking ratification of the treaty, as will be shown in 
another chapter. During the years 1825-1841, the Federal govern- 
ment had removed the Indians from east of the Mississippi River 
to reservations west of the river. This had brought some nineteen or 
twenty tribes into Kansas Territory. Barely had the last tribe, the 
Miamis, been settled when the western settlers and speculators 
urged Congress to remove them. A third factor involved the Pacific 
railroad. By 1859 and 1860 it was becoming increasingly clear that 
sooner or later Congress would pass a bill for a railroad which would 
connect the East with the West. The Kansas railroad companies 
were straining every nerve to link their roads with the Pacific rail- 
road. Here again it was necessary to have their interests represented 
in Congress. 
To get concerted action on the railroad question a convention was 
held at Topeka, October 17, 1860, to decide upon a railroad system 
for the Territory, and devise some means for securing from Congress 
grants of land for the construction of railroads. The convention 
divided over the question of representation. The Atchison, Wyan- 
dotte, and Topeka interests were pitted against the Leavenworth, 
Douglas, and Doniphan County interests. The majority group who 
got control of the convention were led by Chairman W. Y. Roberts 
and General William Weer of Wyandotte, General Lane’s lieutenant, 
S. C. Pomeroy and Benjamin F. Stringfellow of Atchison, and C. K. 
Holliday of Topeka. Their main railroad interests were centered 
in the Atchison and Topeka, and the Kansas Central Railroad 
companies. The latter was to connect Wyandotte and Fort Riley 
via the Kansas Valley. S. C. Pomeroy and General Lane were the 
senatorial aspirants who, if elected, would represent the interests 
of this group in Congress. Although General Lane was absent, he 
was ably represented by his lieutenant, General Weer, who made 


## Page 30

22 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
many of the motions in the convention. After Lane’s election to 
the United States Senate, Weer was his private secretary until 
Senator Lane appointed him Colonel of the Fourth Kansas Volun- 
teers. Although General Lane was from Lawrence, he disagreed with 
Governor Robinson, among other things, over the route of the 
Leavenworth, Pawnee, and Western Railroad Company. Robinson 
wanted the road to enter Lawrence north of the river, whereas 
Lane preferred the south side.5* The minority group was led by 
Governor Robinson and R. S. Stevens, of Lawrence, and Thomas 
Means and Thomas Ewing, Jr., of Leavenworth. Their main rail- 
road interests were centered in the Leavenworth, Pawnee, and West- 
ern Railroad, which was to connect Leavenworth with Lawrence and 
Topeka. Thomas Ewing, Jr., was their candidate for the United 
States Senate. When the majority group, controlled by Topeka 
men, insisted on representation by interests instead of by popula- 
tion, the Robinson faction in the convention bolted and met in the 
Chase House. The real issue that divided the convention concerned 
the location of routes. The Topeka convention carried on in spite 
of the rupture, and on the second day adopted a schedule, drafted 
by Stringfellow, memorializing Congress for grants of land in be- 
half of the following roads: first, a railroad from the western bound- 
ary of Missouri, where the Osage Valley and Southern Kansas Rail- 
road terminates, westward via Emporia, Fremont, and Council 
Grove, to Fort Riley; second, a railroad from Wyandotte City, up 
the Kansas Valley, via Lawrence, Topeka, Manhattan and Fort 
Riley, to the western boundary of the Territory; third, a railroad 
from Lawrence to the southern boundary of the Territory in the 
direction of Fort Gibson and Galveston Bay; fourth, a railroad from 
Atchison via Topeka to Santa Fe; fifth, a railroad from Atchison 
to the western boundary of the Territory.** Here the question re- 
mained awaiting the action of Congress on the Pacific railroad bill. 
It is of interest to note, however, that, except for the first road, the 
recommendations of the Topeka convention were carried out in the 
main, along with supplementary projects. 
The famine of 1860-1861, caused by the drought of 1859-1860, was 
of great political importance in the Territory. The relief work was 
sponsored mainly by the Kansas Territorial Relief Committee and

the New England Kansas Relief Committee. The former was under 
the direction of Thaddeus Hyatt, of New York, an ardent aboli- 
tionist, who had just been released from prison for refusing to an- 
swer questions of the Mason Senate Committee in investigating the 
John Brown raid on Harper’s Ferry. Hyatt’s assistant was S. C. 
Pomeroy, of Atchison, formerly agent of the New England Emigrant 
Aid Society, an aspirant to the United States Senate. His head- 
quarters for distribution of the relief goods were at Atchison. The 
New England Kansas Relief Committee was directed by Thomas H. 
Webb, secretary of the New England Emigrant Aid Company. He 
had selected as his distributing agent George W. Collamore, with 
headquarters at Lawrence. 
The motive back of the Hyatt-Pomeroy relief work, it appears, 
was partly political. It was generally understood by the opponents 
of Pomeroy that he was trying to “ride into the United States Senate 
on the famine horse.” That Hyatt was conscious of the political 
importance of the relief work is evidenced by the fact that when 
his opponents criticised his reports on the need in the Territory as 
“gross exaggerations,” showing “how much persons are willing to 
recelve” rather than how much they actually need, he met the 
criticism by characterizing his opponents as owners of “‘corner-lots” 
whose actions were prompted by the “jealousy of a rival city,” 
“Jealousy of a Senatorial candidate,” and a stupid idea that to tell 
the whole truth about the Kansas famine would destroy their land 
values and trade, and prevent immigration to the Territory. 
There is further evidence of the political influence of the relief 
work. Early in September of 1860, Thaddeus Hyatt and W. F. N. 
Arny, his soliciting agent, in traveling through the Territory, stopped 
in Topeka. In a meeting held there to discuss the question of relief, 
a Mr. Boutwell attacked the Democratic Party and its newspapers. 
Mayor Farnsworth objected because he did not wish politics to be 
dragged into the relief work. The result was that Boutwell was 
hissed down, whereupon Hyatt left the room in disgust.°® The 
political motive was also reflected in the distribution of the relief 
provisions. There were several Democratic strongholds in the Ter- 
ritory, among them, Leavenworth, Wyandotte, Johnson, and Davis 
Counties.57 It would be more profitable to distribute the relief pro- 
visions among Republican voters, especially in the so-called pivotal 
55. New England Kansas Relief Committee, 1860-1861. 
56. Topeka Tribune, Sept. 15, 1860. 
57. The Lawrence Republican, July 14, 1859, and the White Cloud Chief, Dec. 22, 1859, 
give results of elections. 


## Page 32

24 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
counties of Shawnee, Douglas, and Allen, than among the Demo- 
crats. Linn and Bourbon Counties could be neglected for they would 
either vote Democratic or else support Lane. A comparative study 
of the distribution of relief provisions by counties substantiates 
this view. In Atchison and Leavenworth Counties, both located in 
northeastern Kansas, the aid in pounds per capita was thirty-five 
and six, respectively; in Allen and Linn Counties it was one hundred 
and seven, respectively; and in Wyandotte and Douglas Counties 
thirty-eight and six, respectively. In other words, in the counties 
where the relief provisions would fall upon fruitful soil the dis- 
tributions were liberal, regardless of need; whereas, in counties 
where the results were apt to be negative irrespective of the aid, the 
distributions were very meagre. Moreover, when Pomeroy was in 
the United States Senate, the Hyatt brothers, Thaddeus and Theo- 
dore, who had helped him to get there, secured their favors through 
him.®® 
The relief funds, it appears, also greatly aided Pomeroy in secur- 
ing the money necessary to persuade the State legislators that he 
was the proper man for the Senatorial position. Among the numer- 
ous charges brought against him was that of “double entry.” George 
H. Robb, of Doniphan County, secretary of the Wolf River township 
relief committee, explained this practice in a letter written on the 
9th of March, which appeared in the White Cloud Chief on the 21st 
of March. Robb accused Pomeroy of having paid the teamsters 
who distributed the relief goods in clothing, but entered it as so much 
money given in payment for services. Thus the money was ac- 
counted for by paying off teamsters at the rate of from one hundred 
to two hundred and fifty dollars per day. The amount of money 
collected by the Kansas relief committee and sent to Pomeroy has 
remained a disputed question, but the difference between Pomeroy’s 
last statement of what he had received and Hyatt’s statement of 
what had been sent to Kansas amounts to almost one hundred thou- 
sand dollars.®® 
That General Pomeroy was getting money from an unknown 
source was very much in evidence. It was said of him that before 
the famine he was as hard up as the rest of the Kansas settlers, but 
when the relief funds began to come in he had money to spend.

Soon he was able to build a city hall in Atchison, and to rent and 
furnish a Senatorial residence in Topeka at a cost of $2,000, which 
he maintained “for the benefit of pliant and ready members of the 
legislature, at an immense daily expenditure.” He could operate a 
pony express from Atchison to Topeka and still have a margin left 
for the purpose of “operating upon the consciences of the legislators” 
during the Senatorial election.°° Where all this money came from, 
no doubt Pomeroy knew better than anyone else. The evidence 
points to the conclusion, therefore, that the motive of the relief 
work was largely political. It is even doubtful whether Pomeroy 
would have been elected to the United States Senate if it had not 
been for the drought of 1860. 
The famine had further political importance. It created an ab- 
normal situation in the Territory by forcing a great number of the 
Territorial settlers to accept aid, which caused many a pioneer to 
forsake his business or farm and seek escape from poverty in poli- 
tics. This is one explanation for the corruption that was so common 
in the early history of the State.

## Page 34

Chapter II 
The Wyandotte Convention and Its 
Antecedents 
ITHIN a period of less than four years, four conventions had 
framed constitutions for Kansas. The first of these, the To- 
peka convention, assembled on the 23d of October, 1855, and in 
twenty-three days, thirty-seven of the forty-seven delegates agreed 
upon a framework of government. At this time the slavery ques- 
tion occupied an important position; and yet the delegates of this 
Free-State convention, as well as the voters who ratified its pro- 
posals, agreed to a provision whereby the Negro and the mulatto 
both were to be excluded from the State.1_ The presiding officer, 
General James H. Lane, even proposed to continue slavery in the 
Territory for five more years, but failed to carry his resolution, and 
the constitution prohibited slavery. It also exempted from militia 
duty persons with conscientious scruples against bearing arms, pro- 
vided they paid an equivalent in money for such exemption. The 
boundaries of the State were left as they had been defined in the 
Kansas-Nebraska bill, extending west to the summit of the Rocky 
Mountains.2 Already the convention was divided into a Robinson 
and a Lane group. The test of strength came over the Nebraska 
resolution which embodied the principle of nonintervention. Gen- 
eral Lane and G. W. Smith, of Lawrence, and M. J. Parrott and 
M. W. Delahay, of Leavenworth, actively supported the resoluticn; 
Governor Robinson and J. 8. Emery, of Lawrence, opposed it. The 
resolution provided that “the convention approve the principle of 
nonintervention in the local affairs of Kansas, as enunciated by the 
Kansas-Nebraska bill, and . . . recommended to the people of 
Kansas a strict observance of the principle laid down in said act.” 
The resolution failed to carry, but it required a motion of recon- 
sideration to defeat it.3 
The sensation of the convention was provided by General Lane 
challenging G. P. Lowery, private secretary of Andrew H. Reeder, 
first Territorial Governor of Kansas, to a duel. Governor Robinson eee 
1. Andreas, History of Kansas, pp. 173, 174, J. A. Martin’s account. 
2. N. Y. Daily Tribune, Nov. 26, 1855; Webb, Scrap Book, VII, 5, 7, 71. 
8. Webb, op. cit., 7, 71. “The Topeka Movement,” Collections, K. 8. H. S., XIII, 125f. 
(26) 


## Page 35

The Birth of Kansas 27 
and M. J. Parrott were to act as seconds for Lowery and Lane, re- 
spectively. At the appointed hour for the duel the convention was 
interrupted as General Lane and Governor Robinson, with their 
distinguished followers, marched out of the hall. The duel was 
averted when the seconds reached an agreement. The leaders re- 
turned to the hall and the convention resumed its business.‘ 
State officers and legislators were elected under the provisions of 
the Topeka constitution. Charles Robinson was elected Governor, 
the State legislature organized and elected Reeder and Lane to the 
United States Senate. Before the legislature could enact any laws, 
however, Wilson Shannon, Governor of the Territory, had his troops 
on the premises ready to arrest such officers as insisted on carrying 
out a program in opposition to the government in power.®> The con- 
vention that framed the Topeka constitution was without legal 
status. It had not issued from the Territorial legislature or Con- 
gress, but was the product of an unofficial organization, the Free- 
State faction, which threatened to overthrow the pro-slavery faction 
in power in the Territory. The irregularity of its formation and 
ratification might have been cured by Congressional ratification had 
Congress cared to take such action. Although it was never set in 
motion, the Topeka constitution served as a rallying point and bond 
of union for the Free-State Party. 
The second convention assembled at Lecompton, September 7, 
1857. The convention was called by the Territorial legislature, con- 
trolled by pro-slavery men. They framed a constitution protecting 
the institution of slavery and then submitted it to a vote of the 
people, asking them to vote for the “constitution with slavery” or 
for the “constitution with no slavery.” Since the vote in either case 
was for the constitution which protected property already in the 
Territory plus its natural increase, the Free-State Party refused to 
vote at the election held December 21, 1857.7 The vote was 6,226 
for slavery and 569 against. Although the radical wing of the Free- 
State Party ignored the referendum on the constitution, the Robin- 
son-Ewing faction participated in the Territorial election of October, 
1857, and with Governor F. P. Stanton’s help, who threw out the 
fraudulent votes, elected a majority to the legislature.’ To decide 
4. Springfield (Mass.) Republican, Nov. 20, 1855. Webb, op. cit., VI, 239. 
5. Webb, op. cit., XV, 126, 127. Kansas Territorial Clippings, III, 250-252. 
6. J. A. Jameson, A Treatise on Constitutional Conventions, p. 203; R. 8. Hoar, Con- 
stitutional Conventions, p. 20. 
7. T. D. Thacher, ‘Rejected Constitutions,’ Wyandotte Const. Conv., 710, 711. 
8. Kansas Scrap Book, Biography, Robinson, IV, 58, 59. 


## Page 36

28 _ -University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
the issue they passed a law providing for a direct vote on the con- 
stitution. In this election the pro-slavery men refused to partici- 
pate. The constitution was voted down by 10,266 to 164. Congress 
now passed the English bill, which ordered that the Lecompton con- 
stitution be resubmitted to a vote of the people permitting each 
elector to vote for or against the constitution. The election was 
held August 2, 1858. Again the Pro-slavery Party lost, 1,926 to 
11,858.92 The overwhelming defeat of the Lecompton constitution 
practically decided the fate of the slavery question in Kansas. 
According to J. A. Jameson, an authority on constitutional con- 
ventions, the Lecompton constitution was also illegal. It had not 
been authorized by Congress. The English bill served, however, as 
an enabling act for the future. It authorized the people of the Ter- 
ritory to form a constitution and State government “whenever and 
not before” the population equaled or exceeded 93,000, the unit of 
representation fixed by Congress for electing members to the na- 
tional House of Representatives.!° 
While the Lecompton constitution was pending in Congress and 
the administration was exerting every effort in its behalf, the radical 
wing of the Free-State Party thought it expedient to frame another 
constitution. This would serve as a standard around which to rally 
the Free-State Party in order to combat the Lecompton constitu- 
tion, should Congress threaten to approve the latter. Moreover, the 
speculators interested in locating the capital and selling corner lots 
were also pushing the movement.!! The convention met at Min- 
neola, March 23, 1858, but a townsite fraud nearly wrecked it before 
it got started on the constitution. The convention adjourned at 
once to Leavenworth and produced the Leavenworth constitution, 
which was submitted to a vote on May 11, 1858. In general, people 
were not interested in it. The pro-slavery men again refused to 
participate in a referendum. Although the small vote cast was 
favorable to the constitution, the movement in its behalf was for- 
mally abandoned after the crisis passed. The Leavenworth conven- 
tion had been called before the passage of the English bill and the 
constitution drafted by it was therefore subject to the same criticism 
of illegality as the Lecompton constitution, that it was unauthorized 
by Congress. Jameson regarded both this and the Wyandotte con- 
stitution as legitimate, but his position is not supported by the evi- 
dence in either case. ; 
9. Andreas, History of Kansas, p. 170. 
10. Jameson, op. cit., pp. 205-208. 
11. Andreas, op. cit., p. 168. 


## Page 37

The Birth of Kansas 29 
The constitution mania in Kansas, however,-was not yet dead. In 
his message to Congress, December 18, 1858, President Buchanan 
remarked that since the people of the Territory had rejected the Le- 
compton constitution by a large majority, they were entitled to form 
another constitution as soon as they could meet the specifications 
set forth in the English bill.1?_ On the 3d of January, 1859, Governor 
Samuel Medary referred to this subject in his message to the legis- 
lature, saying: 
It is a question, with the people of Kansas, whether they are prepared to 
assume the weighty responsibilities of a state government. Personal ambition 
should not be permitted to step in between them and their true interests. The 
question should be discussed in all its bearings, and brought to a decision 
favorable to the interests of the whole people. Population has much to do 
with the question, it is true; but to the people of Kansas, who have the ex- 
pense of government to pay out of their own pockets, their ability to do so 
is of deep interest to them, and should not be overlooked.13 
The Lawrence Republican feared that the Governor had intended 
to dissuade the legislature from attempting the formation of a State 
government. Why should Oregon, with only eight or nine thousand 
voters, be rushed into the Union, while Kansas, with nearly double 
that number, should be excluded until it had a population of 93,000? 
The Herald of Freedom opposed immediate admission on the ground 
that the people would be unable to bear the financial burden. The 
great majority of the Free-State Party, however, favored immediate 
admission. The Territorial legislature of 1859 was controlled by 
this party and once more turned to constitution making. In the 
Council (the upper chamber) that part of the Governor’s message 
pertaining to this subject was referred to a committee with J. P. 
Root as chairman. On the 7th of January, Chairman Root recom- 
mended “the passage of a law calling for a constitutional convention 
with such provisions as shall enable the entire territory to be fully 
and fairly represented.” 14 The proposal was discussed and agreed 
to in committee of the whole on the 11th, and referred to the com- 
mittee on Federal relations. On the 21st Mr. Holliday, chairman of 
this committee, reported Council bill No. 80, which provided for “the 
formation of a constitution and state government for the State cf 
Kansas.” After the customary readings, the bill was referred to a 
select committee composed of Messrs. Mead, Root, and Nash. The 
following day the chairman of the select committee reported the bill

No. 80 for the House bill. This was agreed to. Thereupon Roberts 
again took control and moved to pass the bill with an amendment

Roberts’ amendment proposed also that the convention be held at 
Atchison. The House agreed and passed the bill as amended.

House amendments. This motion failed 5 to 8. Another attempt 
was made to change the apportionment when Learnard offered the

of the House, relating to the apportionment, excepting so much as

According to the House Journal the changes proposed in the amend-

The location of the convention now became the subject of con- 
tention. Motions for Manhattan and Lawrence were defeated. 
Finally, on Senator Nash’s motion the Council refused to concur in 
the House amendment and substituted Wyandotte for Atchison.'® 
The bill was now sent back to the House which, on the afternoon of 
the 2d of February, adopted all of the Council amendments. The 
agreement came on Mr. Vaile’s motion, following an unsuccessful at- 
tempt by Wright to change the location. The vote was 22 to 8. 
According to the information available the bill now provided that 
the convention should be held at Wyandotte and that the appor- 
tionment of delegates should be as indicated above, except that Wv- 
andotte County would have two instead of three delegates, Douglas 
County seven instead of six, and Weller County be detached from 
Shawnee and combined with Breckenridge and Madison.!® The 
Council was duly notified and the bill was sent to the enrolling 
clerk. On the 8d of February, Senator Holliday informed the Coun- 
cil of errors in the bill. The election of State officers and the ratifi- 
cation of the constitution had both been set “for the first Monday of 
October next.” Holliday introduced a resolution to correct the error 
by inserting in the 7th section of the act “the 1st Tuesday of De- 
cember, 1859” as the day for the election of officers under the consti- 
tution. On motion by Cyrus K. Holliday the resolution passed the 
Council and was sent to the House for its concurrence in the after- 
noon of the same day.”° The Herald of Freedom reported that the 
House that afternoon had concurred in the Council’s request but 
apparently the record was not entered in the House Journal.?4_ On 
the same afternoon, again on Holliday’s motion, another resolution 
was passed directing the enrolling clerk to return Council bill No. 80 
to the Council. The motion failed to specify the purpose.2? After 
that nothing more appears in the Journals with reference to the bill,

but the statutes show that the Governor approved the bill on the 
11th of February.?8 
At this stage an interesting discovery was made. Some contem- 
porary observers learned, but it has since that time not been gen- 
erally known, that the bill approved by the Governor was a different 
bill from that passed by the legislature. The difference between the 
two measures was in the apportionment of delegates to the conven- 
tion. The bill approved by the Governor gave Doniphan five in- 
stead of four delegates, Jefferson one instead of two, Leavenworth 
County ten instead of seven, and Wyandotte none instead of two. 
It also rearranged the grouping of counties in several districts, plac- 
ing Weller, Breckenridge, and Wise in district number 17, and Madi- 
son, Butler, Hunter, Greenwood, Godfrey, and Wilson in district 
number 21. 
That the bill was corrected by the enrolling clerk as instructed by 
the legislature and without changing the apportionment of delegates 
is evidenced through the fact that G. W. Brown had published it in 
the Herald of Freedom on the 12th day of February as corrected by 
the clerk. Brown wrote: 
The Convention law which we published in the Herald of Freedom of 
February 12th was put in type from an exact copy of the bill which passed 
both Houses, the clerk loaning us the Bill for the purpose of copying it. It 
was on the outside pages, which must have gone to press on the 8th of Feb- 
ruary. The Governor, in the interim, had assured us that he would approve 
the Bill on the 9th inst., and we published that as the date of his approval.24 
The bill published in the Herald of Freedom on the 12th of Feb- 
ruary agrees with that passed by the legislature. How the changes 
in the bill were made between the 3d and 12th of February is un- 
known. On the 9th of March, J. P. Root, chairman of the Council, 
informed Hon. Hugh S. Walsh, the Governor’s secretary, of the 
error: 
In looking over the pamphlet of “general laws” just issued, I find the act 
calling for a “constitutional convention” is printed without several important 
amendments, and similar to the original printed bill, save, that it has the 
blank filled with Wyandotte as the place for holding the convention. The 
original bill was drafted previous to the formation of the new county of 
Wyandotte, which was allowed two delegates in the convention, and Leaven- 
worth county seven: The pamphlet bill omits Wyandotte county and gives 
Leavenworth ten delegates. 
It is possible there may have been some mistake about the enrollment of 
the bill though great care was used by both enrolling clerks and committee. 
The amendments I have no doubt will appear on the journals of both SaaS eee 
23. General Laws of Kansas Territory, 1859, p. 297. 
24. Herald of Freedom, June 25, 1859. 


## Page 41

The Birth of Kansas 33 
houses, if however it is found by the enrolled law that Wyandotte county is 
entirely thrown out of the convention, we shall have to take other measures 
to obtain a representation in the same, please inform me as soon as con- 
venient,25 
This letter is important in that it shows that both parties knew 
of the error and yet nothing was done to correct it before the con- 
vention met. This leads to the suspicion that the purpose of the 
error, if it was introduced by artifice, was not political but prompted 
by some interest or interests which involved members of both politi- 
cal parties. Since it was Wyandotte that was disfranchised, largely 
for the benefit of Leavenworth and Doniphan Counties, the first 
suspicion points in the direction of the Leavenworth, Pawnee, and 
Western Railroad interests. After further consideration it appears 
that this was only one factor in the situation, for the railroad ques- 
tion was not as definitely involved in the constitutional convention 
as the location of the State capital. Probably an agreement was 
made between Topeka and the railroad representatives of Leaven- 
worth. Leavenworth would ask the Democrats to vote for the loca- 
tion of the capital at Topeka, and the interests of the Leavenworth, 
Pawnee and Western Railroad Company would be given increased 
representation in the Wyandotte convention. The reader should 
bear these points in mind during the discussion of the Wyandotte 
Constitutional Convention; for whatever may have been the motive 
back of the change in the law, it was bound to result in further dis- 
putes. 
This irregular procedure of the legislature raises the question of 
the legality of the last of the Kansas constitutional conventions. 
Was the error sufficiently important to invalidate it, or was the error 
corrected by popular ratification and congressional approval of the 
constitution? R. §. Hoar, an authority on constitutional conven- 
tions, maintains that ratification of the constitution by the people 
and its approval by Congress could set aside such errors. On the 
contrary, J. A. Jameson insists that even the approval by Congress 
could not make legitimate what in itself had been illegitimate.?® 
The measure that was enforced provided that on the 4th of March, 
1859, the majority vote of the people should determine whether 
another constitution should be framed, the result of the vote to be 
announced by the Governor. If it was favorable the people should 
elect. delegates on the first Tuesday in June and the convention

26. R. 8. Hoar, Constitutional Conventions, p. 52; Jameson, op. cit., pp. 100, 112.

## Page 42

should open at Wyandotte on the first Tuesday in July. The con- 
stitution should be submitted to a vote of the people on the first 
Tuesday in October, and if approved by a majority vote, State of- 
ficers should be elected on the first Tuesday in December, 1859. All 
elections should be by ballot. The electorate was limited to white 
male citizens of the United States living in Kansas, and those who 
had their first papers, including male Indians. Military and naval 
men were disqualified.?* 
The wisdom of the proposal again to frame a constitution was 
extensively debated. The Republican papers, and now even the 
Herald of Freedom, favored it. In general the Democratic papers 
were opposed. The Western Argus of Wyandotte protested that to 
assume a State government now was to assume taxation that would 
injure the Territory for the next twenty years. “We have as yet no 
land grants, no bridges, no public buildings, all of which will be pro- 
vided for us by the general government, if we are not over anxious 
to pay for them ourselves.” None but those who hoped for office 
under the State government were said to favor its formation.?® The 
Lawrence Republican retorted that Kansas had been a Territory for 
about five years, with a Democratic administration at Washington 
all the time, and yet had obtained “from this kind and generous 
Democratic Party ‘no railroads, no bridges, no public buildings,’ ” 
although the Democratic officials had “squandered and pocketed 
some $75,000 of the people’s money at Lecompton.” 79 
On election day the weather was blusterous with rain, snow, and 
sleet. Only a small vote was cast, 5,306 for and 1,425 against a 
constitution. Leavenworth, Jefferson, Doniphan, Douglas, and 
Davis Counties registered the largest opposition vote, while Leaven- 
worth County cast the largest vote for a constitution. The Repub- 
lican Party in the Territory was still unorganized.®° 
On the 7th of June the delegates to the Wyandotte Constitutional 
Convention were elected. This was the first contest at the polls 
since the organization of the Republican Party in the Territory. 
From the standpoint of party strength the election was sufficiently 
important. It will be remembered that the Democrats had dropped 
the slavery issue in the May platform to rally to its support the

The Birth of Kansas 35 
entire Democratic vote of the Territory. The results, however, must 
have been discouraging. The Republicans cast only 7,374 votes but 
elected thirty-five delegates; the Democrats cast 6,155 votes and 
elected only seventeen delegates. Leavenworth, Doniphan, Jeffer- 
son, Johnson, Davis, Morris, and Wyandotte Counties went Demo- 
cratic by a margin of 806 votes, and twenty counties went Repub- 
lican by a majority of 1,219 votes. Wyandotte County, which cast 
over 500 votes, had been disfranchised. North of the Kansas River 
the Democrats won by a majority of 319 votes and elected sixteen 
out of twenty-five delegates; south of the river the Republicans won 
by a majority of 1,538 votes and elected twenty-six out of twenty- 
seven delegates.*!_ The radical wing of the Republican Party was 
strongest south of the river. This may have been a potent factor 
in arranging the apportionment of the Territory. In Doniphan 
County the election of delegates was influenced by the railroad ques- 
tion. The results were most noticeable in the election of R. J. 
Porter, a Republican who was elected over William Lewis, a Demo- 
crat. Porter owed his election to his interest in the St. Joseph and 
Topeka Railroad. Lewis was not interested in railroad speculation. 
The four other delegates elected from Doniphan County were Demo- 
crats. On this point Sol Miller wrote: 
The St. Joseph and Topeka Railroad did the matter. Certain candidates 
were pledged to use their efforts to have that Railroad embraced in the land 
grants asked for in the constitution; and the people in the lower part of the 
county were led to believe that their salvation depended upon the election of 
those candidates. Pretended Republicans of the most ultra stamp voted for 
the veriest Border Ruffians, upon the question.32 
From the standpoint of county or city rivalries the election was 
significant. Douglas County, with a population of 8,637, received 
seven delegates; Doniphan County, with a population of 8,035, re- 
ceived only five; Atchison County, with 7,729 inhabitants, had only 
three delegates, and Leavenworth County, with 12,606 inhabitants, 
had ten delegates. There is no fixed proportion by means of which 
these results could have been obtained. The ratio for Shawnee 
County was one delegate per 1,171 inhabitants, whereas for Atchison 
it was one to 2,576. As will be seen later, Atchison was also bidding 
for the capital and its delegates would not benefit either of the two 
main contenders, Topeka and Lawrence. While the ratio for Doni- 
phan County was only one to 1,607, before the error crept into the 
bill it was one to 2,009. The ratio for Leavenworth County was one 
31. Lawrence Republican, July 14, 1859. 
82. White Cloud Chief, June 16, 1859. 


## Page 44

36 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
to 1,260. Before the change was made, however, it was one to 1,801. 
It will be shown later which of the two contenders for the capital 
benefited by these changes. 
The radical Republicans accused the Democrats of Leavenworth 
of having imported voters “by the hundreds” from Kansas City, 
Weston, Platte City, and St. Joseph, Missouri.*? The Democrats 
had won Leavenworth County by a fair majority and there may 
have been some fraudulent votes cast; nevertheless, the radical Re- 
publicans of Holton, Jackson County, had forcibly broken up the 
Jackson County convention when they realized that they were out- 
numbered. Blood had been shed; several men were wounded by 
shots and others were beaten on the head with clubs and gun bar- 
rels.*4 Both parties still showed frontier characteristics. 
The Wyandotte Constitutional Convention met July 5, 1859. 
Most of the delegates were politically inexperienced, with but local 
reputations. Various explanations were offered for the absence of 
the experienced leaders. It was said that the organization of the 
Republican Party had deprived the old leaders of their following. 
Another explanation was that the experienced leaders had framed 
the earlier constitutions which had been defeated. The settlers had 
elected new delegates, therefore, in order not to prejudice the elec- 
torate or Congress when the constitution would be submitted for ap- 
proval. Moreover, constitution making was no longer a novelty in 
the Territory and some of the old leaders were not enthusiastic over 
the movement. “Constitution making, like the grasshopper had be- 
come a burden,” wrote William A. Phillips. The convention 
opened without the official participation of Governor Robinson, 
General Lane, M. F. Conway, M. J. Parrott, Judge Ewing, S. C. 
Pomeroy, C. K. Holliday, Judge Schuyler, and Judge Smith. 
For the first time in the history of the Territory, Democrats met 
together with Free-State men and Republicans in a constitutional 
convention. A motley group, representing the “great middle class,” 
Benjamin F. Simpson wrote that he knew only four of them. He 
thought that many members had been more unfortunate in that re- 
spect than he. Simpson characterized the delegates as men who 
“had no personal ambition to gratify, no animosities to resent, no 
friends to favor. Their sole aim and object seemed to be 
to frame a fundamental law that embodied every safeguard to the 
83. Topeka Tribune, June 9, 1859. 
34. Ibid., March 31, 1859. The Tribune quotes the Western Argus. 
35. White Cloud Chief, May 12, 1859; Kansas Constitution, Clippings, I, 10. 


## Page 45

The Birth of Kansas 37 
citizen, in favor of human freedom and human rights, and was 
adapted to the wants and conditions of the people of Kansas.”’%¢ 
This was perhaps overstatement, but it contained a kernel of truth. 
The composition of the group is significant. By profession eight- 
een were lawyers, sixteen farmers, eight merchants, three physicians, 
three manufacturers, a surveyor, a mechanic, a printer, and a land 
agent. As to origin, fourteen had been born in Ohio, seven in In- 
diana, six in Pennsylvania, five in Kentucky, four each in New York 
and Vermont, three in New Hampshire, two each in Massachusetts 
and Maine, and one each in Virginia, England, Scotland, Germany, 
and Ireland.3? 
Probably the most able and eloquent man in the convention was 
Samuel A. Stinson, of Leavenworth County. Born in Maine, he had 
attended Bowdoin College. Tall of stature and well formed, with 
a complexion as delicate as a woman’s and with hair “struggling be- 
tween shades of brown and light,” he was a striking figure. His 
mind was well trained. He was a voracious reader, and able to re- 
call at will whatever he had read. He was graceful and fluent in 
speech. Although only twenty-six years old and one of the unpopu- 
lar minority, he led in effective arguments against the majority. In 
1861 he was elected Attorney General of the State on the Union 
ticket and conducted the impeachment cases against the State offi- 
cers involved. Benjamin F. Simpson called him the “Rufus Choate 
of the Kansas Bar.” 38 
A more impulsive man was Judge Solon O. Thacher, of Lawrence, 
editor of the Lawrence Republican. He had wielded an influence in 
organizing the Republican Party, and his paper had upheld the 
Montgomery raids in southern Kansas. As a leader of the radical 
abolitionists he approved the John Brown raids; in general, the vio- 
lations of the fugitive slave law.?® In the convention he took a radi- 
cal stand on the slavery question. This issue, which for all practical 
purposes was dead, he used to rally the majority party to his sup- 
port. His most impressive speech in the convention was made in 
opposition to a resolution offered by Mr. McCune, of Leavenworth 
County, asking that free Negroes be excluded from residence in the 
State.*° 
John J. Ingalls of Atchison, described as the “recognized seholar
