38 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
of the convention,” contributed particularly to the arrangement and 
phraseology of the constitution. Incidentally, the “broad brimmed” 
straw hat that he wore during the sweltering days of the convention 
“was an ever-recurring subject of comment” with the delegates.* 
During the hours of confusion and disorder when the convention 
was held together only by a frail bond of union, it was the tall 
Scotchman, William McCullough, of Council Grove, who “poured oil 
on the troubled waters.” Unlike Benjamin Franklin in the Phila- 
delphia convention, however, it was not his wisdom or his poise that 
brought order out of confusion, but rather his tall, powerful, impos- 
ing form and his “stentorian voice” that cleared the air when he 
shouted, ‘By God, we’ll have order here.” Only then did the fac- 
tious minority subside into growling, and a degree of peace and 
order came once more.4? There were, of course, other men of influ- 
ence in the convention, some of whom will be mentioned in the dis- 
cussion that follows. 
The convention was organized July 5th. This represented an easy 
Republican victory. J. M. Winchell, of Osage County, was elected 
president of the convention, defeating the Democratic candidate, 
J. T. Barton; 32 to 138. John A. Martin, ofAtchison, was elected 
secretary over William Spencer, 32 to 15.4% Winchell had come to 
the Territory as an accredited correspondent of the New York 
Times. Believing that fidelity to partisan friends was the basis of 
all political success, he managed the convention accordingly. It had 
been recorded that Winchell was the beneficiary of antagonisms be- 
tween certain combative localities, that he was interested in a town 
called Superior, in Osage County, and spent most of his time in Law- 
rence, or alternating between Superior and Lawrence.*# 
John A. Martin had come to Atchison in 1857, at the age of nine- 
teen when the city was still controlled by the pro-slavery men. 
Martin was a successful newspaper man, and through the medium of 
his paper, and with the help of other influences less in the open, 
Atchison had become a Free-State town. At the time of the election 
of delegates to the convention, Atchison for the first time went Re- 
publican.*® 
After the election of officers, the members of the convention took 
the oath “to support the Constitution of the United States,” and

the officers of the convention promised to discharge their duties 
faithfully.“° The convention then decided upon the best method of 
procedure. On motion by S. A. Kingman, the president appointed a 
committee of thirteen members to submit plans. Composed of ten 
Republicans and three Democrats, the committee, on the 6th of 
July, reported two plans. J. Stairwalt proposed to draft the consti- 
tution through the medium of six committees composed of thirteen 
members each. J. P. Slough proposed to divide the work among fif- 
teen committees. The latter plan was adopted except that a six- 
teenth committee was added. President Winchell appointed the 
committees. The chairman in every case was a Republican, nor did 
the Democrats have a majority in any committee.47 Benjamin F. 
Simpson observed that the organization of the convention had been 
controlled largely by Winchell, Thacher, Ritchie, Ross, Blood, King- 
man, Hutchinson, Hanway, and Houston, which he attributed to 
the fact that they were “old stagers in territorial politics.” Most of 
the other delegates were comparatively new in politics and un- 
acquainted with each other.*8 The convention was governed by the 
Standing Rules and Regulations of the last House of Representa- 
tives of the Territory. Near the close of the convention there was 
added a two-thirds rule, providing that “no portion of the constitu- 
tion, as adopted, shall be reconsidered nor any rule suspended, ex- 
cept by the vote of two-thirds of the members elected to this con-

Andreas, op. cit., p. 178, lists J. T. Burris of Johnson County as a Democrat and J. T. 
Barton of the same county as a Republican. Evidently he is mistaken, for Barton, originally 
of Virginia, almost invariably voted with the Democrats and Burris with the Republicans. If 
Andreas is correct the Democrats had a majority in the judicial committee and a chairman 
in the committee on schedule. 
48. Ibid., p. 654. 


## Page 48

40 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
vention.” Here was another safety measure aimed against the 
Democratic opposition and it was adopted by a vote of 21 to 17.* 
The convention next considered the question of credentials. This 
involved the counties of Wyandotte, Morris, and Chase, and the 
Platte region. S. A. Stinson raised the issue on the first day of the 
session but Slough opposed this action as premature. Stinson in- 
sisted, and on Griffith’s motion, amended by Slough, the chair ap- 
pointed a committee of five Republicans and two Democrats to in- 
vestigate the credentials of the Wyandotte County delegates. On 
July 6, the committee submitted three reports. The first listed the 
delegates entitled to seats in the convention. The second, the ma- 
jority report, proposed to disfranchise Wyandotte County, because 
the law which had provided for the apportionment of delegates to 
the convention had limited the number to fifty-two and had omitted 
that county. Any departure from it they regarded “dangerous in 
policy and of doubtful legality.”” They failed:to mention the politi- 
cal reason; however, during the discussion they voiced the fear that 
if they deviated from the law the Democrats would later use it as 
an argument to defeat the constitution. The third report, that of 
the minority, proposed to admit the Wyandotte County delegates, 
maintaining that the journals of the late legislature showed that it 
had been the intention of the legislature “to give Wyandotte county 
two members. That from some cause unexplained to the committee, 
in the bill that received the signature of the presiding officers of the 
two Houses and the Governor . . . the county of Wyandotte 
[had been] omitted and been thereby disfranchised.” Since Bennett 
and Welborn had won over their Republican opponents in the elec- 
tion, June 7, it was the duty of the convention to correct the in- 
justice and give Wyandotte County the proper representation.®® 
Stinson led the fight for Wyandotte against the Republicans and 
was successful in persuading the convention at least to consider the 
minority report and give the delegates an opportunity to be “heard 
by attorney,” but he failed to win the case for them. On the 8th of 
July, the convention resolved to dismiss the case by denying Messrs. 
Bennett and Welborn seats in the convention. The vote was di- 
vided 33 to 18. W. R. Griffith moved that the convention should 
grant the Wyandotte County delegates the right to participate in all 
the proceedings of the convention except the right to vote. After a 
long discussion this, too, was voted down, 25 to 25. The Democrats 
49. Ibid., pp. 588-541; also p. 28. 
50. Wyandotte Constitution, Report of Committee, July 6, 1859. 


## Page 49

The Birth of Kansas 41 
had voted against the resolution. They were “unwilling to compro- 
mise the rights of the people of Wyandotte County.” S. P. Slough, 
of Leavenworth, submitted a protest, signed by the seventeen Demo- 
cratic members of the convention, against the exclusion of Bennett 
and Welborn. This document described the omission of Wyandotte 
County in the apportionment for the convention as a “gross fraud 
upon the people of said county.” The protest was received and en- 
tered on the journal. On the 11th of July, G. H. Lillie, of Madison, 
moved to reconsider, and by a vote of 28 to 20 the convention agreed 
to give the Wyandotte County delegates “honorary seats.” Dutton, 
Graham, Hoffman, May, and T.S. Wright had changed over to the 
affirmative,°! the reason for which remains a mystery. Lillie says 
he voted in the negative the first time, but the journal does not con- 
firm this. Ingalls had voted in the affirmative the first time and did 
not vote the second time. Ritchie changed from affirmative to nega- 
tive. 
On the 18th of July, 1859, Dr. J. Edmond Bennett delivered in the 
convention hall a prolonged argument explaining why he refused the 
belated offer of honorary seats in the convention, without the 
privilege of a vote. He regarded it ‘an offer to compromise the 
rights of his constituents,” to which he could never agree, “believ- 
ing that the Wyandott delegates have as full, as legal, and as un- 
questionable rights upon this floor as any delegate present, and 
representing a constituency whose interest is equal, if not surmount- 
ing in magnitude, that of any other section of the Territory.” He 
called the law which had disfranchised them a “farce and a cheat and 
only in keeping with many other acts of [that] God-forsaken legis- 
lature; that it was only breathed into existence by the signatures of 
the speaker of the house and president of the council being privately 
and secretly placed to it and imposed upon the Governor of the 
Territory as the law which had passed, the prima facie evidence of 
which was the signatures of the presiding officers, behind which he 
had no right to go,”’ but behind which the convention had an unques- 
tionable right to look, “to rectify any intentional or unintentional 
wrong.” He argued that the convention could wield sovereign 
power, that its acts when submitted to and confirmed by the people 
of the Territory were “higher than law and above all law now exist- 
ing or hereafter to be enacted, and subject only to the constitution of 
the United States.” The law which had called the convention into 
existence, “ceased to bind it after its permanent existence.” To 
51. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 27, 31, 34, 57, 58-65, 78. 


## Page 50

42 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
substantiate his point he cited the actions of the conventions of Cali- 
fornia, Michigan and New York. He pointed out that in the late 
election contest in Wyandotte County a leading Republican had in- 
timated in a public speech made in Wyandotte, “that if the citizens 
of Wyandott county expected to be represented in [the] constitu- 
tional convention, they should vote for the Republican candidates; 
that it was highly probable the Republicans would have control of 
the convention. In that event they would admit Republican mem- 
bers; but, on the contrary, if the Democratic candidates were elected, 
that Wyandott would go unrepresented.” He concluded his speech 
with the remark: “Sir, the action of the Republican members in this 
convention clearly demonstrates to my mind that you are unwilling 
to permit Kansas to become a State at present if there should be a 
probability of her becoming at once Democratic.” 5 
During the discussion of the Wyandotte question, J. P. Slough 
made the startling statement that the convention was sitting without 
authority of law: 
We are no Constitutional Convention, if we claim our seats by virtue of 
the act of the 11th of February last. That act of the Territorial Legislature 
received diverse and sundry amendments which do not appear in that which 
received the signatures of officers and the Territorial Governor. And, taking 
the other power, that which purports to be the law and was signed by the 
Governor is not the same matter which passed the legislature. [Then shifting 
the argument to the convention’s power to correct the evil he continued:] 
This convention is omnipotent—so to speak—the highest political power re- 
stricted only by the constitution of the United States. . . . If these people 
have been disfranchised we can . . . admit them or any other persons hav- 
ing similar claims, not only to the floor and the privileges of debates, but to 
the right to vote.52 
Robert Graham, of Atchison, who had been a member of the Ter- 
ritorial legislature that passed the convention act, rose to defend 
the honor of the legislature: 
It comes with a very bad grace from these gentlemen—raising a hue and 
cry about fraud, when they themselves are members of the party that has 
inaugurated all the political frauds in the territory. It is certain that a great 
fraud was practiced there, but how it came about I do not know. The bill 
had to go through the hands of clerks, printers and officers of both Houses. I 
do not charge fraud upon any man, but fraud was committed. But to bring 
this charge against the Republican party, who in all things in this matter have 
acted fairly and honorably—it comes with a very bad grace from Leaven- 
worth.53 
5la. Weekly Western Argus, Wyandotte City, K. T., Aug. 6, 1859, in Mrs. Sara T. D. 
Robinson’s Scrap Books, Vol. 1, pp. 77-79. 
52. Wyandotte Const. Conv., p. 42. 
58. Ibid., p. 48. 


## Page 51

The Birth of Kansas 43 
George W. Brown was less positive on the point that the Repub- 
lican Party had acted “fairly and honorably.” “That clique,” he 
wrote, referring to Thacher, Vaughan, and others, “gerrymandered 
the Territory for the Constitutional Convention so that Leavenworth 
and Lawrence might have nearly one-third of the delegates, while in 
the next legislature, apportioned by the same body, they have less 
than one-fifth of the members of the lower House.” *4 It appears 
that Brown was criticising the radical Republican leaders. If he 
meant to say that the apportionment was prompted by an ulterior 
motive, he probably had in mind the Senatorial question, for both 
Thacher and Vaughan were supporters of Lane. 
Prompted by expediency, the Republicans refused to take the po- 
sition that the convention was omnipotent. James G. Blunt said: 
“Did I not fear that it would be used as a club against us, I could 
acquiesce further in the wishes of the gentlemen. But I conceive 
that our only safety is in confining ourselves to the letter of the 
law.” The delegates of course were debating the question whether 
the convention was sovereign and could alter the legislative instruc- 
tions. It is questionable, indeed, whether the convention had the 
authority to alter the legislative act though fraudulently enacted, 
and though it had disfranchised Wyandotte County. The authori- 
ties, Dodd and Hoar, agree that a convention may issue orders to 
fill vacancies; Jameson maintains that it cannot even do that, be- 
cause it would render the convention, pro tanto, self appointing. 
The question was decided on a political rather than on a constitu- 
tional basis. 
After spending the first week in effecting an organization, the con- 
vention was ready to frame the constitution. 
54. Herald of Freedom, June 18, 1859. 


## Page 52

Chapter III 
The Wyandotte Constitution of 1859 
N FRAMING a constitution it is common practice for a conven- 
tion to consult other constitutions and decide upon one asa basis 
for discussion. The Wyandotte convention fell in line with this 
practice. After it had devoted some time to the consideration of 
other State documents, it selected the Ohio constitution of 1850 as a 
basis of action. The vote was twenty-five for the Ohio, twenty- 
three for the Indiana, and one for the Kentucky constitution. The 
convention then proceeded to frame the constitution. Only the 
most controversial problems will be discussed. 
The article on the executive department was introduced by John 
P. Greer, chairman of the committee, and the convention devoted 
most of its time to this article, debating what to do about the sec- 
ond section, which provided for the disposition of election returns. 
The committee’s draft provided that the returning officers should 
submit the results of the elections to the.President of the Senate, 
“who, during the first week of the session” should “open and publish 
them, and declare the result in the presence of the majority of the 
members of each House of the General Assembly.” In case of a 
tie in the election of an officer of the executive department, the two 
houses of the legislature, by Joint vote, should decide the case.1 The 
question was to whom the election returns should be sent; there was 
also the question whether the convention would be performing a 
function that belonged more properly to the legislature, if it should 
provide that the township officers must submit results to the county 
board of canvassers and the county board to the State board of 
canvassers. Samuel Hipple, of Pennsylvania, introduced a sub- 
stitute motion which provided that the returns be directed by the 
returning officers to the President of the Senate and Speaker of 
the House. Thacher proposed to substitute the words, “clerk of the 
several counties” for “returning officers.”” With minor changes the 
committee of the whole adopted the substitute motion. J.T. Burris, 
of Johnson County, had introduced a substitute section in committee 
of the whole but this had been disregarded. When the sections of 
the article were considered in convention, Burris again submitted 
1. Wyandotte Constitutional Convention, p. 346. 
(44) 


## Page 53

The Birth of Kansas 45 
his proposal as a substitute for the substitute. This time it was 
adopted. The section now provided that until otherwise provided 
for by law the clerks of the boards of the county canvassers should 
transmit the returns of every election to the President of the Senate, 
who should “open and publish them in the presence of both houses of 
the legislature in joint convention assembled.”? 
The article was next referred to the committee on phraseology 
and arrangement, of which J. J. Ingalls was chairman. The com- 
mittee reported on July 25, and among other changes, proposed an 
amendment of section two, providing for the transmission of the re- 
turns to the Secretary of State instead of to the President of the 
Senate. The change was agreed to and the article was referred back 
to the committee for arrangement and enrollment. No further 
changes were reported, yet section two in the adopted constitution 
provides for a State board of canvassers composed of the Secretary 
of State, the Lieutenant Governor, and the Attorney General. How 
this got into the constitution the Journal fails to explain. Thacher 
had suggested it early in the discussion but not in the form of a 
motion or amendment.® 
The veto power of the Governor was discussed at some length. 
Although the convention treated this matter in connection with the 
legislative department, it may be discussed here with the executive 
department. William C. McDowell of Leavenworth would have 
denied the veto power to the Governor. This would enable the 
people to locate the blame, if laws had been passed that misrepre- 
sented them. He thought it absurd to entrust so much power to one 
man. He was supported by J. Ritchie of Topeka who was not 
convinced that just because a man had been promoted to the chair of 
Governor, he therefore possessed “any better judgment than when 
he stood among the people.” J. P. Greer was opposed to the veto 
power unless it could be so qualified that the two houses by ma- 
jority vote could override it. If put in that form it would check 
hasty legislation. S. D. Houston preferred to give the legislature the 
power to override the veto by a three-fifths vote. J. G. Blunt, re- 
minding the delegates that the Ohio constitution adopted by the con- 
vention as the basis for action had omitted the clause, favored the 
2. Ibid., pp. 353-355. The original section was taken from the Ohio constitution, the 
Hipple substitute from the Iowa constitution. JIbid., pp. 350, 352-355. 
3. Ibid., pp. 348, 466. Another reference is made to the Executive Department on p. 
541, where it is stated that on Thacher’s motion the convention resumed the work of verifica- 
tion of the article, but the journal omits all details. Miss Rosa M. Perdue in her article on 
“Sources of the Wyandotte Constitution,’ wrote that the section was amended so as to in- 
clude the Board of State Canvassers, but the present writer has failed to find the amend- 
ment. Ibid., p. 679. 


## Page 54

46 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
veto but would authorize the legislature to overrule it by a majority 
vote of its members. On this question party lines were forgotten. 
S. A. Stinson opposed his colleague, William C. McDowell. Stinson 
regarded the veto power as a most wholesome restriction upon the 
legislature, and in this was supported by J. P. Slough and 8. O. 
Thacher who thought it unwise to depart from “old landmarks.” 
Slough and W. R. Griffith referred to the Federal constitution to 
support their contention in favor of the veto power. The proposals 
ranged from one extreme to the other. The convention finally de- 
cided to retain the veto but permitted the legislature to override it 
by a two-thirds vote. Thus the convention fell in line with the 
trends of the time and strengthened the power of the executive at 
the expense of the legislature. 
The discussion concerning the legislative department centered 
around the questions of representation, the legislative power of the 
Senate, and apportionment. On the 12th of July, Thacher, chair- 
man of the legislative committee, reported twenty-eight sections. 
Section two provided that the first Assembly should consist of seven- 
ty-five members elected for one year, and the Senate of twenty- 
five members elected for two years. After the first election the 
number should be determined by law. The Democrats, Slough and 
McDowell, proposed to limit the House to fifty members, while 
Barton moved to reduce it to sixty, but the Republicans rallied to 
the support of Thacher and all attempts to reduce the number failed. 
Upon J. W. Forman’s motion, however, the convention agreed that 
the number should never exceed one hundred representatives and 
thirty-five senators. The Democratic arguments in favor of reduc- 
tion were based on economy, whereas the Republicans based their 
contentions on geographic representation, insisting that each county 
should have at least one representative in the House.®> Whether 
these were the real reasons for the stand taken by the parties is 
doubtful. The Democrats knew that they held a majority in the 
older counties, and the Republicans no doubt remembered that the 
late settlers had come largely from the northern States. 
The 12th section provided that bills might originate in either 
house. While the convention was sitting in committee of the whole, 
with Hipple in the chair, President Winchell submitted the substi- eS 
4. Ibid., pp. 129-132. 
6. 1oid.; pp. 11.25 115-121), 


## Page 55

The Birth of Kansas 47 
tute that all bills should originate in the House of Representatives 
and be subject to amendment or rejection in the Senate. His argu- 
ment was that this would eliminate confusion in the legislature and 
prevent errors. It would elevate the Senate to the position of a 
board of censors and critics. As a precedent he pointed to the fact 
that all revenue bills according to most constitutions originate in 
the popular branch. Thacher strenuously opposed the substitute, 
asserting it had no precedent and that it was unwise to experiment.® 
In the first contention he was mistaken. The Virginia constitutions 
of 1776 and 1830 both contained similar clauses.?’ Thacher opposed 
it further, however, on the grounds that it would compel the Senate 
to wait on the House of Representatives, and in fact would turn 
the senators into lobby members of the House. Ritchie said that if 
the Senate should have the same power as the House of Representa- 
tives in originating bills he would favor but one body. The opposi- 
tion was powerless, however, and the substitute was adopted 28 to 
18 
A much more troublesome problem was the apportionment of the 
State. A committee of thirteen was appointed by the President to 
work out a plan. Headed by Preston, of Shawnee, the group com- 
prised ten Republicans, including Thacher, and three Democrats. 
The committee was to get its information on population from the 
census taken under the act of the last legislative assembly. On the 
20th of July was duly reported a plan of apportionment which sug- 
gested, among other things, that every organized county should have 
‘at least one representative and that unorganized counties should be 
attached to those that were organized. On this proposal Stinson 
remarked: “It is not square miles and acres that are to be rep- 
resented, but people; and although there may be hundreds of 
square miles with nothing on the land, it does not work injustice if 
these square miles are not represented.” The report was recom- 
mitted for further consideration. On the 26th of July the com- 
mittee reported a second time. This time the majority plan provided 
for a Republican scheme of apportionment which precluded a Demo- 
cratic victory in either house. The report contained three sections, 
the third section providing for the following plan of representation:

The Democrats exposed the gerrymandering scheme to the conven- 
tion, but without results. William C. McDowell said that Brown 
County with a safe Republican margin had been added to Atchison, 
a doubtful county, to give both a safe Republican margin. In the 
eighth district Johnson and Wyandotte Counties were Democratic, 
but the large Republican majority of Douglas County would give 
the district thirteen Republican representatives. The convention 
had instructed the committee to give each organized county at least 
one representative. This was defeated in effect by the scheme of 
gerrymandering in districts two and eight. McDowell pointed out 
that in this manner the committee had disfranchised all the Demo- 
cratic counties except Doniphan and Leavenworth. The reason they 
left them alone was because they knew that to attach any county 
to these Democratic strongholds would only add more Democratic 
representatives. The three Democrats of the committee submitted 
a minority report but it was of no avail. The majority report was 
adopted by a vote of 26 to 15. The Democrats, led by Stinson, en- 
tered a protest against the action of the convention, pointing out 
that the apportionment was “based upon no rule of representation, 
and was evidently devised to meet the necessity of a political party.” 
The majority had prevented a full discussion of the measure by 
moving the previous question.? The St. Joseph County Forum and 
the Herald of Freedom severely criticized the action of the majority. 
The Herald regretted ‘that so good an instrument should be marred 
9. Ibid., pp. 37-38, 66-68, 261, 357-861, 475-479, 482, 518, 519, 550. 


## Page 57

The Birth of Kansas 49 
by a foul blotch,” and regarded the disfranchisement as “equal, if 
not greater than any practiced by the Border Ruffians. The ap- 
portionment in the Lecompton Constitution was fair and honorable 
in comparison with this.’”° 
On the 18th of July, S. A. Kingman, chairman of the committee 
on judiciary, presented a report of twenty-one sections. It pro- 
vided that the judicial power of the State should be vested in a 
Supreme Court, district courts, probate courts, justices of the peace, 
and such other courts inferior to the Supreme Court as may be pro- 
vided by law. There was but little debate on the sections except 
number 14 (now 13, for one of the sections was omitted). This had 
to do with the compensation of the judges of the Supreme and 
district courts. The convention, while in committee of the whole 
and on James Blood’s motion, had stricken from the original section 
the clause which provided a minimum salary of $2,000. Most of 
the eighteen lawyers had opposed it. When therefore the section 
was considered in the convention, strenuous efforts were made to 
guarantee the judges a reasonable minimum salary. A series of 
amendments was introduced proposing minimum salaries varying 
from $1,500 to $2,000. The convention finally adopted the clause 
introduced by Burris and amended by Slough providing that “Jus- 
tices of the Supreme Court and judges shall receive such compensa- 
tion as may be provided by law, and not more than $2,000.” Slough 
explained that the intention of his amendment was to make the com- 
pensation $1,500 to each judge. The convention consented to the 
change. The final copy read, however, “‘not less than $1,500 to each 
justice or judge each year.” On Stinson’s motion the Supreme Court 
judges were given the ranking of chief justice and associate justices. 
On McDowell’s motion the number of justices of the peace for each 
county was increased from one to two. In the discussions over the 
article the convention divided on the basis of their professional in- 
terests rather than along political lines. Usually the merchants and 
lawyers voted for higher salaried judges.™ 
The question of suffrage was introduced on July 11, by William 
Hutchinson, of Lawrence, who presented a petition of 252 residents 
of Douglas and Shawnee Counties on the subject of elective fran- 
chise and the rights of females in connection therewith. A long de- 
bate ensued over the questions of receiving the memorial and of 
10. Herald of Freedom, Aug. 18, 1859. For reference to the St. Joseph County Forum 
see Kansas Constitutions, Clippings, I, 45. 
11. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 145, 149, 155, 158, 159-167, 581. 
4—14 


## Page 58

50 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
hearing Mrs. Nichols, who had been sent to the convention by the 
women of southern Kansas to advocate the rights of women. Some- 
what prophetic, even if rhetorical, were the words of S. D. Houston, 
of Riley, who said: 
The time is coming when it will be impossible for any power in this land to 
curtail one single human right. If the Republican party, which has come into 
power for the moment, shall ignore these rights, it will devolve upon some 
other party to develop them. But, sir, whilst we are in power, I would say to 
you gentlemen, let no man hesitate to do right—let us have the advantage of 
the whole field as it spreads itself before our vision. Gentlemen may laugh, but 
it is one thing to laugh and another thing to have a solid pillar of stone to 
stand upon.12 
J. P. Greer doubted the propriety and policy of allowing persons 
who petition the convention to come in and be heard. He thought 
the convention fully competent to dispose of all questions that 
legitimately came before it. William Hutchinson enthusiastically 
pleaded the cause of Mrs. Nichols. Hutchinson declared: 
I am sorry that a question agitating the public mind so universally as 
women’s rights—calling together intelligent audiences in every State in the 
Union—should have become, in our estimation, of so slight importance as to 
be thought unbecoming the dignity of the body to be heard in open conven- 
tion! I think no evil consequences can result fram giving our attention to a 
question so interesting to half the people of the Territory. The day is coming 
when open and fair discussion of all questions pertaining to the constitutional 
rights of women as well as men, must be heard before all such bodies as 
this.18 
The convention agreed to permit the committees on judiciary and 
elective franchise to receive the memorial, and to allow Mrs. Nichols 
the use of the hall for an evening. The petition presented to the con- 
vention by Mrs. Nichols read: 
Greeting: We, the undersigned citizens of Kansas Territory, do respect- 
fully represent to your Honorable Body that, whereas, the women of the 
State have individually an evident common interest with its men in the pro- 
tection of life, liberty, property and intelligent culture; and whereas, the en- 
joyment of these guarantees involves the possession of equal political rights: 
Therefore we, the undersigned, being of full age, do respectifully petition and 
protest against any Constitutional distinctions based on difference of sex. To 
this end your petitioners will ever pray.14 
The responsible committee replied, July 14, that they did not 
deny that sex involved women “in greater and more complicated re- 
sponsibilities,’ but that they were compelled to dissent from the 
conclusion of the petitioners. The rights of women were safe in 
ES Sa Nt ES ye a 
12. Ibid., pp. 72-74. 
18. Ibid., p. 75. 
14. Wyandotte Const., Report of Committee, 1859. 


## Page 59

The Birth of Kansas 51 
present hands: “Such rights as are natural are now enjoyed as fully 
by women as men. Such rights and duties as are merely political in 
their character, they should be relieved from, that they may have 
more time to attend to those ‘greater and more complicated responsi- 
bilities’ which petitioners claim, and your committee admits, devolve 
upon women.” When the miscellaneous article was under discus- 
sion, E. Stokes, of Douglas County, offered a third section, which 
would have authorized the legislature to submit the question to 
popular vote. The section read as follows: 
The legislature may, at any regular session, provide for submitting the ques- 
tion of female suffrage, and all persons twenty-one years of age, and over, 
who have resided in the State six months previous to such election, shall be 
allowed to vote. Ballots shall be prepared with the words “for female suf- 
frage” and “against female suffrage,” and if a majority of all the votes cast 
shall be “for female suffrage,” then at all future elections there shall be no 
distinction in the qualifications of electors on account of sex. 
On motion of J. P. Slough the section was tabled.1® Although 
Mrs. Nichols was unable to persuade the convention to extend the 
elective franchise to women in political elections, nevertheless she 
was able to win enough votes to incorporate a rather vague negative 
clause into the legislative article which was to confer on women the 
right of suffrage in school elections. Even this section, introduced by 
Thacher, provoked considerable opposition, but was finally adopted 
by a vote of 22 to 19. Her efforts were therefore not entirely in vain. 
A large portrait of Mrs. Nichols hangs in the public library in Kan- 
sas City, Kansas, in commemoration of her efforts. She died in Cali- 
fornia in 1885, two years before the Kansas legislature passed the 
bill that conferred on women the right of municipal suffrage.® 
Section one of the article on Election and Suffrage under considera- 
tion provided that “every white male person . . . shall be 
deemed a qualified elector.” Hutchinson moved to strike out the 
word “white.” The motion failed 3 to 37. Hutchinson, Ritchie, and 
Stokes cast the affirmative votes.17 Thus all efforts to extend the 
suffrage were defeated. 
The article on county and township organization submitted by 
Ritchie, chairman of the committee, provided a method for changing 
county lines and county seats, limited the tenure of office of the 
county sheriff and treasurer, provided for the election of county and 
township officers and the removal of the justice of peace and town- 
15. Wyandotte Const. Conv., p. 324. 
16. Ibid., pp. 185-137, section No, 23, Art. II. Kansas City Star, Oct. 15, 1909. 
17. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 300, 301. 


## Page 60

52 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
ship officers. The debate centered on section one, particularly on 
the question, whether a change in county lines and county seats 
should be made upon the approval of the people of the several 
counties affected, or upon the consent of the people residing in the 
given district, and whether the change should be introduced by the 
legislature. After a long discussion, during the course of which 
numerous substitutions and amendments were offered, the conven- 
tion decided that “the Legislature shall provide for organizing new 
counties, locating county seats, and changing county lines; but no 
county seat shall be changed without the consent of a majority of 
the electors of the county; nor any county organized, nor the lines 
of any county changed so as to include an area of less than four 
hundred and thirty-two square miles.” 1° 
On July 11, Robert Graham, of Atchison, chairman of the com- 
mittee on corporations and banking, submitted seven sections on 
banking and currency. The article prohibited the establishment of 
banks except under the general banking law. It provided for regu- 
lation of State banks and prohibited the State from becoming a 
stockholder in any bank. The discussion centered on section two 
involving ~the questions of circulating notes and their collateral 
security. The original section was regarded as too indefinite. Presi- 
dent Winchell introduced a substitute motion which required that 
all general banking laws should require as collateral security for 
circulating notes, a deposit with the Auditor of State, consisting of 
bonds of the several States or of the United States equal in amount 
to the circulating notes of the bank. These bonds were to be valued 
at the cash rates of the New York Stock Exchange. The substitute 
motion was adopted with modifications. S. A. Kingman moved to 
substitute the clause, “bonds of the State of Kansas,” for that speci- 
fying “the several States.” This was agreed to, but on Graham’s 
motion it was struck out and the original wording was retained. 
Wrigley of Doniphan attempted to amend section six so as to require 
that a majority of the stockholders of any bank should be resident 
householders of the county in which the bank was located. This 
was unsuccessfully argued for by Messrs. Wrigley, Greer, Graham, 
Hutchinson, J. Blood, and McClelland. The object was to prevent 
the speculators from exploiting the people. Wrigley told of the ex- 
perience of people in Indiana where the billholder at times could not 
find the bank or the place where he could present the note for pay- 
18. Ibid., pp. 189, 190, 195-200, 221-228, 584. 


## Page 61

The Birth of Kansas 53 
ment. He might find the bank in a stump or in the hat of some 
president walking in the northern swamps. Blood introduced sec- 
tion eight which was adopted and added to the article. The sec- 
tion provided that before a banking law could be enforced it would 
have to be approved by a majority vote of the people voting at a 
general election.!® 
The article on corporations presented by Graham elicited but 
little discussion. Six sections were presented, none added, but some 
changes were made. On Slough’s motion, section one was struck 
out and replaced by clauses taken from the first and second sections 
of the article on corporations in the Ohio constitution. The essen- 
tial difference was that the substitute prohibited municipal corpora- 
tions from being created by special law.28 While in committee -of 
the whole, and again on Slough’s motion, the second section was 
amended so as to make stockholders liable for double the amount of 
their investment. 
The question of the Kansas Claims was discussed at length. The 
tabular report printed in the Journal showed 463 claimants with a 
total loss of $676,020.21, incurred during the years of the Kansas 
Civil War, 1856-1857. The Commissioners appointed by the Terri- 
torial Governor and Legislature to examine the claims, had allowed 
$412,978.03. In the convention the question was referred to the 
Committee on Ordinance and Public Debt. The report of the com- 
mittee provided that “no debt of the Territory [should] be assumed 
by the State, except by a law passed by a vote of two-thirds of each 
branch of the legislature.” A debate followed in which the claim- 
ants opposed the report on the ground that it was equivalent to 
repudiation. J. Blood and Houston led the attack and the section 
was stricken out. On McDowell’s motion the convention added a 
resolution to the article on resolutions which provided that ‘“Con- 
gress be further requested to assume the debt of the Territory.” 
The Democrats had favored inclusion of provisions on the debt 
question, but the Republicans doubted their sincerity. They feared 
that the Democrats wished to incorporate defects into the constitu- 
tion which they would use as arguments to defeat its ratification.” 
The section on internal improvements, introduced by S. E. Hoff- 
man of Woodson County as an amendment to the article on finance 
and taxation, prohibited the State from becoming a party in con- 
structing and carrying on works of internal improvements. If the

21. Ibid., pp. 94, 403-480, 482, 558, 592. Lawrence Republican, July 21, 1859.

Federal Government made grants of land or property to the State 
for this purpose, the State should devote thereto the avails of such 
grants. P. S. Parks opposed the amendment and pointed out its 
pitfalls. He cited as an example the debts incurred by Indiana dur- 
ing the thirties of the 19th century and proposed an out and out re- 
striction. S. A. Stinson introduced a substitute for the substitute, 
that the “State shall never be a party to carrying on works of pub- 
lic improvement.” This was adopted, and helped to keep Kansas 
from beeoming a partner in the construction of bridges and rail- 
roads.?2 
On the 18th of July a homestead exemption clause was submitted 
to the convention by 8. D. Houston, chairman of the committee on 
the miscellaneous article. It provided that real, personal, or mixed 
property to the value of $2,000 should be exempt from forced sale 
under any process of law, and should not be alienated without the - 
just consent of husband and wife, but no property should be exempt 
from sale for taxes or for the payment of its initial cost. A long 
discussion followed in which President Winchell, Houston, Griffith, 
Thacher, Blunt, and Kingman participated. Houston scorned the 
“large credit system” on the ground that “it just puts everything into 
the hands of the creditor.” It appears that by the term “large credit 
system,” he referred to a condition under which a creditor could lay 
claim, by means of an unrestricted mortgage, to everything a person 
owned. He thought that one of the most wholesome provisions that 
could be inserted in the constitution would be one reading, “That 
after . . . the first of January, 1865, no debts shall be collected 
by law in the State of Kansas. . . . We only ask for any per- 
son who comes into our state and makes a home for his family that 
it shall not be swept away from him by any ruthless intervention.” 
S. A. Kingman thought it wrong to place a limitation on the value 
of the homestead. “One hundred thousand dollars is as disgusting 
to me as one thousand. . . . In either case it is opposed to the 
principle that a home is a home, . . . the hearthstone, the 
fireside around which a man may gather his family, with the as- 
surance that neither the hand of law . . . nor all the uncertain- 
ties of life can eject them from the possession of it.” §. O. Thacher 
regarded the distinction between an exemption and a homestead law 
as one without a difference. He was unwilling to allow any man “‘to 
sport on one hundred and sixty acres, obtain credit from friendly 
men, and then turn around and cheat them out of it.” James G. 
Blunt argued for the tenant and the laboring man who were with- 
22. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 325, 328-334. 


## Page 63

The Birth of Kansas 55 
out a home and protection because they were too poor to afford a 
homestead. On President Winchell’s motion that part of the article 
which limited the homestead to a definite value was changed to one 
hundred and sixty acres of farming land or one acre within the 
limits of an incorporated town or city plus the improvements 
thereon. J. P. Greer proposed to strike out the section which au- 
thorized the sale of the homestead for indebtedness, but his proposal 
failed. The convention adopted the article with instructions that 
it be submitted to a vote of the people as an independent article. 
The homestead exemption clause is notable in that it made no dis- 
tinction between the homestead of a man or woman.?8 
The Negro question was discussed at length. The convention con- 
sidered the Fugitive Slave Act, the education of the Negro, the ad- 
mission of free Negroes and mulattoes, and Negro suffrage. James 
Hanway from Franklin County wrote that the Negro “is of such 
an important personage that whenever you find a representative and 
Democrat talking together Mr. Sambo must be introduced and his 
case duly attended to. It is in the convention, outside of the bar, 
in the streets, hotels, boarding houses and even in the fashionable 
parlour of our Wyandotte friends.” 24 When the Bill of Rights was 
under consideration the last clause of the first section provoked a 
long debate. It read: “. . . the right of all men to the control of 
their persons, exists prior to law and is inalienable.”” The Democrats, 
led by William C. McDowell, considered this an enunciation of 
“higher law” principle such as should not go into any constitution 
adopted at Wyandotte; for if it expressed a truth, then no man could 
be made amenable to any criminal law. “It would place us in the 
attitude of the commission of solecism; recognizing somewhere the 
right and power to punish crime, yet in the Bill of Rights [we would 
abrogate it] by asserting that the control of the person is above, 
beyond and anterior to all law.” Benjamin Wrigley maintained that 
its object was to set at defiance the Federal Fugitive Slave Law. 
S. O. Thacher argued that the principle of the Declaration of Inde- 
pendence permeated the Federal Constitution and did not fasten 
slavery upon anyone. The clause in article IV, section 2, of the 
Federal Constitution pertaining to those who “owe service” was 
a declaration that no contract should be invalidated. He spurned 
the thought that the “constitution recogniz[ed] the right of property 
in man.” As the debate continued James G. Blunt denounced the 
23. Ibid., pp. 270-271, 309, 338, 339, 342-345, 520-523, 556. 
24. James Hanway, Papers, letter of July 18, 1859. 


## Page 64

56 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Fugitive Slave Law as “inhuman and infamous,” and beyond doubt 
a violation of the Constitution of the United States: 
I will here be frank to declare that while I will never interfere with the 
institution . . . where it exists in the States by municipal law, neither 
will I in any manner be responsible for any of its sins. While I shall use no 
means to induce slaves to flee from their masters, neither will I suffer myself 
to be used as an instrument for their capture and return. . . . while the 
fugitive slave act remains upon the statute books I shall ever consider it a 
Christian duty to disregard its cruel mandates. No wicked and infamous law 
shall ever deter me from feeding the hungry and sheltering the weary, and 
exercising towards a fellow-being the kind offices of a common humanity. “As 
ye would that others should do unto you, do ye even so unto them,” 
and I desire here to say that the officer who shall ever command me, as one 
of the posse comitatus, to aid in the execution of the fugitive slave law offers 
to me a direct insult and he may expect me to act accordingly. I will ever 
hurl defiance at its commands, despite corrupt and tyrannical power.25 
After several delegates had admitted that the clause was am- 
biguous, and several attempts had failed to change the section, S. 
A. Kingman introduced a substitute that quieted the troubled water. 
This provided that “All men are possessed of equal and inalienable 
rights, among which are those of life, liberty and the pursuit of 
happiness.” It was adopted, 42 to 6. Messrs: Burnett, Hutchinson, 
Houston, Ritchie, Stokes, and Winchell voted in the negative; the 
Democrats voted in the affirmative. The section prohibiting slavery 
was adopted without debate, 48 tol. J. W. Forman cast the nega- 
tive vote.”6 
The question of closing the schools to the Negro arose in con- 
nection with the second section of the article on education. William 
C. McDowell of Leavenworth asked the convention to insert the 
word “white” after “admission of,” so as to close the schools to all 
but white pupils of both sexes. The amendment failed, 26 to 25. 
Thereupon S. A. Stinson moved to add the clause: “but no Negro 
or mulatto pupils shall be admitted to such schools.” In support 
of his proposal he said: ‘““We come from Leavenworth county, repre- 
senting a popular sovereignty constituency, whose will is in favor of 
excluding Negroes from the common schools of Kansas and we don’t 
wish to let an opportunity go by without so expressing that senti- 
ment.” A debate followed, during the course of which J. P. Slough 
remarked: “I shall never consent, by my vote, or by any action of 
mine, that those upon whom Nature’s God has stamped inferiority, 
shall ever associate with my children in our common schools.” He ©

favored the idea of educating the blacks, but would keep a separate 
fund for this purpose and use what the Negroes themselves con-

other side. Thacher said: “Gentlemen, I trust this proposition comes

our great struggle for liberty, what lesson would it teach to the

and unnecessary as this?” On Blunt’s motion, Stinson’s prohibition

While the miscellaneous article was under consideration William 
C. McDowell moved to add the section: “No Negro or mulatto shall 
come into or settle in this state after the adoption of the constitu- 
tion.” On Ritchie’s motion the amendment was tabled 28 to 20. On 
the 25th of July the subject was reopened by P. S. Parks of Leaven- 
worth but the chair declared it out of order. Negro suffrage was 
eventually ruled out, however, by retaining the word ‘‘white” in the 
restriction clause. 
In 1865 Governor Robinson wrote that the word “white” had been 
inserted in the Kansas constitutions “more to conciliate favor at 
Washington than to conform to the wishes of the people of the 
State.” Personally he favored Negro suffrage. His argument was: 
For white and colored people have a common origin, are endowed alike 
with intellect, with moral and religious natures and have a common destiny. 
If this proposition is correct it follows from necessity that both alike are 
entitled to equal civil, political, moral and religious rights, according to the 
principles laid down in the Declaration of Independence by our ancestors and 
according to the immutable laws of God himself who is no respector of per- 
sons. . . . No valid argument can be produced against the right of suffrage 
for the colored man.?8 
The Democrats at the convention were willing to grant suffrage 
to the Indians who had adopted the habits of the whites, but not 
to the Negro. William A. Phillips wrote that it was generally con- 
ceded that the Indian who could chew a plug of tobacco and drink 
a half pint of raw whiskey belonged to the white class.” 
Two attempts were made during the convention to authorize the 
legislature to regulate or prohibit the sale of alcoholic liquors, ex-

The boundary question elicited much heated discussion, probably 
more than any other subject before the convention. It had already 
been considered by the Territorial legislature. Senator C. K. Holli- 
day of Topeka had introduced a resolution on the 7th of January, 
1859, proposing to annex “that portion of Nebraska lying 
south of the Platte river.” The Senate had adopted the resolution 
on the 14th by a vote of 8 to 5. The argument advanced in its sup- 
port was that it would give Kansas the required population for state- 
hood.*!_ In the convention the subject was introduced on the 11th of 
July by T. H. Townshend of Douglas County, when he submitted 
the credentials of the delegates from the Platte River region. After 
that the question was considered time and again until the closing 
days of the eonvention. The Republicans were afraid of it. They 
feared that through it the Democrats might win enough votes to 
annex the region and gain political control in organizing the State 
government, and that the Democrats, therefore, were supporting the 
project for political reasons. Southern Nebraska was a Democratic 
stronghold. John A. Martin wrote that Governor Medary’s son and 
private secretary had written a letter to the Nebraska people, May 
16, 1859, urging them to elect delegates to the Wyandotte conven- 
tion, but to proceed quietly.22 Some of the Republicans supported 
the Democrats on this project, among them 8S. D. Houston of Riley. 
Houston also made a vigorous attempt to retain the western bound- 
ary of Kansas at the 27th meridian, corresponding to about 104 
degrees west longitude, as given in the Organic Act. It was argued 
that Houston was motivated by the idea that to annex the Platte 
River region would enhance the chances of Manhattan for the 
capital of the State. On the other hand, it was rumored that the 
reason the delegates of Topeka and Lawrence were opposing the 
project was that they feared it would weaken their chances for the

81. Council Journal, 1859., pp. 70-76. Messrs. Crozier, Challis, Holliday, Harding,

Libbett, and Babcock, in the negative.

## Page 67

The Birth of Kansas 59 
capital. Houston thought it was nothing short of folly for the 
Republicans to refuse the richest agricultural land available. He 
was also interested in western gold and the Pacific railroad, and 
thought that Kansas needed the Platte region to satisfy the popula- 
tion requirement for statehood. The Democrats denied that they 
supported the project for political reasons, and challenged the Re- 
publicans to name some “reason becoming the dignity and import- 
ance of the subject and of the convention” against the proposition. 
“Will it benefit materially the interests of the State of Kansas? If 
it will, I tell you, you have no other alternative than to vote for it,” 
argued Stinson. The Republicans refused to be told. Thacher re- 
torted: 
We have been invoked by all the powers of logic and rhetoric to ignore the 
political aspect of this case . . . and look at the question dispassionately. 
Now, sir, I say they argue an impossibility. Had these gentlemen from South- 
ern Nebraska seen the sky lurid with the flames of their burning homes, the 
soil of these beautiful prairies crimson with the blood of their brothers and 
fathers, or their wives and children flying over the land for a place of refuge 
from crime and outrage . . . committed by the party which is substantially 
in the ascendency in this portion of the country proposed to be annexed, they 
would not think of making such an appeal to us. . . . Sir, before I would 
again see the people of Kansas brought beneath the heel of that oppression, I 
would oppose it until I sank into the floor.34 
When the question came to a vote it was defeated, 19 to 29. Some 
of the Republicans lived to regret that they had opposed the an- 
nexation of the Platte region. On July 29, 1882, J. A. Martin wrote 
that he felt that no Republican delegate of the convention was living 
who did not deplore that decision of the convention, but it was too 
late.?> 
The other boundary lines provoked but little discussion. The 
western boundary was extended to the 25th meridian, 102 degrees 
west longitude. The change came with the motion of Caleb May of 
Atchison. The convention had agreed to the 23d meridian before 
May introduced his proposal.3° The southern boundary was located 
on the 37th parallel. A map of Kansas and Nebraska, approved by 
the Commissioner of Indian Affairs, J. C. Manypenny, shows that 
the 37th parallel was also the dividing line between the Osage and 
Cherokee reservations. This was erroneous, but it may have in- 
fluenced the convention in selecting this parallel for the southern

60 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
boundary. The permanency of this line was conditioned, however, 
by the willingness of the Indian tribes to be included within the 
territory of Kansas. Consequently, the 37th parallel remained im- 
permanent until February 23, 1867, when the Quapas, the last of 
the Kansas tribes, ceded their rights to Kansas.** 
Looking at the boundary question today it could be said that 
anything but geographic conditions explains the Kansas State lines. 
The southern half belongs to the Arkansas River watershed, and 
the northern half to the Missouri River watershed. Even the north- 
ern half has no geographic unity. To annex the Platte region might 
have merely accentuated the economic problems of the State.3® 
The question that provoked the most heated discussion of all was 
that of the location of the capital. The convention was stirred to 
action when P. S. Parks of Leavenworth presented a resolution, July 
22, asking for a committee of five to investigate the charge that 
William Hutchinson of Lawrence had offered to give a city lot to 
E. M. Hubbard of Doniphan if he would help to locate the capital at 
Lawrence. The motion to investigate was unanimous, 49 to 0. The 
resolution was introduced for the benefit of Topeka, and to cast a 
shadow on Lawrence. In justice to Lawrence,-as Stinson put it, the 
convention decided to vote for the temporary location of the capital 
before the committee on “Scullduggery” would report. A motion by 
Graham provided for two ballots. On the first the delegates would 
vote their choice without limitations. If the results showed that 
no place had received a majority of all votes cast, then a second 
ballot would be taken in which the choice would be confined to the 
four places that had received the largest number of votes on the 
first ballot. On motion of James C. Blunt the convention decided 
that the first State legislature should provide by law to submit the 
question of the permanent location of the capital to a popular vote 
of the people. The city which received a majority of all votes cast 
would be the State’s capital. In accordance with Graham’s resolu- 
tion the convention voted, on July 23, on the temporary location of 
the capital. The first ballot gave Topeka fifteen votes, Lawrence 
six, Atchison five, Emporia, Mound City, Manhattan, Olathe, and 
Minneola two each, and fourteen other votes had been cast at 
random. Since only three towns had leading votes the chair ruled, 
by common consent, to limit the second ballot to Topeka, Lawrence, 
37. Nyle H. Miller, “Surveying the Southern Boundary Line of Kansas,” K. H. Q. I, 
104, 105; G. W. Martin, “The Boundary Lines of Kansas,’ Collections, XI, 55-61. 
38. Bulletin No, 251, Agricultural Experiment Station, Manhattan. See map of 1930. 


## Page 69

The Birth of Kansas 61 
and Atchison. This ballot made Topeka the temporary capital of 
the State by one vote more than was necessary. The vote was 
Topeka twenty-six, Lawrence fourteen, and Atchison six. The 
Leavenworth and Doniphan County delegates had voted for Topeka. 
In fact, all the Democrats had voted for Topeka. Lawrence had 
received seven votes from Douglas and one each from Lykins, Bour- 
bon, Woodson, Coffey, Anderson, Franklin, and Johnson Counties.®? 
This point is of significance. It will be remembered that the change 
introduced in the act providing for the Wyandotte Constitutional 
Convention, pertaining to the apportionment of delegates, had given 
Leavenworth County three additional delegates and Doniphan 
County one more. When the convention voted to locate the State 
capital, the delegates from these two counties all voted for Topeka. 
It will be remembered also, that when the bill providing for the 
convention was passed by the legislature, C. K. Holliday of Topeka 
recalled the ‘bill twice after it had been sent to the enrolling clerk. 
Moreover, it will be remembered that in discussing the significance 
of the election of delegates to the Wyandotte convention from the 
standpoint of city rivalries it was shown that the apportionment of 
delegates per county was not based upon population or any unit of 
measurement, but had been gerrymandered for the benefit of some 
particular interest or interests. Furthermore, when S. O. Thacher of 
Lawrence proposed to submit the entire question of the location of 
the capital to a vote of the people, rather than to decide its tem- 
porary location in convention, the opposition defeated the proposal, 
because it had a majority vote in the convention favoring Topeka. 
These facts point to the conclusion that the error which crept into 
the act providing for the apportionment of delegates to the Wyan- 
dotte Constitutional Convention, was purposed by certain legisla- 
tors and lobbyists to help locate the capital at Topeka. The trade 
was made with the Democrats of Leavenworth and Doniphan 
County, giving them four more delegates in the convention by dis- 
franchising Wyandotte County, and taking one vote from Jefferson 
County. There was a possibility that both Wyandotte and Jefferson 
County delegates would have favored Lawrence. It is possible that 
the railroad question had figured in the trade, that is, that the 
promoters of the Leavenworth, Pawnee, and Western Railroad had 
bargained for a larger representation of their interests. The evi- 
dence supporting this theory, however, is not so clear. T. D. 
Thacher of Lawrence accused Topeka of having “sold out to the 
89. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 454, 455. 


## Page 70

62 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
northern annexationists,’*° which probably was not the case. At 
least the Topeka delegates did not vote for annexation. 
The charges of bribery and pooling of votes implicated the citizens 
of both Topeka and Lawrence. E. M. Hubbard testified under oath 
that Hutchinson of Lawrence had offered him a “good lot” if he 
would vote for Lawrence, and he further accused Hutchinson of 
saying that he would make “the same offer to any other Democratic 
member of the convention.” Hutchinson denied the charges. Presi- 
dent Winchell said that Hubbard had protested before the conven- 
tion that Hutchinson had been allowed to evade the charge. The 
committee had asked him whether he had offered “lots” to Hubbard. 
Since he had only offered a “lot” he had denied the charge. Hub- 
bard averred that Hutchinson had made the offer in the presence 
of Messrs. Barton and Burnett. At this stage Colonel Slough 
introduced a resolution asking for a second committee of five to 
investigate the charge of perjury against Hutchinson. The com- 
mittee divided on the question. Colonel Slough and McCune de- 
clared Hutchinson guilty of perjury and asked for his dismissal. 
G. H. Lillie and James Blood reported Hutchinson innocent. Robert 
Graham declared both Hutchinson and Hubbard guilty. S. O. 
Thacher now informed the convention that he could name two other 
gentlemen who had offered their own votes and those of their asso- 
ciates to locate the capital anywhere in exchange for votes to annex 
the Platte region. McDowell and Stinson did not deny the charge, 
but they bitterly resented the inference that it was an act of corrup- 
tion and bribery, and demanded an investigation. It was July 28. 
At this point in the debate a scene was precipitated that almost 
disrupted the convention. J. A. Martin has left a vivid description 
of it: 
Mr. McDowell arose, and was proceeding to make a speech, when the 
President interrupted him by desiring to know if he intended to confine him- 
self to the rules of debate. Mr. McDowell replied that he expected to call 
things by their right names. The Chairman told him that he could not pro- 
ceed. McDowell, fairly foaming at the mouth, and trembling with passion, 
roared out that he “had a right to call a lar a liar, and a villain a villain.” 
The President ordered the Sergeant-at-Arms to preserve order, and make the 
gentleman take his seat. The Sergeant was proceeding to enforce the order, 
when McDowell raised a loaded cane and said “let the Sergeant-at-Arms try 
to put me down.” Then ensued a scene of excitment which it would be dif- 
ficult to describe. The members all over the hall rose to their feet. The 
President’s gavel, commanding order, fell fast and loud. The lobby, which 
was crowded with spectators, was in a state of intense excitement, and cries of

“go on!” echoed all over the Hall. The Democratic members cried, “Go on, 
McDowell.” Slough rose and commenced rolling up his sleeves. Hipple cried 
“go on Me.” and every Democratic member was on his feet. The Sergeant- 
at-Arms was advancing towards McDowell, when Mr. McCullough, a tall, 
powerful man and a Republican delegate from the 18th district, who sat on 
the Democratic side of the House, jumped upon his feet, and shaking his fist 
at Slough and the Democratic members, said “We intend to have order. Take 
your seats, or, by God, we’ll make you. Come on, boys.’’41 
Order was restored, and, to allay the excitement, the question was 
tabled. 
The location of the capital, no doubt, had involved the convention 
in corrupt practices, but the details have remained obscure. The 
Wyandotte correspondent of the Herald of Freedom wrote July 31st: 
“If there has ever been anything in our political history more dis- 
graceful to us as a people than the proceedings of this convention, I 
should be sorry to know anything about it.” The Topeka Tribune 
quoted from a Topeka letter of July 18th that Lawrence was cir- 
culating subscription paper soliciting contributions in money and 
property to buy off members of the constitutional convention to 
locate the capital at Lawrence. Fifty thousand dollars had been 
subscribed by the Lawrence men who entered Wyandotte the follow- 
ing Tuesday with the money to complete the task.*? On the other 
hand the Topeka men had not been idle. On July 14, C. K. Holli- 
day wrote: “I have now been at this place [Wyandotte] just one 
week, looking after Capital matters and my political prospects.” 
On the 24th, after the convention had voted to locate the capital at 
Topeka, he wrote again: “Tt kills me politically, however, for a time 
at least, but present pecuniary good is worth more to me than pros- 
pective political position . . . Tomorrow night we give a supper 
to the Topeka friends—soon after which I will return to Topeka.’ 
On the 14th of July, 1859, the Lawrence Republican quoted its 
Wyandotte correspondent as follows: “All Topeka is here urging the 
claims of their beloved town for State capital with great power. 
Besides the delegates from Shawnee county I notice here Dr. 
Fletcher, Mr. Murphy, Col. Holliday, and Mr. Cummings of the 
Tribune.”44 On November 14, 1898, William Hutchinson in a 
letter to the Secretary of the State Historical Society explained his 
action in the case as follows:

Lyman Allen and C. W. Babcock, of Lawrence, came into my room at 
Wyandotte one evening when the capital question was pending, and said they 
had come down to work for Lawrence, and had city lots they were offering to 
secure the measure. Some time afterward I did mention to Mr. Hubbard in 
substance what my neighbors had told me, but in no way intimating that I had 
any lots to offer, for at that time I did not own a lot in Lawrence. I had no 
thought of any improper action toward Mr. Hubbard, but merely spoke of 
my neighbors’ zeal in the case.45 
When the convention had finished its work and William Hutchin- 
son offered the resolution to adopt and sign the constitution, Colonel 
Slough arose to express his regret that he, for one, could not sign the 
constitution nor vote for its adoption. He admitted that the instru- 
ment in the main was a model document, for he was not aware of 
ever having “read a state constitution better framed in most things 
than this;” still it had omitted provisions that ought to be in it and 
contained others that were objectionable. He referred to the con- 
vention’s failure to annex the Platte River region, to give the Indian 
suffrage rights, to exclude the Negro and mulatto; and expressed his 
contempt toward the registry law, the large legislature which he re- 
garded as extravagant, and especially the apportionment clause 
which he said was based upon no principle whatsoever except to 
deliver the Territory into the hands of the Republican Party.*6 
The Democrats had met in a caucus at Wyandotte the evening be- 
fore, July 28. The meeting had been a stormy one and had divided 
the Democrats. Governor Medary had spoken long and ably in 
favor of signing, supported by McDowell and Stinson of Leaven- 
worth; but Colonel Slough, Forman, Wrigley and others had stoutly 
opposed the Governor, and by a majority of four votes had placed 
the Democrats on record as opposing the constitution.47 This ex- 
plains why the following day Colonel Slough was supported by the 
Democrats. The vote on the Hutchinson resolution was 34 to 13. 
The Republicans all voted in the affirmative except T. S. Wright, 
who was absent because of illness. The negative votes were all cast 
by Democrats, but Hubbard, J. Wright, Perry, and Brown had not 
voted. The seventeen Democrats also refused to sign the constitu- 
tion. §. O. Thacher, who answered the protest of the Democrats, 
averred: 
Upon this Constitution we meet our opponents upon the popular arena. 
It is a better, a nobler issue than even the old Free State issue. They have

46. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 566, 567.

thrown down the gauntlet, we joyfully pick it up. The members of this con- 
vention have richly earned the love and gratitude of their constituents. They 
have perfected a work that will be enduring. . . . We part, sir, from this 
hall, having discharged our arduous duty. Let us part as friends, transferring 
the discussion and the analysis of our work to those for whom it has been 
wrought.48 
The Democrats had reasons for objecting to the Wyandotte con- 
stitution. They had fought valiantly for the admission of the 
Wyandotte delegates, but had failed. In their effort to annex the 
Platte region they had left no stone unturned. They had tried 
everything short of bribery and were even accused of that. While 
in itself the project might have been a worthy one, the Republicans 
were afraid to trust their “friends” on the other side of the House. 
The Democrats had tried again and again to defeat the gerryman- 
dering schemes of the Republicans, but their exposure of these 
schemes had availed them nothing. Consequently, they chose to 
threaten to stay the rule of the Republicans by protesting the docu- 
ment. Their protest against the few privileges granted the Negro 
would at present appear rather strange, but it was in accord with the 
prevailing views of the time, including that of the Free-State people 
of Kansas.‘ All in all, the action of the Democrats should be re- 
garded as a political stroke rather than outright opposition to the 
constitution. 
The Wyandotte convention had voted to use the Ohio constitution 
as a model for the Wyandotte constitution. It is generally known, 
however, that there were many deviations from the model.®°° 
It is of interest to note that most of the State constitutions framed 
before 1861 placed the article on the legislative department before 
that on the executive department. The exceptions were the Mary- 
land constitution of 1851, the Lecompton constitution, the Florida 
constitution of 1831, and the Wyandotte constitution. Since then 
many other constitutional conventions have done likewise,*! in 
accord with the shift in emphasis from the legislative to the execu- 
tive department. 
In discussing the sources of the Wyandotte constitution many de- 
tails will be omitted for the sake of brevity, since a detailed study 
of the subject has already been published by Rosa M. Perdue.®?

66 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
The work of tracing the sources of a constitution is so tremendous, 
however, that further contributions may readily be made. 
The conclusions reached as to the sources of the Wyandotte con- 
stitution are based on a comparison with other constitutions in 
existence at the time. The text used as a basis of comparison for 
this study was Francis Newton Thorpe’s seven volumes on The 
Federal and State Constitutions. This comparison has forced the 
author to disagree rather frequently with Miss Perdue’s conclusions. 
Often it was impossible to determine whether a section or phrase had 
been copied from one or several constitutions because the section 
was found in several constitutions and any one of them might have 
served as a model. In other cases the punctuation, the spelling of 
words, or capitalization of words helped to decide the question. As 
an example, section 12 in the Bill of Rights is found in the Leaven- 
worth and the Ohio constitutions, yet it appears that it was copied 
from the Leavenworth rather than the Ohio constitution, as Miss 
Perdue has it, because the spelling of the word “offence,” the capital- 
ization, and the punctuation follow the Leavenworth constitution.>* 
It should be noted that the Leavenworth constitutional convention 
had used the Topeka constitution of 1855 as a model.®4 
The article on the preamble and Bill of Rights was introduced by 
Hutchinson, a native of Vermont. According to Miss Perdue, all 
but the first section of the Bill of Rights was modeled after the 
Ohio constitution.®®> Yet section 13, relating to treason, is not found 
in the Ohio constitution, but resembles the section in the Federal 
Constitution and in the Minnesota constitution.°® Moreover, sec- 
tions 2, 6, 9, 12, 15, and 20 follow the Leavenworth constitution 
more closely than the Ohio constitution, while other sections might 
have been modeled after either the Leavenworth or Ohio constitu- 
tions. Only sections 3, 4, and 5 follow the Ohio constitution. Sec- 
tion 10 of the Wyandotte constitution, which may have been taken 
from either the Ohio, Leavenworth, or Pennsylvania constitutions, 
has omitted the first sentence relating to the indictment by a grand 
jury, found in the aforementioned constitutions and in others, leav- 
ing the Kansas constitution silent on the question. The convention 
journal failed to mention this subject but the Atchison Union 
criticised the Wyandotte convention for omitting it.27 Thus the

## Page 75

The Birth of Kansas 67 
Kansas constitution failed to provide for a grand jury. In the 
preamble the clause, “in order to insure the full enjoyment of our 
rights as American citizens,” appears to be Stinson’s contribution. 
The rest of the preamble may have been taken from any number of 
constitutions. 
The executive article was introduced by Greer of Ohio and follows 
the “Buckeye” constitution of 1851 in sections 1, 3, 11, 12, 13, and 
14; the Leavenworth constitution in sections 2, 4, and 6; the Iowa 
constitution in section 8; and either the Ohio, Leavenworth, or To- 
peka constitution in sections 5, 7, 9, 10, 15, and 16. In section 1 
the Wyandotte constitution provides for the office of Superintendent 
of Public Instruction, not included in the Ohio constitution, but 
found in the Oregon, Indiana, Kentucky, and Wisconsin .constitu- 
tions of 1857, 1851, 1850, and 1848, respectively.*® 
The legislative article introduced by Thacher, a native of New 
York, likewise contains provisions taken from many constitutions. 
The New York constitution was followed in sections 1, 3, 5, 8, and 
10. Sections 2 and 23 appear to be original. Section 23 provided 
that “The Legislature, in providing for the formation and regulation 
of schools, shall make no distinction between the rights of males 
and females.” ®® Section 12, which provided that all laws should 
originate in the House of Representatives, has a precedent in the 
Virginia constitutions, Miss Perdue’s statement to the contrary not- 
withstanding. Sections 6, 16, and 24 follow the Ohio constitution of 
1851; section 7 and 21 resemble similar sections in the Wisconsin 
and New York constitutions, and sections 4, 9, 11, 18, 15, 19, 25, 26, 
27, and 28 may have been taken from either the Leavenworth or the 
Topeka constitution.*° Section 22, which protects the legislators 
from arrest while in session, resembles the section in the Indiana 
constitution more than similar sections in the Oregon, Leavenworth, 
and Topeka constitutions. The sources of the other sections are 
difficult to trace. 
The article on the judiciary was introduced by S. A. Kingman, a 
native of Massachusetts. Ten of the eleven committeemen were 
lawyers, Blunt was a physician. In this article section 5 and a part 
of 1 and 4 follow the Organic Act of 1854; sections 2, 13, 19, and 
part of 11 follow the Ohio constitution; section 18 and a part of 4,

the Leavenworth constitution; section 6, the Iowa constitution; sec- 
tions 7 and 8, the Minnesota constitution; sections 9, 14, 15, 16, and 
17 may have been derived from the Wisconsin, Ohio, Leavenworth, 
Topeka, or Michigan constitution. Sections 3, 10, and 20 may be 
original.6t To this article the lawyers contributed of their ex- 
periences. This accounts. for the difficulty in tracing the origin of 
most of the sections. 
The article on county and township organization, introduced by 
Ritchie, a native of Ohio, appears to have been based on the Ohio, 
Indiana, Illinois, Minnesota, Iowa, Michigan, and Virginia con- 
stitutions.°? The idea of a mixed system of government may have 
been derived from the Indiana, Illinois, Ohio, or Michigan consti- 
tutions. E. B. Greene attributed the origin of the county-township 
form of government in Illinois to the manner in which the State 
was settled. The early settlers had come largely from the South 
and had established the county system, but when the new settlers 
from New York and New England arrived, being used to the 
township form of government, their influence was responsible for 
the change in the constitution of 1848, which stipulated that the 
legislature should pass a law authorizing the majority of the voters 
in any county to adopt the township system. Under this system the 
county board should represent the various towns. During the years 
following the change, the northern central counties were generally 
organized on this plan.**? That provision of the Kansas constitution 
which sets the minimum limit of the county at 432 square miles must 
have been taken from the Iowa constitution. 
The article on education was introduced by Griffith, a native of 
Indiana. The committee comprised seven members: Middleton, 
Stokes, and Hipple were native sons of Pennsylvania, Houston and 
McClelland had come from Ohio, and May from Kentucky. The 
interest in the article lies in the fact that it introduced the office of 
county superintendent, which is without constitutional precedent. 
The office was not new, however, as there existed several statutory 
precedents. Two Territorial acts of Kansas passed in 1858 and 1859 
had provided for the office.6* An act passed by the State legislature 
of Pennsylvania in 1854 had also provided for the office. The func-

tions defined in the Territorial laws resemble those of the Pennsy]-
