Kansas statutes. Certainly the committeemen of the Wyandotte 
convention were acquainted with the Territorial laws and the three 
native sons of Pennsylvania would have remembered the office in 
their former state.® 
The article on banks and currency, introduced by Robert Graham, 
of Atchison, was based on the Leavenworth and Topeka constitu- 
tions, except section 3 which followed the Iowa constitution. The 
article on corporations followed the Ohio, Leavenworth, Topeka, 
Michigan, and Oregon constitutions. The section on homestead ex- 
emption had a precedent in the Leavenworth and Michigan consti- 
tutions, and that on suffrage followed the Illinois, Topeka, Leaven- 
worth, Indiana, and Oregon constitutions.® 
To continue the description of the sources of the constitution 
would be to elaborate what is already apparent, namely, that the 
delegates in the Wyandotte convention did very little creative work, 
but gathered mostly from the latest constitutions those sections 
which experience or judgment warranted their incorporating into the 
Kansas constitution. Miss Perdue asserted that five of the six 
original sections in the Wyandotte constitution had been thoroughly 
tested as laws of other States before they had been adopted by Kan- 
sas, and that the only real experiment in the Kansas constitution 
was the provision that all bills should originate in the House of 
Representatives. It must now be added that even this section was 
not original, for, as previously mentioned, such a provision had been 
a part of the Virginia constitutions for seventy-four years before it 
was incorporated into the Kansas constitution.** While it may be 
true that the Ohio constitution was used by several committees as a 
model for a number of their sections, yet before the convention had 
adopted the given article, many changes had been made. Moreover, 
it seems that quite as often the Leavenworth or Topeka constitu- 
tions were followed. It is true, however, that the Leavenworth and 
Topeka constitutions, in many sections, appear to be based on the 
Ohio constitution; hence, where the wording may be that of the

66. Thorpe, op. cit.

“for equal education for the sexes, and for the election by the bar of a judge pro tem. of 
the district court,’ for “a county superintendent of public instruction, and an outlet of the 
method of distributing the public school funds to the districts, and of a revaluation and sale 
of school lands,’ and for all bills to originate in the House of Representatives. 


## Page 78

70 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Leavenworth or Topeka constitutions, the origin of the idea ulti- 
mately may reach back to the Ohio constitution. But the Ohio 
constitution was not an original document either. Robert Stone, of 
Topeka, wrote that “the Ohio constitution was in the main founded 
upon that of New York.” & It does not seem fair to give the credit 
to the Ohio constitution where the convention followed the Leaven- 
worth or the Topeka constitutions. One is obliged, therefore, to dis- 
agree with Miss Perdue in her assertion that the Ohio constitution 
“was closely adhered to in all cases where its provisions were 
adapted to conditions in Kansas,” ® for quite often the Topeka cr 
Leavenworth constitutions were adhered to rather than the Ohio 
constitution, although the meaning of the sections in these constitu- 
tions did not vary a great deal, if at all. 
The sagacity of the Wyandotte convention, as Robert Stone so 
aptly pointed out, consisted in its selecting of the best and most 
progressive provisions from the most recently adopted constitutions, 
and amalgamating them into a consistent and harmonious whole.” 
No attempt was made to centralize executive responsibility in the 
hands of the Governor by giving him authority to appoint the high 
executive officers, or to prevent hasty and ill.considered legislation 
by means of a “bifurcated session,” or by providing for a single 
chamber legislature elected by popular vote under a system of pro- 
portional representation, although an attempt was made by the 
Democrats to provide for a small legislature. These suggestions of 
reform did not make their appearance until the end of the nineteenth 
and the beginning of the twentieth century. The constitution was, 
however, in keeping with the trends of the time. The Governor pos- 
sessed the veto power, although not the item veto.71 He also pos- 
sessed the power to grant pardons, fill vacancies, and convene and 
adjourn the legislature. Popular control had been introduced. Suf- 
frage was granted to all white male persons who were citizens of 
the United States or else had declared their intentions of becoming 
citizens. The long ballot was provided for in the State and county 
elections, including an elective judiciary. The power of the legisla- 
ture had been limited both directly and indirectly. Special laws 
68. Wyandotte Const. Conv., pp. 697 ff. Stone wrote: “Two of the older states, New 
York for the North and Virginia for the South, have furnished the model for most of the constitutions of the several states.” 
69. Ibid., p. 694. 
70. Ibid., p. 698. 
71. J. Q. Dealey, Growth of American State Constitutions from 1776-1914, Chap. V. 
Dealey is wrong when he says Kansas introduced the item veto in 1859. 


## Page 79

The Birth of Kansas 71 
were not to be passed where a general law was applicable, the debt 
of the State was limited and could not be changed except by a direct 
vote of the people. In conclusion, it could be said that the Wyan- 
dotte convention had recognized the important constitutional trends 
of the time. On the other hand, it had introduced very few, if any, 
innovations. 


## Page 80

Chapter IV 
Ratification, and Election of State Officers, 
1859 
HE refusal of the Democrats to sign the Wyandotte constitu- 
tion, and the answer of Thacher accepting the challenge, made 
the question of ratification a party issue. The Herald of Freedom a 
Free-State paper, also came out boldly in denunciation of the con- 
stitution, and the race was on. To the Democrats the campaign 
amounted to a life and death struggle, for should they be defeated 
in the contests and Congress admit the State, the Republicans would 
replace the administration Democrats. To the Republicans the cam- 
paign meant not only a fight for ratification, but also a political 
scramble for office. The result was that the arguments for and 
against the constitution crowded the newspapers, and politicians 
flooded the towns. 
George W. Brown admitted that the constitution had many good 
features but opposed it because of its omissions and defects. He 
feared it would produce a radical change in civil and criminal law 
because the clause in section ten of the Bill of Rights, “no person 
shall be a witness against himself,” prohibited judgment by confes- 
sion. The constitution provided for an extravagant legislature and 
for a useless Senate. It made all officers under the constitution sub- 
ject to a political tribunal by providing for their impeachment for 
misdemeanors in office. It disfranchised civilized Indians and based 
suffrage on color and sex in violation of the principle of equal rights, 
which it claimed as the basis of civil government. It had prevented 
a system of graded schools by providing a system of common 
schools, and placed cities on the same level with the most sparsely 
settled school districts. Representation should be based on popula- 
tion and not on counties, and the system of gerrymandering should 
be corrected. Brown feared the banking clause was unwise, claiming 
it would flood the State with notes from other States. The consti- 
tution struck “down the prohibition of the passage of ex post facto 
laws,” it failed to prohibit the passage of laws that would impair 
the obligation of contracts, it failed to prevent the taking of private 
(72) 


## Page 81

The Birth of Kansas 73 
property for public use without just compensation, it contravened 
the Constitution of the United States by its failure to provide for a 
grand jury system. He also maintained, but falsely, that there were 
no age or residence requirements for the suffrage in the election of 
State and Judicial officers; and that section 23 in article 2 did not 
confer the right of suffrage in school matters on females, in spite of 
the constitutional provision to the contrary. All in all, he regarded 
it “an office seeker’s constitution.” It would be too expensive, for 
it would cost the state $100,000 more annually than the Territorial 
government.! Also, Abelard Guthrie opposed the constitution, fear- 
ing that the people were already oppressed by taxation and that 
only swindlers and demagogues had time to engage in electioneering. 
He had returned from Washington just in time to cast his vote 
against the constitution, and said he had done so because it with- 
held the elective franchise from the Indian.? 
In addition to these arguments the Democrats emphasized, among 
other things, their opposition to the settlement of the boundary, the 
failure of the constitution to define the duties and limit the powers 
of representatives of the people, and to exclude the free Negro from 
the State. They pointed out that the Topeka constitution had con- 
tained a clause restricting the Negro in this way, but the Wyandotte 
convention had voted it down. This they interpreted as a desire on 
the part of the Republicans to invite the Negro and give him suf- 
frage rights. They denounced the “infamous” apportionment pro- 
vision and the homestead clause.* They criticized the constitution 
because it allowed women to vote in matters pertaining to the com- 
mon schools; because it subordinated the Senate to the House; be- 
cause it was the officeseeker’s scheme to gain possession of the gov- 
ernment, and because it was monarchical and anti-republican and 
destroyed the independent power of the judiciary. The constitution 
would make Kansas the place of refuge for thieves, felons, and prof- 
ligates of every description from every State in the Union. Ata 
party meeting in Topeka, September 17, the Democratic committee 
wrote an address to the people of Kansas in which it emphatically 
denounced the “action of the majority of the late constitutional 
convention”; pledged its “opposition to the unfair, unjust, illiberal 
instrument framed by said convention,” approved and endorsed “‘the

. Abelard Guthrie, Diary, July 7 and Nov. 4, 1859.

action of the Democratic members of the late constitutional conven- 
tion,” and reaffirmed the “principles of the Tecumseh convention.” ® 
Some of the erstwhile Democrats had become lukewarm, however, 
and were experiencing a change of heart, or else they were political 
opportunists. Among them was ex-Governor Stanton who, while he 
had entertained grave objections to the instrument, was quoted as 
saying that a candid consideration of all the circumstances had 
caused him to conclude that the “highest interests of the people of 
Kansas demanded its adoption.” In so grave and important a mat- 
ter “as the laying of the fundamental law of the State which was 
to endure . . . as long as time should last,” the people should 
rise “above all party considerations.” Although he was opposed to 
the boundary as defined, to the apportionment clause, and to the 
subordination of the Senate to the House, nevertheless he felt that 
most of the objections could be remedied by amendment if they 
were found to work badly in practice. He feared that if the Demo- 
crats had had a majority in the convention they would have ar- 
ranged an apportionment that would have rendered their success in 
the first legislature not only probable but certain.? Stanton had his 
eye on the United States Senate. Probably he swung a number of 
Democratic votes for the constitution. McDowell wrote shortly be- 
fore the 4th of October that Leavenworth would vote a majority 
for the constitution.® 
The Republicans had an array of eminent and some notorious 
politicians stumping the territory. Among them were Hon. M. J. 
Parrott, Judge Conway, Colonel J. C. Vaughan, Hon. J. M. Winchell, 
H. P. Johnston, Governor Robinson, General Lane, James Blood, 
Colonel Leonard, Captain Montgomery, W. A. Phillips, T. Dwight 
Thacher, and others less prominent.? These men had at their com- 
mand such papers as the Lawrence Republican, the Emporia News, 
the Freedom’s Champion, the Mound City Herald, the Osawatomie 
Herald, the Cottonwood Falls Press, the White Cloud Chief, and the 
Manhattan Express.1° These papers were often merciless in their 
attack on the Democrats. Their most used and most effective 
weapon was their cry against the “great black dragon.” They 
characterized all Democrats as “border-ruffians,” men without

## Page 83

scruples, whose ultimate aim was to make Kansas a slave State. 
They made it appear that the Kansas Democrats were instructed 
from Washington, the Democratic headquarters. Thus wrote the 
Lawrence Republican: 
Who opposed the Constitution! The federal office holders to a man. Their 
bread and butter is involved; and, besides, they act under orders from Wash- 
ington. The old pro-slavery leaders, Easton, Abel, Stringfellow and their clans. 
They hate a Free State, and want to keep Kansas a slave territory as long as 
possible. The Administration Democrats generally adhere to the English bill, 
and hate Kansas, because she has so thoroughly and completely disorganized, 
demoralized, and broken down the administration party. Hence they wish to 
keep her out of the Union. Here you have the entire opposition to the con- 
stitution summed up: office holders, Buchanan Democrats, old pro-slavery 
leaders.11 
The Republican leaders made it clear that every vote cast against 
the Wyandotte constitution was “a vote against a Free-State and in 
favor of a Slave Territory.” The Democrats were just trying to 
deceive the public when they asserted that the contest for a free 
State in Kansas was settled. They pointed to the practice of slavery 
in the Territory and asserted that “this question will not be settled 
in favor of freedom, until we are admitted as a free State.” 22 In 
the northern counties they drew the railroad question into the fight. 
The Wyandotte Commercial Gazette told its readers that the suc- 
cess of Wyandotte depended upon the construction of the Kansas 
Central Railroad. The construction of the railroad was dependent 
upon the grants of land, which in turn were dependent upon the ad- 
mission of Kansas, and this depended upon the ratification of the 
constitution.? In the southern counties it was argued that the de- 
feat of the constitution would result in the annexation of the south 
Nebraska territory and thereby work a hardship on the South."* 
The Republicans did not try to meet the arguments of the Demo- 
crats but played upon the emotions, prejudices, and hatreds of the 
people. 
On the 12th of September, the officers of the Wyandotte Consti- 
tutional Convention issued a proclamation directing the attention 
of the people to the pending ratification of the constitution and to 
the homestead exemption provision. The proclamation provided 
that every white male person twenty-one years of age should have 
the right to vote, provided he was a citizen of the United States or 
11. Lawrence Republican, Sept. 29, 1859. 
12. Ibid., Sept. 15 and 29, 1859. 
13. Wyandotte Commercial Gazette, Oct. 1, 1859. 
14. Constitutions, Wyandotte, K. 8. H. S. Vault.

76 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
had declared his intention to become one; and provided he had re- 
sided in Kansas six months and in the township or ward in which he 
offered to vote thirty days, and had been duly registered.° The 
county board of canvassers should make their returns to J. M. 
Winchell, President of the Wyandotte Constitutional Convention. 
The proclamation was based on the schedule made at Wyandotte. 
Acting Governor Walsh, in Governor Medary’s absence, had also is- 
sued a proclamation to govern the ratification procedwe. It was 
based on the act which had provided for the constitutional conven- 
tion, and stated that all qualified voters under the Territorial law 
could vote on the constitution. The difference between the two 
proclamations was a matter of liberality. The Governor’s procla- 
mation defined as qualified voters “all male Indians who had been 
citizens of the United States” and had a ten days’ residence require- 
ment, whereas Winchell’s proclamation excluded the Indian and had 
a thirty days’ residence requirement. The Governor had dispensed 
with the registry law and required that the election returns be sent 
to Medary at Lecompton instead of to Winchell at Topeka.” Con- 
fusion resulted. Whether the discrepancy in the two sets of election 
requirements was ever straightened out is doubtful. To obviate the 
difficulties over the election returns, the officers of the Republican 
Central Committee issued a third proclamation and instructed the 
county officers to make out two certified copies of the election re- 
turns and send one to the Governor at Lecompton and the other to 
Winchell at Topeka.18 
The election was held October 4, 1859, and for the first time in the 
history of Kansas “all parties cast a full, free and independent 
vote.” 19 Nearly 16,000 votes were polled, with a majority of almost 
two to one in favor of the constitution. The vote was 10,421 for and 
5,530 against the constitution. Only Johnson and Morris Counties 
showed a majority opposed. The homestead exemption clause, al- 
though it, too, was ratified by a large vote, lacked a majority in 
Atchison, Doniphan, Jackson, Leavenworth, and Wyandotte Coun- 
ties, the Democratic strongholds.2° A comparative study of the 
election returns of 1859 reveals that a number of Democrats voted 
for adoption. It shows, too, that the Republicans, with a strenuous

effort in behalf of the constitution, must have brought many a dor- 
mant Republican back to life. Not more than five or six hundred 
Democrats could have voted in favor, and yet the vote for the con- 
stitution exceeded by 2,411 the highest vote cast for any one of the 
Republican candidates in the State election of 1859. This conclu- 
sion is reached by comparing the votes on the constitution with 
those cast in the election for State officers in December, 1859. 
Whether the Central Committee of the Republican Party was 
able to get aid from the East in the ratification contest is not known. 
That it made an attempt to get such aid is revealed in a letter of 
John A. Martin to President Winchell: 
The Central Committee have not yet been able to get any “material aid” 
.from the East, although they have used every exertion to arouse our friends 
there. We need it very much. The Democrats have plenty of money—they 
are determined to spare no exertions to defeat us, and will use every means to 
accomplish their purpose. We shall know in a few days whether the Republi- 
cans of the East are disposed to help us.?1 
With the ratification of the constitution a matter of history, the 
candidates for State offices increased their efforts. Notwithstanding 
the denunciation of the “infamous” constitution, the Democrats 
were willing to sacrifice themselves by running for office under it. 
Little is known of the Democratic Party rivalry before the day of 
the nominating convention held at Lawrence, October 25. The at- 
tendance was small, and only twenty-one counties were represented. 
Colonel Slough called the convention to order and G. H. Fairchild 
was elected chairman. The contest between the Administration 
forces and the Douglas wing was in evidence, but the officeholders 
carried the day. According to the Lawrence Republican, “prac- 
tically all officeholders in the territory except postmasters were dele- 
gates to the convention.” 22 The Medary family was well repre- 
sented. Governor Medary was there with his son, Samuel A. 
Medary, from Riley County, and his son-in-law, C. W. Blair from 
Bourbon County, whom Tom Corwin had defeated in Ohio in the 
election for Congress the year before.2? The convention had nom- 
inated Medary for Governor; J. P. Slough, Lieutenant Governor; 
Joseph Williams, Chief Justice; John A. Halderman, Congressman; 
O. G. Thurston, Attorney General; J. K. Goodkin, Auditor; Robert 
L. Pease, Treasurer; A. P. Walker, Secretary of State; J. S. McGill, 
Superintendent of Public Instruction; R. T. Mitchell, Associate

Judge for the short term and Samuel A. Stinson for the long term.** 
The White Cloud Chief wrote that nearly every Democratic candi- 
date had either been a member of the Wyandotte convention and 
had refused to sign the constitution or else had been in the caucus 
where the command to reject the constitution was issued.» Only 
Slough and Stinson, however, were members of the convention. 
Medary was in the caucus, but the names of the other caucus men 
were not revealed. A resolution of the Democratic convention en- 
dorsing Douglas was hooted and hissed down. It was generally con- 
ceded that the convention had repudiated Douglas and sustained 
Buchanan.”6 
The Republican Central Committee had issued a call for the State 
convention to meet at Topeka October 12, 1859. Leavenworth 
County was entitled to nine delegates, Douglas seven, Atchison five, 
Doniphan and Shawnee four each, Johnson, Wyandotte, Lykins, 
Linn, Bourbon, Jefferson, and Coffey three each, Riley, Allen, An- 
derson, Franklin, and Breckenridge two each, and the remaining 
counties one each.?" On the 18th of August the Wyandotte corre- 
spondent of the New York Times wrote that the approach of the 
autumnal elections had brought out numerous candidates for official 
honors. Among the most prominent lights for Governor he men- 
tioned Colonel Holliday of Topeka, ex-Mayor Adams and Rev. H. P. 
Johnston of Leavenworth, and Charles Robinson of Lawrence. For 
Representative to Congress he mentioned Judge Conway of Law- 
rence, Colonel Learnard of Burlington, Colonel Delahay of Leaven- 
worth, General Pomeroy of Atchison, Dr. Root of Wyandotte, and 
Dr. Danford of Linn County.?8 On the 15th of August, H. Wilson 
of Natick, Massachusetts, wrote Charles Robinson and expressed 
the interest of the East in the Kansas election: 
Our friends here in the East are not a little anxious about your new state. 
Don’t for God’s sake let the Democrats carry it. You must look to the matter. 
They are stronger than I wish to see them. I think it was a mistake to 
organize the Republican party before coming into the Union but you decided 
otherwise and now do not be defeated .29

“not a politician,” never had been, and so long as he had his reason

was a statesman and not a politician. The Douglas County Repub-

the State convention. The Thacher clique (really the Robinson 
men) at first denounced Lane and Conway for using the funds of 
the Republican committee to defeat Robinson, but at the proper 
moment “Thacher showed the white feather by seeking a compro- 
mise, and at the opening of the polls, a written compact was made 
between Lane and his opponents.” 3! For this occasion Thacher and 
Robinson had buried their differences. The Topeka Tribune pointed 
out that the agreement had been that “in consideration of the friends 
of Lane voting for Robinson for governor, the friends of Robinson 
should go for Lane for United States Senator.” ®2 George W. Brown 
wrote, however, that it had been a “perfect sell” on the part of Lane. 
It had not tied Lane and his friends to act for Robinson at Topeka, 
but merely “to labor in the county convention for the election of 
Robinson delegates to Topeka.” ®* The Lawrence Republican 
pointed out that the delegates nominated and approved at first did 
not include Thacher, but upon subsequent motions made by Lane 
and Conway his name was inserted.*4 The delegates who finally 
attended the convention from Douglas County were Lane, Conway, 
J. Miller, J. T. Ferrill, T. D. Thacher, W. R. Davis, and Jesse Mc- 
Pherson.** All efforts to adopt resolutions to instruct the delegates 
failed. It seems that Robinson did not attend the county conven- 
tion. 
In Leavenworth County were two aspirants for Governor, H. P. 
Johnston and ex-Mayor Adams. The two factions were equally 
powerful and neither candidate would yield. Hach faction, there- 
fore, elected a full set of delegates. The supporters of H. P. Johnston 
were charged with fraud, the charge being directed against Cham- 
pion Vaughan of Leavenworth.*® 
The Topeka State convention opened October 12. General S. C. 
Pomeroy called it to order. Colonel W. A. Phillips was elected

When the time came for the nomination of candidates, Lane and 
Conway attempted to have the nomination for Congressman decided 
first, but were overruled. Charles Robinson was nominated for 
Governor by T. D. Thacher, and H. P. Johnston by Champion 
Vaughan. In the first ballot Robinson defeated Johnston 43 to 34. 
The other candidates elected were: J. P. Root, Lieutenant Gover-

William Tholan, Treasurer; B. F. Simpson, Attorney General; Wil-

o’clock a.m. Sol Miller wrote that before the convention met, ‘the 
wire-pullers had conceded the governorship ta Leavenworth; but her 
greedy belligerents threw it away.” He had preferred a northern 
man as Governor for economic reasons.*® 
The campaign lasted almost two months. The Harper’s Ferry in- 
cident of October 16 and 17 had given the Democrats a new cam- 
paign issue. They had denounced the Republicans as abolitionists 
and supporters of John Brown and Montgomery, but now they 
spared no effort to place the responsibility for Harper’s Ferry upon 
the Republicans. They pointed out that John Brown, Gerrit Smith, 
James Redpath, William Lloyd Garrison, and Wendell Phillips were 
Republicans, and argued that the Harper’s Ferry incident was but 
an example of how the Republican Party was going to make war 
upon the South, and citing in support, statements by James Red- 
path.4!' The Atchison Union wrote: 
A year ago Old John Brown was a Republican hero—sans peur et sans 
reproche. He had no compeer in the affections and admiration of his Republi- 
can brethren. Lane and Montgomery, though ambitious to excel him, had 
neither of them climbed so high in distinguished consideration and regard, nor 
nestled so deep in the hearts of our Republican fellow citizens. Now mark

the change. Brown is denounced as an insane fanatic who ought to be “hung

In another article entitled “Old John Brown” the Union wrote 
that “no man in Kansas has pretended to deny Old John Brown led 
that murderous foray which massacred” the Doyles, Wilkinson and 
William Sherman at Pottawatomie.*? The Democrats published one 
of Governor Robinson’s letters to John Brown, using it to connect 
Robinson with the Harper’s Ferry incident. 
George W. Brown came to the support of Governor Robinson, Dr. 
Root, and Messrs. Ewing, Kingman, and Hillyer, and wrote that 
none of them was tinctured in the least with Old John Brownism, 
that Governor Robinson had opposed Brown’s “jayhawking move- 
ments” in southern Kansas from the first and in consequence was 
under the ban of those who had advocated the prolongation of the 
“bloody issue” in Kansas. Of the candidate for Representative to 
Congress he could not speak so confidently. “Mr. Conway has been 
on the most intimate terms with Old John Brown and it is said that 
Mr. Conway was Brown’s legal adviser,” and that the latter left 
boxes of Sharpes’ rifle cartridges or Colt’s navy revolvers or both 
“with Conway, either for safekeeping or collection, when in town, 
in cog., last summer.” 44 The Topeka Tribune warned the people 
not to elect a “land-grabber” as Governor. It preferred Medary, 
who had “the reputation of being the best governor Kansas has ever 
had, and the Republican press so declares.” *° 
The attack of the Democrats put the Republicans on the defen- 
sive. During the first two weeks of November, Governor Robinson, 
S. C. Pomeroy, H. J. Adams, and H. P. Johnston toured the northern 
counties of the Territory, while J. P. Root, William A. Phillips, 
J. W. Robinson, and S. C. Smith toured the central and southern 
counties. The next two weeks they exchanged sections. Martin F. 
Conway denied that John Brown had stopped at his house “when in 
Lawrence, in cog., last summer.” Brown had visited Lawrence in 
the fall of 1858, and at that time he had left relief papers with him, 
but Conway said nothing about the ammunition. He asserted that 
he had no knowledge of “ ‘Old Brown’s’ plans, either in Kansas or

There is not an accredited newspaper in the U.S. which has not condemned,

best interests of the Government to its dictation, that party is also just as 
strenuous in opposing all attempts at interference with it in the States where 
it legally exists.47 
The Republican leaders tried to convince the electorate that the 
office seekers of the Democratic Party were still holdovers of the 
“Old Border Ruffian” type, and untrustworthy. F. P. Stanton boldly 
supported the Republican Party, saying that there was a time when 
he hoped the Democratic Party would purify itself of the stain of 
Lecomptonism, but they failed to do so. He blamed the Democratic 
Party for all the misrule and outrages in Kansas. The Republican 
ticket was worthy of confidence and he would vote for it.*® 
As has been indicated, the campaign was devoted to the slavery 
question, the slinging of mud, and the Harper’s Ferry insurrection. 
Some attention was given to the question of railroads. John J. 
Ingalls wrote his father that “the men are rather inefficient generally 
and would hardly rank third class in New England, but the poorest 
state government would be preferable to this condition of none at 
all.” #9 Sol Miller expressed his objections as follows: 
Three objections we will name here: Firstly, the convention made a mistake, 
and selected several of the candidates from Northern Kansas. Secondly, we 
think there are a few more Robinsons in the territory, whose claims have been 
overlooked. Thirdly, we believe the principal part of the ticket was pre- 
arranged by the wire-pullers, and secured by trading and dickering. A part 
of the program was understood while the constitutional convention was in 
session, and the arrangement extends to the choice of United States Senators, 
the railroad interests, and so forth.5° 
Ingalls and Miller, however, both supported the Republican ticket. 
Two Republican newspapers had been started in Kansas that as- 
sisted in the campaign, the Kansas State Record, of Topeka, with 
E. G. and W. W. Ross as editors, and the Neosho Valley Register, 
with 8. S. Prouty as editor. —Two Democratic papers were likewise 
established, the Olathe Herald, with Devenney and Giffen as edi-

Where the money to finance political campaigns comes from is 
always of interest. Among the John Brown Papers is a letter of 
M. F. Conway addressed to G. L. Stearns, of Boston, Massachusetts, 
in which Conway asked Stearns for money for the campaign. He 
wanted it by June 1, but did not specify the amount. Conway did 
not ask for a gift, because he feared it would be asking too much in 
view of what Stearns had done for him. He promised, therefore, to 
pay “good interest.” °° On the 19th of November the Topeka T'rib- 
une quoted the Herald of Freedom: 
A few weeks since, we learn, he [Conway] wrote to Gov. Morgan of New 
York, for money; that it was needed to help carry the election. A draft of 
one thousand dollars was forwarded him, and cashed by a banker in this 
place, who marked the notes which were paid out. This money, so far from 
being used against the democracy, has been paid out to partisan tools, so that 
by their help Mr. Conway could control the nominations in this county 56 
On the 5th of July, 1859, Thomas H. Webb, of Boston, wrote R. J. 
Hinton: 
You say that assistance is needed for the rightful conducting of the campaign. 
Some considerable, for these hard times, has already gone out—perhaps a little 
more can be furnished, if absolutely requisite. . . . I will have another 
talk with some of our trusty ones, and will also see what can be done in the 
way of supply of speeches, and other documents.5? 
Sol Miller, in referring to the campaign, wrote: “Money was 
squandered freely, slanders were circulated unsparingly, and local 
prejudices were worked upon incessantly.” °°

The way the money was spent is likewise of interest. Thomas H. 
Webb warned Conway that there were serious charges against him. 
The charge which would be the most likely to prove injurious was 
“that during the late electioneering groceries were kept open at your 
expense, where persons might go, and did go and imbibe freely with- 
out money and without price.” It had injured the name and fame 
of one such establishment which was owned by the Emigrant Aid 
Company, and had seriously damaged the cause of temperance. 
Both Howe and Stearns called on Webb in regard to the charges.*? 
The returns of the election of State officers, held December 1, 1859, 
showed that the entire Republican ticket was elected. Martin F. 
Conway received the lowest popular vote of any of the successful 
candidates. He lost heavily in Leavenworth and Bourbon Counties. 
Where Governor Robinson had a popular majority of 2,513 and 
Ewing 2,709, Conway had only 2,097.° Of the seventy-five repre- 
sentatives, the Republicans elected sixty-four. Nine of the eleven 
Democrats were elected in Leavenworth County. Only three Demo- 
cratic senators were elected, all in Leavenworth County.*t The Re- 
publican scheme had been successful. There was a report of fraud 
in Doniphan County and the election was contested, with the result 
that all the votes in the precincts where irregular voting had oc- 
curred were thrown out. This gave the Republican Party the ma- 
jority in the county except that the Democrats elected one repre- 
sentative to the State legislature. Charges of irregular voting were 
also brought against two of the most populous Republican townships 
in Shawnee County, but nothing was done about it.62 With the Wy- 
andotte constitution ratified and the State officers elected, interest in 
the Kansas question shifted to Washington.

## Page 93

Chapter V 
Admission of Kansas to the Union 
1860-1861 
N accordance with section 22 of the schedule attached to the con- 
stitution, W. F. N. Arny was sent to Washington to present 
copies of the Wyandotte constitution to Congress and the President. 
He arrived there December 23, 1859, and presented the copies to 
Representative-elect Martin F. Conway. The following day Con- 
way asked President Buchanan to communicate the constitution in 
a message to Congress as in the case of Oregon. The President had 
delivered his message to Congress on December 19, and thought it 
unnecessary for the constitution to reach Congress through him un- 
less there was but one copy.! In his message of the 19th the Presi- 
dent had failed to mention the Wyandotte constitution and the ques- 
tion of admiggion. Conway therefore proceeded to Congress and 
placed the copies of the constitution in the hands of Representative 
Galusha A. Grow of Pennsylvania. 
On the 15th of February, 1860, Grow introduced a bill to admit 
Kansas under the Wyandotte constitution. It was considered in the 
House intermittently until April 11, when it passed by a vote of 134 
to 73.2. The bill was supported by Grow of Pennsylvania and David 
W. Gooch of Massachusetts and opposed by Clark of Missouri and 
Maynard of Tennessee. Since the real opposition was in the Senate 
and the arguments against the bill were much alike in both houses, 
the debates will be discussed later. 
On the 21st of February, William H. Seward, of New York, intro- 
’ duced Senate bill No. 194, asking for admission of Kansas. On the 
12th of April, 1860, the clerk of the House reported the House bill 
to the Senate. The following day the Senate referred the House bill 
to the Committee on Territories, with James S. Green, of Missouri, 
as chairman. On the 16th of May, the chairman reported it back 
with instructions from the committee to report the bill without 
recommendations.* After that the bill was debated, intermittently, 
1. Messages and Papers of the President, V, 552; White Cloud Chief, Jan. 5, 1860. 
2. Congressional Globe, 1859-1860, pp. 795, 1662-1672. 
83. Globe, pp. 848, 1672, 1698, 2117. 
(85) 


## Page 94

86 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
until Robert M. T: Hunter of Virginia, June 5, moved to postpone 
it. The motion carried, 32 to 27. Two days later Edward Wade, of 
Ohio, moved to reconsider the Kansas bill, but the motion was lost, 
26 to 32.4 While the bill was in the hands of the Committee on 
Territories, an attempt to annex the South Platte country failed by 
a tie vote. Six committee members were present. Mr. Latham, of 
California, contrary to expectations, had voted against annexation.° 
On the motion to postpone the Admission bill, all but two of the 
Democrats voted in the affirmative, Pugh of Ohio and Latham of 
California. Kennedy voted with the Democrats, and Crittenden, 
Nicholson, Douglas, and Clay were absent.® A spirited debate had 
preceded the vote to postpone, participated in by Sumner, Collamer, 
and Wade in favor of the Admission bill, and Green, Chestnut, and 
Louis J. Wigfall in opposition. Wigfall, of Texas, had taken oc- 
casion to malign the Kansas people with the suggestion that they 
were not respectable enough for Texans to associate with. The 
North and Europe, he said, had been drained of vagabonds who had 
been sent to Kansas as emigrants, with the result that the outlaws 
and traitors were in the majority in the Territory. Evidently he 
had been reading the Kansas newspapers. Even J. A. Halderman, 
of Leavenworth, a Democrat, had suffered under the stinging de- 
nunciation of these words and remarked that if he had been privi- 
leged to address Congress he would have denounced these charges in 
terms warranted by their falsity.’ The main arguments, however, 
centered about the objections to the bill and the attempt to change 
the boundary. The opponents of the bill said that those best in- 
formed as to the population of the Territory placed it at 69,700 
which was more than 23,000 below the legal requirement for state- 
hood. Moreover, they maintained that the boundaries of Kansas 
provided for in the constitution included within their limits lands 
belonging to the Cherokee Indians and that these lands could not be 
included “within the Territorial limits or jurisdiction of any State 
or Territory” without the Indians’ consent. This restriction was 
embodied in the treaty of 1835 and also in the Kansas-Nebraska 
Act. The way out was to annex the Platte region on the north and 
cut off the Cherokee strip on the south. Green of Missouri there- 
fore proposed an amendment providing for the change in bounda-

## Page 95

The Birth of Kansas 87 
ries.8 When the Senate was ready to vote on the amendment, 
Hunter of Virginia moved to postpone the bill. The motion was 
successful. Collamer of Vermont, a member of the Committee on 
Territories, contended that there was no reason to withhold the ad- 
mission of Kansas, that the constitution was republican in form, and 
the population, based on the vote on the constitution and the cor- 
rected estimate of the Committee of the Territorial Legislature, 
would total more than 97,000. Oregon had been admitted with a 
population of less than 50,000, and only two years before the op- 
ponents had been willing to admit Kansas with a population of less 
than 93,000, and with a boundary no less open to the objection that 
it violated the Indian treaty.2 The Democrats were unwilling to 
add another Republican State just before the Presidential election. 
The Kansas question made a good campaign issue for the Republi- 
can Party also outside of Kansas. 
The Kansas Democrats were active in Washington, laboring 
against admission. Messrs. Pettit, Elmore, Mitchell, Davis, Jones, 
McDowell, Isaacs, and Lumb were among those who used their in- 
fluence in Congress against admission unless the boundaries should 
be changed so as to include the Platte River region and the region 
including Pike’s Peak. The Democrats proposed to the Kansas Re- 
publicans in Washington that, if they would include the Platte River 
region, the Democrats would support the admission of Kansas.'° 
This the Republicans refused to do. 
When the people of Kansas learned that Congress had tabled the 
Admission bill they lost patience and raised the question of respon- 
sibility. Many blamed Stephen A. Douglas. The Kansas State 
Record wrote that with all his pretended zeal for the will of the 
people, Douglas “had not the moral courage to plead for the admis- 
sion of Kansas, lest, in doing so, he should offend the South. He is 
as bold as a lion to do wrong; but he hasn’t the courage of a mouse 
in the cause of humanity and justice.” 11 Others thought of calling 
still another constitutional convention and framing a fifth constitu- 
tion. This proposal was advanced by disappointed politicians and 
those jealous of the success of Topeka in the capital contest.1? Still 
others considered the idea of establishing an independent govern- 
ment under the Wyandotte constitution, should Congress refuse to 
8. Globe., pp. 2617-2622. 
9. Globe, pp. 2462-2464. 
10. Lawrence Republican, Feb. 2 and 9, 1860. 
11. Kansas State Record, Aug. 11, 1860. 
12. Ibid., July 14, 1860; Topeka Tribune, Jan. 14, 1861.

admit Kansas under this document.1* Most of the people were will- 
ing to wait and give Congress another chance. The Presidential 
election would be over by the time the next Congress convened, and 
other obstructions would then be removed. In the meantime the 
Kansas drought had produced a famine. Incomes had vanished and 
people suffered for lack of food and clothing. 
When the second session of the 36th Congress met it fell to the 
lot of Jacob Collamer of Vermont to guide the admission bill through 
the Senate. On December 11, 1860, he called up House bill No. 23. 
Again he had to face the Democratic opposition under the crafty 
leadership of James S. Green of Missouri. The bill was before the 
Senate from the 11th of December until it was passed by that body 
on the 21st of January, 1861. It was concurred in by the House on 
the 28th, and approved by the President on the 29th of January.'* 
Both parties were vigorous in debate. On December 24, 1860, 
Alfred O. P. Nicholson of Tennessee, in a powerful speech on behalf 
of the South, criticised the North for failing to abide by the Fugitive 
Slave Law. He pointed out the difference in the ethics of the South 
and North on the question of slavery and said that the North had 
incorporated their ethical principles into a political platform, sup- 
ported by a political organization, “which must, from its principles, 
be confined to one section, and that the stronger section, with the 
avowed purpose of so construing and administering the constitution 
as finally to extinguish the institution of slavery.” He concluded 
with a plea for peaceful separation, in case no satisfactory agree- 
ment could be reached between the two sections.®> On the 18th of 
January, Green again advocated the amendments he had introduced 
in the previous session. The boundary amendment proposed to add 
the Platte River region or the north and cut off the Cherokee neutral 
lands and the Osage lands on the south. The amendment was de- 
feated, 23 to 31.1° Douglas said he would support the amendment 
provided it were ratified by a majority of the Kansas people and the 
change could be made without delaying the admission of Kansas. 
As to Green’s objection that the boundary as defined in the consti- 
tution would interfere with the Indian treaty, Douglas said that the 
United States Constitution, and all laws and treaties in accordance 
with it, were the supreme law of the land, anything in the constitu- 
tion or laws of any State to the contrary notwithstanding. No Kan-

sas constitution or law could impair any of the rights secured in the 
Indian treaties, and he could not regard Green’s objection an in- 
superable one.17 On January 19, Graham N. Fitch, of Indiana, 
moved to add new sections 4 and 5 to the measure, constituting 
Kansas a judicial district of the United States, with “like powers 
and jurisdiction as the district court of the United States for the 
District of Minnesota.” The purpose of the amendment was, ac- 
cording to Fitch, to establish a Federal District Court in Kansas to 
decide whether the Territorial legislature had the power to abolish 
slavery within its jurisdiction. The lower court had decided against 
this. It was his opinion that if the amendment should fail the case 
upon appeal would be lost for want of a court through which it 
could be carried to the United States Supreme Court. The amend- 
ment failed, 26 to 27.15 Douglas objected to it because it was not 
approved by the Judicial Committee. Also, he disagreed with the 
Senator from Indiana that the admission of Kansas would remove 
the right of appeal. There was no necessity to delay admission any 
longer. Turning to the Senator from Missouri he said: 
The Senator . . . well knows that this Kansas question has been here 
for years, and no consideration on earth could suffice to stop it in this body 
three years ago, when it came under the Lecompton constitution. It was not 
stopped then to be amended for the want of Judiciary or any other clauses; 
but it was forced through. We are told, first, that Kansas must be kept out 
because her Northern boundary is not right, when it is the same now as it 
was then; next that she must be kept out because her Southern boundary is 
not right, when it is the same now as it was then; again she must be kept out 
because of the Indian Treaties, though the same objection existed then as 
now; again she must be kept out because she has not population enough, 
though she has three times as many people as were there then; and, finally, 
this bill must be delayed now because it does not contain a judiciary clause. 
I do not understand why these constant objections are being interposed to the 
admission of Kansas now, when none of them were present in regard to the 
Lecompton constitution, three years ago, nor in regard to the admission of 
Oregon, which has since taken place.19 
If Douglas’s points are valid, why did he not think of them in the 
previous session? 
Green offered another amendment affecting school lands. He 
claimed that two-thirds of the school sections, 16 and 32, which 
should have been reserved for schools in each township, had been 
sold in violation of the Organic Act. Douglas knew of only one sec-

tion in Atchison County that had been sold in violation of the act. 
Green’s amendment would have made it impossible for the State to

He proposed to confirm the title of those who in good faith had paid

On the 21st of January, 1861, the Senators from Mississippi, Ala- 
bama, and Florida withdrew. The South Carolina Senators had re- 
signed in November of 1860, before Congress convened.*! In his 
farewell address Jefferson Davis defended the right of secession, jus- 
tifying his belief on the ground of State sovereignty. He regretted 
that the situation demanded the withdrawal of Mississippi from the 
Union, and the severing of ties of affection established. He enter- 
tained no personal feeling of hostility to the Senators from the 
North, but ‘wished them and their people well.” He asked forgive- 
ness for any pain he might have inflicted in the heat of the discus- 
sions and expressed his willingness to forgive others.22 Rhodes 
writes that the audience was affected to tears by “the plaintive music 
of his voice” and quotes the statement from the Memoirs of Jefferson 
Davis that “that night Davis wrestled in prayer, offering up more 
than once the supplications: ‘May God have us in his holy keeping, 
and grant that before it is too late, peaceful council may prevail.’ ” 78 
After the Southern Senators had withdrawn, the Vice-President 
called up the unfinished business of the admission bill. Green of 
Missouri withdrew the Pike’s Peak territorial amendment, but Fitch 
of Indiana reintroduced the amendment on the judiciary, previously 
lost by 26 to 27. The amendment now passed, 29 to 28.24 A com- 
parison of the votes shows that Crittenden, who had voted in the 
affirmative the first time, now voted in the negative; whereas 
Latham, Kennedy, Pearce, and Slidell, who had not voted the first 
time, now voted in the affirmative. Since no further amendments 
were pending, the Senate passed the bill as amended, 36 to 16, and 
sent it to the House for its concurrence.2° 
The question arises, Did the admission of Kansas have to wait 
for the withdrawal of Southern Senators? That is doubtful. There 
were sixty-six members of the Senate. Thirty-six had voted for ad- ee Se a ace or RAE Oe) Lee

mission, more than a majority of the total number. It is possible 
that the withdrawal of the Southern Senators influenced the North- 
ern Democrats to vote for admission. On the other hand, the Presi- 
dential election was over and the motive for withholding admission 
had been removed. In comparing the vote on Hunter’s motion to 
postpone the bill, July 5, 1860, with the vote on admission, the fol- 
lowing changes appear. On the 5th of July, Bright and Fitch of 
Indiana, Rice of Minnesota, and Johnson of Tennessee, had voted 
to postpone; on January 21, 1861, they voted for admission; Doug- 
las, Crittenden, Thompson, and Morrill, who before the election had 
not voted, now voted for admission. This gave Kansas nine addi- 
tional votes, which, when added to the twenty-seven cast for Kansas 
on July 5, accounts for the thirty-six votes. The result would have 
been the same, therefore, even though all the Southern Senators had 
been present and voted against admission. There were other 
changes. On the 5th of July, Bayard and Saulsbury of Delaware, 
Gwin of California, Lane of Oregon, Pearce of Maryland, and Nich- 
olson of Tennessee, had voted to postpone; on January 21, they did 
not vote. In addition there were the nine Southern Senators who 
had voted to postpone but had left the Senate before the vote for 
admission was taken. The answer to the query therefore depends 
on what motive prompted the Northern Democrats to change their 
votes. The answer may be that it was the Presidential election 
which caused the northern Democrats to vote against admission in 
the first session, and the withdrawal of the Southern Senators that 
influenced them to vote for admission in the second session of the 
36th Congress. 
The House received the bill on January 21, and on the 28th Grow 
of Pennsylvania asked for the privilege of suspending the rules to 
take up the Kansas bill. The House yielded graciously, 119 to 41. 
On the same day the Senate amendment was concurred in and the 
bill passed the House.?® The President signed the bill on the 29th 
of January and notified the House and Senate on the 30th and 31st, 
respectively. The conflict had lasted five long years, three months 
and six days. 
The news of the admission of Kansas was announced by the 
Leavenworth Daily Conservative in an extra edition, January 29, 
1861.27 It was an occasion of great rejoicing. The Lawrence Re- 
publican wrote: 
26. Senate Journal, pp. 236, 237. 
27. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Jan. 30 and 31, 1861.

92 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Our whole town was elated. Men ran from place to place proclaiming the 
glad tidings. Cheering and music and all manner of exultation was heard 
everywhere through our streets. A deputation was immediately sent to Capt. 
Bickerton’s for that celebrated old piece, the Sacremento, and it was brought 
to town after dark and thirty-four guns fired at about twelve o’clock, and 
renewed at sunrise this morning. . . . never in the history of Kansas was 
such exultation known amongst our people.28 
Judge Simpson, of Lawrence, wrote that “toasts were drunk; songs 
were sung; speeches were made, and—well, the truth is that my 
recollection is not good after midnight. You must recollect that the 
main question then was admission, not prohibition.” 29 The Leaven- 
worth Daily Conservative turned to poetry: 
Jewhillaker! what Jlorious news this is at any rate, 
Which makes us fellow-citizens and lords of a new State! 
‘Tis “Bleeding Kansas” now no more, nor “Kansas Territory,” 
But a 2:40 sovereign nag careering in her glory. 
Go ‘lang! Stand by! We'll beat the world, against the which we are 
pitted, 
Since Congress has resigned the reins, and Kansas is admitted. 
Let South Carolina now secede, if fractious spirits bind her; 
We can without her scud along, and so we needn’t mind her. 
Why; all the cotton States might go from ‘Yankee land to sever, 
And blithe, highmettled Kansas still could make good time as ever. 
Go ‘lang! Stand by! etc.30 
The Act of Congress, which governed the admission of Kansas, 
contained five sections. Section one specified the boundaries of the 
State and asserted that the Indian treaty rights were to remain un- 
altered until the Tribes should signify a desire to extinguish them. 
Section two pertained to representation. Section three repealed the 
ordinance and resolutions in the constitution and substituted the 
following provisions: Sections 16 and 36 in each township were to 
be set aside for common schools; if already disposed of, other lands 
equivalent in value should be reserved; seventy-two sections were 
to be set aside for a State University; ten sections should be set 
aside for public buildings; twelve salt springs and six sections of 
land with each spring were to be selected to be given by the Federal 
Government to the State, the selection to be by the Governor; in 
addition, the State should receive five percent of the net proceeds of 
28. Lawrence Republican, Jan. 31, 1861. 
A S Charles Robinson, Kansas Conflict, Chap. 17, p. 14 of Manuscript, K. S. H. S., opeka. 
30. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Jan. 31, 1861. 


## Page 101

The Birth of Kansas 93 
the sale of all public lands within its boundaries, and this money 
should be used by the State for public roads and internal improve- 
ments; to offset these concessions, Kansas should agree not to tax 
the Federal lands within the State. Sections four and five comprised 
the Fitch amendment and made Kansas a judicial district of the 
United States.3! 
Not until January 23, 1862, did Kansas ratify the Act of Admis- 
sion. The first legislature had passed an ordinance accepting the 
conditions of admission, but the Commissioners on Public Lands 
reported, January 14, 1862, that they had no record of it, nor had 
it been published in the book of laws.3? Since Congress had given 
the State only a year within which to ratify the ordinance and select 
the lands, it became necessary for the second legislature to act at 
once. The resolution was passed on the 23d of January, 1862, and 
approved by the Governor on the same day, and the admission of 
Kansas was legally completed.*?

## Page 102

Chapter VI 
The Robinson Administration, 1861-1863 
HE birth of Kansas came at an unpropitious time. The sea- 
son of the year was winter. Kansas granaries were empty after 
the long extended drought. Financial resources were exhausted, and 
the citizens were subdued by famine. In the words of E. C. Mann- 
ing, the child had arrived “bawling” and hungry.t Not only had 
the hardships that accompanied the long generation period fostered 
sectional hostility, but they had also aroused world-wide interest in 
the outcome of the struggle. 
It was in very difficult circumstances, therefore, that Governor 
Robinson took the oath of office and began the task of setting the 
government in motion. The State was beset with ambitious office 
seekers, border warfare threatened the peace of southern Kansas, 
vicious factions divided the State, and civil war endangered the 
Union. 
On the 9th of February, 1861, Governor Robinson took the oath of 
office, administered by Caleb S. Pratt, of Douglas County, and pro- 
claimed Kansas a State in the Union. He called upon all officers 
elected under the constitution to enter upon the discharge of their 
respective duties, and upon the State legislature to convene at To- 
peka on Tuesday, March 26.2, He called the voters’ attention to the 
fact that there were five vacancies in the Assembly which had to be 
filled by special election before the legislature could convene.? A 
few days later he went to Topeka to inspect the temporary seat of 
government. He avoided publicity and shunned! the reception 
planned for him.* During the last week of February he left for the 
East. Thomas Ewing, Jr., had sent him a telegram from Washing- 
ton asking him to come ae once.® 
Governor Robinson had not been without political ambitions. 
Although he had been cautious in revealing his aspirations, it is not 
known that he ever refused a nomination tendered him. He was 
1. E. C. Manning, ‘In at the Birth,” Collections, VII, 204. 
2. Lawrence Republican, Feb. 14, 1861. 
8. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Feb. 18, 1861. 
4. Topeka Tribune, Feb. 16, 1861. 
5. Kansas State Journal, Feb. 28, 1861; White Cloud Chief, Mar. 21, 1861. 
(94) 


## Page 103

The Birth of Kansas 95 
more interested in a Federal appointment, however, than in the 
Governorship. Mrs. Robinson, too, preferred to picture her future 
in Washington society. On the 30th of December, 1860, she wrote 
the Governor from Belcherton, Massachusetts, that she had some- 
times thought she had done wrong in “favoring—perhaps urging” 
him to advance his claims for an office in Washington. Yet one 
could live as pure a life there as in the most retired place and the 
light coming from a position at Washington would “be a beacon to 
many more than in the latter circumstances.’”® The Federal office 
referred to was that of Commissioner of Indian Affairs. Thomas 
Ewing was helping Robinson in his attempt to get the appointment. 
In case they were successful Robinson would throw his influence to 
Ewing in the election of United States Senators.7’ Both seem to have 
aspired to positions beyond those to which they were elected. There 
is some evidence that Governor Robinson would have been willing 
to give up the position as Governor for the Indian office,’ which 
would have tied up with the Governor’s interests in Quindaro and 
with his railroad projects. The President, however, already had 
promised the position to William P. Dole, of Ilinois.® 
In governing the State, Governor Robinson was following the 
“Fabian policy,” suggested to him by A. A. Lawrence, who in 1856 
had urged “the greatest forbearance, total discouragement of all 
aggression, a deadly though smiling quiet.”!° This had already been 
his policy during the Territorial period. When General Lane was 
busy organizing the Republican Party, Robinson wrote: ‘As to the 
organization of parties, I have but a word to say. I am simply a 
looker-on, and disposed to let the politicians make their own bed.” 
When the Harper’s Ferry incident threatened the reputation of the 
Kansas people and the Free-State Party, he distinguished between 
the views of the radical abolitionists of Kansas and the conservative 
leaders of the Free-State Party. He pointed out that the true Free- 
State leaders had opposed the activities of Old John Brown, Captain 
Montgomery, and General Lane. To him it was clear beyond a 
doubt that the leaders in both the Harper’s Ferry insurrection and 
the southern Kansas troubles had codperated in word and deed.”

96 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
He used this issue to discredit the Lane faction, which quickened 
the hostility of the two opposing factions. As Governor of Kansas 
Robinson attempted to restore peace and order, and tried to ad- 
minister the State as economically as possible. In line with this, 
he favored a small legislature. His attempt to control the political 
patronage of the State heaped upon him severe criticism and in- 
volved the factions in further disputes."® 
Governor Robinson’s failure to convene the legislature immedi- 
ately after admission occasioned a political controversy. The ac- 
cusation was that he had delayed the meeting to enable him to 
arrange the political patronage of the State before the Senators 
reached Washington. Lyman Trumbell of Illinois wrote Delahay 
that the bill to admit Kansas had been hurried through “for the very 
purpose of getting the Kansas Senators” to Washington before the 
4th of March. “The fate of the country may depend upon two 
votes. Robinson must have known this.” So far as Trumbell had 
the ability to prevent it, neither Robinson nor any person acting 
with him should gain anything by this course.1* The friends of Lane 
circulated petitions in Leavenworth, Topeka, and other towns asking 
the President-to withhold all appointments until the Kansas Senators 
reached Washington. General Lane himself had arranged with 
Representative Conway that all appointments should be withheld 
until after the Senators were elected.° The real reason for delaying 
the meeting of the legislature was to enable Governor Robinson to 
find out whether he himself might get the appointment as Indian 
Commissioner. In case he was successful he would throw his in- 
fluence to Ewing before the legislature convened and thus elect 
Ewing to the Senate. The two together would then control the rail- 
road interests of the State.16 When Robinson failed to get the ap- 
pointment, Ewing withdrew from the Senatorial race. 
The newspapers reported that Washington was crowded with Kan- 
sans anxious to serve their country and be well served in return. 
A Washington reporter for a Leavenworth paper wrote that, at the 
Department of the Interior, every other column had a Kansas ap- 
plicant leaning against it. Hutchinson of Lawrence, always waiting 
in the hall of the State Department, wanted a consulship. The re-

porter expressed surprise at seeing so many business men, who sup-

Among the favored citizens of the State were M. W. Delahay, 
appointed Surveyor General of Kansas and Nebraska; Judge John 
T. Burris, United States Attorney for the District of Kansas; C. B. 
Keith, Agent of the Kickapoo Indians; James B. Abbott, Agent of 
the Shawnee Indians; W. W. Ross, Agent of the Pottawatomie In- 
dians; J. C. Burnett, Register of the Land Office at Fort Scott; F. 
G. Adams, Register of the Land Office at Lecompton; A. Law, Regis- 
ter of the Land Office at Kickapoo. The Pottawatomie Agency had 
been promised to George W. Deitzler, but Lane’s influence in the 
Senate had given the position to Ross.18 Most of the successful 
candidates were supporters of Lane. Keith had been recommended 
by Pomeroy.’® It was General Lane’s persistence that helped him 
to win out at Washington. 
In May, 1861, a dispute had arisen as to the qualifications of the 
State Treasurer, William Tholan. Governor Robinson had refused 
to recognize him because of his failure to procure the $200,000 bond 
required for the office. The bond was to bear the signature of five 
or more guarantors who would meet with the approval of the Gover- 
nor.2° The State legislature had made the requirements.*!_ Tholan 
had supported Lane during the Senatorial election in April, 1861. 
In his place the Governor appointed, in June, 1861, the Hon. H. R. 
Dutton of Brown County. 
The meeting of the State legislature occasioned much feverish 
activity. Almost a month before its organization Topeka was filled 
to overflowing with legislators, State officers, political office seekers, 
and lobbyists. The Senatorial candidates had arrived early and 
established headquarters. General Pomeroy had chosen the Chase 
House, General Lane and Colonel Delahay the Garvey House. The 
Ritchie buildings were used by the State officers. A hotel hastily 
constructed near the Chase House, accommodated other candidates. 
Continuous lines of persons could be seen radiating from these build- 
ings. From early morning until late at night the streets were dotted 
with comers and goers. With this mass of men there was but one 
business, “that of caucusing on the senatorial question.” ‘“Combina-

tions and ruptures, instructions and appointments, claims and dis-

that the destiny of Kansas [was] the election of Senators and after 
that either the deluge or the millennium.” ”? 
In this atmosphere the legislature was organized. Sixty-four Re- 
publicans and eleven Democrats had been elected to the House, and 
twenty-two Republicans and three Democrats to the Senate. A 
Lane-Parrott combination had been arranged for the election of 
United States Senators. This combination had elected W. W. Upde- 
graph Speaker of the House.?? Updegraph’s strongest opponent had 
been a Pomeroy supporter, J. W. Scott of Allen County. In the 
Senate, Lieutenant Governor J. P. Root presided, and John J. 
Ingalls had been elected secretary.24 The State legislature adopted 
the rules and regulations of the Territorial legislature for the joint 
and separate meetings. 
In his message to the legislature on March 30, Governor Robinson, 
after summarizing the Territorial history of Kansas, recommended 
that the legislature urge the Federal Government to assume the 
Territorial indebtedness and the losses incurred during the period of 
the Kansas Civil War. These losses he estimated at $100,000 and 
$500,000, respectively. He specified the legislation necessary to 
execute the provisions of the constitution, establish a system of State 
courts and define the jurisdiction of each, improve the system of 
education, establish benevolent and penal institutions, organize the 
State militia, apportion the State for the election of Senators and 
Representatives, assess the taxes, provide for printing the public 
documents, establish property rights for women, provide for the 
permanent location of the capital, remove the suits from the Terri- 
torial to the State courts, and provide for a State seal. Since the 
Territorial laws would remain in force until they expired or were 
repealed, it would not be necessary to attend to all those things at 
once. The legislation that should be attended to immediately 
should provide for a system of State courts, locate the capital, and 
apportion the State. Governor Robinson asked the legislature to 
provide for a codifying committee, to be authorized to bring the 
Territorial laws into harmony with the provisions of the State con- 
stitution. The legislature should reduce the House of Representa- 
tives to forty-five and the Senate to fifteen members. This would 
make for efficiency and economy and serve the “public interest quite 
22. Kansas State Journal, March 28, 1861. 
23. Topeka Tribune, March 30, 1861; House and Senate Journal, 1861, pp. 3-13. 
24. Senate Journal, 1861, p. 3 ff. 


## Page 107

The Birth of Kansas 99 
as well as the present number and reduce the per diem aggregate 
nearly one-half.” As to the threatening danger of civil war, he 
wrote: 
If it is true that the continued existence of slavery requires the destruction 
of the Union, it is time to ask if the existence of the Union does not require 
the destruction of slavery. If such an issue be forced on the Union it must 
be met, and met promptly. . . . Kansas, though the last and least of the 
States in the Union, will ever be ready to answer the call of her country.25 
The message had in view the welfare of the new State and was 
well received. 
The legislature at once proceeded to elect the United States Sena- 
tors. The candidates for the positions were Thomas Ewing, F. P. 
Stanton, M. W. Delahay, M. J. Parrott, S. C. Pomeroy, and James 
H. Lane. There were some Democratic aspirants like Judge Wil- 
liams and P. T. Abell, but it was evident that the party had no 
chance in the contest. Thomas Ewing had entered his name in the 
race, but shortly before the election he withdrew in favor of Parrott. 
The Truth-Teller explained Ewing’s withdrawal as due to financial 
reasons. Ewing had placed $3,000 in the hands of his lieutenants to 
help win the election, but when he discovered that Pomeroy had 
“$5,000 up and plenty more in reserve ‘he drew out.’” 26 According 
to Ewing’s letters however, he had little hope of being elected to 
the Senate unless Robinson was appointed Indian Commissioner.27 
Ewing was considered a candidate from the north side of the Kan- 
sas River, competing with Parrott and Pomeroy. 
M. W. Delahay and F. P. Stanton were less conspicuous than 
Parrott, Pomeroy, and Lane. It appears that Delahay never seri- 
ously entertained the thought of winning the contest. His hopes lay 
in Washington. Stanton, however, was deeply interested in the 
contest. He had served in the popular branch of the Federal legis- 
lature in 1854, and had voted for the Kansas-Nebraska bill. He 
had come to Kansas an Administration Democrat as Secretary of 
the Territory, but had changed parties at the time of the State elec- 
tion under the Wyandotte constitution. It was Stanton’s victory in 
the Senate over Parrott and Lane, April 1, overruled by subsequent 
action, that broke the Lane-Parrott combination and led to the 
Lane-Pomeroy combination.”® 
Hon. M. J. Parrott of Leavenworth had been the Territorial dele- 
gate and representative in Congress the past three years. An anti-

Parrott paper said of him that if his past record in Congress was a 
criterion of his future course there was no need of electing him to the 
Senate.2® The Leavenworth Times pointed out that the twelve Re- 
publican candidates for the State legislature from Leavenworth 
County, who had been defeated in the election by the Democrats, 
had all opposed Parrott.2° He was supported, however, by the 
White Cloud Chief, the Leavenworth Daily Conservative, the West- 
ern Kansas Express, and the Elwood Free Press. The Chief and the 
Express supported him because he represented their railroad in- 
terests.21_ Parrott’s “bowers” were Babcock and Wilder of Leaven- 
worth. If he were elected Wilder would control the patronage north 
of the Kansas River and Babcock south of it. Wilder had been 
promised the position of Surveyor General and Babcock the Super- 
intendency of Indian Affairs at St. Joseph, Missouri.?? As early as 
the 15th of February Lane and Parrott had effected a combination 
to support each other in the election.*? This agreement was broken 
by Lane when his own election appeared in danger. 
S. C. Pomeroy was a native of Northampton, Massachusetts, and 
the blood of Bunker Hill patriots was said to course in his veins. 
He had served two terms in the Massachusetts legislature and in 
1854 had come to Kansas as an agent of the New England Emigrant 
Aid Society. It was generally understood at the time that his con- 
nection with the relief work of 1860-1861 was prompted by a polit- 
ical motive.34 Just before the State legislature convened, Pomeroy 
had rented and furnished a “Senatorial residence” in Topeka. The 
opposition papers expressed fear that the money used on the resi- 
dence would greatly affect the “relief supplies.”®° The newspapers 
that supported him were the Kansas State Record, the Mound City 
Report, and the Freedom’s Champion. The Methodist Conference 
which convened at Atchison had endorsed “Pomeroy and Company 
in full.” 6 The Leavenworth papers, the White Cloud Chief, and 
the Western Kansas Express actively opposed him because of his 
railroad interests. He was an active promoter of the Atchison and 
St. Joseph railroad. Sol Miller warned the voters of Doniphan, 
Brown, Nemaha, and Marshall Counties not to jeopardize their rail-

road interests by voting for Pomeroy.’? The citizens of Manhattan 
had also instructed their representatives to elect United States 
Senators whose interests would be identified with the Kansas Valley, 
and who would codperate with each other in Congress “to obtain a 
grant of land to aid in the construction of the Leavenworth, Pawnee, 
and Western Railroad.” It was well understood that on the railroad 
question the people of Riley County opposed the election of Pom- 
eroy. To win the support of the county it was said that Pomeroy 
had executed a “written pledge” to work for a grant of land for the 
Leavenworth, Pawnee, and Western Railroad Company.*® Shortly 
before the election Pomeroy appealed to Hyatt for money to help 
him in the election. He wrote: 
I am preparing to go to Topeka upon the 20th. I shall “sink or swim,” I 
don’t know which—I shall be obliged to be at considerable expense—I may 
want to draw upon you for $500. Tell me, if I may! Telegraph me—I shall 
not invest in myself only so much as is necessary to win! If I do win, I am 
your humble servant forever afterwards.39 
Pomeroy had decided to win the election at any cost. 
James H. Lane, whose political ambitions have been referred to 
in other places, will from now on occupy a leading part in the Kan- 
sas drama. On the 3d of June, 1858, he had committed an act of 
violence which temporarily checked his political activities. Lane 
had been a potential candidate for the United States Senate ever 
since his arrival in Kansas. Since his election to the Senate in 
March of 1856 under the Topeka Constitution, he had watched 
every opportunity that presented itself to advance his candidacy for 
this position should Kansas be admitted. When in 1859 the Wyan- 
dotte constitution was ratified and the chances for admission were 
favorable, he again got busy. In a secret meeting at Miller’s Hall 
in Lawrence, October 17, 1859, he was nominated*® by a committee 
composed of Judge Miller, M. F. Conway (a candidate for United 
States Representative from Kansas), J. B. Abbott, A. J. Totten, and 
J. A. Finley.41 Conway and Lane had pledged to support each 
other in the nomination and election to Congress.42 Lane left no 
stone unturned to win the election. He had almost a year and a 
half to cultivate the field. In March of 1860 he devoted his time to

denouncing Governor Robinson and the “Bond Swindle.” The Gov- 
ernor had connected Lane with the Harper’s Ferry insurrection, 
and Lane reciprocated by connecting Robinson with spurious claims. 
It appears that bonds to the amount of $97,000 had been issued on 
the strength of the warrants granted for losses in the Kansas civil 
war incurred before November 1, 1859, that $45,000 worth of bonds 
were reported issued to Shaler W. Eldridge, $24,000 to Governor 
Robinson, $10,000 to Anna M. Jenkins, and $16,000 to other claim- 
ants.43 The claimants all belonged to the Robinson faction. Gen- 
eral Lane was in need of a campaign issue. When he realized that 
the bond deal was unpopular and would make good campaign am- 
munition he took to the stump, denounced it as a “Bond Swindle” 
and in vituperative language censured his arch enemy, Robinson. 
Lane is understood best in his own language. Here is a part of his 
Leavenworth speech: 
T-have said that name [Robinson] should never profane my lips or disgrace 
my pen. I regard him as the Benedict Arnold of his age, and pray God that 
he may feel the just indignation of an outraged people; but if the resurrection 
horn should reach the dark abyss of crime into which he has plunged himself, 
may he not rise with perjury on his lips or Kansas bonds in his pockets.44 
Sol Miller wrote that Lane was the last man who ought to go 
around “howling” about the bond swindle. ‘Perhaps one-fourth of 
the claims are for losses caused directly by himself and his pilfering 
band.” 4° Among the many charges brought against Lane was that 
of inconsistency. On this point the Topeka Tribune wrote: 
If he praised Capt. Montgomery at Osawattomie, he swore he would hang 
him at Fort Scott. If at Mound City he was a radical Republican, . . . at 
Barnsville, he cursed the “nigger” and declared that he would not have him in 
the State, whether bond or free. . . . To “cap the climax,” in one of his 
speeches, with his lips fresh from repeating an abominable lie, he raised him- 
self to an erect attitude, lowered his voice, wrinkled his brow, stuck his hand 
upon his heart, and said: “If there is one thing above another that the people 
of Kansas know, it is that Jim Lane is an honest man.” 46 
Probably Lane’s most successful bait for attracting votes was the 
farming out of the offices which he would have at his disposal if 
elected. It was charged that he had promised the same office to 
different men. In commenting on this point the Lawrence corre- 
spondent of the Tribune wrote: 
Lane’s promises of appointments for office are beginning to leak out, and it

frequently happens that half a dozen are promised the same office. Thus Sam 
Walker of Douglas, McDowell of Leavenworth, Williams of Osawatomie, and 
your John Ritchey, are all promised the Marshalship of Kansas; three or four 
gentlemen in this city with, I presume, as many more in your place, are

When Sol Miller heard that Lane had promised even the con-

has no more right to bargain off that nomination than his distin- 
guished ancestor had to promise the world to Christ.” 48 To at-

August, 1860, asking Governor Medary to call an extra session of 
the legislature to pass a stay law for the Territory.4® Lane, too, 
would win at any cost. His letter to Colonel Delahay, marked 
“strictly confidential,” tells the story: 
I have at last arranged for John Speer one of my truest and most faithful 
friends to get hold of the Republican. provided I can raise for him 500$ on 
twelve months—I will be over on Thursday certain in the stage to raise that 
amount for that time—For days and weeks I have been laboring night and day 
to accomplish this result, and now there must be no failure in this—I scarcely 
know where to go but raise it, I must and will—Think and keep thinking until 
I come over—Delahay with the Republicanl,] Tumes and Record we can ac- 
complish almost anything. 
I have written to Buell that he must get together 5,000$ and the moment 
he hears of our admission he must start with it to Kansas. I have strong 
hopes he will do so. I do not believe the whole batch of our opponents can 
raise as much. Delahay they shall not beat me by corruption. The people 
are for me the members are for me & by God they shall not defraud me out 
of my election. I rec’d a letter from Conway this morning, he says we are 
coming in & no mistake. I have written him that he must be on hand when 
the legislature meets. 
I have written to Abbott one of the members from this county now in 
Pikes Peak to hold himself in readiness to come home at a moments notice. 
I am ready for the fight completely perfectly prepared if we can close the 
Republican arrangement that must be done if we barter everything in doing tt. 
Delahay I will have the Stage drop me at your house, perhaps Speer will be 
with me. Say not one word but think how the 500 is to be raised. I fully 
appreciate that it is a mammoth task likened unto the moving of a mountain 
but it must be done & that too at Leavenworth. God helps those who help 
themselves. The purchase of the Republican makes everything smooth and 
certain. The men from several Districts are all favorable.5° 
Stephenson wrote that he got the money, but does not mention 
the five thousand dollars from Buell.
