The papers that-supported Lane were the Lawrence Republican, 
the Kansas State Record, and the Leavenworth Daily Conservative. 
John Speer and Nicholas Smith purchased the Lawrence Republican 
from T. D. and S. O. Thacher.5! In Speer, Lane had an ardent sup- 
porter. The Record was edited by E. G. and W. W. Ross of To- 
peka. This support was not based on mere love and loyalty for 
Lane. W. W. Ross received the Pottawatomie Indian Agency. To- 
peka supported Lane and in return got the capital and the land 
office. Lane also became the owner of eighty-one city lots in To- 
peka. On February 22, 1862, Holliday notified Lane of the taxes 
due on these lots.®? How the matter was arranged remains a 
mystery. The suspicion of a bargain was voiced at the time. The 
Leavenworth Daily Conservative was controlled by D. R. Anthony 
and D. W. Wilder, who also supported the Lane-Parrott combina- 
tion. The votes in the State legislature from Leavenworth County, 
however, were controlled by the Democrats. 
The motion to elect one United States Senator from Kansas south 
of the Kansas River fell upon a confused Senate, April Ist. It was 
introduced by E. P. Bancroft of Emporia, a Parrott man. The 
Parrott, Pomeroy, Stanton, and Ewing men,** wrote P. P. Elder years 
later, had combined with the Democrats the night before to pass the 
resolution. The move was unknown to the Lane men. The next 
morning the lobby filled up early with the enemies of Lane to watch 
him fall. Governor Robinson was among them. A heated parlia- 
mentary battle ensued which lasted till high noon. The resolution 
passed when Bancroft asked for the previous question. Parrott, 
Stanton, Lane, and A. J. Isacks were nominated for the Senate. It 
was understood that the leading rival candidates south of the Kan- 
sas River were Lane and Stanton. Parrott and Pomeroy were the 
leading rivals north of the river. The nomination of both Parrott 
and Lane for Senator south of the river, however, had confused the 
Lane-Parrott men and split their votes. The result was the election 
of Stanton by thirteen votes to Lane’s seven and Parrott’s five. 
Senator Elder’s explanation was that he had nominated Parrott, and 
being a good Lane man first, had expected the Parrott men to fol- 
low and by their combined vote they would have defeated Stanton; 
but when Senator Osborn had nominated Lane it had split the vote.

The Journal has it, however, that Osborn and not Elder nominated 
Parrott. It fails to mention who had nominated Lane. In any 
case the records show a drifting away from the Lane-Parrott com- 
bination. Some of the Parrott but anti-Lane men had voted for 
Stanton. 
This election put the Lane and Pomeroy men in a quandary. Un- 
less the action of the Senate could be overruled by a vote to elect 
the Senators in joint session, Lane and Pomeroy would be left out. 
To pass this resolution it was necessary to effect a new combination 
between Lane and Pomeroy in order to defeat Parrott. Lane could 
furnish Pomeroy seven positive Lane men who would vote for 
Pomeroy in joint session and Pomeroy could furnish Lane one man 
in the Senate who would vote with the Lane and Parrott men in 
favor of a joint session. Elder wrote that the idea had originated 
with H. P. Johnston and was worked out by Johnston and Elder. 
Isaac T. Goodnow wrote that he called on both candidates, Pomeroy 
and Lane, and arranged the combination, adding that the two candi- 
dates that night slept together in the “ ‘Bean House,’ but not till 
after one o’clock.” ** It is possible that both parties worked on the 
combination. The agreement was made at the Fort Sumptuous 
House, also known as the “Bean House,” and the stipulation was 
drawn up in writing. In the Governor’s correspondence is a copy of 
the pledge drawn up by the thirteen senators who voted for the 
resolution to go into a joint convention to elect the United States 
Senators. Included is the name of John A. Martin whom Pomeroy 
had selected as his representative to vote for a joint convention to 
defeat Stanton and Parrott.°? 
With the alliance perfected the House voted, April 2, to go into 
joint session with the Senate to elect the Senators. The date set 
for the election was April 4. The joint resolution reached the 
Senate on the 3d. The Stanton and some Parrott men fought des- 
perately to kill the resolution, but all motions to delay action were 
voted down 13 to 12. Finally Lynde, of Grasshopper Falls, who led 
the Lane forces in the Senate, moved to concur in the House resolu- 
tion and called for the previous question. The resolution was adopted 
13 to 12. The thirteen Senators who had signed the pledge on the

1st of April carriedthe resolution.®® Parrott appears to have been 
ignorant of the intrigue and favored the joint session, but four of 
his supporters, Bancroft, Dutton, Hoffman, and Lappin, believed it 
unwise and voted against it.>® 
On the 4th of April the legislature met in joint session to elect 
the Senators. The procedure agreed upon for the election was that 
the secretary should call the roll of the Senate and as the names 
were called the members should arise and announce their choice, 
viwa voce, for two United States Senators. The clerk of the House 
would repeat the same process for the representatives. After they 
had voted the secretary of the Senate and clerk of the House should 
“foot up the rolls.” The speaker of the House and president of the 
Senate should then announce the results of the election.® 
At 2:30 p. m. the secretary of the Senate started to call the roll 
and for two long hours the process continued. One eye witness 
wrote, “the wildest confusion and excitement prevailed, and chang- 
ing votes was the order of the day.”®! Another remarked: “Of the 
98 members, 58 changed their votes from one to six times; the 
record shows 297 votes, of which 76 occurred in the Senate, and 221 © 
in the House,~being an average of a little more than three to each 
member; but as 40 gentlemen remained steadfast to the end, these 
eccentricities must be distributed accordingly.”®? ‘No mortal could 
keep the run of the voting,” remarked Sol Miller, “and when it was 
completed, it was asserted, the result was obtained by a sharp prac- 
tice of Clerks and eaves droppers.’®? The alliance between Lane 
and Pomeroy, wrote the Leavenworth Herald, was for a time broken 
and Parrott was elected by seven votes; but one of the clerks re- 
fused to return the results to the Speaker and President of the con- 
vention, ‘pretending that he could not get the thing straight, whilst 
all the time mysterious little strips of paper were telegraphed to and 
fro about the room.” ®* The vote for General Lane is reported to 
have fluctuated between 49 and 64 votes, that of Parrott between 
49 and 60. When the results were announced, Lane had 55 votes, 
Pomeroy 52, Parrott 49, Stanton 21, Isacks 11, Kingman 8, Delahay

2, Conway 1, and Houston 1.6 In 1878, J. J. Ingalls, secretary of 
the joint convention, wrote that he had sent the original tally list 
kept by him to F. G. Adams, Secretary of the State Historical Society. 
He wrote: “The paper marked (1) shows the vote with the change 
made by each member of the Legislature during the progress of the 
ballot. The paper marked (2) shows the state of the vote when 
the balloting closed, and is the sheet from which the result was

reproduced. The Senatorial candidates listed, but whose names are 
not always legible, were: Lane, Pomeroy, Parrott, Stanton, Delahay, 
Kingman, Shannon, Winchell, Isaacs and Ewing. The tally-list 
otherwise is self explanatory. Only 42 of the 98 legislators present 
did not change their votes during the progress of the ballot. 
The results of the election must be attributed to a number of in- 
fluences. In the first place, both of the successful candidates were 
known to the settlers because of public service they had rendered. 
General Lane had led the radical wing of the Free-State Party dur- 
ing the trying days of 1856-1857. Pomeroy had been a prominent 
leader of the New England Emigrant Aid Company, but was espe- 
cially well known as a distributor of relief supplies during the 
famine of 1860-1861. In the second place the corrupt actions of the 
candidates and legislators had affected the results. The Leaven- 
worth Herald wrote that some of the deserters of Parrott had com- 
plained bitterly when they were paid in [llinois money, which sold 
at a discount.“ The Rev. Werter R. Davis was reported to have 
promised Parrott his support in the morning and voted for Pomeroy 
in the afternoon.®* Some of Lane’s men who had promised to sup- 
port Parrott, later sold out to Pomeroy. Sol Miller blamed the re- 
lief contributions for Pomeroy’s success: 
But greater than all was the Aid question, which has placed Pomeroy in 
his present position. The charitable people of the East, in their sympathy for 
starving Kansas, have been innocently furnishing the means wherewith to 
demoralize our people, corrupt our legislators, and bribe the election of a man 
to the Senate, who was not the choice of the people—for we have not the 
slightest doubt that charity money was lavished freely to secure this result. 
We shall now hear no more of starving Kansas, for Pomeroy has gained all 
he sought for.®9

The Leavenworth Conservative wrote: ‘Are Senators elected by 
clerks and eaves-droppers? . . . Every man who tried to keep 
the record will agree with us that it is mere guesswork to tell who 
was elected.” In the same issue it stated: “They were two long 
hours taking the vote and for one long hour Parrott had fifty-six 
votes and was elected.” 7 J. J. Ingalls later confessed that “it was 
through his ‘manoeuvering’ as secretary of the Senate during the 
voting that Parrott was defeated.” 
The influence of political patronage had been a powerful factor. 
A month after the election the Kansas State Journal observed: 
Who are these numerous appointees, upon whom the Senators have hastened 
to pour down the copious showers of their favor? Of nine appointments 
recently announced to us, eight have been conferred upon members of the 
Legislature which elected those Senators. We refer to the appointments of 
Messrs. Burnett, Elder, Martin and Farnsworth of the Senate, and Messrs. 
Colton, Lowe, Keith and Ira Smith of the House. . . . Were those men 
elected to the Legislature to buy their way into Federal office by the corrupt 
disposition of their votes for Senators? 72 
Governor Robinson wrote that “at this election every appliance 
was used ever brought into requisition in the older States.” 
The railroad question was probably the greatest single factor in 
the election. ~The same interests that had controlled the railroad 
convention at Topeka in October, 1860, which had decided upon a 
railroad system for the Territory, also influenced the election of 
United States Senators. The opposing faction keenly felt the 
pressure that was used against them. Sol Miller had warned the 
northern counties that to vote for Pomeroy would jeopardize their 
cause: 
The interests of Atchison, where all or most of Pomeroy’s property is located, 
and in which all his hopes are centered, demand that this road [a northern 
railroad] should be crushed out. This object was had in view, when the 
Topeka Railroad Convention was packed; and we now behold the champions 
of the Atchison and Topeka movement espousing Pomeroy’s cause.%3 
The citizens of the city and county of Leavenworth greatly de- 
plored the defeat of Parrott and the election of Pomeroy, but found 
consolation in the election of Lane. Resolutions were passed ex- 
pressing “an abiding confidence in his integrity and ability,” and 
committed to him specially the charge of their “interests at Wash- 
ington,” feeling assured that their confidence in him would be amply 
70. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, April 7, 1861. 
71. White Cloud Chief, October 23, 1862. 
72. Kansas State Journal, May 2, 1861. 
73. White Cloud Chief, March 11, 1861. 


## Page 119

The Birth of Kansas 111 
rewarded.’* The Emporia News lamented the election of Pomeroy 
because, identified as he was with the peculiar interests of Atchison, 
his influence would be “antagonistic with those of the Neosho 
Valley.” 
After the election, and before the Senators left for Washington, 
Jim Lane stopped at Atchison, where there was “a big hurrah over 
him.” In response he told what tremendous things he should bring 
to pass. “There should be immense crops this season; the Pacific 
Railroad should run up the Kansas Valley, as nature designed; peo- 
ple in other parts of the State might build branches connecting with 
that Road, if they so desired, but the main trunk must run up the 
Valley aforesaid.’’6 
The interests of the railroads were so closely tied up with those of 
the State that it is necessary to show what was done to promote 
them during the Robinson administration, both in the State legis- 
lature and in Congress. 
Since most of the Kansas railroad companies had already been 
incorporated during the Territorial period and the Topeka railroad 
convention had memorialized Congress on land grants for certain 
railroads, it was evident that further railroad progress in Kansas 
would have to await the action of Congress on the Pacific railroad 
bill. This bill was not enacted until the first of July, 1862. The act 
provided that the Pacific railroad should have two branches east of 
the hundredth meridian of longitude west; one with its eastern 
terminal at some point on the Missouri River in Kansas, and the 
other with its eastern terminal on the western boundary of Iowa. 
These branches were to converge and form one road on the hun- 
dredth meridian aforesaid, at a point between the southern margin 
of the valley of the Republican River and the northern margin of 
the valley of the Platte River, in Nebraska Territory. The Leaven- 
worth, Pawnee, and Western Railroad Company was authorized to 
build a road starting “from the Missouri river, at the mouth of the 
Kansas river, on the south side thereof, so as to connect with the 
Pacific railroad of Missouri, to the aforesaid point, on the one hun- 
dredth meridian of longitude west.” The railroad was to run via 
Fort Riley. Section 13 of the act provided that the Hannibal and 
St. Joseph Railroad Company of Missouri could extend its road from 
St. Joseph via Atchison, to connect and unite with the road through 
Kansas, upon filing its assent to the provision of the act, and if 
74, Leavenworth Daily Conservative, April 10, 1861. 
75. Emporia News, April 13, 1861. 
76. White Cloud Chief, April 18, 1861. 


## Page 120

112 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
actual survey should render it desirable, this company could con- 
struct its road northwest from St. Joseph, Missouri, so as to connect 
with the Iowa instead of the Kansas branch, provided the Kansas 
legislature would consent to it. The Leavenworth, Pawnee, and 
Western Railroad Company could construct its road from Leaven- 
worth to unite with the road through Kansas. The aid granted the 
Kansas companies was in the form of land grants, amounting to 
6,400 acres per mile of railroad constructed. The aid granted the 
Hannibal and St. Joseph company for constructing its road, how- 
ever, was limited to 100 miles of road.”7 On the 2d of July, 1864, 
Congress amended the act so as to have the L., P., & W. R. R. Co. 
build “from the mouth of the Kansas river” instead of the south 
side, and build the road via ‘Lawrence and Topeka, or on the bank 
of the Kansas river opposite said towns.’’’8 
Senators Lane and Pomeroy had fought hard for the Kansas pro- 
visions. Pomeroy attributed much of their success to the assistance 
given them by the California delegates.“”? On his own motion, Lane 
had been included on the special railroad committee in 1862. The 
committee had added several amendments to the House bill, but 
not all were retained. The insertion of Fort Riley, giving direction 
to the L., P.,.& W. R. R., was at the suggestion of Lane, who had 
asked also for a government loan of $360,000 for the same road, but 
the latter was struck out. The Kansas senators had objected to the 
insertion of the so-called Henderson amendment, which authorized 
the Kansas legislature to decide whether the Hannibal and St. 
Joseph company should extend its road to connect with the Iowa or 
the Kansas branch of the Pacific railroad.*° : 
On the 3d of March, 1868, Congress made further concessions to 
Kansas for railroad purposes. Grants of land ten sections in width 
on each side of the track were given to the Atchison and Topeka 
Railroad Company to build a road and telegraph from Leavenworth 
via Lawrence and the Ohio City Crossing of the Osage River to the 
southern line of Kansas in the direction of Galveston, Texas. A 
branch of this road should connect Lawrence with the Atchison, 
Topeka and Santa Fe road at its intersection with the Neosho River. 
A second railroad should lead from Atchison via Topeka to the 
western boundary of the State in the direction of Fort Union and 
Santa Fe, with a branch road connecting it with the first road at the

78. Ibid., XIII, 361.

point of intersection with the Neosho River.8!. C. K. Holliday of 
Topeka and Senator Pomeroy of Atchison were among the main 
promoters of the railroad lines built by the Atchison, Topeka and 
Santa Fe Railroad Company. 
By treaties with the Indian tribes, the Federal Government had 
made further concessions to Kansas railroad companies. On the 
30th of May, 1860, the Senate ratified a treaty with the Delawares 
by which the lands not assigned to them in their “Home Reserves” 
were to be sold to the L., P., & W. R. R. Co. at $1.25 an acre.8? 
According to Andreas it had yielded the company 233,966 acres. 
A similar treaty had been made with the Pottawatomie Indians on 
the 15th of November, 1861, giving the same company the right to 
purchase at $1.25 an acre the Pottawatomie lands not included in 
the “Home Reserve.” *? Under this treaty the company acquired 
425,000 acres. 
Influential men in the company had no doubt profited by these 
negotiations. Although a thorough study of the subject has not 
been made, certain revelations have been noted. Among the Ewing 
Letters is a statement which lists the number of acres given to in- 
fluential men who no doubt aided in negotiating the Delaware and 
Pottawatomie treaties. The list of beneficiaries in case of the 
Delaware treaty, with number of acres received, were:

names of these friends of Faut and Usher. The beneficiaries of the 
Pottawatomie treaty were primarily Messrs. Stone, Isacks, and 
Ewing. Together they had received 26,160 acres. The mission 
contiguous to the Pottawatomie lands got 960 acres, other persons 
not named 1,280, and to “Johnson, option of commuting $200,000 
stock for average land 4,000 acres.” ** The arrangement pertaining 
to the Pottawatomie treaty lands was made, it appears, before the 
treaty was ratified. On the 10th of January, 1863, Abelard Guthrie, 
who was greatly interested in the development of Quindaro, re- 
corded in his diary an experience with Senator Pomeroy: 
Called to see one Senator Pomeroy who says he did not get either my 
letters written to him in the summer in relation to payment of the interest on 
the railroad bonds held by the Delaware Indians in security for the payment 
of the money due from the Pawnee & Leavenworth railroad company for a 
grant of lands. This surprised me very much but as I have heard that the 
Senator received seventeen sections of this land, for his neutrality in the 
transaction he having heretofore strenuously opposed the grant as a fraud upon 
the Indians as indeed it was. If this report is true it may account for the 
failure of the mails in transmitting my letters as it was with the view of 
defeating this grant that my letters were written.85 
Another point of interest connected with the treaties, involves the 
Robinson-Lane quarrel. R. 8. Stevens and Governor Robinson had 
been made directors in the Leavenworth, Pawnee, and Western Rail- 
road Company. When the time arrived for the company to pay for 
the Delaware lands and it had no money wherewith to pay, it de- 
cided upon bonds as a way out. This necessitated a supplementary 
treaty with the Indians and its ratification by the Senate. Chief 
Justice Ewing was selected to interview the Senators. General Lane 
insisted that before he used his influence in behalf of the treaty, 
Robinson and Stevens would have to give up their seats in the 
Directory and two of his henchmen be put in their places. This 
forced the resignation of Robinson and Stevens, who were suc- 
ceeded by Josiah Miller and Chester Thomas, both of whom were 
also members of the Pacific Railroad Company.®® The treaty was 
ratified August 6, 1861, and the company permitted to continue its 
project. This change in directors of the company also helps to ex- 
plain Senator Lane’s support of the interests of the Leavenworth, 
Pawnee, and Western Railroad Company in the United States Sen- 
ate, in 1862, during the debate on the Pacific railroad bill.

The Birth of Kansas 115 
Although most of the railroad questions were being decided by 
Congress, the question of the Henderson amendment had been re- 
ferred to the State legislature. This amendment, a part of the Pa- 
cific railroad bill, authorized the State legislature to determine 
whether the Hannibal and St. Joseph Railroad Company should con- 
nect with the Iowa branch of the Pacific road or with the Kansas 
Valley branch. It was inserted in the bill by Senator Henderson of 
Missouri just before the Senate adopted the bill. The State legisla- 
ture divided on the question. On the 20th of August, 1862, represent- 
atives of the northern tier of counties at a convention pledged them- 
selves “to secure the election of unqualified Union men to the legis- 
lature, who are identified with us in our Railroad interests, and who 
when elected,” would be influential in securing the passage of a rail- 
road directly west from St. Joseph, Missouri, to the main branch of 
the Pacific road. The question came to a vote in the Kansas legis- 
lature in February, 1863, and the project for the northern road was 
defeated, 50 to 23 in the House and 16 to 6 in the Senate.87 Sol ° 
Miller found occasion to criticise his opponents, accusing them of 
logrolling: 
It is remarkable that up to the time when the Bridge Bill was introduced 
here, the other day, the Atchison men opposed every suggestion concerning it. 
They said their constituents were unitedly opposed to the Bridge scheme. 
Leavenworth has been to the northern counties begging them to unite with 
them in support of the Bridge bill. Failing to secure the northern vote, they, 
have made the arrangement with Atchison. . . . These are facts.88 
In the House, Leavenworth had voted with Atchison on the ques- 
tion, but in the Senate, with Doniphan. 
There was great interest and enthusiasm over the railroad ques- 
tions. Lobbyists were ever present when the question was brought 
before the legislature. In February, 1862, when the Kansas legis- 
lature discussed a memorial to Congress asking for railroad grants 
with the purpose of securing the ratification of the Pottawatomie 
Indian treaty, Sol Miller remarked: “If railroads were built by 
gas, this morning’s work would have completed the road to the Pa- 
cific.” He had observed during the discussion ‘a number of dis- 
tinguished men from Leavenworth, including Judges, Senators, and 
Chief Justices, passing about among the members, engaging them 
in earnest conversation. Of course their talk was upon general 
or religious topics, and they took no interest in the memorial!’®®

## Page 124

116 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
In closing the discussion on railroads the writer must express agree- 
ment with the statement made by George W. Glick of Atchison, 
that with the exception of the Osage Valley and Fort Riley railroad 
mentioned in the memorial to Congress in 1860, “the results of that 
first great railroad convention in Kansas have proven the prophetic 
wisdom and foresight of the men who signed the call as well as of 
those who participated in its proceedings.”®° And now to return to 
the establishment of State institutions. 
After the election of United States Senators, the legislature settled 
down for business. Among the questions calling for immediate at- 
tention were those pertaining to the location of the State institutions. 
On the 12th of April Charles Starns of Leavenworth introduced the 
penitentiary bill, which located that institution at Leavenworth, 
the site to be determined by commissioners, two of whom were to be 
Leavenworth men. The bill passed the legislature without much 
opposition, but difficulty arose in connection with locating the site. 
The first contract was cancelled. The second, made with Abel Whit- 
ney of Delaware township, specified a tract of forty acres situated 
on Seven Mile Creek, five miles south of Leavenworth.®! The land 
was surveyed, a warranty deed was executed to the State, and on 
the 23d of January, 1862, the Governor transmitted the report of 
the commissioners to the legislature.°? Governor Carney reported in 
his message to the legislature, January 13, 1864, that the site on 
Seven Mile Creek was impractical and that it lacked water supply. 
The legislature of 1864, therefore, passed another bill permitting the 
newly appointed commissioners, C. A. Buck of Jefferson, John S. 
Penton of Atchison, and D. G. Campbell of Johnson County to re- 
locate “the State penitentiary within sixty days after the approval 
of this act, at some practicable point within the limits of the city 
of Leavenworth, or its vicinity.” The commissioners, however, de- 
cided not to change the location.®* In 1864 the city of Lansing was 
established near the site selected for the State penitentiary. A 
board of three directors visited the State prisons in New York, Ohio, 
Michigan, and Illinois and decided upon the Joliet plan as a model 
for the penal institution of Kansas. In the summer of 1864, Messrs. 
Carely and Adams began work on the building.

The Birth of Kansas LAG: 
The permanent location of the State capital was still undecided. 
The constitution had provided that the first legislature should sub- 
mit the question to a popular vote, and a majority “of all votes 
cast at some general election should be necessary for such location.” 
The question was first considered in the House, where several bills 
on the subject were introduced and rejected before a solution was 
found. The bill that survived came from Andrew Stark of Linn 
County.°* It provided for an election to be held on the 5th of 
November, 1861, and annually thereafter until some place had re- 
ceived a majority of all votes cast.2°> Even the Stark bill, sponsored 
by the friends of Topeka,®* was defeated at first by a vote of 30 to 
26, but was reconsidered, and finally passed the House, May 20, 
by a vote of 47 to 4. 
In the Senate, while in Committee of the Whole, the Stark bill 
on the location of the capital was considered in conjunction with the 
penitentiary and State University bills. The capital bill was 
amended so as to limit the second vote to the two places receiving 
the highest number of votes at the first election, provided no place 
had received a majority of votes in the first election..* It now 
agreed with the rejected bill introduced in the House by Oliver Bar- 
ber of Douglas County, and foretold trouble.°® All other amend- 
ments were rejected. When the bill was brought to its third read- 
ing it was again considered in conjunction with the University and 
penitentiary bill and held up, on motion of P. P. Elder, until the 
Senate had passed the bill locating the University at Manhattan. 
The bill as amended then passed the Senate by a vote of 18 to 7.%° 
The House refused to concur in the Senate amendment. The first 
conference committee failed to find a solution. Senator Wood’s mo- 
tion for a second committee was entertained. When it became 
known, however, that if a second committee failed to reach a settle- 
ment the bill would automatically be killed by virtue of the rules of 
the Senate, the motion was dropped. Rather than give the capital 
to Topeka by default the Senate receded from its position and with- 
drew the amendment, 15 to 5.1° The act as passed provided for an 
election to be held on the 5th of November, 1861, and annually

thereafter until some place had received a majority of all votes cast. 
Topeka had won over Lawrence. 
Five months intervened between the signing of the bill and the 
November election. In the meantime the radical Republicans led 
by James H. Lane had tried to oust Governor Robinson and the 
State officers on the ground that their term expired two years after 
the election to office rather than two years after the admission of 
the State. Although the matter does not seem apropos, nevertheless 
it may have influenced the vote on the capital. Both Lawrence and 
Topeka waged a spirited campaign. Money was spent freely and 
bargains were sought. Newspapers were used to advantage. To- 
peka claimed the support of the Smoky Hill Union, the Elwood 
Free Press, the Wyandotte Gazette, the Manhattan Express, and 
the Atchison Union. Lawrence was supported by the Council Grove 
Press, the Olathe Mirror, the Leavenworth Times, the White Cloud 
Chief, and others. The Leavenworth Conservative claimed it 
boosted Leavenworth, but in fact it opposed Lawrence.1°4 
Open favoritism was shown by the papers. The Manhattan 
Express wrote that by helping Topeka against Lawrence they were 
promoting their own interests in the University. In the November 
election Riley County cast 144 votes for Topeka and none for Law- 
rence.1°2 The Lawrence Journal wrote of an agreement between To- 
peka and the Leavenworth bankers in which Topeka had agreed to 
help the Leavenworth bankers crush the Lawrence bank and in re- 
turn the Leavenworth bankers had agreed “to tranfer the franchises 
of the people of Leavenworth to the Topeka side of the capital 
question.” 1°? The Topeka Record, in quoting the accusation of the 
Lawrence Journal, did not deny the agreement; but mentioned that 
General Stone, President McDowell and Judge Johnson of Leaven- 
worth, and Principal Eiler of the Pleasant College at Western had 
stopped to call in Topeka. Stone and Johnson were connected with 
the Leavenworth, Pawnee, and Western Railroad.1% 
The question was voted on the 5th of November. The newspapers 
had predicted a victory for Topeka. The results vindicated their 
predictions. Out of a total of 14,288 votes cast Topeka received 
7,859, Lawrence 5,334, Leavenworth 95, Emporia 158, Baldwin City 
400, and 442 votes were distributed among other towns.1® Leaven-

worth County cast 1,042 votes for Lawrence and 905 for Topeka. 
Shawnee, Riley, Morris, Jefferson, Jackson, Franklin, Davis, Atchi- 
son, Doniphan, Nemaha, Pottawatomie, and Marshall Counties cast 
large majorities for Topeka; whereas Douglas, Anderson, Bourbon, 
Breckenridge, Johnson, and Allen Counties voted for Lawrence.}® 
Whether the results of the election could be regarded a criterion 
of the popular will is questionable. Various methods had been used 
to influence voters. A municipal election in Topeka in 1862 be- 
trayed the city’s activities in connection with the capital question.!°7 
An investigation of the “Council Proceedings” of Topeka, beginning 
with the 5th of August, 1861, and ending in February of 1862, re- 
veals a surprising amount of activity. The Council had divided on 
the question of how much money it should spend on the election to 
locate the capital, and to whom the money should be entrusted. 
The dispute caused the resignation of Mayor Cox, who had vetoed 
a proposition to raise money, but whose veto the Council had over- 
ruled. On the 5th of August, Messrs. C. K. Holliday, Hamel H. 
Home, James Fletcher, Edmund G. Ross, and J. C. Bartlett ap- 
peared before the Council, as a committee from the citizens of To- 
peka, and asked for $20,000 to be used to help locate the capital at 
Topeka. On the 7th of August, 1861, the Council moved to appro- 
priate $10,000 for this purpose, on the 2d of December it voted $9- 
500 more, and on the following day it authorized E. G. Ross and 
James Fletcher to pay all debts contracted “for printing and all other 
purposes.” This was in response to Ross’ request of the preceding 
day. Mr. Giles of the Auditing Committee had also appeared be- 
fore the Council on the 2d of December and reported that the “Ex- 
ecutive Committee” of the city of Topeka, the members of which 
he failed to mention, had pledged $20,250 to secure the location of 
the capital. The significant fact is that the money was paid in 
small denominations, varying in size from one to one hundred dol- 
lars; a large part of it was paid in the smaller denominations.1°° 
Topeka was not alone in being accused of bribery. The Kansas 
State Record accused Lawrence of having imported “hundreds of 
voters” from the Delaware lands at the rate of twenty-five cents 
per head.°® The Conservative reported that the Leavenworth 
Times had first sold out to Topeka, but had changed over to Law-

In January of 1862, C. K. Holliday, President and Special Trustee 
of the Topeka Association, donated twenty acres of land on which to 
locate the capital buildings."* The title had been transferred from 
its original holder, Isaiah Walker, to the Topeka Association in 
July of 1859. The State legislature accepted the gift in February 
of 1862. Four years later the State legislature provided for the con- 
struction of the east wing of the statehouse. Additions were made 
from time to time, including the dome. The building was finally 
completed in 1903 at a cost to the State of $3,200,588.92.114 
The question of locating the State University passed through two 
sessions of the State legislature and into the third, where it was 
finally decided. The first legislature had agreed to locate the Uni- 
versity at Manhattan. The House had voted ’in favor of this bill, 
43 to 19, and the Senate, 17 to 8, but Governor Robinson had re- 
turned it without his approval. He advanced two reasons; in the 
first place the bill was unfair because it was based upon a proposed 
donation of land which other sections of the State had not had the 
opportunity to make; in the second place it was untimely because 
the State lacked the available funds for a State University. An at- 
tempt made to pass the bill over the Governor’s veto failed in the 
House, 38 to 20.% Professor J. D. Walters of the Kansas State 
Agricultural College wrote in 1902 that in 1861 Governor Robinson 
had been willing to trade the University for the capital, but the 
Manhattan delegates had already promised to support Topeka for 
the capital and refused to make the change. All attempts to 
locate the University at Lawrence or any other city instead of Man- 
hattan were defeated. 
In the second legislature Manhattan, Emporia, and Lawrence

were still contending for the University. The House considered the 
Manhattan bill first and adopted it, 45 to 16. In the Senate the bill 
became tangled with the Lane-Robinson quarrel. Lane, busily pack- 
ing the Senate for the impeachment proceedings, had been unable to 
get enough votes to oust the four State senators, accused of holding 
military offices, so he held up the vote on the University bill for 
trading purposes. The Manhattan men finally promised to assist 
in ousting the military men, on condition that the incoming senators 
who would replace the ousted senators would vote for Manhattan on 
the University bill. Lane agreed to this, but the Lane men failed 
in their promises and the University bill was lost in the Senate by 
one vote, 11 to 12.417 James Humphrey wrote that one or two of the 
new senators had been Methodist preachers and he was inclined to 
think that Lane rather than the preachers had falsified.1'8 
Having failed to locate the State University in the second session, 
the question carried over to the third legislature. The three rivals 
had now been reduced to two, by an agreement to locate the State 
Agricultural College at Manhattan. This had been made possible 
by the Morrill Act of July 2, 1862. C. Y. Eskridge, of Emporia, 
introduced a bill to locate the University at Emporia. Before the 
bill was read a third time, however, Lawrence was substituted for 
Emporia. The vote was taken in committee of the whole and was 
tied, 33 to 33. E. Russell, of Doniphan, who was in the chair, then 
decided for Lawrence. Again the question had been complicated 
by other interests and had invited logrolling. This time it was the 
railroad question, more particularly the Henderson amendment, 
which authorized the Kansas legislature to decide whether the 
Hannibal and St. Joseph company should extend its railroad to 
connect with the Iowa or the Kansas branch of the Union Pacific 
railroad. The northern tier of counties was interested in securing a 
northern railroad, and Lawrence wanted the University. The en- 
tire Douglas tier of counties north of the Kansas River, including 
Jefferson, had been pledged, and “every member from Johnson, 
Miami and Linn counties (except Mr. Christie, who lies dangerously 
ill, and Mr. Campbell) was induced to support Lawrence.” Leaven- 
worth had divided evenly, four and four.!2° In the Senate the bill 
passed 18 to 5 in favor of Lawrence. 
The location of the University at Lawrence was conditioned upon

the city’s promise to give $15,000 to the University endowment 
fund and to donate forty acres of land for a campus. In partial 
fulfillment of this promise the city council of Lawrence accepted 
Governor Robinson’s proposition to give forty acres to the school 
if the city would give him a half block of land on the east side of 
Mt. Oread. The endowment money was made up by the Amos A. 
Lawrence gift of $10,000, originally pledged to the Free State Col- 
lege, plus a $5,000 note given by leading citizens of Lawrence.’ 
Governor Carney was able to cash the note just in time to fulfill 
the condition of the contract and thus prevent the University from 
being located at Emporia. On the 2d of November, 1863, a procla- 
mation by the Governor declared the State University permanently 
located at Lawrence. 
Kansas was among the first of the States to accept the proffered 
endowment of the Federal Government for a State agricultural col- 
lege. On the 3d of February, 1863, the State legislature agreed to 
comply with the provisions of the Morrill Act and on the 16th of the 
same month the legislature accepted an offer of the Trustees of 
Bluemont college to donate to the State its college building, the 
library of several hundred volumes, some apparatus, and one hun- 
dred acres of land. Emporia was still without a State institution. 
It has been reported that Judge L. D. Bailey, a native of Massachu- 
setts, had suggested the idea of a normal school to Eskridge. When 
he approved the idea, Bailey drew up a bill to locate such a school 
at Emporia, Eskridge introduced it, and the legislature passed it 
without opposition.!2? Other State institutions were established 
from time to time. Seneca asked for the deaf and dumb asylum and 
Wyandotte for the insane asylum. The Legislature of 1863, how- 
ever, located the first State insane asylum at Osawatomie, and the 
following legislature located the State deaf and dumb asylum at 
Olathe. The blind asylum was located at Wyandotte.128 
The judicial system of the State was provided for in a series of 
six or more acts. On the 25th of April, 1861, Governor Robinson 
approved the act that established the district courts. They were 
made courts of record and given jurisdiction in all cases above the 
powers granted justices of the peace. Section three authorized the 
judges to exercise all judicial functions allowed or required under 
the statutes of the Territory, and section five provided that all 
121. F. H. Snow, ‘Beginning of the University of Kansas,” Transactions, VI, 70-75. 
ee Lyman B. Kellogg, “The Founding of the State Normal School,” Collections, 
123. White Cloud Chief, Feb. 19, 1863; N. L. Prentis, History of Kansas, pp. 140, 141. 


## Page 131

The Birth of Kansas 123 
Territorial acts applicable and not inconsistent with the State laws 
and the constitution should remain in force.1** The act of May 22, 
1861, divided the State into five judicial districts and provided a 
district court for each. The court sessions were to be held at the 
county seats.1*5 
On the 21st of May, 1861, the act which organized and defined the 
Jurisdiction of the State Supreme Court was approved. It was to 
be a court of record. In addition to the original jurisdiction con- 
ferred upon it by the constitution it was vested with authority to 
issue writs of error and certvorarz in criminal cases and supersedeas 
in any case. The rules of the Supreme Court under the law of the 
Territory were to govern under the State. It was to hold court 
annually at the seat of government. No provision was made to 
nullify acts of the State in conflict with the constitution.1*° 
The act providing for the organization of the justices’ courts was 
approved May 22, 1861. The justice of the peace under the State 
was to have the same power as under the Territory. The act was 
not to be so construed, however, as to interfere with the jurisdiction 
of the municipal officers, or officers exercising police jurisdiction in 
incorporated cities.1?7 
The Governor signed the act providing for the probate court on 
the 3d of June, 1861. The court was vested with original juris- 
diction in all matters relating to the probate of last wills and testa- 
ments, to descents and distributions, cases affecting minors, persons 
of unsound mind, and habitual drunkards. It was given concurrent 
jurisdiction with the Supreme and district courts in cases of habeas 
corpus. It was a court of record and all records were to be open for 
inspection without charge.!° 
The State legislature had retained the civil and criminal codes 
adopted by the Territorial legislature of 1859, plus the amendments 
added in 1860. The civil code of 1859 was substantially like that 
of 1858, but with some modifications and additions. A comparison 
of the Territorial civil code of 1859 with those of other States points 
to the conclusion that it was borrowed from the Ohio statutes of 
1853. It was almost a verbatim copy, although the names of courts 
differed and other minor changes had been made.!2® The criminal

126. Ibid., pp. 449-451.

## Page 132

124 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
code of 1858 was quite different, however, from that of 1859. In 
bulk, for example, the act of 1858 covered only twenty-six pages, 
whereas that of 1859 covered forty-six pages. The criminal code 
and the statute of crimes and punishments adopted in 1859 resemble 
the Missouri statutes of 1845 and 1855, respectively.1°° Kansas re- 
tained the Missouri provision for grand juries until 1864. The act 
of February 12, 1864, abolished them except in cases where the 
judge of the court should direct otherwise. Hereafter, trial of 
offenses was to be “upon information.” This provision was incor- 
porated in the code of criminal procedure of 1868.13. In commenting 
on the new laws adopted in 1858, the Kansas Weekly Herald wrote: 
“They have based their laws on civil and criminal procedure on the 
Ohio statutes.”!°? In reporting the results to the legislature in 1859 
the Codifying Commission wrote: “The code of civil procedure re- 
mains substantially the same. The code of criminal procedure of 
1858 is materially altered, and it is hoped, materially improved.’’*? 
The State legislatures of 1861 and 1862 contributed but little to the 
civil and criminal codes except in the form of amendments. The 
legislature of 1861 defined treason, misprison of treason, and misde- 
meanor. It made treason punishable by death.134 The act adopting 
the Common Law as a rule of action had been passed by the 
Territorial legislature in 1859, and was retained by the State Codi- 
fying Commission of 1862. The act regulating trials of impeach- 
ment was passed by the second State legislature shortly before the 
trial of the State officers and became a law without Governor Robin- 
son’s signature.135 
The first State legislature had done nothing to incorporate the 
Territorial laws into the State statutes. The legislature of 1862, 
however, appointed a commission of three, S. E. Hoffman of the 
Senate, and F. P. Baker and Davies Wilson of the House, to com- 
pile the laws applicable to the State. The report of the commission 
was adopted by the legislature and approved by the Governor on 
the 6th of March, 1862.1°¢ 
The Supreme Court of Kansas was organized in a special session 
of the legislature on the 28th of October, 1861. Hon. Thomas Ewing,

Jr., Hon. Samuel A. Kingman, and Hon. Lawrence D. Bailey con- 
stituted the personnel of the court. It adopted rules of practice, 
admitted attorneys, and then adjourned to hold its first regular 
session in January, 1862. The Supreme Court of the Territory had 
ceased to function after the 29th of January, 1861, despite the pro- 
vision in the schedule of the constitution to the contrary. Its journal 
and appearance dockets were transferred into the hands of the State 
Supreme Court officers. As originally constituted, the Supreme 
Court continued less than two years. During this time it had heard 
nine cases. The first case was that of George A. Crawford v. Gov- 
ernor Robinson, which will be considered later. The resignation of 
Chief Justice Ewing, shortly before the November election of 1862, 
led to a dispute between Nelson Cobb and John H. Watson. Since 
Chief Justice Ewing’s resignation had been dated October 20, less 
than a month before the election, Governor Robinson, in accordance 
with the constitution, had appointed Cobb to fill the vacancy. The 
Republicans had disregarded the appointment and elected Watson. 
The case came before the Supreme Court, and the Court decided 
for Cobb and ruled the election of Watson a nullity.487 The United 
States District Court, under Archibald Williams, was organized in 
April of 1861. 
The powers and duties of the State officers were set forth in three 
statutes. On the 26th of April, 1861, the Governor approved the 
“Act prescribing the duties and liabilities of the Treasurer of the 
State.” This officer was placed under a $200,000 bond to be signed 
by five or more securities and approved by the Governor. He was 
required to make annual reports to the legislature and submit his 
books to them for inspection. On the 3d of June, the Governor 
approved the act defining the powers and duties of his own office, 
and those of the Secretary of State, the Auditor, and the Attorney 
General. The Auditor was required to make annual reports to the 
legislature, making him, like the Treasurer, responsible to the legis- 
lature instead of to the Governor. The accounts of the Secretary of 
State were to be open to the inspection of the Governor or the legis- 
lature. The Attorney General was required ‘to prosecute any 
official bond, or contract in which the State is interested,” at the 
request of the Governor, Secretary of State, Auditor, Treasurer, and 
Superintendent of Public Instruction. He was to advise and con- 
sult with the district attorneys.°° The duties of the State Super- 
137. E. A. Austin, ‘““SSupreme Court of the State of Kansas,” Collections, XIII, 95-98. 
138. General Laws of Kansas, 1861, pp. 208-218. 


## Page 134

126 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
intendent were given in article one of the common schools act. It 
placed the common schools of the State under the supervision of the 
State Superintendent. He, too, was required to make annual re- 
ports to the legislature. The act also set forth the duties of the 
county supintendents, and provided for school districts and the es- 
tablishment of graded schools.1°° The foregoing analysis shows that 
the Governor was left without the assistance necessary to enforce 
the laws of the State, except insofar as he could command the re- 
spect and cooperation of the State officers through the force of his 
personality and the appeal of his own policies. 
On the 22d of May, 1861, the legislature passed an act to regulate 
elections, prescribed the qualifications of voters and the method of 
preventing illegal voting. This was important because it set the 
date for the election of the second Governor in November of 1862. 
The Lane faction later contested the legality of this part of the act. 
The law of June 3, 1861, which prescribed the manner of contesting 
the elections of State and county officers, members of the legislature, 
and other officers, resembles the Iowa act of 1860.14° 
An act establishing the salaries of the State officers, justices of 
the Supreme Court, judges of the district courts, and members of 
the legislature was approved May 20, 1861. The compensation 
varied from allowances of $3 a day for the legislators to $2,000 a 
year for the Governor. The Attorney General received $1,000 a 
year, the State Superintendent and the Treasurer, $1,200 each, the 
Auditor and the Secretary of State $1,500 each, the associate justices 
and the judges of the district courts $1,500 each, and the Chief 
Justice $1,800.141 
Another act providing for the organization of new counties was 
approved by the Governor on the 4th of June, 1861. The initiative 
to effect such organization was left with the resident free-holders of 
the unorganized counties. The population necessary to start an 
organization was six hundred. This requirement being met, twenty 
free-holders could start a movement by petitioning the Governor to 
approve their action. The commissioners, three in number, would 
then name some place centrally located for a temporary county seat 
and divide the county into convenient townships, each township 
constituting an election district. A date would then be set for an

election where the citizens of the provisional organization would 
either approve or disapprove of the action of the commissioners.!4? 
The Territorial act of 1860, relating to townships and township 
officers, and that of 1859, providing for the incorporation of towns 
and villages, were retained by the State. The probate court of the 
county had the power to declare a town or village incorporated. 
The act providing for the compensation of county and township 
officers was approved June 4, 1861. 
To help the State out of its financial difficulties the State legis- 
lature had authorized bond issues. A detailed account of these acts 
will be given later in connection with the account of the impeach- 
ment proceedings against Governor Robinson and others in 1862. 
The question of the Territorial claims relative to the Kansas Civil 
War losses, was considered by the State legislature of 1861 and 1862. 
The Territorial legislature of 1859 had passed three acts pertaining 
to the claims question. The act of February 7, 1859, and the supple- 
mentary act of February 11, had provided for a commission of three 
to investigate the losses actually incurred and had transformed the 
claims into Territorial debts. The third act, also of February 11, 
1859, known as the Funding Act, had provided for the payment of 
$100,000 worth of these claims. It has been mentioned elsewhere 
that the bonds issued under this act were held by the Robinson 
group. The first State legislature passed a bill to prevent payment 
of these bonds. The bill passed both houses with but one dissenting 
vote, but the Governor vetoed it.1*4 
The State legislature had also voted to assume the Territorial 
debt, but Governor Robinson vetoed that too. He argued that the 
State legislature had passed the bond acts and that to assume the 
Territorial debt in addition to this would have the effect of depre- 
ciating the value of the State bonds for which the credit of the 
State had been pledged. Furthermore, he regarded the act un- 
constitutional because the legislature had failed to comply with 
article 11, section 6, of the constitution, which provided that such 
an act should be submitted to a direct vote of the electors of the 
State.145 The question came up again in the second legislature. 
The House asked the Attorney General, S. A. Stinson, whether the 
State was liable for the debts of the Territory. Judge Stinson re-

plied that the State was liable for these debts to the same extent that

the State Supreme Court of Wisconsin in the case of Bazter vs. 
the State.46 All attempts made by the legislature to settle the 
question during Governor Robinson’s administration failed. 
The legislature of 1861 had retained in force the Territorial act 
of 1860 providing for the assessment of taxes. This act had pro- 
vided for exemptions, stipulated the duties of county assessors, pro- 
vided for a county board of equalization, permitted the township 
trustees to determine the rate or amount of taxes to be levied and 
made the county treasurer the collector of the taxes. The legislature 
of 1862 had merely amended the Territorial act in regard to exemp- 
tions to be made and reduced the period of grace of the delinquent 
taxpayer.147 
On the 28th of February, 1862, the State Treasurer reported to the 
House of Representatives an income of $45,750.60 since the com- 
mencement of the fiscal year, January 29, 1861. Of this sum 
$11,415.31 had come from the county treasuries, $261.36 from the 
sale of capital materials, $353.93 from school funds and $33,720 
from the sale of State bonds. The expenditures of the State for the 
first year were estimated at $85,570.148 
The Lawrence Bank, which had been organized in the spring of 
1859, was used as a depository for the State funds. 8. W. Eldridge, 
James Blood, Governor Robinson, R. S. Stevens, and Robert Mor- 
row, were directors of the bank. After the admission of the State 
the bank was reorganized under the State laws and the Kansas 
State bonds “were deposited with the auditor for the security of the 
circulation” of the bond issues. The select committee of the House 
reported February 24, 1862, that the Lawrence bank had on de- 
posit in the Auditor’s office $3,000 of the ten percent war bonds, 
$16,000 of the seven percent State bonds, and $16,000 of the Terri- 
torial claim bonds.1*°

lature and a design was agreed upon in May of 1861. Considered 
first in separate committees of the two houses, which failed to come 
to an agreement, the question was referred to a conference com-

## Page 138

Chapter VII 
Factional Conflicts: Lane vs. Robinson 
1861-1862 
HE Robinson-Lane feud was fast dividing the new State into 
LL two bitter factions. The enmity was of long standing. General 
Lane’s temperament and Governor Robinson’s biting invective did 
not make for coéperation, especially since both were aspiring to win 
the leadership and direct the forces of the State. This rivalry had 
been steadily nursed by both leaders and their supporters during 
the Territorial period and was now threatening to paralyze the new 
State. 
The argument became so heated that the Wyandotte Commercial 
Gazette referred to the language used by Lane and Robinson as 
“very undignified specimens. of English literature,” and said that if 
one-fourth of what they said of each other was true “they were both 
unfit for the positions they held.’”’+ A Democratic paper remarked: 
“Of course we'do not know who is right; but Robinson is Governor, 
and Lane a would-be Senator. They are both good Republicans, 
both great men, both are mixed up in the bond question, both love 
the dear people; but unlike Brutus, they love Rome less and Caesar 
more. The fight is among the Republicans,” and the writer advised 
his Democratic contemporaries “‘to let them fight.” 2 By May, 1861, 
the feud had become so pervasive, however, that the Leavenworth 
Daily Conservative wrote: “On all public questions there is a 
Robinson version and a Lane version, and neither is the truth. 
Men anxious to know what to do, and how to act, in disgust strike 
a balance and wish to Heaven that there could be some cessation 
to this all blighting feud.” ? 
The historian today stands in bewilderment before the incriminat- 
ing charges and wonders where the truth lies. The conflict was 
carried into the political and military departments of the State and 
biased the current publications and correspondence. The political 
conflict will be considered first.

Lawrence Republican, March 22, 1860. f A cake eaeareats

The second Tuesday in June, 1861, was the date set by the legis- 
lature for the election of a Representative to Congress.* Martin F. 
Conway stated that he had served only about thirty days at the 
time Congress adjourned, March 28, 1861, and asked to be reélected. 
He was again supported by Eastern capital. George L. Stearns 
of Boston had asked James Montgomery of Mound City to work for 
Conway’s nomination, and Stearns authorized Montgomery to draw 
on him up to $500.5 Montgomery managed to get himself elected to 
the Republican nominating convention, but not without difficulty.® 
The Topeka convention, held May 22, 1861, nominated Conway 
on the first ballot by thirty-eight votes against eight for Williams 
and six for Lynde. Senator Lane was there, but the convention 
occasioned no excitement. The papers made no mention of a Demo- 
cratic candidate, and the campaign passed off very quietly. Con- 
way was reélected with practically no opposition.” 
The November election of 1861 created more excitement. The 
interests of both factions were again involved. General Lane’s mili- 
tary ambitions were being hampered by Governor Robinson. Lane 
had neglected his erstwhile coveted office as United States Senator, 
for which he had paid so dearly, financially and morally, and was 
seeking military honors. He wanted to control the military patron- 
age of the State and sought the office of Brigadier General of the 
Southern Expedition comprising Kansas, the Indian Territory, and 
Arkansas. Governor Robinson studiously guarded the military 
powers which he thought rightfully belong to the Governor of Kan- 
sas. When Lane saw that his bitter enemy was foiling his military 
ambitions, he retaliated by seizing upon the disputed question of 
the expiration of the State officers’ term as a means of removing 
Robinson.’ The opportunity to strike came in the election of No- 
vember 5, 1861. : 
The question of when the State officers’ term expired had been 
debated by the legislature. That body had decided that it expired 
two years after the date of the admission, basing this conclusion 
upon the Attorney General’s interpretation of the constitution. The 
Attorney General, B. F. Simpson, had taken the position that there

had been no State government until Congress had admitted Kansas, 
and the terms of office could not commence until there was a govern- 
ment under which to act.® The legislature, therefore, had not pro- 
vided for an election of State officers in November, 1861. There had 
been those in the legislature who contended that the two-year term 
expired on the second Monday in January, 1861, among them John 
Pettit of Leavenworth whose opinion was quoted in the Kansas 
State Record of Topeka.’® Following this preliminary dispute, how- 
ever, the papers had dropped the subject until after the Governor’s 
election proclamation of September 30, 1861. 
The proclamation provided for an election to be held on the 5th 
of November, 1861. At this time the people should elect representa- 
tives to the State legislature, fill vacancies in the Senate, elect a 
State Treasurer in place of William Tholan, who had failed to 
qualify, elect an Attorney General of the State in place of B. F. 
Simpson who had resigned, elect county officers, vote on the loca- 
tion of the State capital, and vote on the proposed amendment to the 
State constitution and the banking law.14 
Events now followed in rapid succession. In a letter dated Octo- 
ber 4, 1861, Governor Robinson had preferred charges of plunder, 
robbery, and arson against the Kansas Brigade in command of 
General Lane.!2 The Brigade had invaded Missouri and burned 
the town of Osceola. On the 5th the newspapers came out with the 
Governor’s election proclamation. On the 6th Governor Robinson 
and William E. Prince, Adjutant General at Fort Leavenworth, met 
at Fort Leavenworth and “desecrated the Sabbath,” as Lane put it, 
by conspiring to destroy the Kansas Brigade.1? Two days later, 
Lane appeared before a crowded house in Leavenworth and in a 
fiery speech denounced Governor Robinson and General Prince as 
traitors to their country. His feelings had been hurt by the Gov- 
ernor’s unsparing criticism of his military exploits. He defended 
his actions and demanded from the Kansas people a new military 
department.14 This speech was capitalized by the Lane-Leaven- 
worth faction. It was read before two of Lane’s regiments; the 
Leavenworth Conservative distributed 10,000 copies of it, and saw 
to it that it was published in the Kansas newspapers. The Con-

servative later estimated that the speech had been read by a “mil- 
lion and a half people.” ** On the 10th of October, A. C. Wilder, 
Chairman of the Republican Central Committee, announced the 
meeting of the State committee to nominate candidates for the 
November election. The committee would meet at Topeka October 
16. The day before the committee met the Conservative announced 
that a petition had been circulated and signed by more than 3,000 
Republicans and Democrats, voters of Kansas, demanding an elec- 
tion of State officers. The petition was presented to the chairman 
of the nominating committee for consideration. It read as follows: 
Sir: We, the undersigned citizens, suffering, in common with others, from 
the impotency or malice of the present State Executive, and earnestly desiring 
a State Government that will in a patriotic and energetic manner defend our 
people from invasion—knowing that by the plain and emphatic provisions of 
the State constitution the term of our State Officers expires on the first day of 
January, and that the Legislative enactment continuing the State Officers be- 
yond that time is null and void, and that there is not sufficient time, before the 
election, to hold a nominating convention, do respectfully pray your Honor- 
able Body to nominate a full State Ticket, of efficient Union men, without 
reference to their political antecedents—men who will conduct the State 
Government with reference to the good of the whole country and not upon 
mere personal grounds.16 
The following day the committee met, and in response to the 
petition nominated a full ‘State Union Ticket.” George A. Craw- 
ford of Bourbon County was nominated Governor; Joseph L. Speer 
of Jefferson County, Lieutenant Governor; J. W. Robinson of 
Riley County, Secretary of State; S. A. Stinson of Leavenworth, 
Attorney General; H. R. Dutton of Brown County, Treasurer; 
James R. McClure of Davis County, Auditor; H. D. Preston of 
Osage County, Superintendent of Public Instruction.17 The attempt 
to obstruct Lane’s military career had been turned into a scheme to 
remove Robinson from office and destroy him politically. The plan 
showed the earmarks of the crafty Lane’s handiwork, but it had 
some serious loopholes. These the sharp mind of the Governor 
would detect and use to frustrate Lane’s scheme. 
The Republican committee had used good judgment in nominat- 
ing the candidates. They had placed important Democrats on the 
ticket; George A. Crawford and 8. A. Stinson among them. In 
his campaign speech at Leavenworth, Crawford said he had been 
selected by the Republicans because a “Democrat would better

16. Ibid., October 15, 1861; M. W. Delahay, Papers, M. W. Delahay to Lincoln, Nov. 
30, 1861. 
17. Ibid., Oct. 19, 1861. 


## Page 142

134 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
harmonize the conflicting elements in the State; that to nominate 
one of their own party would only be to perpetuate the old war be- 
tween General Lane and Governor Robinson.” He appealed to the 
people for a union of parties to save the Union.1® This was a noble 
plea. Its purpose was, however, to win votes to oust Governor 
Robinson. No doubt the Republican committee thought it safer to 
let the Democrats ‘‘pull the chestnuts out of the fire” than to burn 
their own fingers. Should the attempt to remove Governor Robin- 
son fail, the odium of the defeat could be shifted to the Democrats. 
In case they won, the Republican Party would have removed him 
for the sake of the Union, and the Lane-Leavenworth combination 
could have their way. It seems that back of this combination were 
two objects: first, to oust Governor Robinson because he had op- 
posed and thwarted the aims and ambitions of Lane; and secondly, 
to make Leavenworth the business center of the military machine.?® 
It is of interest to note that at this time Judge Ewing left Robinson 
and drifted into the Lane camp.”° 
The campaign was short but spirited. The Lane faction based 
their arguments upon article 1, section 1, of the constitution which 
provided that -the State officers should “hold their office for the 
term of two years from the second Monday of January, after the 
election, and until their successors are elected and qualified.” As 
to the date of the election they cited the 11th section of the 
schedule, which had set the time of election on “the first Tuesday 
of December, 1859.” From this they argued that the State officers’ 
term expired on the second Monday of January, 1862.21. Sol Miller 
called it the “veriest quibble trumped up for the occasion.” He 
maintained that the framers of the constitution had expected that 
Kansas would be admitted at the ensuing session of Congress. Their 
intention had been that the State officers should serve for two years 
from the admission of Kansas. To argue otherwise would mean that 
“the terms of all the Representatives elect, expired on the first of 
January last, before the admission of Kansas.” This would render 
all enactments of the legislature void, including the election of 
United States Senators. He criticized the Republican Central Com- 
mittee for exceeding its authority in nominating an entire State 
ticket. A secret cabal had concocted the new program and “sent

21. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Oct. 28, 1861. This was Thomas Mean’s interpre- 
tation. His opinion was accepted by the supporters of the Union ticket.

The Birth of Kansas 135 
their agents with the committee to Topeka, where they succeeded in 
accomplishing their ends.’ Governor Robinson did not participate 
in the campaign. He rested his case on the law and its proper in- 
terpretation. George A. Crawford traveled from town to town de- 
livering campaign speeches and giving dinner parties. Sol Miller 
warned Crawford not to depend too much on the assumption that 
victuals would purchase success. ‘Poor human nature is such, that 
a man will forget these little kindnesses as soon as his stomach is 
empty. And you know you are not able to board the whole State.”?8 
In the election, held November 5, 1861, the largest number of votes 
was cast for S. A. Stinson, Attorney General, who received 11,971 
votes. H. R. Dutton was elected State Treasurer with 11,748 
votes.24 The state board of canvassers, composed of Governor Rob- 
inson, H. R. Dutton, and Charles Chadwick, refused to report the 
results for those offices not mentioned in the Governor’s proclama- 
tion. Most counties, however, had sent in a complete report. Ac- 
cording to these reports G. A. Crawford had received 5,152 votes, 
not even half the total number of votes cast at the election.2”>. Many 
people had refused to vote the Union ticket. For example, in 
Breckenridge County the vote for Crawford was only 55, whereas 
Dutton had polled 492 votes. Similar although smaller differences 
existed in Morris, Chase, Doniphan, and Nemaha Counties. In 
Leavenworth, Jefferson, Butler, Atchison, and Douglas Counties the 
county board of canvassers had sent in only the returns for the offices 
provided for in the Governor’s proclamation. Thus the people of 
Kansas had repudiated Lane’s scheme to oust Governor Robinson.?® 
Other questions were voted on. The constitutional amendment 
was adopted by a vote of 3,733 to 3,343 against it. This gave the 
banks a right to issue circulating notes of one dollar denomination. 
The capital question was permanently settled. The banking law was 
defeated because it was not approved by a majority of all votes 
cast at the election. The vote was 4,655 for, and 2,087 against the 
law. When J. Brewer of the Kansas Supreme Court made the state- 
ment, in the case of Edward Pape v. Capitol Bank of Topeka, that 
no banking “law has ever been submitted to a proper vote,” he was 
mistaken.?*

136 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
When the State board of canvassers refused to count the votes 
cast for Governor, Crawford’s only legal recourse was to bring the 
case before the State Supreme Court. On the 16th of November, 
he wrote a letter to Governor Robinson in which he proposed to sub- 
mit the case to the Supreme Court in January, 1862, and agreed 
to abide cheerfully by its decision.22 The Governor’s response, al- 
though no record of it is available, must have been in the affirmative. 
At least the case was opened January 18, 1862. Judge S. W. John- 
ston, Hon. S. A. Stinson, and R. Crozier appeared on behalf of Craw- 
ford, and ex-Governor Wilson Shannon represented Governor Robin- 
son. Crawford had made application for a writ of mandamus to 
compel the State board of canvassers to count the votes cast for 
State officers in the November election. The case was ably argued 
on both sides and lasted a week. The application for the writ, how- 
ever, was unanimously overruled by the Supreme Court. Delivering 
the opinion of the court,?® Chief Justice Ewing argued that the only 
statute fixing the time for electing State officers was that approved 
May 22, 1861, providing for an election of a Governor at the general 
election of November, 1862, and each second year thereafter. He 
found nothing.in the constitution that would nullify it. To reason 
that the State officers’ term commenced on the second Monday of 
January following their election to office would complicate matters. 
It would mean that the terms of the members of the House of Rep- 
resentatives had expired before the admission of the State. All laws 
passed by the first legislature would, therefore, be null and void for 
want of the operative existence of one branch of that body; for, un- 
like the officers of the executive department, “the members of the 
legislature may not continue in office beyond the regular measure 
of their terms, until their successors are elected and qualified.” He 
concluded his argument by saying: 
There are no provisions in the constitution that can possibly be construed 
as fixing a date for the commencement of the terms of members of the Legis- 
lature, except Sec. 2, Art. 2, and Sec. 1 of Art. 1. . . . From these sections, 
no date for the commencement can be inferred except the date when the mem- 
bers are declared elected, or the 2d Monday in January next after their elec- 
tion; both which, resting as they do on inferences not necessarily to be drawn 
from the text, and being irreconcilable with other express provisions of the 
constitution, must be rejected. Hence as no date is fixed in that instrument 
from which those terms begin, and as the whole subject is within the defined 
powers of the legislature, it was competent for that body to fix it. 
And as the officers of the Executive Department are to be chosen at the time 
28. The letter appeared in the Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Dec. 24, 1861. 
29. White Cloud Chief, Jan. 22, 1862.

Thus the first Lane scheme concocted to remove Governor Robin- 
son from office collapsed. Sol Miller delighted in joshing the op- 
position about their spoiled play. “Is not the Supreme Court an 
obtuse and hard hearted institution? Did not all the great legal 
lights of the State advise them to decide otherwise? . . . Didn’t 
the Conservative make the matter as plain as mud, every day, for 
two or three months past?” Crawford had already appointed his 
private secretary. Preparations had been made for a big oyster and 
. champagne supper and a régular “bust” to be held at the Chase 
House, and then Judge Ewing had to spoil it all. “After the ad- 
journment of the Court we saw one sorrowful looking individual 
have Judge Ewing by the buttonhole, out in the snow, for a long 
time, trying to convince him that he had decided wrongly, and to 
reason him into reversing the decision.” #1 
General Lane, however, did not give up the fight. In the mean- 
time the Civil War was going on, and it is necessary to discuss the 
participation of Kansas in the military conflict. 
The war had created new problems for the young State and com- 
plicated old ones. In the first place the attitude of the people to- 
ward the war was divided. Some would crush the secessionists, 
others would let the “erring brothers go in peace.” Still others re- 
garded the war as a conflict for supremacy between the industrial 
North and the agricultural South and sympathized with the South. 
Thus wrote the Wyandotte Weekly Western Argus: 
We are convinced that before the experiment of self government will be 
successful, practical statesmen must devise some plan which shall prevent one 
section of the country combining simply for the purpose of warring upon and 
crushing out some interest of another section. . . . Let us suppose the 
tables turned, and that a Northern instead of a Southern interest is exciting 
the popular frenzy. Suppose the South and West were thoroughly aroused by 
their orators and politicians to the belief that the only impediments to their 
prosperity is the manufactories of New England and the iron works of New 
York and Pennsylvania. Let every election in the South and West be 
fought on these hobbies. . . . an anti-manufacturing President . . . be 
elected by the South and West, . . . backed by an anti-manufacturing 
Congress. Suppose that the platform which these officers are pledged to 
30. Daily State Record, Topeka, Jan. 9, 14, 1862. 
81. White Cloud Chief, Jan. 22, 1862.

138 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
carry out contains resolutions which as explained and upheld by them, are 
utterly hostile to, and incompatible with the existence of Northern manufac- 
tures; what would New England do, what would New York and Pennsylvania 
do, if it was their local interest in controversy? Would devotion to the Union 
keep them true to a government which was preying upon and destroying 
them? We believe that honest Abe will prefer to destroy party and repudiate 
his platform than violate his oath of office. But Southerners do not so be- 
lieve, . . 32 
A second problem was the impotency of the State. In 1883 Goy- 
ernor Robinson wrote: “At the admission of the State into the 
Union not a dollar was found in the treasury, nor a gun or pound of 
amunition in its armory. A few private arms were among the 
people, and there had been public arms, but they were scattered be- 
yond recovery.” *? The people were still suffering from the famine, 
and thieves and robbers were increasing in number. Robinson wrote 
that as soon as the war broke out “these men gathered on the border 
and no property was safe that could be driven or carried away.” 34 
_ In addition to the internal problems were the external dangers. 
On the eastern border was a slave State, threatening to secede. 
Secessionists were holding conventions along the border in Missouri, 
threatening to-invade and subdue Kansas. On the west and south 
were Indians who were hostile or likely to become so. Both the in- 
ternal and external problems were complicated by the quarrel be- 
tween Senator Lane and Governor Robinson. 
To help solve the internal problems the State Government issued 
war bonds and organized the State militia. The sale of the bonds 
gave the Governor $18,600 with which to organize, discipline, and 
service the State militia, and muster into service the regiments 
called for by the War Department. On the 25th of April, 1861, he 
ordered that the northern division of the State should organize two 
brigades and five regiments and the southern division two brigades - 
and six regiments. The regiments of the northern division should 
be located at Leavenworth, Atchison, Holton, Vermillion, and Junc- 
tion City; those of the southern division at Lawrence, Paola, Barns- 
ville, Auburn, Le Roy, and Emporia.®® Governor Robinson appointed 
James C. Stone of Leavenworth as Major General of the northern 
division and Samuel Walker of Lawrence as Major General of the 
southern division. R. B. Mitchell was appointed Adjutant General;

George W. Collamore, Quartermaster General; William Mitchell,

According to Governor Robinson, nearly two hundred militia com- 
panies were organized at once.?7 Fifty members of the legislature 
organized into the “Capital Guard” under the leadership of Colonel 
Clarkson. 
It appears that the State militia was unable to cope with the 
problem of maintaining order. On the 8th of April, 1861, the 
notorious Quantrill was captured by Captain Sneider for horse- 
stealing, and imprisoned at Osawatomie. Quantrill’s proslavery 
friends, however, secured a writ of habeas corpus from the judge, re- 
leasing him from prison and permitting his escape. On the 26th of 
July Mr. Zulavsky of Mound City wrote that nearly all of Captain 
John E. Stewart’s Company, Stewart included, had turned into 
horse thieves. “They go to the borders and take Union men’s stock, 
as southern rebels have left the borders.” 38 Stewart had been a 
preacher and abolitionist during the Kansas Territorial days and 
was now Captain of Company C of the 9th Regiment of Kansas 
Volunteer Cavalry. On the 3d of August a correspondent of the 
Wyandotte Gazette described conditions as critical along the Kansas 
border in the vicinity of Barnsville, Trading Post, and West Point. 
Ten days later E. 8. Lowman wrote that Olathe was in utmost con- 
fusion. “A secret organization had been formed for the purpose of 
plundering the people, ostensibly to operate against those who 
[were] reputed as lukewarm on the Union question but really to 
rob every man of property.” He stated that the people were in 
danger of being robbed by “Jayhawkers from both sides of the 
line.” 9 On the same day, Captain J. E. Hayes of the Fourth Regi- 
ment of Kansas, by proclamation, warned all who were engaged in 
any marauding expeditions that he had a force under his command 
“that would not only be used to put down traitors but all who were 
engaged in disturbing peaceful citizens.” 4° The plundering bands, 
however, became so dangerous “stealing horses, destroying crops, 
burning houses, driving off stock and committing murders” that Sol 
Miller thought “Jayhawking was the only system that could reach 
the villains who were causing all these troubles.” *1 By this he
