meant that the settlers should organize and defend themselves. On 
the 14th of January Governor Robinson wrote that the criminals, 
thieves, and highway robbers had become so numerous in some 
localities that “they have the Community under complete subjec- 
tion and control. Citizens of property and influence have been 
cowed into silence, . . . Even many have endorsed robbery, 
that they might be able to retain their own property.” ‘The Gov- 
ernor asked that the criminal laws be amended to make it possible 
to punish the violators, and he recommended a speedy trial of crimi- 
nals in any court within the State. In January, 1862, the legisla- 
ture passed a concurrent resolution asking Governor Robinson to in- 
form them what military protection, if any, he had at his disposal to 
protect the Kansas settlers. The legislature also authorized him to 
appeal to Major General Hunter for military assistance. On the 
12th of February the Governor informed the legislature that he 
could not use the militia without an appropriation of money. He 
had, however, referred the matter to Major General Hunter.** 
Although there were temporary checks, in the main the freebooters 
continued their depredations even into Governor Carney’s adminis- 
tration. ; 
Governor Carney raised a force of a hundred and fifty men and 
employed them as a patrol along the border. So long as this patrol 
was protecting the border, Kansas citizens lived in peace, but when 
it was withdrawn, Quantrill invaded the State, sacked Lawrence and 
murdered scores of its citizens. It was reported that Governor 
Carney paid the border patrol out of his personal funds, costing him 
more than $10,000.*4 
The Democrats accused of disloyalty were preyed upon. Among 
them were newspaper men. The Atchison Bulletin, the Marysville 
Platform, the Junction City Frontier, and the Leavenworth Inquirer 
were among the papers blacklisted. The Inquirer criticised the Re- 
publicans for preying on the “owners of Southern plantations,” say- 
ing that “The Black Republican papers are very generally urging 
the prosecution of the war, not for the purpose of restoring the 
Constitution and the Union, but for the purpose of driving out the 
owners of Southern plantations to enable northern men to take pos- 
session of, and enjoy them.” #° General James G. Blunt arrested 
Burrell B. Taylor, editor of the Leavenworth Inquirer, and his

associates in business on June 15, 1862. They were all released, 
however, except Taylor. Kansas City also had Southern sympa- 
thizers. At one time it was reported that “not less than a dozen 
Palmetto flags were waving over Kansas City.” 
Some of the so-called secessionists left Kansas. On the 12th of 
September the Conservative wrote that eight of the “most ultra 
secessionists” of Atchison had left town. They had been afraid of 
being attacked by Jayhawkers. General Stringfellow, an ardent 
proslavery leader of Territorial fame, however, had remained, having 
satisfied the citizens of the town by donating potatoes to the Union 
volunteers.*6 That much of the property of the secessionists was 
confiscated is evidenced by newspaper reports. Thus wrote the 
Conservative: “Our Jayhawkers are out every day in the county, 
and make their expeditions pay. They have captured over 100 
stand of arms, some amunition, several beeves, etc. So long as a 
secessionist in this part of the country has anything in the shape of 
beef, I am confident no one here will starve.” * 
At one time there was danger of war between Kansas and Mis- 
souri. The danger grew out of the President’s proclamation calling 
for volunteers to help suppress the rebellion. Governor C. F. Jack- 
son of Missouri replied to the President’s proclamation that there 
could be no doubt but that “these men are intended to form a part 
of the President’s army to make war upon the people of the seceded 
States. Your requisition, in my judgment, is illegal, unconstitutional 
and revolutionary in its object, inhuman and diabolical, and cannot 
be complied with. Not one man will the State of Missouri furnish 
to carry on any such unholy crusade.” 4% A little later Governor 
Jackson issued a proclamation calling for 50,000 State troops to 
repel invasion and protect the “lives, liberty and property of the 
citizens of the State.” He told his fellow Missourians that they 
owed their first allegiance to their own State and that they were 
under “no obligations whatever to obey the unconstitutional edicts 
of the military despotism which [had] enthroned itself at Washing- 
ton, nor to submit to the infamous and degrading sway of its 
wicked minions in [Missouri]. . . . Rise then,” he exclaimed, 
“and drive out ignominiously the invaders who have dared to 
desecrate the soil which your labors have made fruitful, and which 
is consecrated by your homes.” *°

## Page 150

142 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Governor Robinson sensed the seriousness of the situation and 
issued a counter proclamation of war against Missouri, June 17, 
1861. It stated: 
The Governor of Missouri having declared war against the Government of 
the United States, and called for 50,000 State troops to be used in its over- 
throw, war is virtually declared against the State of Kansas also. 
Should the Government of Missouri succeed against the United States forces, 
Kansas cannot hope to escape invasion. Indeed it is to be feared the invasion 
of the State will be one of the first acts of the rebel army. 
In view of these facts the Governor called upon the citizens to 
organize military companies and hold themselves in readiness to 
enter upon active service at call. To keep informed, Governor 
Robinson, in the spring of 1861, sent George F. Earl to the Missouri 
border to watch developments.°° 
The warfare that followed was mostly of the guerrilla type. On 
the 17th of August, 1861, the Wyandotte Commercial Gazette wrote 
that on the border most of the people were sleeping in corn fields. 
Men, women and children did not dare remain in their homes lest 
they be attacked by the prowling bands of secessionists. ‘Almost 
every house in the vicinity has Union refuges from Missouri in it. 
There are rumors of an attack every day, and the men are nearly 
exhausted by their ceaseless watchings.”” On the 30th of August the 
Hannibal and St. Joseph Railroad was attacked by secessionists 
with gun fire about six miles west of Palmyra and eighteen miles 
from Quincy. One man was fatally wounded.®°! That same day 
Major General J. C. Fremont established martial law throughout 
the State of Missouri.>? In August, 1861, after the battle of Wil- 
son’s Creek, in Missouri, the eastern part of Kansas feared that the 
Confederate General Price would invade Kansas and make good 
his threat “to wipe Kansas off the map.” This threat had produced 
a bitter controversy among the Union authorities as to the best 
method of warding off the Confederate General. Senator Lane was 
confident that the best point from which to defend Leavenworth was 
“in the neighborhood of Fort Scott,” and asked General Fremont 
for reinforcements. Governor Robinson disagreed with Lane and 
on the first of September informed Major General Fremont that 
Kansas was in no danger of invasion, provided the Government 
stores at Fort Scott were sent back to Leavenworth and the Lane 
Brigade was removed from the border. “It is true small parties of

Secessionists are to be found in Missouri,” he wrote, “but we have 
good reason to know that they do not intend to molest Kansas, in

But what we have to fear, and do fear is that Lane’s 
‘Brigade’ will get up a war by going over the line, committing dep- 
redations and then returning into the State.”” He asked Fremont 
to remove the supplies and relieve Kansas of the Lane Brigade. 
General Fremont agreed with Governor Robinson and on October 
4, ordered General Lane to fall back to Fort Leavenworth.*? 
The ink of Robinson’s letter was barely dry when the news spread 
that Osceola, Missouri, was burning. Lane’s Brigade had invaded 
the State. Wherever it went it spread terror and left ruin in its 
wake. Osceola was the county seat of St. Clair County, with a 
population of about 2,500. It was reported that Lane had burned 
“all the stores, shops and dwelling-houses in the town except eleven,” 
and a livery stable. On the 15th of October, this brigade visited 
Pleasant Hill, Missouri, and took the property of two stores and 
distributed it among the Union families. The ostensible motive 
was “to check Price’s advance upon Kansas by cutting off his sources 
of supply.” °* Incidentally, however, it was reported that Lane’s 
Brigade had driven off “millions of dollars’ worth of cattle, horses 
and mules,” that they had taken $8,000 from the Osceola bank, that 
“seores of ladies lost silk dresses,” broadcloths, and supplies of all 
kinds. An anonymous letter of August 30, 1862, addressed to the 
editor of the Missourt Republican and published in the Kansas 
Conflict, contains information that “Lane sent his wife, just after- 
wards, $1,000 in gold, a lot of silk dresses, and other women fixings, 
with a letter cautioning her not to attract too much note.” It was 
reported that Lane even paid off some of his debts in Leavenworth. 
Theodore Hyatt wrote that°in the Osceola raid Lane and his men 
had taken $100,000 in cash.*® Even the military authorities, Gen- 
erals Halleck and McClellan, complained of the demoralizing in- 
fluence this practice had on the army. On the 19th of December, 
1861, General Halleck wrote: “I receive almost daily complaints of 
outrages committed by these men [forces under Lane and Jennison] 
in the name of United States, and the evidence is conclusive so as 
to leave no doubt of their correctness.” Yet the government never

got a cent of the money. On the 11th of February, 1862, General 
McClellan wrote Secretary of War Stanton that the practice of 
seizing and confiscating “the private property of rebels,” had be- 
come so “fixed and general” that he was convinced that “orders 
arresting it would not be obeyed, and the only way of putting a 
stop to it would be to remove the Kansas troops to some other field 
of action.” ®* It was only natural that these raids should provoke 
retaliation and revenge. Soon after, therefore, the town of Hum- 
boldt, in Allen County, Kansas, was burned by secessionists from 
the Indian country. On September 7, 1862, Quantrill with two 
hundred and thirty men dashed into Johnson County and sacked 
Olathe. It was reported that he had taken away with him fifty 
horses and mules, attached to the best wagons he could find, loaded 
with provisions. He also carried away $2,000 in cash and fifty 
recruits, and had killed two men in the act. About a month later 
Quantrill again invaded Kansas, and this time he sacked the town 
of Shawnee, about ten miles west of Westport. The town was 
robbed, thirteen buildings were burned, and three men killed.*” 
Thus the border raids continued, culminating in the Quantrill raid 
on Lawrence, August 21, 1863. 
In January, 1862, Governor Robinson explained to the legislature 
his failure to preserve the peace. No appropriation had been made 
“by the last legislature for arming, equipping, or subsisting the State 
militia, and consequently it could not be used for our protection.” 
The money derived from the sale of the “war bonds” had all been 
spent after the First, Second, and Third Regiments were mustered 
into service. When the incessant calls for assistance kept coming 
from all parts of the State he had made an attempt, “under the 
general authority of the constitution, to call into the field a portion 
of the militia to protect the people from invasion; but no person 
could be found willing to furnish them provisions.” ®8 On the 4th 
of March, he again notified the legislature of the unprotected con- 
dition of the citizens in the border counties. Part if not all of the 
troops in the State had been ordered to march south to reinforce 
General Curtis’ army in Arkansas. His last words of warning were: 
It is for the Legislature to decide whether, in any emergency, the Executive 
shali have the means to protect the State, or whether it shall be left power- 
less, as during the past year. The responsibility is entirely with you gentle- 
men. My duty is accomplished when I have informed you of the condition of 
the State.

57. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Sept. 9 and Oct. 19, 1862.

## Page 153

The Birth of Kansas 145 
The next day a bill was introduced in the House to make appro- 
priations to protect the border settlers, but it did not pass.5® 
The main reason, however, why the peaceful settlers of Kansas 
were practically without protection during Governor Robinson’s 
administration was the Lane-Robinson conflict. It had divided the 
legislature into two hostile factions, and the opponents of the ad- 
ministration refused to appropriate money and vote power into the 
hands of Governor Robinson. Closely related to this conflict was 
the struggle for control of the military patronage and the eagerness 
of some of the politicians to command a brigade and organize 
regiments for the United States service. A third factor was the lack 
of money in the State. The Jayhawkers and secessionists, therefore, 
could plunder practically at will during the Robinson administration. 
The struggle for control of the military patronage of the State 
outlasted the Robinson administration. Senator Lane had ingrati- 
ated himself with the Federal administration in order to win favors. 
When President Lincoln issued the proclamation for volunteers, 
Lane organized a “Frontier Guard” of about fifty Kansas office 
seekers lingering at Washington, and offered their services to the 
Secretary of War. Among them were General Lane, M. W. Delahay, 
W. W. Ross, S. C. Pomeroy, Thomas Ewing, Jr., J. Phillips, J. C. 
Vaughan, A. C. Wilder, D. R. Anthony, and William Hutchinson. 
The Secretary of War accepted the offer and for a brief period they 
served to protect the President in the White House.® 
The first major quarrel over the patronage came in connection 
with President Lincoln’s first call for volunteers. On the 17th of 
April, Governor Robinson sent a communication to the President 
stating that Kansas would furnish “1,000 men for the enforcement 
of the laws if desired.” ©! Some time in April, General Lane wrote 
Robinson that the President had requisitioned Kansas for two regi- 
ments to be equipped from the arsenal at Fort Leavenworth. On 
the 2d of May, G. A. Colton from Washington arrived at Topeka 
with a dispatch for Governor Robinson. It was supposed to be the 
requisition order for the two Kansas regiments. The requisition 
order for troops, however, was missing.®* The dispatches “enclosed 
in a sealed envelope were directed to Col. Lane” and contained only 
the authority for arms to be furnished to two regiments, “each con- 
sisting of eight companies of infantry and two of rifles.” © It ap-

pears that the President had not assigned any quota to Kansas on

Early in May, Lane returned to Kansas and asked the people to 
stand by the Government. He spoke at Leavenworth, Topeka, and 
Lawrence, urging men to enlist. His statements revealed the fact 
that he had returned to Kansas to assist in forming and preparing 
the Kansas volunteers for the government service. He admitted 
that the President had made no formal requisition but had simply 
indicated his willingness to receive two Kansas regiments if their 
services were tendered.** The Topeka Tribune criticised Lane 
severely for having made false and contradictory statements as to 
the order from the War Department. The papers criticised also the 
abnormal enthusiasm of some of the military leaders in protecting 
the Federal Government and leaving the State unprotected. 
The First Kansas Regiment, recruited in May, assembled at Camp 
Lincoln, near Fort Leavenworth. On the 3d of June, it was mustered 
into the service of the United States, with George W. Deitzler as 
Colonel; Oscar E. Learned, Lieutenant Colonel; and John A. Halder- 
man as Major. The Second Regiment, recruited in May and early 
June, was assembled at Lawrence and mustered into the service of 
the United States on the 20th of June, with Robert B. Mitchell as 
Colonel; Charles W. Blair, Lieutenant Colonel; and William F. 
Cloud as Major. The officers were commissioned by Governor 
Robinson. The Second Regiment had been in the United States 
service only three months when it was mustered out. 
During the first half of June the papers carried the news that 
Hon. Frederick P. Stanton had been authorized by the Secretary of 
War to raise one regiment in Kansas and two in New Mexico, and 
Stanton was authorized to “look after the army in Kansas and New 
Mexico.” ®* When Lane heard this he went back to Washington 
and secured authority from the administration to organize two more 
Kansas regiments. They were to be accepted by the War Depart- 
ment for three years or during the period of the war. The result 
was that Stanton’s regiment was never filled. Lane published his

follows: 
“On the 20th inst., I was duly appointed a Brigadier General in 
the volunteer force of the United States and thereupon received the 
following order.” Then followed the letter from the War Depart- 
ment notifying him that it would accept “two regiments for three 
years or during the war, in addition to the three regiments the De- 
partment has already accepted from the Governor of Kansas, to be 
raised and organized by you in Kansas.’”’ Lane continued: “The 
President has authorized the formation of a Brigade of five regi- 
ments in Kansas. He has been pleased to place in my hands the 
honor of leading the gallant sons of the youngest State of the Union.” 
Immediately following this letter appeared the following statement: 
“Gen. Lane has assigned to me the duty of receiving and organizing 
the troops provided for in the foregoing order . . . ,” signed by 
William: Weer.®® As early as May 25, the Robinson papers carried 
the information of Lane’s aspirations to become a Brigadier Gen- 
eral.®® Lincoln wrote the Secretary of War, June 20, 1861, that 
“after reflection he had concluded that we need the service of such 
a man out there at once; that we better appoint him a brigadier 
general of volunteers today, and send him off with such authority 
to raisea force ... . as you think will get him into actual work 
quickest. Tell him when he starts to put it through, not to be writ- 
ing or telegraphing back here, but put it through.” 7° 
The Fourth and Fifth Regiments, like the Third, were never 
filled by the recruiting officers before they were mustered into serv- 
ice. Lane had been authorized to muster them with officers, wrote 
Governor Robinson, before they were filled. The Third and Fourth, 
and a small portion of the Fifth, were later consolidated as the 
Tenth, and what remained of the Fifth was eventually filled by 
transferring companies to it. The latter regiment was organized in 
July, 1861, as the Fifth Regiment of Kansas Volunteer Cavalry with 
H. P. Johnston of Leavenworth, Colonel; John Ritchie, Lieutenant 
Colonel; and James H. Summers, Major.” 
With Lane a Brigadier General the dispute shifted to the United 
States Senate. As soon as it became known that Lane had accepted

the position, Governor Robinson appointed F. P. Stanton Senator 
to succeed him. The Governor did not wait for Lane to declare the 
position vacant, but based his action on the provision of the United 
States Constitution relative to occupancy of two offices at the same 
time. On the 12th of July, 1861, Senator Foote of Vermont pre- 
sented, therefore, what purported to be the credentials of F. P. 
Stanton as Senator to replace Lane. Senator Lane was present and 
rose to defend himself. He said it looked like an attempt to bury 
a man before he was dead. Although admitting that he had been 
employed in raising a brigade in Kansas, and that when it was 
filled, if the brigade desired it, he would take charge of it; never- 
theless he would not surrender the office of United States Senator 
until this had taken place. Moreover, if he should surrender it, he 
would not give it to the Governor who was actuated by hostile feel- 
ings, but to the people of Kansas. He wanted the people to select 
his successor.‘ The question was referred to the Judiciary Com- 
mittee. 
Stanton presented his case before the committee, and pointed out 
that before accepting the appointment he had telegraphed the Secre- 
tary of War-and asked whether Senator Lane had been appointed 
Brigadier General, and if so whether he had accepted. The Secre- 
tary had replied: “Lane is appointed and has accepted.” Upon 
receiving this information he had left for Washington, where he 
found General Lane defending his position. He had learned from 
Parrott that General William Weer was present when Lane took the 
oath and paid the fee to the officer for administering it. Since then, 
however, Stanton had discovered the manner in which Lane had 
protected himself: 
It seems that Gen. Lane procured a blank commission and a copy of the 
requisite oath at the Adjutant General’s office, and was requested to return 
them both, when duly executed—the one to be recorded, and the other to be 
placed on file. The commission or letter of appointment was executed in due 
form, presented to Gen. Lane, and accepted by him; but neither this letter 
nor the oath was returned to the office of the Adjutant General. The Secre- 
tary of War informs me that he inferred Gen. Lane’s acceptance from the fact 
that he anxiously solicited the appointment, and gladly received the paper 
which conferred it. . . . As Brigadier General, he authorized Gen. Wm. 
Weer and Col. A. J. Ege, of Kansas, and perhaps others, to proceed to raise 
the two regiments mentioned in the Secretary’s order of the 20th of June.73 
Stanton pointed out, however, that the title to every office was 
“grounded on two things—the election of the party, and his be-

ing sworn in.” It was sufficient, therefore, that General Lane had 
taken the oath of office. As precedents he cited the cases of Rex 
v. Ellis (Hast., 252) and “The People v. Stevens, (5 Hill, 625) 
where the court had ruled that ‘when a person [had] been elected 
to an office, he [was] admitted to the full possession and enjoy- 
ment of it upon taking the requisite oath, provided no other position 
[was] prescribed.’ 7* General Lane’s claim was that so long as he 
had not returned his commission to be recorded, nor his oath to be 
filed, he had not accepted the position.7”> On the 29th of July the 
Judiciary Committee decided in favor of Stanton and so reported to 
the Senate. Lane asked for a further hearing. The committee 
agreed to this and thereby gave Lane a chance to defer action until 
the Senate adjourned.”® 
The Senate again considered the question on the 15th of January, 
1862. The original motion had said that Lane was not entitled to 
his seat. Senator Clark moved to strike out the word “not” in the 
resolution of the Judiciary Committee, and after some discussion 
the Senate agreed to strike the word. The vote was 24 to 16. The 
wily Lane had won again. James Christian, who was in Washing- 
ton at this time, attributed Lane’s success to the Secretary of War, 
Edwin M. Stanton. Senator Lane was now at liberty to continue 
his military plans.” 
Meanwhile many things had happened. On the 20th of August, 
J. C. Fremont, Major General commanding the Western Depart- 
ment with headquarters at St. Louis, was authorized by the Secre- 
tary of War to instruct Governor Robinson to raise three regiments 
of infantry in Kansas. One should act as Home Guard, the other 
two were to serve in the Territory of New Mexico. The enlistment 
was for three years or during the period of the war.7® Governor 
Robinson planned to use the Home Guard to restore order in the 
State.7® The ink was scarcely dry on the Fremont authorization 
when General Lane was back in Kansas. On the 23d of September 
he had burned Osceola, and on the 8th of October he appeared in 
Leavenworth prepared to fight Governor Robinson, who had criti- 
cised his Missouri raid and had tried to oust him from the Senate. 
After hurling terrific invectives against Governor Robinson and Gen- 
eral Prince of Fort Leavenworth on the 8th of October, he asked

his supporters assembled in convention at Leavenworth on the 9th, 
to adopt a series of resolutions which would aid him in affecting a 
change in the military department of Kansas. These resolutions 
asked for a new Military Department, “including Kansas, the Indian 
Country, and Arkansas, with the base at Fort Leavenworth. The 
Government should place at the disposal of the commander of such 
division at least 10,000 troops, consisting of all arms, especially 
artillery.” The command should be given to General James H. 
Lane, who was to go to Washington and procure such a military 
division, if possible. In a letter addressed to President Lincoln, 
October 9, General Lane informed the President of the requests and 
added: “If this can be done, and I can have the command of the 
department, I will cheerfully accept it, resign my seat in the Senate, 
and devote all my thoughts and energies to the prosecution of the 
war.” 8° Lane also wrote letters to other influential men at Wash- 
ington, including the Secretary of War. 
General Lane used these resolutions also as a basis of action. By 
creating a new division he would get rid of General Fremont, through 
whom Governor Robinson was securing his authority to organize the 
Kansas troops, and through whom, it appears, Governor Robinson 
was keeping the military contracts away from Leavenworth.8! Lane 
would thus make Leavenworth the business center of the Kansas 
military machine and satisfy his Leavenworth supporters. More- 
over, he had hoped thereby to provide a military position for him- 
self. General Fremont was removed in November, in the first days 
of the month, on a charge of extravagance, and on the 12th Lincoln 
created the new department.*? From that time until the beginning 
of February, 1862, Lane kept the Kansas people under the impres- 
sion that the President had appointed him Major General command- 
ing the Kansas Department, and that he would command the South- 
ern Expedition.®? As usual, Lane proceeded to appoint his military 
staff without Government authorization, recruited soldiers, and gave 
instructions.** He returned to Kansas and induced the State legis- 
lature to pass resolutions expressing their “implicit faith and con- 
fidence in the skill, courage, patriotism and capacity of General 
Lane to command the expedition which [was] supposed to be about

to be led by himself south.” §° General Hunter, however, announced 
to his troops that it was the intention “of the major-general com- 
manding the department to command in person, unless otherwise 
expressly ordered by the government.” 8 The news spread like 
wildfire and shocked Lane’s friends. Lane was forced to explain. 
He wrote that he had “made every effort which self-respect. would 
permit to effect this arrangement with Major General Hunter. I 
failed.” The only thing left for him, he continued, was to return to 
his seat in the United States Senate. The Daily State Record 
thought it a gross injustice to Lane and the gallant men he had 
rallied around him.®* 
While publicly General Lane expressed great confidence in “Old 
Abe” in spite of the fact that the President had appointed Hunter 
Major General, privately he was greatly wrought up. A person who 
traveled with Lane from St. Joseph to St. Louis on his return to 
Washington, made the statement: 
Jim was very bitter in his denunciations of Lincoln, pronouncing him a “d-d 
liar, a demagogue, and a scoundrel.” That he had promised him the appoint- 
ment of Major General, in place of Hunter, upon the strength of which he 
had made speeches and promises, and laid extensive plans; but the President 
had backed down from his promise, leaving him (Lane) before the public in 
the light of a braggart, a fool and a humbug—closing his tirade by repeating 
that Lincoln was a “d-d liar, a demogogue, and a scoundrel.” 88 
The facts in the case appear to be that the President had no idea 
of Lane out-ranking Hunter. Only if Hunter should decline the 
position would it be tendered to Lane. 
On the 31st of January, 1862, President Lincoln wrote Secretary 
of War Stanton: “General Lane has been told by me many times 
that he is under the command of General Hunter, and assented to it 
as often as told. It was the distinct agreement between him and 
me, when I appointed him, that he was to be under Hunter.” On the 
10th of February the President wrote General Hunter and Senator 
Lane: 
My wish has been and is to avail the government of the services of both 
General Hunter and General Lane, and, so far as possible, to personally oblige 
both. General Hunter is the senior officer, and must command when they 
serve together; though in so far as he can consistently with the public service 
and his own honor oblige General Lane, he will also oblige me. If they can-

not come to an amicable understanding, General Lane must report to General

Governor Robinson and General Prince had used their influence 
to discredit Lane. Hamilton R. Gable of Missouri also advised the 
President against assigning Lane to the position because of his dep- 
redations committed in Osceola, Missouri. Gable favored Hunter 
because he would “keep Kansas robbers and thieves out of Mis- 
souri.” 9° General Hunter questioned Lane’s sincerity in the matter. 
He wrote that in the opinion of those that knew Lane best it was not 
his intention to accept the military position, “his hue and cry for 
that position having only been raised at a time when he thought it 
probable that Stanton (or whosoever was Governor Robinson’s 
nominee) might oust him from the Senate. They say he will never 
resign his seat in the Senate unless he can have supreme control of 
this department.” 1 General Lane was a zealot and cared very 
little about methods. His extreme hatred for anybody who dared 
to oppose him led him to continue the fight, and the Lane-Robinson 
conflict, therefore, went on unabated. 
Before Governor Robinson’s administration came to an end, 
thirteen regiments were ordered recruited. The organization of the 
first five has been mentioned. The Sixth Regiment of Kansas Volun- 
teer Cavalry was organized in the spring of 1862, with William R. 
Judson, Colonel; Lewis R. Jewell, Lieutenant Colonel; and William 
T. Campbell, Major. The regiment was used to disperse the bands 
of raiders under Quantrill, Gordon, and others of southwest Mis- 
souri.°? The Seventh Kansas Volunteer Cavalry was organized on 
the 28th of October, 1861, with Charles R. Jennison of Leavenworth, 
Colonel; D. R. Anthony, Lieutenant Colonel; and Thomas P. Her- 
rick, Major. Colonel Jennison was removed from office on charges 
of misconduct.°? The Eighth Kansas Volunteer Infantry was re- 
cruited in accordance with instructions given to Governor Robinson 
by General Fremont. It was intended for home and frontier service 
and was organized in November, 1861, with H. W. Weessels, Colonel ; 
John A. Martin, Lieutenant Colonel; and E. F. Schneider, Major. 
The Ninth Regiment was the Kansas Volunteer Cavalry. It was 
organized on the 27th of March, 1862, and was assembled at Fort

Leavenworth. Edward Lynde of Grasshopper Fails was appointed 
Colonel, Charles S. Clarke of Iola, Lieutenant Colonel, and J. M. 
Pomeroy, Major.** The Tenth Regiment was the Kansas Volunteer 
Infantry. As previously stated, it was composed of the Third and 
Fourth Kansas Regiments and a small part of the Fifth. Its organ- 
ization was completed the 3d of April, 1862, and it was assembled at 
Paola, Kansas. Colonel William F. Cloud of Emporia, Lieutenant 
Colonel Henry Williams of Osawatomie, and Major Otis B. Gunn 
were the major officers. The remaining three regiments were raised 
under the President’s call of July 2d, 1862. Three regiments of in- 
fantry were called for. Lane was authorized to recruit them. He 
empowered Thomas Ewing, Jr., to raise the Eleventh, Charles W. 
Adams the Twelfth, and Cyrus Leland the Thirteenth. The major 
officers of the Eleventh were Thomas Ewing, Jr., Thomas Moon- 
light, and Preston B. Plumb. The Twelfth Regiment did boundary 
service. Its major officers were Charles W. Lawrence, Lane’s 
brother-in-law, J. E. Hayes of Olathe, and Thomas H. Kennedy of 
Lawrence. The Thirteenth Regiment was assembled at Camp Stan- 
ton, near Atchison. Thomas M. Bowen was appointed Colonel; 
J. B. Wheeler of Troy, Lieutenant Colonel; and Caleb W. Wood- 
worth of Atchison, Major. 
The quarrel over the appointment of military officers reached a 
climax in August, 1862, in connection with commissioning the of- 
ficers of the Eleventh, Twelfth, and Thirteenth Regiments. On the 
18th, Lane wrote Stanton, Secretary of War, that Governor Robin- 
son had intimated that while he was willing to approve of the com- 
pany officers elected by the men composing the companies he re- 
served the right to designate the Field and Staff officers of the regi- 
ments. General Lane, therefore, asked the Secretary for instruc- 
tions. In reply Stanton wrote: “You are directed to report the 
names of the officers selected to the department. The Governor will 
be requested by the Department to commission them. Upon his 
refusal the President will issue commissions.” 9® On the 20th of 
August, Governor Robinson sent Major T. B. Eldridge to interview 
the Secretary of War, Edwin M. Stanton, to ascertain the “true 
condition of affairs in this department,” and give the War Depart- 
ment information about the irregular transactions of the recruiting 
service in Kansas. The message he bore read in part:

Genl. Lane claims full power over recruiting in Kansas. He ignores the 
State Government, has had no communication with it and is as hostile to it 
as Jeff Davis is to the Federal Government. Having heard nothing upon the 
subject of recruiting from the War Department I desire to learn precisely what 
authority Genl. Lane possesses and what is desired of the State Executive 
if anything. I have been informed that I am reported to the War Depart- 
ment as disloyal and this may account for the silence of that department 
relative to Genl. Lane’s mission and authority. I believe I have organized 
more troops according to population and at less cost to the government than 
any other Governor. I have endeavored to do everything in my power to aid 
the government in subdueing this whole rebellion and know not why this 
State should be regarded as disloyal. No Governor, I dare say, has been so 
harrassed, annoyed, and insulted as I have been by Genl. Lane and Genl. 
Blunt.97 
He asked Stanton, therefore, to remove the two generals from 
power. 
On the 28th of August, Major Eldridge wrote Governor Robinson 
that he had had the interview with the Secretary of War and from 
it he concluded it was evident that “the Lord (of the Department) 
is not on our side,” that Lane had received full authority to recruit. 
As to the authority to commission officers the Secretary of War had 
informed him that Lane had his instructions and Governor Robin- 
son had “the law on the subject and ought to Wnderstand it.” Stan- 
ton had refused to commit himself on this subject, his excuse being 
that it would draw him into the Robinson-Lane quarrel.°® On the 
same day Secretary Stanton wrote Governor Robinson that he was 
sending him a copy of the letter to General Lane granting authority 
for recruiting volunteers in Kansas. The Secretary wrote Robin- 
son: “The extent of that authority and how far the action of Gen- 
eral Lane comports with it you can judge as well as anyone else.” 
The letter to Lane, dated July 22, 1862, contained the appointment 
of Lane as Commissioner “for recruiting in the Department of 
Kansas,” requested him “to raise and organize one or more Brigades 
of Volunteer Infantry to be mustered into the service of the United 
States for three years or during the War,” and granted him “full 
authority . . . to establish camps, and provide for the main- 
tenance of discipline and the supply of the troops with the muni- 
tions of war.” °° The Secretary of War concluded the letter to 
Robinson with an expression of regret that there was ill feeling be- 
tween “the Executive of Kansas and the Military Commander and

General Lane, at a time when all men should be united in their 
efforts against the enemy.” 1° The statement greatly irritated the 
Governor and he retorted with an eight-page letter in which he re- 
ferred to Lane and Blunt as traitors to the State government and 
recounted their sins. General Lane, he wrote, had been “sent here 
to usurp the functions of Governor and dictator of Kansas.” He 
had waged an unceasing warfare against the State Executive, at- 
tempting to bring about the Governor’s removal from office through 
an election and the courts, one year before the term expired. Fail- 
ing in this Lane had attempted his removal by impeachment, “grossly 
perjuring himself in order to effect his object.” Lane had procured ~ 
the appointment of General Blunt as Brigadier General for the pur- 
pose of insulting every commissioned officer in the service of Kan- 
sas. After Blunt’s appointment “his assignment was procured to 
this Department as an insult to the State Executive, his hostility to 
it being well known.” General Lane had used his authority to 
recruit soldiers for political purposes and at the expense of the 
Federal government. ‘The grossest misrepresentations were made 
to procure enlistments, and for political effect. . . . The Exec- 
utive of the State was denounced as a traitor and threatened with 
arrest.””’ General Lane had claimed authority to draft whomever 
he pleased and when he pleased, thus inducing large numbers to 
enlist as volunteers who would not have enlisted and whose families 
suffered great distress thereby. Lane had represented “that the 
recruits would be permitted to engage in promiscuous robbery and 
plunder on their own account,” and had said “he had blank com- 
missions signed by the President for all officers to new regiments. 
: He appointed recruiting Commissioners and officers to re- 
cruit colored Regiments. . . . This is being done in Kansas by 
the agent of the Secy. of War while the President is represented 
throughout the Country as having forbidden it absolutely.” 
Of General Blunt, Robinson wrote that as soon as the General 
took command he replaced all officers in the regiments with Lane 
men, and was aided in this by the Secretary of War. He refused to 
muster officers appointed to fill vacancies unless they were satisfac- 
tory to him. “If Genl. Blunt wants the assistance of any portion of 
the State Militia he makes no application to either the Commander 
in Chief or any other Militia officers authorized to command, but 
issues orders directly to the rank and file.” Governor Robinson 
closed the letter with these words:

Robinson, Aug. 28, 1862. 


## Page 164

156 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
The above are a few of the transactions in this department but they give 
only a faint idea of the despotism and terrorism attempted and practiced here 
by men who openly boast that the State Executive is repudiated by them and 
the War Department and yet you say “if any way appears by which the 
Department could establish harmony and unity of action it would be promptly 
pursued.” What would you have me do? Shall I quietly submit to see this 
State insulted in every manner possible and favor the parties giving the insult. 
Shall I say to the people who elected Genl. Lane U. S. Senator and myself 
Governor that Genl. Lane is both Senator and Governor by consent. I readily 
concede that you have the power to subvert for the time being the State 
Government of Kansas, but you have not the power to get my approval of 
the act. The people of Kansas will shed their last drop of blood for the 
preservation of their government, but their reserved rights and their State 
Government they hold as dear as they do the Federal Government, and they 
look upon the power that would overthrow and subvert their State Govern- 
ment with as little favor as upon the rebellion that would overthrow National 
Government. 
Kansas has furnished her quota of troops most cheerfully for the support 
of the Government and she will furnish the last man in the State if needed 
and all she asks in return is that her own local government may be respected 
and that the rebellion be speedily crushed.1° 
Governor Robinson was pleading not only for justice, but for the 
safeguarding of the rights of the State against encroachments by 
the Federal Government. 
When the rosters of the field, staff and company officers of the 
regiments were presented to Governor Robinson, October 4, he 
refused to recognize them. He agreed to approve the company of- 
ficers selected by the soldiers, but reserved the right to designate 
the field and staff officers. This authority he said was vested in 
the Governor. Moreover, he had good reasons to believe that many 
of the officers on the list had never been elected by the soldiers and 
some were not qualified to serve.!°* When Secretary of War Stan- 
ton asked Governor Robinson to grant the commissions he replied: 
“While he [Stanton] had the power to override the Constitution and 
laws, he had not the power to make the Governor of Kansas dis- 
honor his own State.” 1° To avoid the issue, Governor Robinson 
referred the matter to Major General S. R. Curtis, commanding the 
Department of Missouri. On May 4, 1861, the War Department 
had issued an order that the commissioned officers of a company 
should be appointed by the Governor of the State which furnishes

the regiment. It seems that the officers recommended by Lane, how-

It is now questionable whether President Lincoln ever approved 
of the military interference by General Lane and Secretary of War 
Stanton in the affairs of the Kansas Governor. When Lane con- 
tinued this practice of forcing regimental officers upon the Governor 
and carried the practice into Governor Carney’s administration, the 
Governor voiced his complaint to the President. Whereupon Lincoln 
wrote Lane, April 27, 1863: 
The Governor of Kansas is here asking that Lieutenant-Colonel J. M. 
Williams, of a colored regiment there, shall be removed; and also complaining 
of the military interference of General Blunt in the late election at Leaven- 
worth. I do not know how, if at all, you are connected with these things; but 
I wish your assistance to so shape things that the Governor of Kansas may be 
treated with the consideration that is extended to governors of other States. 
We are not forcing a regimental officer upon any other governor against his 
protest. Cannot this matter be somehow adjusted? 104* 
It is possible that if Governor Robinson had carried his grievances 
to the President rather than to the Secretary of War some of the 
trouble could have been avoided. 
The Federal draft law and its application to Kansas had been 
misinterpreted. In his anxiety to fill the Eleventh, Twelfth, and 
Thirteenth Regiments, General Lane had left the Kansas citizens 
under the impression that the law applied to them, and threatened 
to force them to join the army. The Conservative warned the 
people that the Secretary of War had ordered that ‘Any person 
liable to draft, who should absent himself from his county or State 
before such draft is made, will be arrested” and conveyed to the 
nearest military post, placed on military duty, and compelled to 
pay the expenses of his arrest plus $5 as a reward to the officer who 
made the arrest.1°° The Secretary of War had ordered the Gover- 
nors of the several States to furnish their quotas of the 300,000 
militia called for by the order of the President, August 4, 1862. 
Governor Robinson stated that he had received no such order from 
the War Department, that no quota had been assigned to Kansas, 
and no draft had been ordered from the State.1°* This statement 
is substantiated by the words of the Secretary of War, Stanton, re- 
ported to Robinson by Eldridge, August 28: “I have not called

on.” When Eldridge asked the Secretary of War: “Then why is 
Genl. Lane authorized to raise troops from Kansas?” Stanton re- 
plied: ‘Well, because he said he could raise two or more brigades 
at once and I consented.” 197 Sol Miller wrote that the Federal 
Government had not intended to call upon Kansas for a single man 
under the levy of August 4, 1862, until it had been solicited to do so. 
He regarded the impressment of soldiers as an outrage upon the 
dearest rights of the people. Men had been forced into the army at 
the point of the bayonet or the muzzle of the revolver. It was done, 
he asserted, “‘to fill up the companies to the required standard which 
could not be filled up otherwise.” 1° On the 8th of October, Senator 
Pomeroy spoke in Atchison and expressed his fear that Kansas was 
making too great sacrifices. Out of a voting population of sixteen 
thousand, upwards of ten thousand had enlisted for the term of 
three years. Crops had been left in the fields, homes had been deso- 
lated, and families were suffering for the necessaries of life. He 
said that as soon as the last call for more troops had been issued he 
had called at the War Department and was informed “that no draft 
would be applied to Kansas. I therefore say-it was wrong for any 
one to deceive the citizens of this State with threats of a draft.” 19 
The Kansas State Journal wrote that “with one-third of her popu- 
lation begging charity of the East, it is more than ridiculous—it is 
criminal, for Kansas to turn out nearly four men in proportion to 
her population for this war, to one furnished by the old, rich and 
self-helping Empire State.” It questioned whether more than one 
regiment would have been asked of Kansas if her ambitious political 
leaders and military chieftains had not, “for solely selfish ends, bad- 
gered the President and War Department, into a reluctant accept- 
ance of more.’ 11° 
Not satisfied with mustering the white Kansas citizens into serv- 
ice, General Lane proceeded to recruit the Africans likewise. Con- 
gress had passed a militia act, July 17, 1862, which provided that 
slaves of Southern sympathizers should become free upon rendering 
military service in the Union army. It gave the President full dis- 
cretion in the enlistment of Negroes.444 General Lane informed the

Secretary of War, August 5th, that Kansas would furnish two regi-

that the Secretary of War had made no reply. On the 23d of 
August, however, Stanton wrote Lane: “Regiments of persons of 
African descent can only be raised upon express and special au-

as saying to Major Eldridge: “Lane nor anyone else has any au- 
thority to raise Negro Regiments. This question of the Negro busi- 
ness has not been determined on.” As late as the 23d of September, 
1862, the Secretary of War wrote General Lane: “You are not au- 
thorized to organize Indians, nor any but loyal white men.” 1? 
The Leavenworth Conservative and the Washington Dispatch re- 
ported that the President had objected to Negro troops on the ground 
that they would endanger rather than help the cause of the Union. 
If they are accepted “at least 40,000 soldiers would leave the army 
and the Border States would secede.” 43 In spite of the President’s 
public objection, however, the Kansas colored regiments were or- 
ganized. General Lane appointed Captain J. H. Williams of the 
Fifth Kansas Cavalry to enlist Negroes north of the Kansas River 
and Captain H. C. Seaman for the Southern District. Captain 
Montgomery wrote that when they found it difficult “to induce 
Negroes to enlist,” they had resorted to the draft. There is evi- 
dence that some of the Jayhawkers crossed the border into Missouri 
to recruit Negroes for Lane’s Brigade.''4 The organization of the 
First Kansas Colored Voluntary Infantry was completed May 2, 
1863. The major officers were J. M. Williams, Colonel; John 
Bowles, Lieutenant Colonel; and Richard G. Ward, Major. The 
Second Kansas Colored Voluntary Infantry was organized in Octo- 
ber, 1863.15 According to Stephenson, however, the two colored 
regiments were combined and mustered into the United States serv- 
ice, January 13, 1863. 
The Secretary of War had ordered the organization of two Indian 
Regiments prior to May 4, 1862, according to a statement attributed 
to General Blunt who assisted in organizing the Indian Regiments. 
This statement is challenged, however, by the letter of Stanton to 
Lane dated September 23, 1862, already quoted, which said that

Lane was “not. authorized to organize Indians, nor any but loyal 
white men.” 146 The Indian troops were recruited, nevertheless, ac- 
cording to James G. Blunt, from the loyal Cherokee, Creek, and 
Seminole Indians in Kansas. The Confederate Government had 
made overtures to the Cherokees, Creeks, Seminoles, Choctaws, and 
Chickasaws, promising them that if they would help drive the 
settlers out of Kansas they could have their Kansas lands restored.1* 
The military enthusiasm of Kansas exceeded that of any north- 
ern State. The Government expected a contribution of 12,931 
soldiers from Kansas during the Civil War, whereas the State 
actually furnished 20,151, and nearly all for three years. The mili- 
tary history of the Kansas soldier has been discussed at great 
length by Andreas and W. E. Connelley and will be omitted here. 
Although the Lane-Robinson military controversy was concerned 
mainly in the control of the military patronage, it also involved the 
military organization itself. It was evident that the faction which 
organized the regiments would also control the military contracts. 
The Leavenworth Daily Conservative characterized the issue in 
these words: 
The Topeka Record has lately stated that Gov. Robinson was working with 
the capitalists in St. Louis to prevent the creation of a Military Department 
in Kansas. St. Louis wants to prevent the creation of a Military Department 
in Kansas. St. Louis wants the contracts and the furnishing of supplies, and 
fears Leavenworth as a rival. Robinson has joined hands with the jobbers to 
defeat Lane as a Major General. So long as these arguments were urged as 
bearing upon the State Capital question we paid no attention to them. Now 
we should like to know the truth.118 
Of the Kansas towns, Leavenworth must have gotten the lion’s 
share of the military trade. The Kansas military conflicts were 
also entangled with the impeachment of the State officers. The 
struggle over impeachment will be considered next.

## Page 169

Chapter VIII 
Impeachment of the State Officers, 1862 
HORTLY after the admission of Kansas to the Union in 1861, it 
became necessary for the legislature to issue bonds to help carry 
the expenses of the government. The first State legislature, there- 
fore, voted to make two issues, $150,000 in seven percent bonds and 
$20,000 in ten percent bonds. The bill calling for the seven percent 
bonds had originally provided for $250,000 but the Committee on 
Finance and Taxation had reduced it to $150,000. In this form it 
passed the legislature. The Governor signed it and the bill became 
a law on the 3d of May, 1861.1 
The bill authorized Messrs. Austin M. Clark and James C. Stone 
of Leavenworth to negotiate the sale of the bonds. These consti- 
tuted an obligation of the State, which were to be paid in fifteen 
years from the date of issue. Clark and Stone were ordered to 
inform the legislature on what terms they could sell the bonds, 
which were to be in denominations of not less than $500, with coupons 
attached. The bill also provided for an annual tax levy to pay the 
interest, and another levy high enough to create an adequate sinking 
fund for the redemption of the bonds.? 
Since Messrs. Stone and Clark had failed to negotiate the sale of 
the bonds within the seventy-day period allotted, the legislature 
passed another bill supplementary to the seven percent act of May 
third. Effective as of the 7th of June, 1861, the supplementary act 
gave power to the Governor, Secretary of State, and Auditor, or a 
majority of them, to sell $100,000 of the State bonds for not less 
than 70 cents on the dollar. It provided also that the Treasurer of 
the State should prepare bonds to the amount of $100,000, with 
coupons attached, due in fifteen years, bearing interest at the rate of 
seven percent per annum, payable semiannually. 
The State had difficulty in selling the bonds, and not without 
reason. In the first place, conditions in the country at large were

(161) 
11—14 


## Page 170

162 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
unfavorable. When President Lincoln took office, March 4, 1861, 
he found “the treasury practically empty, the administrative depart- 
ments disorganized, customs receipts almost at a standstill, the debt 
increasing, and government credit ebbing away.” * Several factors 
had worked to produce these conditions. The panic of 1857 was 
still affecting business conditions in general. The sources of income 
of the Federal Government were derived chiefly from the sale of 
public lands and tariff duties; yet the tariff of 1857 had lowered 
many of the duties and enlarged the free list. This had helped to 
produce the economic distress. Moreover, the election of a Republi- 
can President, Abraham Lincoln, had caused the Southern banks to 
withdraw large amounts of money on deposit in Northern banks; 
it had caused loans to be contracted, and produced another panic, 
forcing the Government to issue more Treasury notes. The public 
credit was so low in December of 1860, and the public mind so 
disturbed, that it was necessary to pay from ten to twelve percent 
interest to float the notes at par.5 Furthermore, the outbreak of the 
war had caused the Missouri State sixes to drop from 694% on Jan- 
uary 18, to 3714 on June 12, 1861.6 Added to the distressing condi- 
tions in the United States in general, was the hazard involved in the 
purchase of Kansas bonds. In the first place, the famine of 1860- 
1861 which had brought business in Kansas practically to a stand- 
still, had also impaired the State’s financial credit. Hyatt’s articles 
begging for money and relief goods for starving Kansas were still 
fresh in the minds of Eastern bankers. In the second place, the fact 
that Missouri, the eastern neighbor of Kansas, was a border state, 
reacted unfavorably upon the credit of Kansas. Although it is now 
held that Missouri was practically saved for the Union by the defeat 
of General Sterling Price on August 10, 1861, at the battle of Wil- 
son’s Creek, yet it was not until midwinter of 1862 that Price was 
actually driven out of southwest Missouri.? During the time, there- 
fore, when the State officers of Kansas were trying to negotiate the 
sale of bonds, the financial credit of Kansas was hanging in the bal- 
ance; for, had Missouri seceded, Kansas would have been overrun 
by the Confederate armies. Moreover, the California State seven 
percent bonds were listed on the New York Stock Exchange, June 7 ; 
1861, at only 7634 cents on the dollar; and yet California had been

a State for ten years and had an established credit record whereas 
Kansas was a new State without a credit record and with the odds 
against her. In the face of these facts, however, the Kansas legis- 
lature, on June 7, 1861, prohibited the sale of the Kansas State sevens 
for less than seventy cents on the dollar. 
Several fruitless attempts were made to sell the bonds. Before the 
acts had passed the legislature, the Senate asked the Governor to 
telegraph some prominent banking house in New York and find out 
whether the Kansas bonds would be worth the “price of like bonds of 
other northern States.” The Governor telegraphed “Duncan Sher- 
man & Company,” bankers of New York, on April 20, and they re- 
plied: “Do not think they could be negotiated at present.”® Twenty 
days latter the Governor notified the legislature of the failure of 
Clark and Stone to sell the bonds. The report was dated May 10, 
1861, and stated: ‘We have taken every means in our power to as- 
certain whether the bonds could be sold, and if so, at what price, 
and we are satisfied that at this time it would be useless to attempt 
ete 
The Treasurer, H. R. Dutton, now tried to dispose of the bonds. 
He attempted to sell the war bonds to Messrs. A. M. Clark and 
J. C. Stone of Leavenworth and to Joseph Mann of Pennsylvania, 
but failed. About the time of the battle of Bull Run he was in New 
York, but the banks even refused the ten percent war bonds.!° On 
his way home he stopped at Buffalo, N. Y., where he met R. S. 
Stevens, who was on his way to Washington. Stevens bought $31,000 
of the war bonds at 40 cents on the dollar and took $29,000 of the 
seven percent bonds with him. He gave Dutton a receipt for them. 
This happened July 31, 1861, when the Missouri sixes were selling 
at 4114.11 After this transaction Dutton returned to Kansas and 
Stevens left for Washington. Here Stevens sold $26,000 of the war 
bonds at 95 cents on the dollar to Caleb B. Smith, Secretary of the 
Interior. Smith, however, refused the seven percent bonds.’? The 
one transaction had netted Stevens $14,300. He now became in- 
terested in more Kansas bonds. 
Robert S. Stevens was a Democrat and a business friend of Gov- 
ernor Robinson. During Buchanan’s administration he had been 
under contract to build houses for the Sac and Fox Indians. When

the Kansas Senators arrived in Washington the agent of the Sac

elected to the State Senate. He had practiced law, was a director 
and stockholder of the Lawrence Bank, possessed business acumen, 
and seemed to know his way about in Washington.* He had 
moved from Lecompton to Lawrence. 
After the transaction with the Secretary of the Interior, Stevens 
returned to Kansas by way of Dayton, Ohio, where he conferred 
with Robert G. Corwin, a resident of Dayton. Mr. Corwin, a 
brother-in-law of Caleb B. Smith, was an attorney at law, and was 
a claims agent at Washington, doing business with the War and the 
Interior Departments. At this meeting Corwin informed Stevens 
that the seven percent bonds could be sold to the Secretary of the 
Interior, provided he would modify his proposition as to price. 
Thereupon Stevens “wrote a new proposition” the details of which 
are unknown, gave what bonds he had to Corwin, and returned to 
Kansas, where he arrived in early October.1* After this interview 
Stevens and Corwin worked together. 
While in Kansas Stevens interviewed the State officers on the 
bonds. The testimony regarding the interview is indefinite, but it 
appears that at this time the Secretary of State and Auditor were 
of the opinion that they had a right to sell $50,000 of the seven per- 
cent bonds at 40 cents on the dollar and the $100,000, covered by 
the Supplementary Act, at 70 cents on the dollar. They reasoned 
that the $50,000 not covered by the Supplementary Act could be 
sold below 70 cents on the dollar, and they arranged with Stevens 
to sell $50,000 of the seven percent bonds at 40 cents and about 
$37,000 at 70 cents. When the proposition was presented to the 
Governor for his signature he refused to sign it, although he “be- 
lieved it good policy for the State to sell them for even less than 
sixty cents if they could not get more.’’ Nevertheless, before they 
parted the State officers gave Stevens $29,000 of the seven percent 
bonds in the 500 denomination and all the bonds in the 100 denom- 
ination that would not be needed to pay up scrip. The Governor 
signed them and Stevens sent them to Washington by Dr. Wood- 
ward to be delivered to Corwin. Following that Stevens left for 
southern Kansas.1¢

At this juncture Senator Pomeroy wrote Secretary J. W. Robin- 
son that there was a chance to sell the State bonds, and asked Robin- 
son to send him all the bonds he possessed.17 Pomeroy had gathered 
his information from an interview with the Secretary of the In- 
terior. This stirred Robinson and Hillyer to action. They gathered 
all the State bonds they could obtain from private holders and 
prepared to leave for Washington. Robinson left on the 28th and 
Hillyer on the 29th of October. Robinson stopped at Chicago to 
visit friends and arrived in Washington the 8th of November, four 
days later than Hillyer.5 When Hillyer arrived he sought an in- 
terview with Senator Pomeroy. It seems that Pomeroy now re- 
fused to confide in Hillyer and told him that it would be impossible 
to effect negotiations with the Department of the Interior, that Com- 
missioner William P. Dole had just returned from the West and 
was opposed to investing Indian Trust money in Kansas bonds, and 
that he believed “the funds used in this were nearly exhausted.” 
Dole had promised, however, to find out how much Indian money 
was available, and a few days later he informed Pomeroy that 
there was sufficient money “to take all the Kansas bonds, but it 
was doubtful if the money would be so invested,’”’ Pomeroy advised 
that the negotiations be left in the hands of Stevens who should be 
made the agent of the State.19 
Hillyer took the advice of Pomeroy and telegraphed Stevens to 
come to Washington. By this time J. W. Robinson had arrived and 
he signed the telegram with Hillyer. Also, they sent a letter to 
Stevens who received their communication while in southern Kansas. 
Stevens left the State on the 23d or 24th of November and arrived 
at Washington on the 30th.2° On Sunday, December Ist, he met 
Robinson and Hillyer and on Monday he met Robert G. Corwin. 
Corwin knew the mind of the Secretary of the Interior and advised 
Stevens to ask the State officers for a contract which would author- 
ize him to negotiate the sale of the bonds. In the evening Stevens, 
Corwin, and the State officers went to see Pomeroy. They found 
him and Mrs. Pomeroy entertaining several men. After they had 
finished with the preliminary conversation, Pomeroy, Stevens and 
Corwin withdrew and left the State officers, J. W. Robinson and 
Hillyer, with Mrs. Pomeroy in the parlor. Corwin later testified 
that they consulted about the propriety of selling the entire issue of

the $150,000. They wanted Stevens to get in or buy in all the bonds 
of the State, including the $68,000 that had already been sold, and 
sell the entire issue to the Government. Stevens agreed to do this.”* 
The point of interest here is that Stevens and Pomeroy refused to 
confide in the State officers; which may explain why Pomeroy left 
Hillyer under a false impression as to the Department of Interior’s 
willingness to purchase Kansas bonds. It appears that at this secret 
meeting were gathered the three men who planned to defraud the 
State and divide the spoils of the contemplated negotiations. That 
evening they must have agreed upon the method of procedure, for 
when they returned to the parlor they were ready to draw up the 
agreement with the State officers whom they would use as tools to 
play the game. The contract was antedated as of October 25, 1861, 
and the place designated as the office of the State Auditor, Topeka, 
Kansas. The agreement read as follows: 
The undersigned, Executive officers of this State, authorized by law to dis- 
pose of, and sell, the seven percent bonds, . . . do hereby constitute and 
appoint Robert S. Stevens, Esq., an agent to sell and dispose of said Bonds, 
giving him, the said Stevens, full power and authority to negotiate, dispose of, 
and sell the entire issue of said $150,000 of said Bonds, for the benefit of the 
State of Kansas, hereby ratifying . . . all .-.. . and whatever said 
Stevens may do in the premises.22 
The agreement bore the signatures of Charles Robinson, Governor; 
J. W. Robinson, Secretary of State; and George S. Hillyer, Auditor 
of State. Secretary Robinson had signed the Governor’s name.?8 
The State officers had discussed the matter with the Governor be- 
fore they left for Washington. One of them later testified that the 
Governor had made the statement “he would consent to any ar- 
rangement that could be made, to sell the bonds.” The Governor 
testified, however, that he had not authorized anybody to use his 
official signature, but had told the State officers that “any arrange- 
ment for the sale of bonds that they might make according to law 
[he] would approve of.”24 The first statement would implicate the 
Governor, the second would clear him. Which one dare we trust? 
The agreement had been drawn up by Stevens. Hillyer later ad- 
mitted that it had been drawn up on the 3d of December.” It prob- 
ably was antedated to give the Federal officers the impression that

Having received full authority to negotiate the sale of bonds, 
Stevens offered them to the Secretary of the Interior subject to 
this communication: “Having been duly appointed an agent, for 
the purpose of negotiating and selling the bonds of the State of Kan- 
sas . . . JI hereby propose to sell to you, as Trustee of certain 
Indian Tribes, the entire issue of bonds . . . being one hundred 
and fifty thousand dollars, at eighty-five cents on the Dollar. I 
enclose my authority to sell said bonds, as given me by State offi- 
cera * 
The negotiations now proceeded more rapidly. Robert Corwin 
and Senator Pomeroy acquainted the Secretary of the Interior with 
the proposition. On the 7th of December, the Commissioner of the 
Indian Office, William P. Dole, approved the purchase. A few days 
previous Senator Pomeroy had represented Commissioner Dole to 
Auditor Hillyer as opposing the purchase of Kansas bonds. On the 
7th of December, however, Dole wrote: 
I feel free to state that in my judgment the prospects of Kansas as a young, 
growing and vigorous State, abounding in all the elements of future prosperity 
are such as to warrant the belief that her stocks bearing 7 percent interest, and 
purchased at the rate proposed by Mr. Stevens as Agent for Kansas viz.: at 
“Highty-five cents on the dollar” must in the end be a safe and profitable in- 
vestment.27 
Commissioner Dole testified that the first person to approach him 
on the proposition had been the Secretary of the Interior, December 
7. If that is true then Senator Pomeroy had misrepresented the 
facts to Auditor Hillyer in saying that Commissioner Dole had op- 
posed the investment. Either in pretense or in earnest the Secretary 
of the Interior, however, still appeared to be unconvinced that the 
investment would be safe. On the 14th of December, he wrote the 
President that the United States was holding in trust $40,000 for the 
Kaskaskia Indians and $15,000 for the Iowas which according to 
the treaties of 1854 the President might invest in safe and profitable 
stock. He asked the opinion of the President as to the propriety of 
investing the money in Kansas bonds which the State authorities 
offered to sell at 85 cents. It was his opinion that the bonds “would 
form a safe investment for the Indians.’””° It appears that Stevens

and some of the State officers had also brought the question to the 
attention of the President. The President’s answer was in the affirm- 
ative, provided the Kansas delegation would acquiesce in the sale 
and the Secretary of the Interior thought it a safe investment.”? 
Since the Secretary had already expressed his opinion there remained 
only the first condition to be met, the approval by the Kansas dele- 
gation. To expedite matters Corwin drew up a letter addressed to 
the President approving the sale of the bonds and asked the Kan- 
sas delegation in Congress to sign it. Pomeroy and Conway signed 
at once. Lane, however, refused to sign, not because he opposed 
the sale as in violation of the bond act, but because it would be un- 
wise to put money into the hands of R. 8. Stevens before a Sena- 
torial election. Lane’s lieutenants, J. C. Stone, John C. Vaughan, 
T. C. Weed, Champion Vaughan, and others had warned him to be 
very cautious in the matter since there might possibly be an election 
for United States Senator in Kansas, and Stevens had been men- 
tioned as a possible candidate. It would be imprudent to give his 
competitor money to play with during the election, especially since 
they figured that Lane had but a majority of one over Stevens in 
the Senate.2° Whether that was the ostensible or the real reason, 
Stevens knew Lane’s weakness for money and engaged Mr. George 
A. Reynolds in a private conversation. Reynolds was Lane’s private 
secretary and business manager, and Lane had procured his election 
to the Kansas legislature. By paying Reynolds $1,000, Stevens was 
able to get Lane’s signature to the letter.*1_ Lane at first denied he 
had signed the letter and characterized the signature as a rank for- 
gery, but admitted later that he might have signed the paper without 
reading it, as the signature bore a very close likeness to his own. 
That General Lane had signed the letter was testified by Auditor 
Hillyer, who had secured Lane’s signature, also by Messrs. Stevens, 
Corwin, Caleb B. Smith, and Pomeroy. Even George A. Reynolds 
remembered having heard Lane tell Hillyer that he would sign the 
letter.3? 
The letter stated that the State legislature had duly authorized the 
issue of the seven percent bonds, that they had been duly executed 
and were now held by the State for sale. It stated further that R. S. 
Stevens was the “duly authorized agent of the State of Kansas to

General Lane asked the Secretary of the Interior to send the 
money to the State Treasurer under the direction of Pomeroy and 
Conway. Pomeroy and Conway had advised the Secretary, how- 
ever, to pay the money to Stevens, which he did. R.S. Stevens and 
the Secretary of the Interior now concluded the negotiations. The 
agreement was dated December 19, 1861, and read: 
It is agreed between the United States, by C. B. Smith, Secretary of the 
Interior, and the State of Kansas, by R. S. Stevens, Agent of the State, duly 
appointed to sell the bonds of the said State, that the United States purchase 
the Bonds of said State, dated July 1st, 1861, and payable in fifteen years in 
the State of New York, with interest at the rate of seven percent per annum, 
payable semi-annually in New York, to the amount of one hundred and fifty 
thousand dollars, and which is the whole amount of the issue of said bonds, 
and which bonds are to be paid for at the rate of eighty-five percent of their 
par value. Ninety-five thousand six hundred dollars of said bonds are delivered 
to the Secretary of the Interior at this time, and he has paid to the State of 
Kansas, on account of the same, the sum of $55,000, and no further sum is to 
be paid until the residue of said bonds shall be delivered, when the balance of 
the purchase money shall be paid.34 
The agreement between the State officers and Stevens, signed 
December 3d but bearing the date of October 25, had made Stevens 
the agent of the State. While it did not specify that he was to pur- 
chase the bonds from the State, yet it had given him “full power 
and authority to negotiate, dispose of, and sell the entire issue 
of said . . . bonds.” From all indications Stevens never ap- 
proached the State officers with an offer to purchase the bonds until 
he was positive he could sell them to the Secretary of the Interior. 
They had talked about selling a part of the seven percent bonds at 
40 cents par value and the rest at 70 cents while still in Kansas, yet 
according to the testimony of Stevens and Governor Robinson, the 
Governor had refused the proposition as illegel. After that inter- 
view, nothing more had been done until the State officers reached 
Washington. This statement is based on the correspondence be- 
tween Secretary J. W. Robinson and his assistant Secretary Weir.*° 
According to the information available, it appears that the State 
officers and Stevens had reached an agreement on the sale price of

54, Lotd ap. Vide 
35. Daily State Record, Feb. 12, 1862, and Impeachment Proceedings, p. 226, 227. They 
contain letters dated Dec. 10 and 18, 1861. See, also, Stevens’ testimony, p. 262.

the bonds December 18. This information is based on Secretary 
Robinson’s letter to Weir of December 18 and Stevens’ testimony. 
Stevens testified that a day or two before he had entered into con- 
tract with Caleb B. Smith he had told the officers to decide upon 
the price he was to account for to them if the sale was made. The 
testimony continued: 
They said seventy cents. I told them I would not pay over sixty cents. 
[Then] one of them proposed sixty-five cents, which I declined. We 
then returned to our Hotels. . . . On the same day, Mr. Hillyer came 
to my room and said that the Secretary would not take sixty cents. I replied, 
“very well, let the trade drop then,’ . . . he left, with the understanding 
that the negotiations were ended. That evening they came to my room, and 
said that they had concluded to take sixty cents. I drew a contract, allowing 
me all I could get over sixty cents.36 
According to the testimony of Messrs. Pomeroy, Hillyer, and 
J. W. Robinson, Senator Pomeroy had advised the State officers to 
accept 60 cents. Hillyer wrote he had told Pomeroy that the law 
limited them to 70 cents, yet in spite of that Pomeroy had advised 
the acceptance of the offer at 60. Hillyer continued: ‘My informa- 
tion, as well as knowledge and belief, was that, if we failed to sell 
to the Government, we lost our only chance; according to our judg- 
ment and that of Senator Pomeroy, this was our only chance.” 37 
Both he and Secretary Robinson asserted that they did not know 
Mr. Stevens was getting 85 cents on the dollar. This testimony was 
never disproved in the impeachment proceedings. Furthermore, even 
Senators Pomeroy and Lane asserted that they did not know about 
the terms, which of course is incredible. The whole mess caused the 
prosecuting attorney, W. R. Wagstaff, to remark that the “State 
officers were either fools or knaves.” He implied, of course, that 
they were knaves. On the other hand, the attorney for the defense, 
F. P. Stanton, said it was perfectly plain that the State officers were 
made the “innocent dupes of this deep laid scheme.” 38 
In the light of the prices of State and Federal bonds, listed on the 
New York Stock Exchange, December 19, it is difficult to understand 
why the Federal government paid Stevens 85 cents on the dollar, 
when the Missouri State sixes were listed at 8734, the United States 
fives, maturing in 1874, at 7934, the United States sixes, maturing 
in 1867, at 88, and the California sixes (listed the next day), at 78. 
Hither the Federal government was bidding up the Kansas bonds to 
strengthen the new State, or else the Secretary of the Interior and 
36. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 202, 260, 261, 384. Stevens’ testimony.

his assistants were to share in the profits. One point in favor of the 
first explanation is the fact that at the time of the negotiation 
Stevens was represented to the Federal officers as the agent of the 
State, although he had forced the State officers to sell the bonds at 
his price the day before.®9 
When the House committee investigating the bond deal asked 
Stevens to name the parties that had been associated with him in 
the negotiations and shared the profits with him he declined to 
answer, “for the reason that the parties are not residents of the 
State; it was an arrangement between these gentlemen and myself, 
and whatever I paid and agreed to pay came out of my own profits, 
and belonged to me, according to the contract with Robinson and 
Hillyer.”*° When they asked him whether the State officers were 
implicated, he said they could speak for themselves.4! Stevens’ 
statement, that those who shared in the profits were nonresidents of 
Kansas, excluded J. W. Robinson, Hillyer, and Governor Robinson. 
It was admitted, however, that the State officers had personal bonds 
for which they received 70 cents on the dollar; Secretary Robinson 
had $4,400 in face value and Hillyer $2,600.42 Aside from that, 
however, the available evidence points to the conclusion that Sec- 
retary Robinson and Auditor Hillyer did not share in the profits 
derived from the sale of the State bonds. Nor is it reasonable to 
think that Senator Lane, an enemy of Stevens, shared in the profits 
except in so far as Stevens found it necessary to purchase his signa- 
ture to complete the negotiations. 
Governor Robinson disclaimed any knowledge of, or connection 
with, the illegal transaction. The available evidence is in his favor. 
Of course there were reasons for suspecting him. R. 8S. Stevens and 
he were stockholders and directors of the Lawrence Bank whose 
issues were secured by the State bonds of Kansas. The face value of 
the bonds in the bank was estimated at $20,000.4% Moreover, in 
his message to the legislature, January 14, 1862, Governor Robinson 
had dismissed the financial question with the statement: “For the 
financial condition of the State, reference is made to the reports of 
the Auditor, Treasurer and Secretary.” #4 In spite of the fact that 
the principals and agents in the sale of the bonds had returned to

Topeka, he made no mention of the success or failure of their en-
