incident, in February, 1862, cast suspicion on the Governor. The 
White Cloud Chief related it as follows: 
Suit had been ordered by the State to recover from R.S. Stevens the money 
belonging to the State, and his property was about being attached. He had 
been before the Committee, who propounded to him the question whether 
Gov. Robinson had received any of the proceeds of the sale of the bonds. This 
he declined to answer. The Committee permitted him to step out of the 
room, until they consulted with reference to his reply, he promising to return 
when called for. But he did not again make his appearance, and was shortly 
afterward seen in a buggy in company with Gov. Robinson, going full tilt in 
the direction of Lawrence. . . . We cannot say that Robinson and Stevens 
were engaged in anything disreputable, but their running off looks mighty 
suspicious.4® 
On the 6th of March, Sol Miller wrote that the Governor had 
since showed him a letter from General Hunter which explained 
why Robinson left town with Stevens that afternoon. General 
Hunter had requested the Governor’s immediate presence at Leav- 
enworth.*® Whether or not Stevens’ escape with the Governor was 
&@ mere coincidence, at the time it looked suspicious, and the op- 
position made the most of it. The investigating committee of the 
House used it as proof of Governor Robinson’s guilt, and the House 
was sufficiently impressed by it to pass the resolution to impeach the 
State officers. 
Robert G. Corwin of Dayton, Ohio, admitted that he had assisted 
Stevens in the negotiations and had derived some profit from them, 
but hastened to add that it was but very small.47 This leaves 
Senator Pomeroy unaccounted for. 
Pomeroy’s previous and subsequent records make him a suspicious 
character. His management of the relief work in Kansas during 
the years 1860 and 1861 was not to his credit. His subsequent 
record was even worse. In the memory of a few Kansans may still 
live the picture of that dramatic scene of January 29, 1873, when 
S. C. Pomeroy was branded as the instigator of an $8,000 bribe. 
The place was Topeka, and the occasion the election of a United 
States Senator. Pomeroy was running for a third term. His op- 
ponent was John J. Ingalls. The race was close and the situation 
tense. Fifty-eight legislators had pledged in secret caucus their 
45. White Cloud Chief, Feb. 20, 1862. 
46. Ibid., March 6, 1862. 
47. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 150-156, 178 f., deposition of Corwin. 


## Page 181

The Birth of Kansas 173 
vote for Ingalls, but it took sixty-seven to elect. The night before 
the election word went out that Pomeroy’s bitter opponent, A. M. 
York, had switched to his side. According to the Pomeroy ,oli- 
ticians, that would clinch the election. The following day was 
election day. Pomeroy sat in the lobby of the Tefft House while the 
session convened, smoking blissfully in calm anticipation of victory. 
When the Secretary of the Senate called the roll he was interrupted 
by Senator York, who asked for the floor. An expectant hush spread 
over the crowded hall. The Pomeroy supporters smiled; now would 
come the startling switch by the State Senator from Ingalls to 
“Old Pom.” A few Ingalls supporters thought otherwise. Chairman 
Stover yielded the floor and York strode slowly forward. Necks 
were craned to watch the Senator’s every move. As he neared the 
platform he plucked two packages of greenbacks from his pocket, 
flourished the bills before the astonished assemblage, and after the 
preliminary remarks, said heatedly: “Mr. President and gentlemen: 
I visited Mr. Pomeroy’s room in the dark and secret recesses of the 
Tefft House Monday night, and there my vote was bargained for, for 
a consideration of $8,000. Of this $2,000 was paid to me that night, 
$5,000 the next afternoon and I was promised the additional $1,000 
when my vote had been cast in his favor today.” Amid a stillness 
that seemed fairly to shriek, Senator York tossed the bills to the Sec- 
retary and asked him to count them. Meanwhile the crowd leaned 
over in shocked expectancy and York continued his indictment: “[I 
undertook] the deception of acceptance that by this exposé I might 
purge the fair name of Kansas from these conditions and this man 
I promised, in consideration of $7,000 in hand paid to vote 
for him and I here and now redeem that pledge by voting for him a 
term in the penitentiary not. exceeding 20 years.” 48 
And what was Pomeroy’s connection with the negotiations of the 
State bonds? The manner in which the plot was revealed was not 
dramatic as in 1873, but the principle involved in the act was the 
same. Senator Pomeroy was connected with the sale of the bonds 
early in the game, advising the State officers how to proceed. To 
begin with he lured them to Washington, hinting that there was a 
possibility of selling the bonds. Pomeroy advised Hillyer, who 
arrived first, not to negotiate directly with the Secretary of the 
Interior. He next advised him to make R. S. Stevens the trusted 
agent of the State. When Stevens arrived in Washington, Pomeroy, 
Stevens, and Corwin left the State officers with Mrs. Pomeroy in the 
48, Kansas City Star, Jan. 18, 1926. 


## Page 182

174 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
parlor, while they met in another room to complete the details of 
the deep laid scheme. When they returned from the secret meeting 
they presented a contract to the State officers to make Stevens the 
trusted agent of the State with full power and authority to negotiate 
and sell. The Sate officers fell into the trap and signed away their 
authority. The next day Stevens wrote the Secretary of the Interior 
that he would sell the bonds at 85 cents on the dollar. After they 
had convinced the Federal officers that the investment was safe be- 
yond risk, they returned to the State officers and forced them to sell 
the bonds at 60 cents on the dollar, giving themselves 25 cents profit 
for every 60 cents paid the State. When Hillyer told Pomeroy that 
to sell at 60 cents was in violation of the State law, Pomeroy advised 
violation of the law. When Stevens informed the investigating com- 
mittee that the parties who were to share in the profits were “not 
residents” of Kansas, he did not necessarily mean to exclude Pome- 
roy who was living in Washington, although legally a Kansan. 
These facts in themselves point to the conclusion that Stevens, Cor- 
win, and Pomeroy, who met in secret conclave in Senator Pomeroy’s 
room the night of December 2, 1861, were among the villains, if not 
the only ones, who plotted the scheme to defraud the State, and who, 
should the negotiations be successful, would-.divide the profits. A 
letter of Theodore Hyatt to his brother Thaddeus, dated March 7, 
1862, states that Senator Pomeroy advised violation of the law in 
order that he might share the profits with R. S. Stevens. Theodore 
Hyatt wrote: 
Well Pomeroy’s interest in the matter was here that through his influence 
with the Secretary of Interior at Washington the Bonds were sold and for this 
he was to secure a commission which amounted to several thousand dollars 
and this he promised to hand over to me when received.49 
Pomeroy had borrowed $10,000 from the Hyatt brothers (the 
note was dated May 15, 1861, although the money may have been 
borrowed at the time of the senatorial election) and had promised 
them that as soon as he got his commission from the sale of the 
bonds, he would make good a part of it.5° 
By the 20th of December, the negotiations to complete the pur- 
chase had been made. That day Caleb B. Smith notified Commis- 
sioner William P. Dole that he had purchased 246 Kansas bonds at 
85 cents on the dollar, amounting to $54,910. He had made the 
purchase for the benefit of the Confederate tribes of Weas, Kaskas- 
kias, Peorias and Piankeshows, and Ioways. Stevens had delivered 
49. Theodore Hyatt’s Letters, letter of March 7, 1862. 
50. Ibid., letter of July 15, 1861. 


## Page 183

The Birth of Kansas 175 
to the Secretary of the Interior, $95,600 in State bonds for which 
the Secretary would pay approximately $55,000. No more would be 
paid until Stevens presented the rest of the bonds, amounting to 
$54,400. These were in the hands of private parties and it was up 
to Stevens to purchase them at a price low enough to realize a 
profit. This necessitated secrecy, and explains in part the request of 
the negotiators that the sale price be kept a secret. 
The position in which the State found itself with reference to the 
seven percent bonds may now be summarized. Of the $150,000 in 
bonds to be issued, it appears that by this time the State had issued 
$149,400 ; $54,400 of this amount was in private hands. The amount 
realized had already been used by the State to help defray expenses 
and buy up scrip. Of the $95,600 in bonds sold by Stevens, only 
$87,200 had been owned by the State. The remainder, $8,400, had 
been privately owned. The State officers had sold the $87,200 in 
State bonds to Stevens at 60 cents on the dollar, and realized from 
it $52,320 minus the seven percent interest. Stevens had paid the 
State $30,000. The balance was on deposit in New York and would 
be released as soon as Stevens furnished the remaining $54,400 in 
bonds. A movement to impeach the State officers was set in motion, 
however, which interfered with the contract. 
The movement to impeach the State officers originated with the 
Lane faction and remained under its sponsorship. Upon request 
from the House of Representatives, the information concerning the 
sale of the State bonds was presented before it by Governor Robin- 
son, January 30, 1862. Following this presentation, Representative 
Anderson of Circleville introduced a resolution to investigate the 
accounts of the State officers. The Speaker of the House, M. S. 
Adams of Leavenworth, one of Lane’s supporters, appointed Messrs. 
Martin Anderson, Sidney Clarke, Thomas Carney, B. W. Hartley, 
and W. T. Jones a committee to make the investigation. Of the 
personnel of the committee Sol Miller wrote that if it was intended 
“to bring about a certain result, which had been predetermined, re- 
gardless of the evidence elicited by the investigation, then the proper 
material was selected; but if the object was straightforward, im- 
partial justice, then it was not to be looked for in that crowd.” 51 
General Lane had been in Kansas since the 26th or 27th of January 
and had the House under his control. As evidence of this, it may 
be noted that Lane’s lieutenant, George A. Reynolds, introduced a 
resolution in the House asking the President to appoint Lane a 
Major General. The House suspended the rules by a vote of 52 to

13, and passed the resolution, 60 to 7.5 Of the House committee 
that took the depositions it was said that they appeared to be “in 
secret conclave with a certain U.S. Senator and several distinguished 
outsiders, more frequently than they were at work in the committee 
room.” *3 Judge Ewing was among the “distinguished outsiders” 
referred to. Ewing had broken with Governor Robinson in Noy- 
ember, 1861, and was making friends in the Lane camp.®4 He had 
made large investments in Leavenworth city lots and was struggling 
to get out from under the load, for the famine of 1860 had upset the 
plans of real estate investors in the Territory. Moreover, Ewing 
again had Senatorial aspirations. If Senator Lane should be made 
Major General and resign his position as Senator, Ewing expected 
to be his successor. Lane had promised Ewing that he would sup- 
port him.’ Thomas Ewing, Jr., was an example of how readily 
men changed their personal relations when it became expedient to 
do so. The financial stress of the time caused men to cast about for 
relief and was largely responsible for such erratic actions. So long 
as Ewing could profit by supporting Governor Robinson, the two 
remained partners; but when he found it to his advantage to break 
with Robinson and support Lane, a bitter enemy of Robinson, he 
made the change. Lane’s control over political patronage and his 
military and railroad connections had, no doubt, influenced Ewing 
to seek Lane as a friend. Moreover, Thomas Carney, a prominent 
business man of Leavenworth and a member of the investigating 
committee, was aspiring to be the next Kansas Governor. Lane’s 
promises to make Leavenworth the base of the new military depart- 
ment, if he could outwit Governor Robinson and his friends, ap- 
pealed to the Leavenworth men. These interests, the military, the 
economic, and the political, promoted by Lane, Ewing, and Carney, 
were no doubt influencing the findings of the investigating commit- 
tee, for if they could remove Governor Robinson or ruin his reputa- 
tion, victory would be assured.®® The House, therefore, increased 
the power of the investigating committee as needed.®” 
On the 12th of February, the committees appointed to investigate 
the affairs of the State reported. The evidence presented by the 
Committee on Printing, with Sol Miller as chairman, showed that 
some of the printers had worked out a “systematic combination to

obtain printing contracts at exorbitant rates.” Swindling had been 
winked at by officers of the State. Some of the printers had com-_ 
bined to cheat the State by agreeing upon the price. To eliminate 
competition each would bid upon a different kind of work. The 
prices asked were therefore far too high. For example, for composi- 
tion it was $2 per 1,000 ems, whereas one-half that amount would 
have made a good profit. The men guilty of this agreement were 
J. F. Cummings of the Kansas Tribune, Topeka; John Speer of the 
Lawrence Republican, and E. G. Ross of the Kansas State Record. 
E. G. Ross had secretly sent in a second proposal, slightly under- 
bidding his partners on the entire printing job. J. H. Bennett of 
Grasshopper Falls, however, had put in an independent bid for 
about one-half the rate asked by the others and his proposal had 
been accepted. Thereupon Ross charged D. H. Weir, who in 
Robinson’s absence had let the contract, with being in partnership 
with Bennett. These charges led to the investigation. The com- 
mittee’s findings implicated the wrong parties from the standpoint 
of the Lane faction, and were therefore brushed aside to make room 
for the impeachment of the State officers.58 
On the same day Anderson, chairman of the Investigating Com- 
mittee, presented his report. He asserted that the Treasurer of the 
State had exceeded his authority by issuing $40,000 in war bonds 
when the law authorized only $20,000; nor should he have sold them 
for less than par value. He also asserted that the State officers had 
authorized R. 8. Stevens as agent of the State to sell the 7 percent 
bonds, with the understanding that he should get all he could realize 
above 60 cents on the dollar. The Committee believed the author- 
ization illegal and held Stevens responsible to the State for 85 cents 
on the dollar. Furthermore, the State officers had defrauded the 
State of money by giving Stevens the semiannual interest amounting 
to $3,052. They figured that the interest had not accrued against 
the State because the sale had been transacted after the lst of Janu- 
ary. In their opinion the State officers had entered into collusion 
with R. S. Stevens to defraud the State of a large sum of money. 
The committee accordingly reported a resolution to impeach the 
State officers and recommended its adoption. It read: 
Resolved, That Charles Robinson, Governor, John W. Robinson, Secretary 
of State, and Geo. 8. Hillyer, Auditor of the State of Kansas, be and they are 
hereby impeached of high misdemeanors in office.59

## Page 186

178 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
The House adopted the resolution 65 to 0. The movement to 
impeach the State officers followed the questioning of Stevens 
concerning his escape with Governor Robinson to Leavenworth, 
and the vote was influenced by the impression it left upon the House. 
Both houses made preparations for the trial. They adopted the 
rules used by the State of Missouri in the impeachment trial of 
Hon. Albert Jackson.®° The legislature passed a bill to create a 
court for the trial. This became a law on the 5th of March, with- 
out any action on the part of the Governor. 
Senator Stevens notified the House of his willingness to settle 
the question out of court before the legislature in session should 
adjourn, and presented several propositions. The select committee 
to whom these propositions were referred stated, February 24, that 
they had been able to agree with Senator Stevens that the latter 
should pay, within ninety days, a sum into the State treasury that 
would be equal to 76 cents on the dollar of the bonds sold. Before 
the House came to an agreement, however, Senator Stevens had 
withdrawn the offer. He gave as his reason for the withdrawal the 
“determination, in the minds of some persons, to misconstrue the 
motives” which had prompted him to submit the proposition.* Sid- 
ney Clarke, supported by other members of the House, had protested 
the idea of the House entertaining a proposition from State officers 
who had sought to defraud the State. Stevens had presented his 
propositions through the State officers. 
The House now, February 20, took up the charges against the 
State officers. The Impeachment Committee charged that Secretary 
Robinson and Auditor Hillyer were guilty of high misdemeanors 
for having betrayed the trust reposed in them. Each was charged 
with eight counts. The charges against Governor Robinson were 
five in number. Secretary Robinson was summoned on the 21st and 
pleaded not guilty. On the evening of the 26th of February, the 
House voted to accept the articles of impeachment against Auditor 
Hillyer and Governor Robinson. Those against the Governor were 
adopted by a vote of 53 to 7. F. P. Baker, W. W. Dickenson, J. B. 
Ingersoll, J. C. Marsell, Sol Miller, Thomas Murphy and Edward 
Russell opposed the articles.®” 
On February 24 the Senate resolved itself into a court of impeach- 
ment for the trial of the Secretary of State. The case of the prosecu-

tion depended, however, upon procuring depositions from persons 
outside of the State. The board of managers was composed of mem- 
bers of the House and the Attorney General of the State. The latter 
moved that the impeachment court adjourn until the first Monday 
of the following June. The date for the trial of Secretary Robinson 
was definitely fixed for the first Monday in June, that of Hillyer 
for the first Thursday, and that of the Governor for the second 
Monday. A motion by Wilson Shannon, Governor Robinson’s chief 
attorney, to quash the trial of the Governor, failed 9 to 11, and a 
request for an immediate trial was also denied.® 
The feeling against the State officers at this time must have been 
intense. Floyd P. Baker wrote that “the very air seemed charged 
with angry feelings against the Governor, Secretary, and Auditor.” 
He felt that nothing had ever exceeded, nor in fact equaled, the 
feelings manifested on that occasion. “It required greater courage 
to vote ‘no’ on the question of the impeachment of Governor Robin- 
son, in the face of the crowd in that old hall, than it did to face an 
army in battle array.” Even his wife had pleaded with him not to 
vote “no” in the impeachment of Governor Robinson because she 
feared the consequences.®* 
Meanwhile the Lane forces had been busy preparing the Senate 
for the trial. They had a fair majority in the House but not in the 
Senate. In an effort to gain the control of the Senate they ousted 
senator after senator and filled the vacancies with their henchmen. 
They started with an attempt to remove R.S. Stevens. There had 
been fraud in Douglas County in the election of 1861. John Speer 
and R. S. Stevens had been rival candidates for the Senate and 
Stevens had been victorious. Speer now took advantage of the feel- 
ing against Stevens and contested the election. J. P. Root, a Robin- 
son man, was chairman of the Senate. At three different times he 
was called upon to appoint committees to investigate the case, twice 
to investigate the election returns and the third time to investigate 
Stevens’ record in connection with the sale of the State bonds. The 
resolution asked that he be expelled from the Senate. In every case 
Chairman Root appointed Robinson men who voted to retain him. 
Three times the Senate was tied on the question and in every case 
Root decided for Stevens. The Senate, however, granted Speer 
mileage and per diem for the time spent in the Senate contesting the 
election of Stevens.

The Lane followers were more successful against the senators 
charged with holding Federal military offices. In June, 1861, S. N. 
Wood had accepted a commission as Captain of the “Kansas 
Rangers.” He had resigned the position on the 10th of January, 
1862, but Brigadier General Curtis refused to accept the resignation. 
On the 29th of January, the Senate voted 13 to 9 to remove him. 
C. S. Lambdin had been elected on the Union ticket in the Novem- 
ber election of 1861, to replace Wood. The Governor had not called 
for an election in the 18th district but the Senate, February 4, dis- 
regarded the irregularity and voted to receive Lambdin, 12 to 9.% 
On the 3d of February, Senator Osborn introduced a resolution 
calling for a special committee to inquire into the military commis- 
sions of Senators Lynde, Broadhead, Gunn, Bancroft, and Phillips. 
The committee was also instructed to determine whether elections 
had been held in the senatorial districts to fill the positions of these 
senators. The committee reported, February 24, that Edward Lynde 
had been commissioned as Lieutenant of the Kansas Volunteers but 
had never been mustered into service. The Senate therefore voted 
to retain him.** The other four, however, had been commissioned 
and also mustered into service. O. B. Gunn had been appointed by 
the Governor to a position in the Kansas Militia and later mustered 
into the service of the United States for three years. Senator Ban- 
croft had been appointed Quartermaster of the Eighth Kansas Regi- 
ment in the Kansas Volunteer Militia, and Phillips Adjutant of the 
Fourth Regiment of the Kansas Volunteers. On the 4th of February 
the committee reported that in the 9th senatorial district Alonzo 
Curtis had received 856 votes, Horatio Knowles 850, and Thomas 
Roberts 849 votes. In the 12th district J. M. Rankin had received 
420 votes and W. A. Shannon 280.8 The question was now post- 
poned three weeks, and before the Senate was ready to vote on it 
Senator Phillips had resigned. This had happened on the 21st of 
February. His resignation gave the Lane group the necessary votes 
to remove the other three senators, which they accomplished by the 
1st of March. Four Lane men, Horatio Knowles, J. M. Rankin, 
C.S. Lambdin, and T. Roberts replaced Broadhead, Bancroft, Wood, 
and Phillips, respectively. The vacancy created by the removal of 
Gunn was filled later by the election of 8S. A. Cobb.®® O. B. Gunn 
protested against the action of the Senate. He said that he was an

officer of the State Militia, called into service by the President’s 
proclamation of May 3, 1861. He was no more an officer of the 
United States than were other members of the Senate who held com- 
missions in the State Militia. Attorney General Stinson said, in 
response to a request of the Senate, that it was his opinion that the 
acceptance of an appointment as an officer of the volunteers, now in 
the service of the United States, by a senator of the State, vacated 
his seat in the Senate.”° Stinson confined his opinion, however, to 
senators who had been accepted by the President under the Act of 
Congress approved July 22, 1861, and not upon the proclamation of 
the President of May 3d, 1861, referred to by Senator Gunn. 
Senator Phillips’ resignation had aroused suspicion in the minds 
of the Robinson men. Sol Miller, who was in Topeka closely watch- 
ing the progress of events, wrote February 27: 
Hitherto, all efforts to oust certain members have failed. But Gen. Lane 
instead of attending to his duties in the Senate, is now here laying the wires to 
punish his ancient enemy, Gov. Robinson. A great deal of caucusing and 
manipulating has been observed going on for several days past, and this after- 
noon the fruits began to be visible. One of the noisiest anti-Lane Senators 
suddenly resigned his seat, and another absented himself from his place, leaving 
a Lane majority present. The Senate straightway went to work and filled 
the place of the resigning Senator, by putting in a man of the desired stripe. 
It is the general impression that the resigned Senator and the absentee have 
made a good thing of it.71 
It has already been mentioned that the University bill had been 
entangled with the question of ousting the military men from the 
legislature. Senator Essick of Riley County held the balance of 
power. He had been casting his vote to retain the military men 
when General Lane pressed him for a trade. Manhattan had a 
sufficient number of Senators to pass the University bill. By voting 
out the military men, however, Manhattan would lose three votes. 
Representative Goodnow wrote that “Gen. Lane had the pledges of 
four or five Senators at our service, sufficient to secure passage of the 
bill, on condition that Mr. Essick would give them his vote to ex- 
clude the military.” Goodnow wrote that he and Mr. Wells carried 
Lane’s proposition to Senator Essick, but were refused. Next they 
approached Lane to allow the University bill to go through first. 
Lane also refused. When Senator Essick discovered that the bill 
could not go through otherwise, he voted against the military. It 
70. Senate Journal, 1862, pp. 176-180; Daily State Record, Jan. 30, 1862. 
71. White Cloud Chief, Feb. 27, 1862.

182 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
was too late, however, and the University bill was lost 11 to 12.” 
This must have happened between the 18th and 21st of February. 
On the 18th, Senator Essick was still voting with the Robinson men 
to retain Stevens. On the 21st, after Senator Phillips had resigned 
his position and the Senate voted to replace him with Roberts, he 
absented himself. Following that he voted with the Lane men.” 
In the State at large a similar spirit prevailed. In the special 
election two senators had been elected to fill vacancies, Messrs. 
John Bayless of Doniphan County and S. A. Cobb of Wyandotte 
County. The election turned on the question whether they favored 
or opposed conviction of the State officers. Mr. Cobb was elected to 
replace O. B. Gunn and Bayless to replace H. N. Seaver. Senator 
Seaver had failed to make his appearance in the Senate whereupon 
his position was declared vacant on the 5th of March. In the elec- 
tion Bayless was opposed by a Mr. Shreve, branded by the Lane 
forces as a Robinson man.’* Representative Eskridge continued the 
attempt to purge the Senate and demanded that Lieutenant Governor 
Root resign one or the other of the positions he held in violation of 
the constitution, either the position as Lieutenant Governor, or that 
of Surgeon in the Ninth Regiment of the Kansas Volunteers in the 
United States Service. Mr. Root emphatically denied the charge of 
violating the constitution and challenged the House to prove the alle- 
gation. Representative Eskridge also asked Governor Robinson to 
abstain henceforth from appointing civil service officers to military 
positions. The Governor reminded the House of the fact that the 
two departments of government were codrdinate branches, neither 
being responsible to the other, but both to their constituents.” 
Nothing more was accomplished. The Senate had been purged of 
the Robinson men, and the cases awaited the hour set for trial. 
The Senate reconvened on the 2d of June to try the cases against 
the State officers. The first days were spent in reorganizing the 
Senate and swearing in the newly elected senators. When the Sec- 
retary called the roll only fifteen senators responded. Among those 
absent were Lieutenant Governor Root, Edward Lynde, and Hoff- 
man. The report was that they had all accepted military posi- 
tions.” It seems that these vacancies were never filled.77 The Lane

## Page 191

The Birth of Kansas 183 
papers interpreted Governor Robinson’s military appointments as 
an attempt to incapacitate the Impeachment Court. The entire 
proceedings appeared to be a contest in which each side attempted 
to outmaneuver the other, rather than a trial conducted in a legal 
and orderly manner. 
In the absence of Lieutenant Governor Root, Mr. Gilchrist pre- 
sided. On the 3d of June, however, the court decided to reorganize 
the Senate and elected Thomas A. Osborn as President pro tem. 
That day twenty-one senators responded to the call. When the 
credentials of the newly elected senators were considered, the coun- 
sel for the defense, N. J. Case, interposed an objection to Mr. Bay- 
less taking the oath. Whereupon Senator Ingalls informed the re- 
spondent that the senators were the sole judges of the election and 
qualifications of their own members and if the attorney for the de- 
fense continued his “impertinent and unwarrantable interference” 
with their deliberations he would have to be forcibly ejected. The 
persistence of Mr. Case left the senator no recourse except to execute 
his threat.7® 
Frederick P. Stanton, counsel for the defense, now presented the 
following reasons why the Senate as a court was without authority 
to act. First, the Senate and the House had adjourned sine die on 
the 6th of March and could not again assemble until the time desig- 
nated by the constitution unless called into special session by the 
Governor. Second, the sole power of impeachment was vested in 
the House of Representatives. This authority could not be dele- 
gated, and no impeachment case could be tried, except upon the 
prosecution of the House of Representatives and during the session 
of that body. Third, “There is no authority for any session of the 
Senate separate and apart from that of its codrdinate branch, the 
House of Representatives.” Fourth, the act regulating the trials of 
impeachment and fixing the compensation could not apply to this 
case because it was enacted subsequent to the passage of the im- 
peachment resolution. This body was, therefore, sitting in direct 
contravention of the provisions of the constitution. The proceedings 
of this body, irregularly and unlawfully assembled, would therefore 
be a nullity. Mr. Stanton presented a lengthy argument in de- 
fense of these allegations, and asked permission to file a bill of ex- 
ceptions that might be used in later quo warranto proceedings in the 
courts of the State. 
78. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 91-92; 105. 
79. Ibid., pp. 107-108. 


## Page 192

184 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
Attorney General Stinson answered the charges for the State. He 
asserted that the question of impeachment was one of those extra- 
ordinary proceedings above precedent. “There is no such thing as 
the common law of impeachment. Whenever a case is presented, 
rules are made conforming or not to the rules which may have been 
adopted in similar cases elsewhere, according to the good pleasure of 
the body before which the trial is to be had.” As proof he cited the 
case of Jackson vs. Missouri in which the Senate had sat as a court 
in the absence of the House, and the case of Judge Chase, tried before 
the United States Senate, where the Senate as a court had adjourned 
to the next session while the Senate as a legislative body still had 
continued in session. He pointed out that the Senate resolution to 
adjourn had been based upon the House resolution instructing the 
managers to move in the Senate that the trial of the impeachment 
cases should be postponed to the 2d day of June.8° The court voted 
11 to 6 against filing the bill of exceptions. The council for the de- 
fense notified the State that if the court decided against them the 
question of adjournment might be carried to the State Supreme 
Court. As Mr. Ewing has so aptly stated, the ‘“Respondent’s Coun- 
sel then remained to ‘watch the proceedings.’ ’’84 
The trial of the State officers began June 5. Hon. Azel Spaulding 
opened the case for the State. The prosecuting attorney, Wagstaff, 
read the depositions from persons without the State, after which 
fifteen witnesses were called to testify. Wilson Shannon called the 
attention of the court to the fact that the witnesses had not been 
properly sworn in. Apparently they were also free to visit the ses- 
sions and listen to the testimony of others. The argument for the 
defense was to be opened by Mr. Case, but Senator Ingalls took 
exception to his acting in that capacity. He said that Case had 
publicly declared that the Senate was a jury packed against his 
clients, and that there was but one senator whose verdict could not 
be bought with money. Case thereby had impugned the integrity 
of the tribunal and was ejected.6? Before many more nights had 
passed, however, it became evident that Case had known whereof 
he spoke. Before the vote in the trial of Secretary Robinson was 
taken, one of the senators had offered to accept a bribe. He had in- 
formed Mr. J. F. Cummings that there were seventeen senators 
ready to vote for conviction, but if he would change his position in 
the matter, other senators would go with him. Cummings had of-

fered him $2,000 in scrip if he would change over. The senator had 
asked Cummings to see Bob Stevens. The next day, June 13, 
Cummings had met the senator again and had offered him $3,000 in 
cash, but it was too late. Lane had promised him a Federal Gov- 
ernment position at a salary of $2,000 a year if he would vote for 
conviction.*? Cummings refused to expose the senator at the time, 
but about a month later, when the Topeka Tribune and Record 
quarreled, he mentioned that it was C. K. Holliday of Topeka who 
had offered to vote against conviction of Secretary Robinson for 
$3,000.84 At this time it was also revealed that at the time of the 
election for the State capital, Holliday had sold out to the Demo- 
crats in order to secure their support for Topeka. 
To exonerate the senators in the eyes of the public Senator Ingalls 
moved that they be asked to take oath and answer the following 
questions: 
1. Have you ever had any conversation with J. F. Cummings in reference 
to the impeachment of J. W. Robinson? 
2. If yea, state when, where, and what was the substance of said conversa- 
tion. 
3. Did you ever, at any time, make any proposition or intimation to J. F. 
Cummings that any pecuniary or other consideration would influence your 
decision, or that of any other Senator, in said impeachment? 
4. Have you had any conversation or interview with J. F. Cummings this 
morning, or any time today, in the third story of the Ritchey block in the 
city of Topeka? 
5. Do you know anything touching the matter now under conversation be- 
fore the Senate, except from the testimony and statements made today in the 
Senate? 85 
The senators answered the questions in Cummings’ presence to 
Ingalls’ satisfaction. 
When Case withdrew from the trial George W. Smith took his 
place. Frederick P. Stanton concluded the argument for the defense, 
stressing the necessity of proving the criminal intent and said that 
according to common law there could be no crime of any kind or of 
any degree without a criminal intent. He reminded the Court of the 
fact that Stevens had been the prime mover in the negotiations, the 
whole scheme had been his invention, and that the defendant and 
his colleagues had been made the innocent dupes; yet the Senate 
had not even removed Senator Stevens but permitted him to partici- 
pate in the trial. He also pointed out that Senator Pomeroy had 
83. Ibid., pp. 368-376. 
84. White Cloud Chief, July 10, 1862. 
85. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 372-376. 


## Page 194

186 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
been in a position to know all the circumstances of the case and yet 
he had advised acceptance of the terms proposed by Stevens.*® 
Attorney General Stinson closed the argument in the case. He 
asserted that with the person’s motive the law had nothing to do. 
All that was necessary was to show that the person intended to 
commit the act. ‘Never before,” he said, “was a corrupt motive 
made the essential test of criminality.” He recognized Stevens as 
the instigator of the crime and likened the State to an apple in the 
paring machine. Stevens, he said, had put the State in his machine 
and with a few turns of the wheel had peeled it to the core. Stinson 
pointed out that at the time Secretary Robinson had agreed to sell 
the bonds for 60 cents on the doijlar he knew that the act he con- 
templated was unlawful. He scorned the assertion of the defense 
that Stevens would have torn up the contract which assured him a 
profit of over $12,000 even at 70 cents on the dollar. He main- 
tained that the State officers had “connived the idea of selling the 
bonds to the Secretary of Interior before they left for Washington,” 
and kept as silent as a grave about it. Secrecy was necessary to 
fleece the individual bondholders as Stevens had already fleeced the 
State. If the State officers never even asked Stevens about the sale 
price of the bonds, then, he asked, “was not their blindness willful, 
their carelessness premeditated and criminal?” In closing he ex- 
pressed his regret that the State had not been more ably represented 
in the proceedings. He did not wish to convict an innocent man. 
“Tf the evidence does not convince, beyond a reasonable doubt, of 
the guilt of this man, then send him forth to the world with every 
stain upon his fame wiped away, . . . But be just to the 
State, as you are merciful to him, and if hiscrime . . . is proved, 
sternly pronounce your righteous judgment.” ** 
Mr. Ewing has aptly noted that “the learned Counsel might as 
well have waived the right to present final argument.” ®® It is 
doubtful whether a single vote was changed by either argument or 
testimony. If senators had changed their votes they did so for other 
reasons. When the vote of the court was taken, June 18, it sustained 
the charge in the first article that the State had been defrauded out 
of its just money with the full knowledge and consent of Secretary 
Robinson and that the Secretary had betrayed the trust reposed in 
him as an officer of the State. The vote was 17 to 4. On none of 
the other counts was he found guilty. The court even refused by a

The Birth of Kansas 187 
vote of 20 to 1 to disqualify him from future officeholding. The vote 
to remove Secretary Robinson from office was 18 to 3 in favor of 
removal.®® Senator Stevens had been excused from voting. 
The trial of Auditor Hillyer was very brief. Since the two officers 
had codperated in the act, the opposing counsel permitted the evi- 
dence offered in the Robinson trial, except Hillyer’s own testimony, 
as legitimate evidence in the case at bar. Several new witnesses were 
summoned, including Secretary Robinson, but no new evidence was 
introduced. On the 14th of June, Senator Barnett rose to a 
privileged question and brought to the attention of the court the al- 
leged attempt by one of the senators to bribe the court in favor of 
the accused, provided he could get enough money to warrant the 
change. The facts have already been presented in connection with 
Secretary Robinson’s trial. Suffice it to say that the question of 
bribery occupied a major portion of the three days given to the 
trial of Auditor Hillyer. The counsel for the defense, Stanton, pre- 
sented a short argument in defense of Hillyer, but the verdict of the 
court was the same as it had been in the trial of Secretary Robin- 
son. The Auditor was found guilty of the same charge as the Secre- 
tary, and by the same vote, 17 to 4. On the remaining six articles 
he was exonerated by decisive votes. The vote to remove him from 
office was 18 to 2.%° 
Immediately following the trial of Hillyer the court took up the 
case against Governor Robinson. In spite of the deep-seated hatred 
manifested against him by such leading papers as the Leavenworth 
Daily Conservative, the Kansas State Record, and others, the court 
showed very little interest in his case. The trial opened in the after- 
noon of June 16, and closed in the evening of the same day. Only 
five witnesses were summoned to testify in the case: ‘Treasurer 
Dutton, Secretary Robinson, Auditor Hillyer, R. S. Stevens, and 
General Collamore, Quartermaster General of the State. Collamore 
was summoned to produce evidence of the fact that the war bonds 
could have been sold to George L. Stearns at par value, but the 
Governor had refused to sell. The State also tried to connect the 
Governor with the sale of the seven percent bonds. In neither case 
was the evidence convincing. Wilson Shannon, the Governor’s 
counsel, said in his defense that no claim of complicity had been ad- 
vanced by the opposition and that all the testimony proved “that 
the governor was not engaged in the transaction.” The result was 
89. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 344-349; also Ewing, op. cit., Kansas Historical 
Quarterly, I, 320. 
90. Impeachment Proceedings, pp. 392-397. 


## Page 196

188 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
that Governor Robinson was decisively acquitted. On the first 
charge that he had violated the War Bond Act, the vote was 2 to 18. 
On the next three charges which related to the seven percent act, 
that the Governor had conspired to defraud the State, that he had 
authorized and approved the sale at 60 cents on the dollar, the votes 
were unanimous for acquittal. 
Why was Governor Robinson exonerated when the other officers 
were decisively convicted? Mr. Ewing has given three reasons: 
first, that he had not directly participated in the bond sale; second, 
that his reputation had protected him; and third, that he controlled 
the State patronage.®! On the contrary, William E. Connelley as- 
serted that Governor Robinson and Treasurer Dutton had used the 
State officers “as cat’s paws to rake the chestnuts out of the fire,” 
and that the Governor had purchased his acquittal by appointing 
senators to military offices.°? The last charge seems unreasonable, 
because if the Governor had intended to do that he would not have 
appointed his friends of the Senate to these military positions be- 
fore the trial, as Ewing has so aptly pointed out, unless it was that 
he had expected an immediate trial before his appointees would 
leave for the field, which request the court had overruled. Nor are 
Ewing’s other reasons entirely convincing. It*should be remembered 
that the Senate had been packed for the trial. Senator Lane had 
been the power in the background. His opponents were not Auditor 
Hillyer and Secretary Robinson, but Governor Robinson. It has 
been reported that with some of his henchmen, at least, it was 
not a question of guilt. They were working for power and might 
convict regardless of evidence. The sudden weakening of a packed 
court at a time when they were ready to convict and thereby re- 
move the greatest enemy of the opposition is hard to explain except 
as a trade. Something evidently had happened before the days of 
the trial that weakened the opposition, the evidence of which has not 
yet come to light. The Daily Conservative dismissed the case with 
the statement: “Charles Robinson, Governor, generally supposed to 
be the biggest rascal in the pile, was acquitted, there being no evi- 
dence to convict him.” 3 In the writer’s opinion, however, Sol Miller 
was nearer the truth when he remarked: “We have no doubt if 
everything were revealed, things would come to light which would 
make every citizen of the State blush, and would throw the Bond 
Swindle so far into the shade, that it would not again be men- 
91. Ewing, op. cit., Kansas Historical Quarterly, I, 324, 325. 
92. Ibid., W. E. Connelley, An Appeal to the Record, p. 41. 
93. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, June 29, 1862. 


## Page 197

The Birth of Kansas 189 
tioned.”°4 Whether the unknown evidence would have connected 
the Governor with an attempt to defraud the State is questionable, 
but it might have revealed how he managed to untie the rope which 
the Lane forces had cast around his neck. 
It is possible that a trade had been arranged between the Lane- 
Ewing faction and the Robinson-Stevens-Pomeroy group sometime 
in April, 1862. Ewing had left the Robinson camp in the late sum- 
mer of 1861. He had yielded to Lane’s request in connection with 
the ratification of the supplementary Delaware Indian treaty, dis- 
cussed elsewhere, which had resulted in the removal of Stevens and 
Governor Robinson from the board of directors of the L., P., & W. R. 
R. Company. In December, 1861, Ewing was working hard to get 
the Pottawatomie Indian treaty before the United States Senate to 
have it ratified to enable the L., P., & W. R. R. Company to build 
the railroad, planned to cross this Indian land. He feared the op- 
position of Pomeroy, a promoter of the Atchison and Topeka rail- 
road. The treaty had been signed by the Indians November 15, 
1861, but was not ratified by the Senate until April 15, 1862.9 In 
a letter to McBratney, Ewing asked him to urge Pomeroy to support 
the treaty. He wrote: “The ratification of that treaty is of vital 
importance to it [the road], and he [Pomeroy] may carry it through 
or defeat it.”®® Moreover, the Lane-Ewing forces had packed the 
Senate against Governor Robinson. In the other camp, Governor 
Robinson was facing the impeachment trial, R. S. Stevens was 
plagued by the legislature for a settlement of the fraud, and Senator 
Pomeroy had urged the State officers to sell at 60 cents and might 
be indicted if investigation was made. Moreover, Pomeroy held 
in his power the fate of the Pottawatomie Indian treaty, which was 
worrying Ewing. Both factions were in such a position, therefore, 
that to continue the fight would mean suicide in one way or another, 
whereas a trade would be beneficial to all. 
There is evidence which indicates that the two factions may have 
tried to come to an agreement before the trial in June. Early in 
April Robert Stevens asked Governor Robinson to go to Washington 
immediately. On the 15th of April Robinson wrote Mrs. Robinson 
from Washington (the same day the Pottawatomie Indian treaty 
was ratified by the Senate), “The P. Treaty is in doubt. The Pacific 
Railroad Pomeroy says will not pass.” On the 17th the Governor

wrote again: “Lane is opposing Stevens and Dutton all he can. 
Collamore had the last part of his last letter published in the Re- 
publican. I have written an answer which they refused to publish 
and it will be in the Star tomorrow. I intend to make the fur 
fly here unless I am permitted to get my business done without hin- 
drance from Lane.” On the 27th Robinson was more composed 
when he wrote: “I think all matters will turn out satisfactorily 
after a short time. Lane files protests against everything & hence 
delay is unavoidable.” °7 What questions figured in the settlement 
is unknown. This much is known however, that Governor Robinson 
was acquitted when the Senate was packed against him, the Indian 
treaty was ratified April 15, 1862, with both Lane and Pomeroy 
supporting it,9* and all attempts to make an investigation of the 
Kansas congressmen were defeated. Moreover, Stevens escaped 
punishment and Senator Lane had attained his desire by ruining 
Governor Robinson, politically. These facts point to the conclusion 
that the two factions came to an agreement about their interests, 
although the evidence necessary to confirm the hypothesis is still 
missing. 
It appears that the legislature convicted the men who had been 
made the scape-goats in the affair, but left the door wide open for 
the villains to escape and continue their work for another period of 
years. R. 8. Stevens at least had offered to correct the wrongs he 
had committed. The two United States Senators, however, con- 
tinued their secret, underhand work until the one, Pomeroy, was 
exposed, and the other, Lane, committed suicide. 
The question of whether the Kansas delegates in Congress were 
implicated in the fraud had been considered in the House of Repre- 
sentatives on different occasions. On the 15th of February, Repre- 
sentative Van Winkle of Grasshopper Falls had introduced a resolu- 
tion authorizing a select committee to investigate the question. It 
was considered on the 15th and 17th of February and on the 4th of 
March. When it appeared too harsh he tempered it to read: “Be 
it resolved, That the members of said delegation in Congress be re- 
quested to fully vindicate themselves before the people of Kansas, 
from the charges of criminality thus implied against them; and failing 
to do this they are hereby requested to resign the seats now occupied 
by them.”®? The House refused to adopt it. Representative R. W.

Potter of Burlington then introduced another resolution asking the 
delegation in Congress to explain their connection with the illegal 
transaction, but it was indefinitely postponed, 30 to 23.1° The 
friends of the Kansas delegation in Congress had prevented the 
investigation. 
The impeachment proceedings had been used by the Lane forces 
to ruin the Governor, politically. The Governor and Mrs. Robin- 
son were fully conscious of this. On the 16th of April, 1862, Gov- 
ernor Robinson gave his interpretation of why Lane had brought 
impeachment proceedings against him: 
For some reason unknown to the subscriber, the great “Lane expedition, 
so-called,’ met with a sad disaster, or was “run into the ground” at or near Ft. 
Leavenworth. Gen’l Lane said he felt as though a train of cars had run over 
him. Something must be done, and a resolution was sent over to Topeka, to 
be adopted by the Legislature, endorsing Gen’l. Lane as a suitable person to 
take command of the “Southern expedition” and for a Major General. This 
resolution was introduced into the lower House and was rejected. Gen’l. Lane 
then visited Topeka in person, told his friends that Robinson’s influence must 
be broken down both in Kansas and elsewhere, and they must get up an im- 
peachment, whether guilty or innocent. About a dozen candidates for the 
U.S. Senate were impatiently waiting to step into Gen’l. Lane’s seat, and he 
told them there was but one way to get him out of it. If he was made Major 
General he would leave it and if the Legislature would endorse him for that 
position the President would appoint him in twenty-four hours after the news 
reached Washington. As nearly all the candidates were assured by Gen’l. Lane 
that their election would be certain if he was out of the way, . . . it went 
through both Houses without opposition. . . 101 
Governor Robinson was permitted to complete his term but after 
that he was retired to private life for a period of ten years. In 1872 
he was elected representative in the lower House, and from 1874 to 
1878 he served as State senator. But he was always on the defen- 
sive. Even after the death of General Lane in 1866, the factious 
quarrel figured time and again in the papers, surviving even the 
death of Governor Robinson, August 17, 1894. After that Mrs. Sara 
T. D. Robinson wielded a very able pen in defense of the Governor 
and his political career. 
Mrs. Robinson’s interpretation of the negotiations of the bonds, 
given after the death of her husband, is interesting. She wrote that 
the Lincoln administration had stopped Stevens’ work with the Sac 
and Fox Indians, and the only reason why the Kansas delegation 
had used him, a Buchanan Democrat, to negotiate sale of the bonds 
was because he possessed the necessary knowledge and acquaint- 
100. Ibid., pp. 478, 474. 
101. Robinson’s letter in the Leavenworth Daily Conservative of May 1, 1862. 


## Page 200

192 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
ance in the Indian-office that it took to make the bargain. She felt 
that Lane might have been prompted by two motives, to profit by 
Stevens’ influence to drive Indian bargains, and to place him under 
obligations so that he would be out of the way when Lane got ready 
to reopen the Jenkins land case. She thought it apparent why the 
Kansas Congressional delegation at Washington allowed Stevens to 
give the State 60 cents when the Interior Department had paid him 
85 cents on the dollar. She continued: 
There are those who believe no trade could have been made with the 
Interior Department without this margin for somebody’s profit and that it 
was well known by those who could and should have prevented it, that the 
Secretary of Interior paid 85 cents when he could have bought them at 60 
cents. Secretary Robinson and Auditor Hillyer . . . were in daily contact 
with the Kansas delegation in Congress, and learned from them that the only 
way to make the trade was to sell them to Stevens for 60 cents and let him 
sell them to the Department for 85 cents. 
Mrs. Robinson thought it amazing, but not more so than other 
things done at the time.1°?_ It is now known that as early as Septem- 
ber 16, 1861, Senator Pomeroy had expected a consideration of 
several thousand dollars for helping to sell the bonds, which in- 
dicates that Stevens and Pomeroy had come to an understanding be- 
fore the State officers had reached Washington.!°? Moreover, on 
the 7th of March, 1862, Theodore Hyatt wrote that in addition to 
the bond deal, Pomeroy had helped to place a rifle contract and as 
a commission for this service he was “‘to receive a certain sum for 
each one [rifle] made, I think $2 each for the first 3,000 and less for 
all others ordered.” 1°4 
And now to return to the State officers, Secretary Robinson and 
Auditor Hillyer, convicted of high misdemeanors and removed from 
office by an irregular session of the State Senate. 
On the 28th of June, Secretary Robinson notified the Governor 
that he was still of the opinion that the pretended session of the 
Senate was wholly unauthorized by the constitution and that its 
judgment therefore was a nullity. He would refuse to vacate his 
position, therefore, until the question had been settled in court. The 
State Supreme Court granted the State officers a writ of quo war- 
ranto, July 9, and the case was argued at Topeka on the 24th of 
July, 1862. The two attorneys, Stinson and Shannon, once more 
faced each other on the question of the validity of the adjournment. 
Chief Justice Ewing presided. The court decided that the sine die

adjournment of the legislature had terminated only the legislative

did not consider the validity of the court’s seating new members,

Mrs. Robinson wrote that the Governor had felt the State officers 
were victims of a persecution aimed at himself and had, therefore, 
regarded it his duty to appoint them to offices from which they had 
been unjustly deposed. He had prepared the papers for their ap- 
pointment, but they had declined to accept, doubting the propriety 
of the course.1°° Governor Robinson then appointed the Secretary 
a Surgeon in the Union army, in which service he died the next 
year while at Fort Smith, Arkansas. The Governor also commis- 
sioned George S. Hillyer First Lieutenant and Quartermaster of the 
Thirteenth Regiment.1°7 
The sale of the seven percent bonds carried over into Governor 
Carney’s administration. In 1863 the State finally disposed of these 
bonds; $95,600 in bonds had been delivered to the Secretary of the 
Interior, of which $31,000 still was unpaid. The State was to fur- 
nish the Secretary with the remaining $54,400 to complete the con- 
tract. This was necessary to receive payment for the $31,000 held 
as security by the Secretary. The legislature, therefore, passed a 
bill, March 3, 1863, which provided for an additional issue of 
$54,400 seven percent bonds. The Governor was made the agent 
of the State to complete the contract. The bonds should not be 
sold for less than 85 cents on the dollar. The act ratified the part 
of the contract already executed, “provided that nothing in this act 
contained shall in any way effect, impair or prejudice any claim the 
State may have against Robert S. Stevens, or the late executive 
officers of the State or their sureties.” 1°° The bill was vigorously 
fought and protested by nine State senators, including F. P. Fitz- 
williams, Sol Miller, and Thomas H. Baker. They argued that the 
new issue was unnecessary. Sol Miller was concerned about the 
motive of the legislators and the Governor in supporting the act. 
He wrote that the men who had been elected on tickets in opposition 
to the regular State ticket, and had come there soured against the

That Governor Carney was willing to capitalize his position can no

It will be remembered that the bill providing for the new issue of 
bonds authorized the Governor to sell them. This he did at 9514 
cents on the dollar. That was well done. The Governor had not 
forgotten, however, that the new issue of $54,400 in bonds had left 
an equal amount in bonds in the hands of private persons for 
speculation. While he was in Washington negotiating the new issue 
of State bonds, in March and April of 1863, he recalled the bonds 
in the hands of private individuals, and wrote J. L. McDowell in re- 
gard to them: 
I understand that there are-some of our State Bonds held by private individ- 
uals that no doubt can be bought at from 70 to 80¢. If you can buy any at 
that price you will do well. I would not let them pass even at 85 cts. You 
will also find.State Scrip a good thing at same prices. If you can find enough 
to buy more than you have funds for call on my partners for the money and 
I will divide the profit with you. I will say this to you, the scrip will be paid 
‘soon, and I can sell the bonds at from 93 to 95¢ in New York so that you 
can at a glance see what you can do provided you can get either. I hope 
you will succeed in making some money, as you can do so as well as not. 
What you do will have to be done quickly as I will be home soon and 
suffice it to say that I hope to put the State on a cash basis, as I shall succeed 
to my satisfaction financially. All of which please keep strictly to yourself. 
Don’t want any one to know about matters until I get home and get 
ready. Suffice it to say I have beat the protest. I enclose an order on Stevens 
for some funds, should you need some. . . . Remember every word here 
is in the strictest confidence. Tuos. CARNEY. 
P. S. From the correspondence I see published in Kansas papers should 
think you could buy both scrip and bonds cash. I mean between Usher & 
Stevens.110 
Had it just dawned upon the Governor while in Washington that 
the seven percent bonds in the hands of private persons would be 
available for speculation, or was he aware of it when he recom- 
mended the new bond issue to the legislature? In the light of the 
Governor’s own letter and Sol Miller’s statement, the matter looked 
very suspicious. To this must be added the Governor’s interest in 
seeing the measure through. These facts point to the conclusion that

110. J. L. McDowell, Papers, Carney to McDowell, April 19, 1863.

Governor Carney had merely discovered a more subtle way of fleec- 
ing the State and its citizens than had the first administration, and 
he got by with it. 
Early in May, 1863, Governor Carney was able to report that he 
had exchanged $26,000 of the $54,400 bonds for that amount of 
war bonds of the State which fell due July 1st of that year. He then 
withdrew the $31,000 worth of bonds from the Secretary and sold 
them plus the balance of the last issue, $28,400 ($59,400 in all), at 
9514 cents on the dollar. That completed the sale of the seven 
percent bonds. What settlement, if any, the State made with R. S. 
Stevens is unknown. On the 26th of February, 1863, he once more 
offered to settle with the State for $8,672 plus interest on the un- 
paid balance, provided the State would absolve him of further re- 
sponsibilities and regard the transaction settled.1* It is not known 
whether the proposition was accepted. It has been reported, how- 
ever, that R. 8. Stevens was permitted to resume his contract with 
the Sacs and Foxes and was even granted a similar contract with the 
Kaw Indians, “and in various ways the profits of patronage were 
bestowed upon him.” 14% General Lane also was successful in induc- 
ing the Land Office to reopen the Jenkins case, which had developed 
out of the land quarrel and twice had been decided against him. 
The case was reopened on the claim of newly discovered evidence, 
and Lane was made the owner of the land to which his shotgun 
and influence as United States Senator gave him title. In closing 
this discussion no statement is more fitting than that of Mrs. Robin- 
son: 
Had the Legislature that impeached the State Officers, turned their attention 
towards Washington and pursued with vigor their investigations, it might 
have saved Kansas from scandal and purified some offices then rotten with 
corruption.114

## Page 204

Chapter IX 
The Election of 1862, and the Close of the 
First Administration 
MONG the anomalies of the American system of State govern- 
ment is the fact that the last eight months or even the second 
year of the Governor’s administration is largely devoted to electing 
a successor, and when the new officers meet the legislature, their 
reports cover the administration of their predecessors, and the pre- 
vious legislature and State officers have practically determined 
everything for the first six months of the new administration. To 
complete the history of Governor Robinson’s administration it is 
necessary, therefore, to give a brief account of the election of the 
State officers of the succeeding administration. 
In the summer of 1862 the Robinson administration was fast draw- 
ing to aclose. The new candidates for office were crowding the pages 
of the newspapers with their claims and qualifications. The Repub- 
lican State committee, in July, 1862, issued & call for the Republican 
State convention to be held September 17, at Topeka. In the city 
of Leavenworth the voters, in the primary election, divided their 
votes for Governor, 375 for Wilder, 205 for M. S. Adams, and 
191 for Thomas Carney. Adams soon withdrew in favor of Carney, 
which put the latter in the lead. The Wilder supporters charged 
that Adams had “sold out” to Carney. A. C. Wilder later shifted 
his aspirations to the position of Congressman. 
Shortly before the Republican convention the Conservative wrote: 
“At the Pomeroy House I found General Lane, . . . [M.] F. 
Conway, . . . and J. L. McDowell, U.S. Marshal.” Lane and 
McDowell were exerting their influence to secure the nomination of 
Carney for Governor. Conway was tooting his own horn. The 
Conservative mentioned the retirement of Chief Justice Ewing 
shortly before the election, and suspected that here was a prear- 
ranged scheme to use this position as capital with which to secure 
needed votes for the governorship. The papers questioned the meth- 
ods of Thomas Carney and charged that he had bought up enough 
of the Leavenworth County delegates to give him a majority.” 
1. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Sept. 3, 1862. 
2. Ibid., Sept. 18, 1862. 
(196) 


## Page 205

The Birth of Kansas 197 
When the convention met, it elected F. P. Baker of Nemaha, a 
supporter of Carney, as president. The Lane-Carney influence 
controlled the convention and nominated Carney, Governor; T. A. 
Osborn of Doniphan, Lieutenant Governor; George A. Crawford, 
Secretary of State; Asa Hargrove of Linn County, Auditor; William 
Spriggs of Anderson County, Treasurer; I. T. Goodnow of Riley 
County, Superintendent of Public Instruction; W. W. Guthrie of 
Brown County, Attorney General; and A. C. Wilder of Leavenworth, 
Congressman, defeating Conway and Thacher for this place. Craw- 
ford declined the nomination for Secretary of State and in his place 
the Hon. W. W. H. Lawrence of Franklin County was nominated. 
It was admitted even by Governor Robinson that “a most excellent 
ticket had been nominated,” but it had been done by “such scandal- 
ous means.” Lane had just received the right to appoint the officers 
of three regiments and had used this power to run the convention.® 
Sol Miller agreed with Robinson and wrote that “trading had been 
the order of the day.” Some of the delegates had traded so often 
that certain candidates had thereby been badly “euchred.” J. J. 
Ingalls had thus lost to Thomas A. Osborn. This had created 
hostility and led to a division in the convention. 
The dissatisfied delegates met in separate convention at Law- 
rence and nominated their own candidates. It was a motley group, 
comprising disappointed office seekers, anti-Lane men, and Demo- 
crats. Among them were men who only a few months previous had 
opposed one another in the impeachment trial, including Governor 
Robinson, J. J. Ingalls, Samuel Stinson, W. W. Updegraph, M. J. 
Parrott, and J. W. Robinson.> Eleven counties with about forty 
delegates were represented. Samuel Stinson, whose influence had 
been “omnipotent and omnipresent,” moved that the convention unite 
with the Democrats. Although the motion carried, the convention 
nominated a full ticket without waiting for the Democrats. This was 
known as the Union ticket. Among the candidates nominated were 
W. R. Wagstaff, Governor; J. J. Ingalls, Lieutenant Governor; M. J. 
Parrott, Congressman; and W. P. Gambell of Leavenworth, Chief 
Justice. The Republican convention had nominated no one for the 
latter position. The Union ticket was composed of Republicans and 
Democrats. The Democrats on the ticket were Wagstaff, Gambell, 
N. S. Goss nominated Auditor, and D. L. Lakin, Treasurer. The 
8. Robinson, Kansas Conflict, Chap. 18, p. 24. 
4. White Cloud Chief, Sept. 25, 1862.

rest were Republicans. The convention was bitterly anti-Lane 
but rather feeble in defending Governor Robinson.® 
The Democratic convention, also held at Topeka, October, 1, 
nominated its own candidate for Congressman, William J. Mathias. 
Otherwise the convention agreed to support the Union ticket. It 
was regretted by some that the bolters and Democrats had failed to 
form a true union party. Some people in Kansas, like some in Ohio, 
Pennsylvania, and other States, thought the time had come for “the 
overthrow of the radical and destructive Republican party.’ 
The campaign was hard fought, although probably never doubt- 
ful. Charges of bribery were hurled against both sides. It appears 
that Senator Ingalls was willing to sell his “birthright for a mess 
of pottage.” He was put on the defensive by T. A. Osborn and 
George Fairchild of Atchison. While trying to defend his record 
as a loyal citizen of Atchison, Ingalls confessed he had been the 
friend of Pomeroy in the election of United States Senators in 
1861, and had done everything in his power to secure Pomeroy’s 
election; “that if it had not been for his ‘manoeuvering,’ when the 
vote was being taken, Mr. Parrott would have been Senator.’ 
During the-campaign, no name appeared more frequently in the 
papers than that of Jim Lane. Although not a-candidate, he had 
probably reached the summit of his power and had become in- 
tolerant and dictatorial. He branded the Union candidates as rebels. 
In a speech at Atchison he declared that the “Provost Marshal at 
Leavenworth had the names of six hundred sympathizers with 
treason already on his books, who would be prevented from voting 
an opposition ticket, and that the new regiments would also be back 
in the State on the day of election to see that everything went 
right.” 2° The Leavenworth Inquirer wrote that “Federal patronage 
has ever been a lever by which to influence the elections in Kansas 
since the days of the Shawnee Legislature, . . . It is used now 
to perpetuate Lane’s power in Kansas, and it has degraded our poli- 
tics beyond that of any other State; . . . [Lane] declares pub- 
licly that the people shall acquiesce in his decision, shall vote as 
he desires. . . .” In another place the Inquirer wrote that in 
“one regiment at least the officers, from Colonel down to Chaplain,

were appointed from delegates, genuine, proxy and bogus, to that 
convention.”14 
In spite of all that had been said and written, the Republican 
ticket, on November 4, 1862, was elected entire. Nothing else could 
be expected. It is very seldom that a bolter’s ticket wins an elec- 
tion. The election returns showed that Thomas Carney received 
10,012 votes and Wagstaff only 5,467, a majority of almost two to 
one. This vote was typical.12 The big majority of the electorate 
had voted a straight ticket. 
The problem created by the resignation of Chief Justice Ewing 
had remained unsolved. Ewing had accepted a position as Colonel 
of the Eleventh Kansas Volunteer Regiment. It seems that he had 
contemplated resigning the judgeship before the meeting of the Re- 
publican convention, and had so informed the convention; but be- 
fore it took action in the premises he had changed his attitude. Sol 
Miller wrote that Ewing was afraid he would not be commissioned 
if Governor Robinson had to do it, and had delayed the resignation 
as Chief Justice until all doubts about the military position were re- 
moved. His resignation was dated October 20, 1862, but was not 
received by the Governor until December 28. That same day, De- 
cember 28, Governor Robinson appointed Nelson Cobb of Lawrence, 
a Democrat, Chief Justice.1? In the election the Republican candi- 
date for his office, John H. Watson, who had been nominated since 
the Republican convention, defeated the Union party candidate, 
Willard P. Gambell, two to one; but the State canvassing committee 
refused to canvass the votes cast for Chief Justice. The House had 
passed a resolution directing the new board to canvass the vote, but 
the Senate had unanimously refused to concur, regarding it a judicial 
question.1* The result was that both Cobb and Watson claimed the 
office. The case came before the State Supreme Court which decided 
in favor of Nelson Cobb. The constitution had provided that unless 
a given vacancy occurred “more than thirty days” before the regular 
election, the Governor shall fill the position by appointment. The 
court ruled that the term “regular election” should be interpreted to 
mean the next election conformable to established law.’ Since the 
vacancy had been officially announced less than thirty days before
