## Page 208

200 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
the election, the court decided for Governor Robinson’s appointee, 
Mr. Cobb. 
Thomas Carney had been a merchant in Leavenworth, Kansas. 
At the time of his election he was a young man of thirty-eight. He 
was born and reared in Ohio. Possessing great business acumen, 
he and Thomas C. Stevens had, in 1857, started a wholesale grocery 
store in Leavenworth, which in a few years developed into a pros- 
perous business. By 1862 it included two extensive warehouses and 
an extensive store at the Sac and Fox agency, with yearly sales 
amounting to more than $500,000.16 At the time he was elected 
Governor, Carney was a State representative. 
The newly elected State officers assumed the responsibilities of 
office on Monday, the 12th of January, 1863, without ceremonial 
formalities. Governor Robinson had frowned on the idea of a pub- 
lic inauguration.1” On the 14th, Governor Carney read his message 
before a joint session of the legislature. It was a lengthy but able 
document. Carney favored an energetic prosecution of the war, and 
emancipation of the slaves. He said that Kansas, with a popula- 
tion of only 107,000, had mustered “more men to combat Rebellion 
than any loyal State in the Union,” meaning, no doubt, in proportion 
to population. It had organized thirteen regiments, but this had 
been done at “immense sacrifice.” Many families had been left 
almost destitute. It was a tragic fact that in many instances the 
faithful mother, and in some cases only children, had been left to 
attend to the household and farm. He asked the legislature, there- 
fore, to make appropriations for the relief of soldiers’ families, and 
for sick and wounded volunteers. 
The Governor then directed the attention of the legislature to the 
interests of the State. He reminded it of the unfulfilled contract 
with the Secretary of the Interior, calling for $54,400 of seven per- — 
cent bonds which were in public hands, and said that the Secretary 
of the Interior had agreed to receive enough of a new issue to make 
good the contract, provided the State would “affirm by law the act 
of its former agent.” He encouraged the multiplication of small 
farms, which would make the State more stable and progressive. 
He reminded the legislature that the State had not received its full 
share of school lands, because the previous grants had not taken into 
consideration the Indian territory, embracing 9,986,441 acres. Out 
of this Kansas should have received 664,478 acres for schools; other 
States had received a proportionate amount. He also mentioned 
16. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Jan. 19, 1862. 
17. Ibid., Jan. 18, 1863. 


## Page 209

The Birth of Kansas 201 
that the Federal act of September 4, 1841 had allotted 500,000 acres 
for internal improvements to each State, but the constitution of 
Kansas had provided that this land should be used for the support 
of common schools. He asked the legislature, therefore, to determine 
to which use the land should be devoted. He expressed great con- 
cern for the educational interests of the State and asked the legisla- 
ture to guard them well. ‘Better straightened means than a starved 
intellect. Better poverty of purse than poverty of heart.” He 
asked the legislators to take advantage of the Morrill act passed by 
Congress. He upheld the clause in the constitution declaring that 
the State should “never be a party in carrying on any works of 
internal improvements,” but Sol Miller noted that he smiled when 
he said it.18 He recommended that the State complete the construc- 
tion of the railroad from Weston to Fort Leavenworth, “the wagon 
roads from Fort Leavenworth to Fort Scoté and south; from Fort 
Leavenworth to Fort Union, via Riley, Larned, and Lyon; and from 
Fort Leavenworth to Fort Laramie and west.’”’ He referred to them 
as military roads. He supported the construction of the Pacific 
railroad and asked the legislature to inaugurate a State railroad 
system that would connect with the Federal road. 
He called attention to the disorders within the State and declared 
that the laws should be maintained and enforced, lawless bands dis- 
persed, and raids and robberies suppressed. Much of the land of 
the State was still in the hands of the Indians, and he favored the 
extinction of Indian titles. Of the 2,273 townships within the State, 
only 765 had been surveyed, including 141 townships of Indian 
lands. This left 1,508 townships within the State unsurveyed. All 
the insurance within the State had been contracted for by foreign 
insurance companies who were getting State protection without pay- 
ing any taxes. He asked the legislature to continue the survey of 
lands and to tax the property of the insurance companies within the 
State. He also asked the legislature to petition Congress to pay the 
Territorial claims incurred during the Kansas Civil War. Last 
but not least, he stressed the importance of general laws applying to 
local communities. ‘Let counties, townships and cities have home 
rule.” 1® Governor Carney had made a careful study of the condi- 
tions of the State and his message, therefore, had historic value. 
Of great significance was the question of what the State should 
do with the 500,000 acres of land received from the Federal Govern- 
ment in 1841. In the minds of some Kansans the main question was 
18. White Cloud Chief, Jan. 22, 1868. 
19. Public Documents, 1862, pp. 5-24. 


## Page 210

202 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
whether this land should be used for the support of common schools 
as provided in the constitution, or for purposes of internal improve- 
ments. Governor Carney had asked the legislature to decide this 
question. During his administration the legislature considered a 
bridge bill which provided that the proceeds from 150,000 of the 
500,000 acres should be used to build bridges. The Lieutenant Gov- 
ernor, T. A. Osborn, however, had cast the deciding vote against the 
bill. The general question remained unsettled until 1866, when the 
legislature provided that the 500,000 acres should be set apart to be 
sold for the benefit of the Northern Kansas Railroad Company, the 
Kansas and Neosho Valley Railroad Company, the southern branch 
of the Union Pacific Railway, and the Leavenworth, Lawrence, and 
Fort Gibson Railroad Company.”° These roads led through territory 
where the land had already been occupied by settlers. The com- 
panies were compensated for the lands thus lost to them by this 
grant of 500,000 acres. The common school fund was thereby de- 
prived of a chief source of income. In spite of the constitutional 
provision to the contrary, the land was given to the railroad com- 
panies, and it appears the matter was never contested in the courts. 
The financial condition of the State in February, 18638, was still 
critical. According to the reports of the State Auditor and Treas- 
urer, there was a Territorial indebtedness of $80,000 including esti- 
mated interest, and a State indebtedness of $194,000, making a total 
of $274,000. To this should be added the $54,400 bond issue neces- 
sary to reclaim the $31,000 held by the Secretary of the Interior, plus 
the interest and warrants outstanding. To offset this debt there were 
the delinquent taxes of 1861-1862, amounting to $132,000, the $31,- 
000 in bonds held by the Secretary of the Interior, and the tax levy 
for the ensuing year. The State officers had not included the war 
tax of over $73,000 levied against the State by the Federal Gov- 
ernment.?!_ During the first administration the Government had 
been in great need of money. It is reported that in 1862 Thomas 
Carney intervened to save the State from financial disgrace by 
advancing it $25,000 from his own purse.2” 
The remaining paragraphs will summarize the constitutional pro- 
visions, principal legislative enactments, and so far as possible the 
general conditions of the State at the close of the first administration. 
Article five of the constitution provided that every white male 
person of twenty-one years of age or upwards, who had established 
20. General Statutes of Kansas, 1868, p. 889. 
21. Leavenworth Daily Conservative, Feb. 5, 1863. 
22. White Cloud Chief, Oct. 23, 1862. 


## Page 211

The Birth of Kansas 203 
a six months’ residence in the State and thirty days’ residence in 
the township or ward, was qualified to vote, provided he was a 
citizen of the United States or had declared his intention to become 
naturalized. In addition to the customary list of exclusions the 
constitution had excluded from voting the soldier, seaman, and 
marine. Any person who had given or accepted a challenge to fight 
a duel or had been involved in executing it, or any person who had 
given or offered a bribe to procure his election, was disqualified from 
holding office. Dueling implied permanent disqualification, bribery 
merely for the ensuing term. The fifteenth amendment of the 
Federal Constitution and.the State amendment of 1912 extended 
the suffrage to the Negro and to women, respectively. In 1864, the 
State amended section three of article five of the constitution, which 
among other things authorized the legislature to make provision for 
absentee voting. 
Article four provided that all elections should be by ballot and all 
elections in the legislature by viva voce. It set the time for general 
elections to be held annually on the Tuesday succeeding the first 
Monday in November. Township elections should be held on the 
first Tuesday in April until the legislature should provide otherwise. 
In 1902 and 1914 the constitution was amended to provide for 
biennial elections, and in 1913-14 the recall of public officers was 
introduced. 
To make the constitutional provisions explicit, election laws were 
passed by the State legislature in 1861. On the 23d of May the 
Governor approved an act regulating elections. It prescribed the 
qualifications of voters, provided the machinery for voting and pro- 
hibited illegal voting. The townships in the county and wards in 
cities should constitute the election districts unless otherwise pro- 
vided by law. The township trustee and any two justices of the 
peace were to be the judges of all elections in townships, and the 
city council or any three members of the council should be the judges 
in city elections. The Australian ballot had not yet been intro- 
duced. The law permitted candidates or their friends to the number 
of three to be present when ballots were counted. The Secretary of 
State, Auditor, Treasurer, and Attorney General, or any three of 
them were to constitute the board of State canvassers. All vacancies 
in State and county offices, and in the Supreme and district courts, 
unless otherwise provided by law, should be filled by appointment 
by the Governor. The act also fixed the terms of the State, county, 
and legislative officers. On the 22d of May the legislature set the 


## Page 212

204 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
date of the general election of State and Federal officers for “the 
Tuesday succeeding the first Monday in November, a. p. 1862, and 
on the Tuesday succeeding the first Monday in November, in every 
second year thereafter.” It also provided for the election of county 
and township officers. In another act of the same year it pre- 
scribed the manner of contesting elections.2? Notice of contested 
elections of State officers was to be filed with the Secretary of the 
Senate and the Senate was to decide upon each case. Seats of 
senators and representatives should be determined by the house in 
which the seat was contested. 
The limited powers of the Governor as indicated in a number of 
the legislative acts have been mentioned in Chapter VII. Suffice it 
to say here that theoretically the Governor was vested with ‘“‘su- 
preme executive power,” but practically he had been robbed of 
all executive powers except those of requiring information from 
executive officers, convening and adjourning the legislature, grant- 
ing pardons and reprieves under legislative regulations and restric- 
tions, keeping the seal, and issuing commissions. This commission- 
ing power was contested by General Lane. The long ballot of State 
officers had deprived the Governor of much of the administrative 
power of the State. ; 
The legislative enactments describing the duties, obligations, and 
compensations of State officers were given in Chapter VII. On the 
3d of June, 1861, the legislature passed an act which provided for a 
State Board of Equalization, the proposed function of which was to 
equalize the State tax among the counties. The board was to be 
composed of the Secretary of State, Treasurer, and Auditor, and was 
to perform its duty at the seat of government on the second Monday 
of September of each year.*4 
The second article of the constitution providing for the legislative 
department of the State limited the number of senators and rep- 
resentatives to 33 and 100, respectively. These limits were changed 
by amendment in 1873, to maximums of 40 and 125, respectively. 
The first legislature consisted of 25 senators and 75 representatives, 
and future changes in size were to be regulated by law. The quali- 
fications and compensation of legislators and the length of the ses- 
sions also had been determined by the constitution. The provision 
that all bills were to originate in the House of Representatives was 
amended in 1864, permitting bills to originate in either house. The 
poe Laws of Kansas, 1861, pp. 133-145, 150-156; Compiled Laws, 1862, pp. 
24. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, pp. 511, 512, 862. 


## Page 213

The Birth of Kansas 205 
Senate had found itself at a loss what to do the first month of the 
session. The provision for an annual session of the legislature was 
changed in 1875 by an amendment providing for biennial sessions. 
At the same time the State also changed the term of office of rep- 
resentatives and senators from one and two years to two and four 
years, respectively. 
In 1861, the legislature had adopted for the government of its 
own conduct the Territorial rules of procedure, provided for State 
printing, and set about to reapportion the State for purposes of 
legislative representation. The first legislature had attempted the 
task of reapportionment, but the two houses had disagreed; which 
failure, according to the Elwood Free Press, was due to a “foolish 
quarrel between the northern and southern Kansas factions” in the 
legislature.2> The first legislature had failed, therefore, to fulfill a 
constitutional requirement*® which it was left for the succeeding 
legislature to satisfy. The apportionment act that then was adopted 
retained the same number of senators and representatives as be- 
fore, but made other changes of some importance. Instead of 
combining counties to carry out a scheme of gerrymandering, as did 
the Wyandotte convention, each county was given one representative 
and only the less populous counties were combined for the election 
of senators.2” The gerrymandering scheme of the convention had, 
therefore, been largely abandoned. 
The first State legislature was in certain quarters severely criti- 
cised. The Kansas State Journal wrote that the trouble seemed 
to be “that its members were elected for the purpose of choosing 
two Senators.” This had worked a corrupting influence on the body 
and disturbed its regular work.?8 Another comment was that “The 
majority were just common farmers, with a slight sprinkling of 
merchants, lawyers, doctors and newspaper men, yet in a very short 
time the same common people turned up as Captains, Colonels, 
Generals, and Governors.” 2° The numerous leaves of absence 
granted by the House often resulted in the lack of a quorum to do 
business. The Secretary of State pointed out that the legislature at 
times had violated the constitution by keeping the bills it had passed 
longer than two days before submitting them to the Governor for his

Press, June 15, 1861. 
26. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, p. 64. The first legislature was to make the ap- 
portionment and no other should be made till 1866. 
27. Kansas State Record, April 6, 1861. 
28. Kansas State Journal, June 6, 1861. 
29. D. E. Ballard, “The First State Legislature,” Transactions, X, 233. 


## Page 214

206 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
approval.2° An important accomplishment of the second legislature, 
however, was the codification of the State and Territorial laws. 
The constitution, in article three, set forth the provisions that 
were to govern the judicial department of the State. Provision was 
made for one Supreme Court, five district courts which could be 
increased by law, county probate courts, justices of the peace, and 
such other courts inferior to the Supreme Court as might be provided 
by law. The limits of jurisdiction were prescribed and the legislature 
authorized to define the specific powers of the courts. The legisla- 
ture of 1861, accordingly, established the State courts and specified 
their responsibilities in detail. In retaining the Territorial acts it 
provided for civil and criminal codes. It adopted the Territorial 
provisions of the English common law and indicated that the various 
laws should go into effect, either on the date of passage, or upon 
publication in the Topeka State Record, as the case might be.*4 
It provided for regulating the trials of impeachment. 
Article nine of the constitution authorized the legislature to pro- 
vide for the organization of counties. The State legislature carried 
out these requirements, as described elsewhere. Since the consti- 
tution, however, only indirectly provided for the establishment of 
townships in the provision, namely, for the election of “such county 
and township officers as may be necessary,” the legislative act of 
1861 authorized the county commissioners to divide the county into 
convenient townships.*? Moreover, the Territorial act of 1860, which 
provided for township officers, had been retained by the State. The 
original plan of county and township government has continued 
substantially to the present time, except that new officers, powers, 
and duties have been added. The office of the probate judge has 
lost prestige and power. By 1862 there were thirty-three organized 
and twelve unorganized counties in the State.?? The establishment 
of county seats often led to heated contests among rival towns.*4 
Article twelve, section five, of the constitution, authorized the 
legislature to make provision by general law for the organization of 
cities, towns, and villages. The State legislature retained the act of 
1859, which provided for the incorporation of towns and villages, 
but in 1862, it provided for the incorporation of cities. The latter ————). Ss eee 
30. House Journal, 1861, pp. 395-399, 407. 
31. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, pp. 1062, 1063; see footnote, 
32. General Laws of Kansas, 1861, p. 115. 
33. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, pp. 884-889. 
34. Kansas History Clippings, IV, 50-52. 


## Page 215

The Birth of Kansas 207 
act, pertaining to all cities or municipal corporations of not less than 
7,000 inhabitants, repealed all acts in conflict with its provisions, 
provided for the mayor-council system of government and divided 
cities into four wards. The officers provided for were mayor, treas- 
urer, clerk, and eight councilmen. The mayor was made conserva- 
tor of the peace, and invested with exclusive jurisdiction to hear 
and determine all offenses against the ordinances of the city. The 
council was vested with power to tax and grant licenses, and to 
limit the indebtedness of a city to $25,000 unless two-thirds of the 
electors should decide to increase the debt.?®> The acts dividing the 
cities of Kansas into three classes were not passed until 1868.°6 
The State constitution provided for the establishment of corpora- 
tions but specified that it should be by general laws. This led to 
much litigation in attempts to define the limits of the constitutional 
provisions. 
Article eight made the Governor the commander-in-chief of the 
State militia, which was to be composed of all able-bodied male 
citizens between the ages of twenty-one and forty-five unless ex- 
empted by State or Federal laws. This article authorized the legis- 
lature to determine how the officers should be elected or appointed. 
In 1861, the State legislature passed an act to organize and dis- 
cipline the militia, which authorized the county assessors to enroll 
the names of persons liable to military duty and required the county 
attorney to send a copy of the enrollments to the Adjutant General 
of the State. The militia was to be used to execute the State laws, 
repel invasion, riot, or insurrection, and could be used in case of war. 
A company should select its own officers, and commissioned officers 
were to be elected by those inferior in rank.®” 
Article fourteen of the constitution provided for the process of 
amendments. The legislature was authorized to propose amend- 
ments by a vote of two-thirds of all members elected, and submit 
them to a vote of the people; a majority of the electors voting on 
the proposition would determine its adoption or rejection. The con- 
vention method also was provided. 
All attempts to amend the constitution under Governor Robinson’s 
administration, however, failed in the legislature. Unsuccessful at- 
tempts were made to amend the section pertaining to the origin of 
bills (article two, section twelve) and that pertaining to bank de- 
35. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, pp. 384 f, 398 f. 
36. General Statutes of Kansas, 1868, pp. 129 f, 154 f. 
37. Compiled Laws of Kansas, 1862, pp. 699 ff. 


## Page 216

208 University of Kansas Social Science Studies 
posits. During Governor Carney’s administration article two, sec- 
tion twelve, was amended to its present form. 
Thus the Robinson administration had set in motion the forces 
of government which ultimately were to bring peace, prosperity, 
and happiness to many persons in Kansas. Obstacles, no doubt, 
which had hindered the development of the State carried over into 
later years and were only gradually removed or outlived. Such 
was the case with the factions, graft, “jayhawkers” (men who took 
the law in their own hands), and intemperance. Other problems 
were not fully understood at the time and have only recently re- 
ceived governmental attention, like the problems of the “dust bowl” 
in southwestern Kansas, soil erosion in eastern Kansas, and the gen- 
eral adaptation of agriculture to the prairie and the plains regions. 
The transportation problem is still unsolved. New political prob- 
lems were to arise with the agricultural, industrial, and cultural 
development of the State. These had not been anticipated. Indeed, 
few of the later problems were anticipated, and when they were the 
solutions were largely borrowed from other States. But much of the 
borrowing represented wise choices; and besides, fairly adequate 
provision had been made for education. The citizens had set in 
motion, at least, forces that would help the State in future years to 
cope with its more complex problems.
